Clouds Over Gethsemane

The Impossibility Of It All
Chapter 21: Summer heat
Scene 1
Vigilance
Ian arrived at the office the next morning with the peculiar exhaustion that came from overthinking. The previous evening's revelation had crystallized into cold practicality during the night hours. He was in love with Jay. This was unacceptable. He would manage it appropriately.
The solution was straightforward. Ensure Jay's continued success and happiness within the company and accept that his own discomfort was the necessary price for keeping things stable. He wanted Jay to thrive, to find joy and fulfilment, even if that meant watching from a distance. 
The selfish part of him that craved to keep Jay close warred with the protective instinct that whispered he should transfer Jay to another department, create space for both their sakes. Yet the thought of losing their daily interactions, the comfortable warmth of their established routine, felt unbearable.
He threw himself into work, but his mind was elsewhere. When the team departed for lunch at one of nearby restaurants, he remained at his desk, absorbed in market analysis. He preferred eating alone in the kitchen, but today the thought of food made him nauseous. His meal prep container sat untouched in the company refrigerator.
An hour later, the team returned rejuvenated. Jay moved toward the kitchen, ostensibly to refill his water bottle. He opened the fridge and paused at the sight of Ian's untouched lunch container.
This wasn't normal. In the year Jay had worked here, Ian's habits had been absolutely consistent. The lunch container was always gone by the time they returned to the office.
The afternoon progressed with meetings and conference calls that required Ian's full attention. He welcomed the distraction that prevented his thoughts from wandering to the sounds of Jay working just outside his office door.
A knock interrupted his brooding.
"Come in."
Jay entered, closing the door behind him. "I wanted to check if your lunch wasn't to your liking today."
Ian looked up, confused. "What?"
"You didn't eat lunch. I thought perhaps you'd prefer something different. I could order takeout if you'd like something specific." Jay's tone was matter-of-fact, as if monitoring Ian's eating habits was a part of his responsibilities. "There's a place that makes excellent soup if you're in the mood for something light."
The observation caught Ian completely off guard. He hadn't realized Jay paid attention to such details, much less that he would feel comfortable addressing them directly.
"I..." Ian paused, uncertain how to respond. "Yes, please. The soup sounds fine."
Jay nodded and retreated to his desk to place the order. Ian stared after him, processing what had just occurred. Jay had noticed his skipped meal and taken initiative to remedy it. The gesture was practical and unassuming, yet it represented a level of attentiveness that felt almost intimate.
Thirty minutes later, Jay collected the soup from the delivery service and brought it to the kitchen, along with proper utensils and a cloth napkin from the supply cabinet.
Ian appeared a few minutes later. 
"This looks appetizing," he said, sitting at the table.
The soup was excellent—rich and comforting, exactly what he needed though he hadn't realized it. Jay had returned to his desk, leaving him to eat in solitude as always. As he ate, he dwelled on the strange progression of his relationship with Jay.
Months ago, Jay had approached him with questions about past acquisitions and Ian had rebuffed him sharply. Today, Jay had essentially directed him to eat lunch, and Ian had complied without protest. The change in their dynamic was remarkable, though he couldn't pinpoint exactly when it had occurred.
The care Jay had shown was sincere and undemanding. No expectation of gratitude or acknowledgement, just attention to Ian's well-being. It was this thoughtfulness that made Jay so valuable as an assistant.
When he returned to his office, Jay was organizing files. Their eyes met briefly, and Jay smiled before returning to his work.
Ian sat at his desk, unsettled by how good it felt to be looked after in such simple ways. Jay probably considered meal monitoring part of his general duties, but the warmth it produced was dangerous. Ian wanted to protect that gentle consideration, to ensure Jay never lost the confidence that allowed him to offer help so naturally.
The thought produced both affection and despair in equal measure. This careful, honest young man deserved to be cherished and protected. Ian wanted to shield him from every possible harm. But doing so would cross lines that existed for good reasons.
Meanwhile, Jay returned to his filing with growing concern. The skipped lunch wasn't an isolated incident—he had noticed Ian's distraction throughout the day, the way he seemed to be avoiding casual interaction more than usual.
He glanced at the gleaming watch on his wrist. Ian’s unexpected gift had been accompanied by an even more surprising apology, an expression of regret for past oversights and missteps. The memory of Ian’s hesitant sincerity troubled Jay deeply, and now today’s skipped lunch and increasingly avoidant behaviour only added to his growing mental tally of concerns. Each unusual action felt like another warning sign.
Combined with Ian's adamant refusal to take vacation time, the pattern indicated stress levels that could become problematic. Ian's work ethic was already intense enough to worry Jay. If he started skipping meals on top of everything else, he would make himself seriously ill.
Jay had grown comfortable enough with their working relationship to address the lunch situation directly, something that would have been unthinkable months earlier. The realization surprised him. When had he stopped feeling that reverent distance that once made Ian seem untouchable? When had concern for Ian's health become something he felt entitled to express?
The transformation had occurred so gradually he hadn't noticed it happening. Now Ian felt less like an intimidating superior and more like... Jay wasn't sure how to categorize it. Someone worth looking after. Someone who mattered to him.
He would need to be more vigilant about Ian's habits. Discrete monitoring of lunch consumption, gentle reminders about breaks, perhaps even strategic scheduling of meetings to prevent excessively long work days. Jay glanced toward Ian's office. The soup had obviously helped, which validated Jay's intervention. Someone needed to ensure he took proper care of himself, and Jay was positioned to notice details that others might miss.
Ian remained in his office, and Jay stayed as well, finishing reports that weren't strictly urgent. At six, he prepared a glass of fresh lemonade from the kitchen supplies—unsweetened, with ginger and mint from the herb selection he'd suggested for the office.
He knocked on Ian's door frame again.
"I thought you might like something refreshing."
Ian accepted the glass without question this time. "Thank you."
"It's all natural ingredients. Ginger and mint are good for fatigue."
He left the lemonade on Ian's desk and returned to his work, remembering how Ian had once prepared ginger tea for him when he'd felt unwell. The gesture had meant more than Ian could have known—a sign that someone in this office actually cared about his welfare.
Now, months later, Jay wanted to offer the same consideration.
Scene 2
Planned departures
Ian made a point of eating his packed lunch every day for the rest of the week. The container disappeared punctually during the team's restaurant visits, which seemed to ease the watchful concern in Jay's expression.
As days passed, Ian's initial panic about his feelings lessened. Nothing fundamental had changed, he told himself. The longing had been present for months, he had simply stopped pretending it didn't exist. Recognition didn't require action.
By Thursday, he'd evaluated the projected workload through December and identified a window. Late October showed the lightest commitments, with most major quarterly reviews completed and year-end preparations still weeks away.
Friday afternoon, he called Jay into his office.
"I've decided to take some time off. Last week of October."
Jay's face lit up immediately. "That's wonderful. A full week?"
"Seven days. Monday through Friday."
"I'll submit my request for the same period." Jay's enthusiasm was unmistakable. "A week seems short, but it's much better than nothing."
Ian felt warmth at Jay's obvious pleasure in his decision. "You don't have to coordinate your schedule with mine."
"I'd rather take time when you do. Thank you for being reasonable about this."
That weekend, Jay threw himself into vacation planning. He browsed travel websites, eventually deciding on a week-long tropical getaway that included a beginner diving certification course.
The price made him pause—a year ago, such an expense would have been impossible. But his financial stability had grown along with his confidence, and the cost fell within reasonable limits.
He booked the trip Sunday evening, then researched local diving schools to supplement the vacation training. Zoe had mentioned several reputable instructors during her own diving stories, and Jay found classes that would prepare him for the more intensive tropical course.
Monday brought Zoe's return, bronzed and energetic from her underwater adventures. The team gathered at lunch, eager for details about her expedition.
"The coral formations were incredible," she said, showing photos on her phone. "Visibility was perfect, water temperature ideal. I spent two days just photographing reef fish."
"How deep did you go?" Luc asked.
"Forty meters on the wall dives. But the best sites were actually shallower—more marine life activity."
Jay looked at the images with fascination. "I booked a certification course myself. Tropical location, beginner program."
"Really?" Zoe's expression brightened. "Where?"
"Lagoon Belt. Week-long package with classroom and open water training."
"That's ambitious for a first course. Most people start with pool sessions."
"They include preliminary training. And I found local classes here to prepare."
Zoe pulled out her phone again. "Which school? I know several excellent instructors in the city."
Jay showed her the diving centre's website, and Zoe nodded approvingly.
"I know someone who teaches there. She's patient with beginners but maintains high safety standards."
"Would you like to come to my first lesson? This Thursday evening."
"Absolutely. We can grab dinner afterwards and discuss equipment basics."
Around the table, the team exchanged glances. Zoe's mentoring relationship with Jay was starting to approach friendship, a development that was fairly unexpected.
Amy returned on Monday as well, bringing the team back to full capacity just as Luc and Max prepared for their own departures. The office was fully staffed for five days before the next round of absences began.
Jay had grown so accustomed to extended hours that he remained late even with full coverage. Tuesday evening, when he typically left promptly for the gym, he stayed until past six o'clock.
Ian noticed the deviation. "Did you quit the gym?"
Jay looked up from his computer. "No, I moved my schedule an hour later. I'll go straight from here."
He opened his office closet to reveal a gym bag. "More convenient than going home first."
"How is your routine progressing?"
Jay's expression turned sheepish. "I'm far from fit, but definitely stronger than a few months ago. The trainer says my form has improved."
Ian was curious about this aspect of Jay's life. He'd spent months maintaining distance, but what purpose did that serve now? He was already compromised. These small insights into Jay's daily existence were the only intimacy he could permit himself.
"What type of training do you focus on?"
"Basic strength training, some cardio. Nothing sophisticated. The gym I chose is more practical than impressive, but it suits my needs."
"Consistency matters more than intensity."
"That's what I keep telling myself when I'm struggling with weights that probably seem trivial to most people."
The self-deprecation was typical of Jay, but Ian detected something else underneath the modest assessment. Jay was proud of his progress, even if he wouldn't admit it directly.
"Building habits takes discipline," Ian said. "The results will follow."
Jay smiled at the encouragement. "Thank you. I should get going if I want to make my session time."
"Have a good workout."
"Thank you."
Ian watched Jay gather his belongings and head toward the elevators. The brief conversation had revealed details about Jay's life that felt almost intimate in their ordinariness—gym habits, personal goals, small victories in self-improvement.
He wanted to know more. What other changes had Jay made in his life over the past year? What did he do on weekends? How did he spend his evenings when he wasn't working late?
The curiosity was dangerous but irresistible. Ian was already emotionally invested, seeking these small glimpses into Jay's world couldn't make the situation worse. If he couldn't have Jay in any meaningful way, at least he could indulge in learning about the person who had become so important to him.
What harm could a few casual questions cause? He was already in too deep for caution to matter, and he intended to take advantage of every opportunity Jay offered.
The decision felt like a small surrender, but also like relief. He would ask about Jay's diving lessons, his vacation plans, his weekend activities. He would show interest in Jay's life because he cared, and because denying that care had become more exhausting than acknowledging it.
He could find peace in that. Love didn't require possession. It could exist as pure wish for another person's flourishing, even if that flourishing led them away from him. Perhaps especially then.
Scene 3
Diving lesson
The full staff roster meant Jay could leave on Thursday promptly at five without guilt—a reprieve that wouldn't return until September when vacation season concluded. Zoe met him at the elevator, holding a waterproof duffel bag containing serious diving equipment.
"Ready for this?" she asked as they descended toward the lobby.
"As ready as I can be for voluntarily submerging myself in water while breathing through a tube."
"That's the spirit."
The dive centre had multiple training pools of varying depths and a classroom area filled with equipment displays. Jay felt his nervousness increase as they approached the facility.
"Remember," Zoe said as they entered, "you've done this before. Today is just building proper foundations."
The instructor, a weathered woman named Ina who clearly knew Zoe well, guided Jay through equipment basics. Mask fitting, regulator breathing, buoyancy control—all concepts Jay recognized but still needed to practice.
"Don't overthink it," Ina advised as Jay struggled with buoyancy in the shallow end. "Your body knows how to float."
Zoe accompanied him in the water but left instruction to Ina, offering encouragement from a distance. Jay appreciated the balance—moral support without interference. His movements were clumsy and his breathing occasionally erratic, but the panic from his first diving experience was notably absent.
By the end of the session, Jay had managed several controlled descents to the pool's deepest section and felt moderately confident about basic skills. Not graceful, but functional enough to imagine enjoying the activity.
"Much better than I expected," Ina concluded as they returned equipment. "September lessons will build on this foundation nicely."
"How many more sessions would you recommend before my trip?" Jay asked.
"Four or five should give you solid confidence for open water diving. "
"You might consider adding pool time for general water comfort," Zoe said as they changed back into street clothes. "There's excellent facilities throughout the city."
"That's a good idea. I want to be completely prepared."
Zoe proposed dinner at a nearby restaurant to discuss the experience, and Jay readily agreed. The invitation felt natural after their successful lesson, though he realized he knew very little about Zoe beyond her office persona.
"You made that look effortless," Jay observed over appetizers. "How long have you been diving?"
"Since college. Being a city kid, it was the only way to experience anything resembling nature."
"You're from here originally?"
"Born and raised. Third generation native, actually, which makes me practically prehistoric by local standards."
"I still feel awkward about living here. Small town to big city isn't exactly seamless."
"Nobody cares where you're from. Trust me, half our office moved here from somewhere else. You blend in perfectly now."
The reassurance was unexpectedly comforting. Jay had assumed his origins were more obvious than they apparently were.
"Speaking of blending in," Jay said, "I've been apartment hunting. Any neighbourhood recommendations?"
"What's your priority? Short commute, nightlife, quiet residential?"
"Definitely short commute. I spend enough time at the office that I'd prefer not to waste hours traveling. But it still needs to be something I can afford."
Zoe launched into detailed advice about areas with convenient metro access that remained reasonably priced. Her knowledge of the city was encyclopedic, covering everything from grocery stores to safety considerations.
"Avoid anything within walking distance of the financial district unless you enjoy paying exorbitant prices for a closet," she warned. "But there are some great neighbourhoods three stops out that give you space without sacrificing convenience."
Jay enjoyed the conversation. Zoe was more talkative and personable than in the office, with practical insights about city living that Jay hadn't considered. Their conversation eventually returned to his upcoming trip, covering travel experiences, equipment recommendations, and underwater photography techniques.
"I never realized you were so adventurous," Jay said.
"Most people don't. I prefer keeping work and personal interests separate."
"But you made an exception tonight."
"You're interested in diving, not just looking for another social activity."
"This has been really helpful," Jay said as they prepared to leave. "Thank you for coming along."
"My pleasure. Text me after your next lesson—I want to hear how the skills progression goes."
Meanwhile, Ian remained in the office well past normal hours, ostensibly reviewing client portfolios but actually achieving very little productive work. The afternoon had dragged without Jay, punctuated by mundane tasks that felt more tedious than usual.
He knew Jay was at his diving lesson with Zoe, pursuing an activity that would enhance his upcoming vacation. This was positive development—Jay expanding his interests, building friendships with colleagues, gaining confidence in new skills.
Ian should feel pleased about Jay's growth and happiness. Instead, he wished he could be the one accompanying Jay to new experiences, sharing in discoveries and celebrations. The thought of Jay laughing with Zoe, discussing vacation plans, produced discomfort that Ian recognized as dangerously close to jealousy.
The realization was unwelcome. He wanted to be part of Jay's life. Instead, he sat alone in an empty office, pretending to work while actually counting the hours until Jay returned tomorrow morning. By seven o'clock, Ian abandoned any pretence of productivity and headed home, taking with him work that would likely remain untouched for the remainder of the evening.
Back in his apartment, Jay researched swimming facilities near the office. He found a modern complex with extended hours, including evening sessions that would accommodate his irregular schedule. Pool time could supplement diving lessons while improving general fitness.
The schedule would require planning around his extended work hours, but Jay didn't mind. Tuesday afternoons would remain reserved for gym sessions, while other evenings could incorporate swimming when he managed to leave by reasonable hours.
The gym training had given him confidence in his physical capabilities that extended beyond simple strength improvement. Adding swimming felt like a natural progression.
He booked a trial membership for the following week, already anticipating the additional preparation it would provide for his diving adventure. The diving lesson had reinforced his excitement about the upcoming vacation while highlighting areas for improvement.
Jay fell asleep reviewing certification requirements and equipment lists. The lesson had gone better than expected, Zoe had proven an unexpectedly compatible companion, and his vacation planning was proceeding smoothly.
Scene 4
Sunset brooding
Ian arrived at the office Friday morning to find Jay already at his desk, reviewing client correspondence. The sight produced a flutter of warmth that Ian immediately recognized as absurd—he had missed Jay for exactly one afternoon, yet his relief at seeing him again felt disproportionate to the brief separation.
"Good morning," Jay said cheerfully, looking up from his work.
Ian's heart responded with a skip. The ridiculousness of pining like a Victorian widow over a few hours apart wasn't lost on him, but awareness didn't diminish the feeling.
"Good morning," Ian replied, and instead of proceeding directly to his office, he paused beside Jay's desk. "How did the diving lesson go yesterday?"
Jay responded with evident enthusiasm. "Better than expected. I managed not to panic underwater, which feels like progress. The instructor thinks I won't drown on my vacation, which is reassuring."
"That's encouraging."
"I'm also planning to add swimming. There's a pool with convenient hours near the office, so I can fit in sessions when my schedule allows."
"Swimming is excellent preparation for diving. Building comfort in water translates well to underwater activities."
"That's what Zoe mentioned. She's been very helpful with practical advice."
The brief conversation concluded naturally, but Ian retained the warmth of it throughout the morning. He savoured these small moments of connection, finding comfort in the glimpses into Jay's life that felt intimate simply because they were so casual.
Jay returned his attention to his computer, unconsciously touching the watch face. The weight on his wrist still felt alien. He had never worn jewellery of any kind, finding accessories excessive and unnecessary. Now the constant reminder of Ian's unexpected generosity felt increasingly significant.
Throughout the day, Ian noticed Jay's expanded social interactions with their colleagues. Previously, Luc had been Jay's primary conversational partner for casual office exchanges. Now Jay chatted with Zoe as well—brief discussions about diving techniques, city neighbourhoods, weekend plans that Ian couldn't help overhearing from his adjacent office.
"Here's my number," Zoe said during one such conversation. "Text me about those pool recommendations I mentioned."
Ian felt an unreasonable stab of jealousy at the casual exchange of personal contact information. He had Jay's number from HR files, but technical access felt entirely different from the personal gesture of voluntary sharing. As Jay's supervisor, he couldn't simply request personal numbers without justification. The jealousy was irrational and petty, but it left Ian feeling moody for the remainder of the afternoon.
As evening approached, Max's upcoming vacation created additional preparation demands that kept both Ian and Jay working past normal hours. Ian deliberately extended tasks that could have been completed more efficiently, reluctant to end the day and lose Jay's company.
By eight o'clock, Jay appeared tired and hungry, gathering his belongings with the obvious intention of departing. He approached Ian's office door, which had remained open throughout the day.
"I'm heading out," Jay said. "Are you planning to work much later?"
Ian glanced at his computer screen, where no meaningful work was actually in progress. "I want to finish something I'm working on. Don't wait on my account."
The lie came easily, though Ian immediately felt guilty about it. He wasn't working on anything pressing, he was simply stalling because he wanted to preserve these moments for as long as possible.
"Have a good evening," Jay said.
"You too."
Ian watched Jay collect his things and head toward the elevators, fighting the impulse to call him back with some fabricated urgent task. After Jay disappeared from view, Ian remained at his desk, staring at the city skyline visible through his office windows.
Two full days without seeing Jay. Forty-eight hours of wondering what he was doing, whether he was enjoying his time off, if he thought about work—about Ian—at all during his personal hours.
Ian had never particularly enjoyed weekends, preferring the structure and purpose of office. Now he was beginning to actively dread them.
The August heat had been oppressive all week. The evening sun was beginning its descent toward the horizon, casting long shadows across the financial district. The sky held that particular quality of late summer light—golden and heavy, suggesting the gradual approach of autumn despite the continued warmth.
As the sun sank lower, painting the office in amber light, Ian acknowledged the futility of his position. He was in love with his assistant, jealous of his friendships, and inventing reasons to extend their shared time. None of it was sustainable, yet he couldn't imagine changing course.
The sunset continued its slow progression, and Ian continued watching, lost in thoughts that offered no solutions to his increasingly complicated feelings.
Jay rode the metro home with lingering concern about Ian's work habits. Staying past eight on Friday evening seemed excessive even by Turner & Associates standards, particularly when no urgent deadlines justified the extended hours.
Back in his apartment, Jay reheated leftover dinner and took a quick shower, exhaustion overtaking him as the week's activities caught up. As he prepared for bed, his eyes fell on the watch he had placed on his nightstand.
The anniversary gift still felt impossibly extravagant, something he probably shouldn't have accepted despite Ian's insistence. The monetary value remained staggering, but the expense wasn't what made the gift significant. It was the fact that Ian had chosen it, had taken time to consider what Jay might appreciate, had wanted to acknowledge their working relationship with something meaningful and permanent.
Jay fell asleep thinking about Ian's earnest apology, the careful way he had presented the gift, the concern he had shown for Jay's well-being throughout their year together. The watch had come from Ian, and that made it precious for reasons that had nothing to do with its cost.
Scene 5
Weekend exploration
On Saturday the heat was so oppressive that it made even the gym feel stifling despite its air conditioning. Jay completed his routine, eager to finish weekend chores before the temperature became truly unbearable. By noon, he had managed laundry, grocery shopping, and basic apartment maintenance, leaving the afternoon free for apartment hunting.
He texted Zoe for recommendations about which areas to prioritize, expecting a brief response with suggestions. Instead, she offered to accompany him on his exploration, which surprised Jay but also pleased him. A local's perspective would be invaluable for understanding neighbourhood dynamics he might miss entirely.
"I know the city better than any real estate website. Plus I have strong opinions about local amenities," she had replied.
They met at a metro station Zoe had selected for its central location among the areas Jay wanted to explore. She arrived with a water bottle, comfortable walking shoes, and a practical shoulder bag, clearly experienced with extended urban hiking.
She indicated the map on her phone. "Strategic planning. We can cover four neighbourhoods efficiently if we're systematic about it."
The heat was brutal, but Jay appreciated seeing potential areas under challenging conditions. If a neighbourhood felt acceptable during August's worst weather, it would be pleasant during milder seasons.
They spent the afternoon moving between neighbourhoods, riding metro lines to different stops and walking through residential areas that offered varying combinations of affordability, accessibility, and livability. Zoe provided running commentary about safety considerations and transportation reliability that Jay would never have discovered through online research alone.
"That building looks nice from the outside," Jay observed about a council house with attractive brick facades.
"Terrible insulation and no decent grocery stores within walking distance," Zoe replied immediately. "Trust me, aesthetic appeal isn't worth freezing in winter or living on takeout."
Several buildings caught Jay's attention as promising options. He made notes about agents to contact once September arrived and his work schedule became more manageable.
By evening, they were both exhausted from hours of walking in relentless heat. Zoe suggested dinner at a restaurant in one of the neighbourhoods Jay had found most appealing—a vegetarian place with outdoor seating and fans that created tolerable conditions despite the temperature.
Jay agreed readily, though vegetarian cuisine wasn't typically his preference. The restaurant was small and welcoming, with an extensive menu that was more appealing than he had anticipated.
"This is actually really good," he admitted, sampling a grain bowl with interesting spice combinations.
"You sound surprised."
"I default to more conventional food choices. But this has a lot more flavour than I expected."
Over dinner, their conversation drifted from apartment hunting to work experiences and personal backgrounds. Jay was relaxed, perhaps due to the informal setting or Zoe's easy-going demeanour.
"Can I ask why you applied to Turner & Associates specifically? It's not exactly an obvious choice for someone without extensive finance background," Zoe asked.
"Honestly? Ian's reputation was a major factor. His management style seemed principled and straightforward, without the complications I'd encountered elsewhere."
"Complications?"
"Previous supervisors who didn't maintain appropriate boundaries. Ian's known for hating office romance, which made the position feel safer."
Zoe nodded with understanding. "That was exactly my reasoning. Being openly gay can be tricky, but Ian's reputation made Turner & Associates feel like a place where I wouldn't have to worry about my sexuality becoming an issue."
The casual revelation surprised Jay, though he tried not to show it. "I'm glad the environment has lived up to expectations."
"It has, mostly because Ian really does provide excellent protection from workplace drama. I hope that doesn't make you uncomfortable. I'm private about personal details, but you seem like someone I can trust."
"Not uncomfortable at all. I may be from a small town, but I'm not quite that provincial."
Zoe visibly relaxed. "I tend to keep my head down out of habit. You never know who might have hidden prejudices, even in supposedly progressive environments. But I felt safe with you because, well, I assumed you were gay as well."
Jay felt heat rise in his face. "I don't really think I'm gay."
"Oh." Zoe looked mortified. "I'm sorry, I shouldn't have assumed—"
"It's alright, honestly. Not a big deal. I'm just not interested in dating anyone, truth be told."
Zoe's expression brightened with recognition. "Ah, so you're ace! That's wonderful. It's nice having one of us queers on the team."
Jay had never heard the term applied to himself before, but it didn't feel wrong. "I never thought much about it. There were always other priorities."
That wasn't entirely true. He had attempted romance back in college, but it had ended in such spectacular failure that he had never felt comfortable exploring that territory again. The memory still made him uncomfortable years later, but it wasn't something he was prepared to confess to anyone, let alone a coworker he was meeting privately for only the second time.
"Fair enough. Labels aren't for everyone anyway."
They continued talking as the evening cooled slightly, bonding over their shared appreciation for Ian's protective aura at work. Both had sought refuge in his reputation, and both had found exactly the security they needed.
"He really is impenetrable steel wall against workplace nonsense," Zoe observed. "I've never felt more comfortable in an office environment."
"Same. He creates this bubble of professionalism that keeps everything else at bay."
"Thanks for spending your Saturday on this. I learned more about these neighbourhoods in one afternoon than I would have managed in weeks of research," Jay said as they prepared to leave.
"It was fun. Let me know when you're ready to look at actual units. I have strong opinions about building management, too."
Jay left the restaurant feeling satisfied with both his apartment prospects and his expanding social circle. Having an actual friendship with a coworker was a new experience, but one he found remarkably pleasant.
Chapter 22: Schemer and imp
Scene 1
Travel anxiety
On Monday morning Jay sat at his desk, staring at his phone screen for the third time in ten minutes. The email from Adventure Diving Tours sat in his inbox like an unexploded device, full of passport requirements, flight information, and medical forms that needed completion.
What had seemed like such a brilliant idea last week now felt monumentally stupid. He had booked the October diving trip in a moment of euphoria, riding high on successfully convincing Ian to take actual time off—a victory that had felt impossible just months ago. The confidence he felt during that conversation had evaporated completely.
"Morning, Jay." Amy's greeting pulled him from his spiraling thoughts.
"Good morning." He managed what he hoped was a normal smile and buried himself in spreadsheets. Max and Luc had started their holidays, leaving the rest of the team to shoulder their workload. The extra hours were a welcome distraction, even if they meant staying past eight most nights.
The morning crawled by. During the daily briefing with Ian he struggled to concentrate. Every time he tried to focus on client reports, his mind wandered back to the same terrifying thoughts. He had never been on a plane. He had never left the country. He had barely traveled beyond the city limits since he moved here. What had possessed him to think he could handle flying solo to a foreign country and jumping off a boat into deep water?
Ian emerged from his office around eleven, scanning the department. His eyes lingered on Jay's desk for a moment longer than usual before he moved toward the conference room.
Jay's phone buzzed with another email from the dive company asking about dietary restrictions and emergency contacts. The panic crept higher in his chest.
Tuesday brought no relief, but there wasn't time to worry about October when August was trying to kill them all with overtime. Jay threw himself into the extra responsibilities, tackling reports that normally would have been distributed among the team.
At six o'clock sharp, he packed up and headed to the gym, holding on to the habit that had become his anchor. The weight of the barbells usually cleared his head, but today even Rus noticed something was off.
"You're pushing too hard today," his trainer said, watching Jay attempt another set despite his obvious fatigue. "What's going on?"
"Just work stuff," Jay lied, racking the weights with hands that trembled slightly. "The usual deadlines."
"Whatever's eating at you, the weights aren't going to solve it. Maybe try talking to someone who actually has ears instead of iron plates."
Jay managed a weak smile but said nothing. How could he explain that the person he most wanted to talk to was the one person he couldn't burden with his pathetic fears?
On Wednesday evening Jay and Ian were alone in the office again, the fluorescent lights casting a harsh glow over empty desks. The building had fallen into its nighttime quiet, broken only by the occasional ping of the elevator.
Jay stared at his computer screen, but the numbers weren't making sense anymore. The diving company had sent yet another email, this one with a packing list that included things he'd never heard of. He put his hands over his face and let out a long sigh.
"Jay? Is something wrong?" 
Jay startled, dropping his hands and looking up to find Ian standing beside his desk. Jay hadn't heard him approach. Ian's suit jacket was gone, his tie loosened slightly, sleeves rolled up to his forearms. It was as casual as Jay had ever seen him during work hours.
Jay laughed bitterly. "No, nothing's wrong. I might have just bitten off more than I can chew with this October trip."
Ian's eyebrows drew together slightly. "The diving course? You seemed excited."
"I was excited. I am excited. It's just..." Jay gestured helplessly at his phone. "I booked this trip thinking I was being so brave and adventurous, but Ian, I've never even been on a plane. I've never left the country. I don't know what I was thinking. They're asking for all these forms and requirements, and I keep staring at them thinking I'm going to mess something up or panic halfway through the flight or—"
"Jay. You're catastrophizing."
"Am I? Because it feels pretty realistic from where I'm sitting."
Ian moved closer, sitting on the chair across from Jay's desk. "You're learning to dive. You'll pass the basic  certifications. Just like you've handled every challenge this job has thrown at you."
"That's different. That's work. This is..." Jay trailed off, struggling to articulate the depth of his anxiety.
"This is you expanding your world. Which is exactly what you should be doing."
The confidence in Ian's words made Jay feel calmer. He had grown to trust Ian's judgment implicitly over the past year.
"I suppose I'll figure it out," Jay said finally.
Ian was quiet for a long moment, watching Jay with an expression that was difficult to read. "Yes, you will," he said eventually. 
"You've probably been flying since you were five."
"Seven. My first flight was for a mountain skiing holiday. I was terrified the plane would fall out of the sky."
"Really?"
"I refused to look out the window for the entire flight. My mother had to bribe me with chocolate just to stop me crying during take-off."
The image of a young Ian crying on a plane was so incongruous with the composed man standing before him that Jay almost smiled despite his anxiety. "Did it get better?"
"Yes, with practice. And realizing that being afraid of something doesn't mean you can't do it anyway."
Jay nodded, though his stomach was still churning. "I keep telling myself that. It's just hard when everyone expects you to know what you're doing."
"No one expects that, Jay. And anyone who does isn't worth worrying about."
There was something in Ian's voice that made Jay look up at him. Ian was watching him with an expression Jay couldn't quite read, looking softer than usual.
"Thank you," Jay said.
Thursday and Friday flew by, consumed by overtime and catch-up work. Jay managed to fill out most of the diving forms, though the flight details still made his stomach clench with anxiety. He had almost succeeded in pushing the entire issue to the back of his mind when Ian called him into his office Friday afternoon.
"Close the door, please," Ian said.
Jay complied, sitting on the chair across from Ian's desk. 
"I've arranged a business trip to the New Continent for the end of September. Garnet Ridge Group has expressed interest in our services, and their CEO is an old university acquaintance. The timing works well—it's before the chaos of quarter-end in October, and the matter has been on my list long enough."
"That sounds like something Leo would typically handle."
"Under normal circumstances, yes. But given my personal connection to their leadership, direct involvement seems more appropriate. More importantly, I believe this presents an excellent training opportunity for you."
"Training opportunity?"
"Business travel will likely become a requirement at some point in your role. Instead of waiting for an urgent situation to arise, it seems prudent to provide you with that experience during a lower-stakes engagement. Administration will handle all logistics. Your responsibilities would mirror those you've successfully managed in previous client meetings."
Jay felt his pulse quicken, but not with panic. Instead, relief began to unfold in his stomach. "You want me to come with you?"
"I believe the experience would be valuable for your development. The meeting itself is straightforward, and having you handle the standard preparation and documentation would be beneficial for both parties."
"Should I prepare anything specific?"
"I'll brief you on Garnet Ridge Group next week. For now, just ensure your passport is current."
"It is." Jay hesitated, then added, "This timing is incredibly helpful, actually. Having some international travel experience before my diving trip will probably make everything less terrifying."
"It is rather convenient, yes."
Walking back to his desk, Jay felt lighter than he had all week. The October trip still loomed, but now it felt manageable. He had weeks to prepare, and he'd have Ian there to guide him through his first international experience.
Behind his closed office door, Ian stared at his computer screen without seeing it. The relief in Jay's eyes had been worth every string he'd pulled to arrange this trip on short notice. The fact that he was being completely transparent about his motivations—and that Jay seemed oblivious to the deeper implications—only reinforced what Ian already knew about Jay's honest nature.
He was walking a dangerous path, using his position to ease Jay's personal anxiety. But watching Jay struggle alone with something Ian could easily help him through had been unbearable. This was the closest he could come to the support he truly wanted to offer without crossing lines that couldn't be uncrossed.
That he would get to spend several days in Jay's company, watching over him and ensuring his comfort, felt like a reward he had claimed for himself, one he knew he had little right to.
But Jay's smile would sustain him through whatever pretences he'd have to maintain. Sometimes, love meant finding ways to care from the shadows, and Ian was becoming an expert at loving Jay Hall in precisely that way.
Scene 2
Wishful thinking
Ian sat in his home office, scrolling through hotel listings on his laptop while the Garnet Ridge files lay scattered across the coffee table. He clicked through photos, searching for somewhere comfortable but not intimate—welcoming and cosy, without the risk of seeming romantic. Other tabs were open with flight schedules and a city map. He took notes on arrival procedures, ground transportation, every detail that might ease the anxiety he’d seen in Jay. Nothing could be left to chance.
The doorbell rang, interrupting his research. Ian frowned at his watch—nearly nine on a Saturday evening. He wasn't expecting anyone. The doorbell rang again, followed by an impatient knock.
"Ian, I know you're home. Your car's in the garage."
Leo's voice could be heard through the door. Ian sighed and set down his papers.
"Good evening, Leo." Ian opened the door to find his friend holding a bottle of expensive scotch and wearing that particular grin that meant trouble. "To what do I owe this unexpected visit?"
"Can't a man drop by to see his dearest friend?" Leo pushed past Ian into the apartment, already making himself at home. "I heard that Garnet Ridge Group suddenly became urgent enough to warrant your personal attention. Strange, considering that it's been sitting in your maybe-someday file for months."
Ian closed the door and followed Leo to the living room, where his friend was already examining the spread of documents.
"Business priorities change. You know this."
"Mm-hmm." Leo set the scotch on the side table and sat down in Ian's armchair like he owned it. "And the fact that you're personally handling what should be my territory has nothing to do with wanting some quality alone time with your assistant?"
"I don't know what you're implying."
"Oh, I think you do. Ian Hagen, the man who delegates travel to subordinates as a matter of principle, suddenly decides that an unimportant client meeting requires his personal touch. Along with his assistant, naturally."
"Think whatever you want to think."
Leo's delighted laughter filled the apartment. "There it is! The classic Ian deflection. You might as well have tattooed 'I'm planning a romantic getaway' across your forehead."
"Are you going to open that bottle, or did you bring it as decoration?"
"Changing the subject already?" But Leo obliged, producing glasses from Ian's kitchen. "I'm just saying, if you're planning to wine and dine young Mr. Hall in exotic locations, you might want to book somewhere with a decent spa. Maybe one of those places with couples' treatments."
"It's a business trip."
"Of course it is." Leo poured generous measures of scotch and handed one to Ian. "I'm sure the romantic sunset dinners and moonlit walks on the beach are just coincidental."
Ian accepted the glass and took a measured sip, Leo's commentary washing over him like background noise. His friend had always been incorrigible, and years of experience had taught Ian that engagement only encouraged him.
"You know what I love about this? You're actually living again instead of hiding behind that fortress of yours. It's been what, five years since you showed interest in anything beyond quarterly reports and gym schedules?"
"I'm ensuring a team member receives appropriate guidance."
"Right. Ian, I've been watching you two for months. The way you smooth every possible bump in his path. It's not mentorship, it's devotion."
Ian's jaw tightened, but he kept his voice level. "Jay is a valuable employee who deserves support."
"Jay is a valuable employee you're completely gone over. You know, you should probably be gentle with the poor puppy. Jay's so desperately in love with you that if you're too distant during your little trip, you might break his heart."
"Don't be ridiculous."
"Ridiculous? Ian, everyone can see it. The boy's been head over heels since day one. Honestly, it's sweet that the romance is finally progressing."
"Jay's feelings toward me is gratitude for a stable work environment. Given his previous trauma, he's hardly likely to develop romantic feelings for a boss."
"Previous trauma that you've spent over a year healing with your patient, soothing care? Oh, my naive friend. You really don't see it, do you? The way he watches you when he thinks no one's looking? The way he hangs on your every word? The absolute devastation when he thought you didn't trust him?"
"That was about respect—"
"Respect my ass. He doesn't look at anyone else the way he looks at you. Hell, he barely looks at anyone else at all. Ian, the way that man looks at you isn't gratitude. It's adoration. He lights up like the sun when you give him the smallest bit of approval—"
"He's conscientious and eager to please. That's what makes him an excellent assistant."
"And the way he worries about you? Makes sure you eat lunch? Stays late just to keep you company? You're brilliant at reading markets and people, but you're completely blind when it comes to Jay Hall."
"You're seeing what you want to see."
"Am I? Then explain why he turns pink every time you compliment his work. Why he stays late whenever you do, even when there's no reason for it."
"He's dedicated to his job."
"He's dedicated to you." Leo drained his glass and reached for the bottle. "Mark my words, this trip is going to be very educational for both of you. Might want to pack some extra supplies. A liter of good lube should—"
"Get out."
Leo burst into laughter at Ian's sharp tone. "Struck a nerve, did I? When was the last time you even let yourself think about him that way?"
Ian stood and moved to the window, his back to his friend. "Leave. Now. I think you've had enough entertainment for one evening."
"All right, all right. I'll behave." But Leo's voice still held traces of amusement. "Just promise me you'll be kind to him, Ian. Whatever happens on this trip."
Ian remained silent, watching the city lights blur and sharpen as he tried to ignore the heat that had started burning low in his belly at Leo's words. The image his friend had painted was both impossible and unbearably tempting.
"There's nothing to be kind about. It's a business trip."
"If you say so." Leo finished his second drink and stood. "But Ian, seriously—I'm happy for you. You found someone worth caring about again. Don't let fear rob you of something real just because you're terrified of making the same mistakes."
"The situation is nothing like before."
"No, it's not. Jay isn't playing games or angling for advantage. That boy would walk through fire for you." Leo paused at the door. "Maybe it's time you stopped punishing yourself for someone else's sins."
Ian waited until he heard the front door close before allowing himself to slump against the window. Leo's words echoed in his mind, painting pictures that Ian had spent months trying to suppress. The idea that Jay might feel something beyond respect was both thrilling and terrifying.
But no. Leo was wrong. He had to be wrong. Jay was innocent, earnest, and nothing more. Anything else was projection, wishful thinking, dangerous fantasy.
Ian returned to his papers, but the numbers swam before his eyes. Leo's crude suggestion hung in the air like smoke, heating his blood. The scotch burned as he finished his glass, but it did nothing to cool the fire Leo's words had ignited.
Ian poured himself another glass and tried to return to his research, but he couldn't concentrate. His mind kept spiraling into territories he'd forbidden himself to explore.
He imagined Jay in a hotel room, nervous about his first international trip, turning to Ian for reassurance. The thought of comforting him, of Jay's warmth and trust, led to darker imaginings—Jay's gray eyes looking up at him with something deeper than gratitude, hands reaching for him, that soft voice whispering his name.
Ian finished his drink and tried to prepare for bed, but his body betrayed him with its urgent response to thoughts he'd suppressed for months. In the darkness of his bedroom, he finally allowed himself to imagine Jay's pale skin beneath his hands.
The release, when it came, was intense—months of control shattered by one evening of Leo's meddling. Ian lay in the aftermath, breathing hard and staring at the ceiling, knowing he'd crossed a line in his mind that he could never uncross.
Scene 3
Meddling
Jay was gathering reports for the weekly review in the print room Monday morning when Leo appeared so suddenly in the doorway that Jay almost dropped the papers. Leo shut the door behind him.
"Perfect timing. I was hoping to catch you."
"Leo. Did you need something printed?" Jay said warily. Leo's unexpected appearances often meant some form of chaos.
"We need to talk about your upcoming international debut."
"Is there something specific about the Garnet Ridge meeting?"
"Since you'll be our international face alongside Ian, administration needs to arrange some appointments for you."
"Appointments?"
"Tailor and styling. Business expenses, naturally." Leo's said as if this were procedure. "You've done excellent work upgrading your wardrobe—those suits look fantastic on you—but international travel requires special considerations. You need to be absolutely impeccable, match Ian's level of presentation."
The compliment about his appearance made Jay's cheeks warm slightly. Over the past year, he'd invested in better clothing, understanding that his role required a certain standard. It seemed he wasn't wrong. "I wasn't aware there were specific requirements for international meetings."
"Not requirements, exactly. More like unspoken expectations. The clients we're meeting operate at a particular level. Every detail matters. Hair appointment just before you leave with the best stylist in the city. He typically handles executives and politicians. Tailor appointment next week so there's time for any adjustments."
Jay nodded slowly. It made sense, even if it felt overwhelming. "If that's what's needed for the company's image."
"It is. This trip matters more than you realize. Since Ian decided to take you with him, it's clear he cares deeply about you, feels responsible for your success here. I haven't seen him invest this much in someone's development since..." He paused, his expression growing distant. "Well, since he learned some hard lessons about trust and workplace relationships."
"What do you mean?"
"Ian's been different since you arrived. More engaged, less isolated. He smiles occasionally. Small changes, but noticeable ones for someone who spent years in emotional exile. You've been good for him, Jay. Better than I think you realize."
Jay stared at his hands, processing this information. "I'm just doing my job."
"You're doing much more than that, and Ian knows it. Which is why I need to ask something of you." Leo's tone changed, becoming more serious than Jay had ever heard it. "Be mindful of how much he's opened up to you. Ian's history with workplace complications left him... fragile. The last time he allowed someone close, it ended badly. Very badly."
"I would never—"
"I know you wouldn't intentionally cause harm. But Ian has given you access to parts of himself he keeps locked away from everyone else. The late nights, the mentorship, the way he watches out for you—it's not standard procedure." Leo's eyes were intent on Jay's face. "Just be gentle with him. Don't take advantage of your unique position so close to him."
"I don't understand what you're suggesting."
"I'm not suggesting anything. I'm just saying that Ian might be more involved in your success than he'd ever acknowledge, and that kind of emotional investment makes him vulnerable. You have the power to hurt him deeply, Jay. I've seen what that looks like, and I'd rather not see it again."
"Leo, I would never hurt Ian. He's been nothing but kind to me."
"I believe that. Which is exactly why I'm trusting you with this information. Just remember that behind all that composure is someone who cares about you more than he should, probably more than he realizes himself. His heart might be more in your hands than you know."
After Leo departed, Jay returned to his desk, his mind reeling. The implications of Leo's words circled through his thoughts like persistent echoes. The idea that Ian might harbour feelings beyond mentorship had never occurred to him. But now, examined through Leo's lens, certain moments took on new significance. Ian's expression when they worked late together. His attention to Jay's comfort and development.
Jay shook his head, trying to dismiss the spiraling thoughts. It was preposterous. Ian was accomplished, powerful, completely in control of every situation, surrounded by brilliant and beautiful people. The possibility that someone like Jay could matter to him beyond utility was laughable.
But Leo's words forced uncomfortable questions to surface. What if there was something deeper behind those moments of guidance? Ian's patient explanations, his protective interventions, the way he'd arranged this business trip with perfect timing to ease Jay's travel anxiety. Was it simple mentorship, or something more personal?
No. Ian was simply noble, a leader who cared about his team's well-being. Jay was reading too much into Leo's words, letting his meddling create problems where none existed. 
But Leo's warning echoed persistently: Be gentle with his heart.
What if Ian's heart really was in Jay's hands, and there was something fragile there that could be broken? The thought of causing Ian any pain made Jay uneasy. 
The questions multiplied while Jay forced himself to focus on his work, but the seed Leo had planted continued to grow, impossible to ignore. Whatever Ian's feelings might be, Jay was certain of one thing—he would rather die than cause harm to the person who'd given him so much.
Jay felt a renewed sense of purpose burning in his chest. If Ian was truly fragile, if he'd been hurt by betrayal, then Jay would do everything in his power to prove himself worthy of the trust he'd been given. He would be the pillar of support Ian deserved, the one person who would never disappoint him.
The rest of the day was productive. Jay threw himself into covering Max and Luc's responsibilities with even greater determination, ensuring every detail was perfect. One more week until the team returned to full capacity. He would make certain everything ran smoothly.
That evening, as the office emptied, Jay made his way to the kitchen. He'd brought lemons and mint from the fridge, planning to make the lemonade Ian had complimented before. He needed to care for Ian properly, to show through actions what words couldn't express.
He returned to Ian's office with a tall glass, condensation beading on the sides, the scent of fresh citrus filling the air.
"Lemonade," Jay said, setting it on Ian's desk. "I thought you might need a refreshment."
Ian looked up from his laptop, and something passed between them—a moment of recognition that felt deeper than their usual exchanges. Jay's eyes burned with unmistakable determination to provide care, to be worthy of whatever trust Ian had placed in him.
"Thank you." Ian's voice was quieter than usual. "That's very thoughtful."
Ian's fingers wrapped around the glass, and for a moment neither of them moved. Leo's words echoed in their minds—warnings and suggestions that had stirred a torrent of feelings. Jay saw Ian's nobility and felt compelled to prove himself worthy of it. Ian saw Jay's burning desire to care for him and felt himself drowning in feelings he'd tried so hard to contain.
Scene 4
Tailor
The doubled workload made the rest of the week pass quickly. The remaining team worked twelve-hour days just to keep pace with their expanded responsibilities. Jay arrived at seven each morning and rarely left before eight in the evening, living on coffee and the occasional sandwich grabbed between meetings.
Ian maintained the same gruelling schedule, his office light burning late into the night. They barely spoke beyond the essential exchanges of files and brief status updates, but sometimes, when Jay delivered printed reports to Ian's office, their eyes would meet for a moment longer than necessary. When Ian passed Jay's desk on his way to meetings, there was an awareness between them that hadn't existed before—glances that spoke of thoughts neither dared voice.
Friday brought no relief from the chaos, but at least the promise of weekend rest. As Jay packed his things at the end of another exhausting day, he remembered the Saturday appointment Leo had arranged. The tailor visit had slipped from his mind entirely amid the week's demands.
Jay took a taxi on Saturday afternoon, watching the city change as they headed toward the exclusive district. The buildings grew more imposing, the shop fronts more elegant, the pedestrians more perfectly dressed. By the time they reached the most prestigious tailor in the city, Jay's confidence had evaporated entirely.
The shop's exterior was all polished brass and dark wood, with a discreet gold plaque by the door. Through the windows, Jay could see mahogany fixtures and rows of expensive fabrics. He paid the taxi driver and stood on the pavement for a moment, gathering his courage. 
A silver-haired man in an immaculate suit greeted him at the door. "Mr. Hall, I presume? We've been expecting you."
Jay was led through the showroom to a private fitting area, where two assistants waited with measuring tape and notebooks. No one asked his preferences or opinions. Instead, they moved around him, treating him like a mannequin to be dressed.
"Mr. Turner provided very specific instructions," the head tailor informed him as they began taking measurements. "The cuts should highlight your natural assets. Fitted through the waist and seat to accentuate your figure. He was quite particular about the details."
Jay's face burned as the assistants discussed his body in such detached terms. They spoke about "enhancing his appeal" and "drawing attention to his best features" as if he weren't standing right there, as if he were a product being packaged for display.
"Turn around please. Yes, the trousers should definitely be cut to flatter the posterior. Mr. Turner was very clear about that requirement."
The humiliation was suffocating. Jay stood still as they pinned and marked fabric around him, their comments growing more specific and degrading with each adjustment. It became increasingly clear that these weren't standard business suits being designed—they were meant to make him look attractive, appealing, seductive even.
"This fabric," the assistant held up a swatch of wool. "It will complement his complexion while maintaining the appropriate drape to accentuate the desired areas."
"Perfect. We'll need him back next Saturday for the first fitting, then again the following week for final adjustments." The senior tailor made a note. "Mr. Turner's timeline is aggressive, but manageable."
"Three appointments?" Jay's voice cracked slightly.
"Quality takes time. Though I'm sure someone in your position understands the value of Mr. Turner's patronage."
The master tailor finally gave a curt nod, snapping the notebook shut. “You will return in a week at the same time for another round of fittings. Do try to be more relaxed then.”
The dismissal in his tone was unmistakable. Jay wasn't a customer here—he was a project, a favour being done for someone who actually mattered.
Jay managed to thank them and escape to the street, his hands shaking as he called another taxi. The entire experience had been a brutal reminder of his place in this world. He was Leo's entertainment, a joke hire who'd forgotten his station. Whatever Leo had said about Ian's feelings was clearly part of a game Jay couldn't understand.
The taxi dropped him at his neighbourhood, and Jay walked slowly toward his favourite cafe. The streets he knew so well felt like a balm after the afternoon's humiliation. This was his world—modest buildings, places where he belonged.
"Jay!" Fay's face lit up as he entered. "I haven't seen you in ages. The usual?"
"I'd like hot chocolate with extra marshmallows. Rough day."
"Coming right up." Fay busied herself behind the counter. "You look exhausted. That new job still keeping you busy?"
"Constantly. Twelve-hour days this week with people on holiday." Jay accepted the steaming mug. "But it's good work. Challenging."
"That's wonderful. Though I have to say, it's a shame you stopped coming regularly. This place isn't the same without our best customer."
Jay smiled, feeling some of the afternoon's tension ease. "I'll try to visit more often. I've missed this."
They chatted about the ongoing park construction across the street and the new bakery that had opened down the block. Normal conversation with normal people who treated him like a human being.
"You know what? I promise I'll make time to come here more often," Jay said as he finished his drink. "This is still my favourite place in the city."
"It better be." Fay grinned. "See you soon, Jay."
Walking home through his neighbourhood, Jay felt his equilibrium returning. The uptown world of expensive tailors and condescending staff wasn't his reality. This was—unpretentious cafes and friendly conversations, people who knew his name and were glad to see him.
Leo was a schemer and an imp, exactly as Jay had always known. Whatever game he was playing, it was all manipulation, and Jay wouldn't let it affect his relationship with Ian. Ian was noble, worthy of every bit of respect and gratitude. He remained the best man Jay had ever known, but he was also completely out of Jay's league. The patronage Jay received was simple generosity from a man who believed in helping others, nothing more.
Scene 5
End of August
Max and Luc were back at their desks on Monday, the office finally returning to full capacity. The relief was palpable as the team no longer had to stretch across multiple responsibilities.
"Look who decided to show up on time for once. We were placing bets on whether you'd get stranded in some remote village again," Amy called out.
"Hey, last year wasn't my fault. Besides, I promised I'd be back. Some of us take our commitments seriously," Max protested with a grin.
"And some of us take our holidays seriously too. Tell us everything. Did you finally try that bungee jumping thing?" Ela added.
"No, though I did spend three days hiking through coffee plantations with no cell service. Completely worth the digital detox," Max said.
"Coffee plantations? That sounds amazing," Zoe looked up from her keyboard.
"Best coffee I've ever tasted. The farmers taught me about processing methods I'd never heard of." Max launched into enthusiastic descriptions of volcanic soil and fermentation techniques while the team gathered around his desk.
Meanwhile Luc quietly organized his desk. Jay noticed his colleague's subdued demeanour, the way he deflected personal questions with sharp wit whenever anyone asked about his time away.
"Relationship troubles sorted themselves out then?" Kim asked bluntly.
"Oh absolutely. Turns out two weeks stuck together really clarifies things. Amazing how much clarity you can gain when you're constantly arguing about whose turn it is to do dishes."
"Rough holiday?" Jay asked when he joined Luc for coffee in the kitchen.
"Let's just say some problems follow you regardless of geography. Thanks for keeping everything running smoothly while we were gone. I heard you two practically lived here."
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks at the implication. "Just covering the necessary work."
With the team complete, the frantic pace of recent weeks finally eased. The late summer heat had also broken, leaving behind pleasant temperatures that made the commute bearable again. Jay could finally leave at normal hours, the freedom feeling strange after months of extended overtime.
Monday evening, he still stayed until eight with Ian as they worked through the backlog together, but on Tuesday, he packed up at five sharp.
"Gym night?" Ian asked as Jay gathered his things.
"Yeah. I think Rus might disown me if I come late again. Will you be alright finishing up alone?"
"Of course. Enjoy your workout."
Jay caught something in Ian's expression, but dismissed it as his imagination. Ian was probably relieved to have time to work without interruption.
At the gym, Rus greeted him with raised eyebrows. "Look who's back at a reasonable hour. Thought you'd been abducted by spreadsheets."
"Close enough. Work's been intense lately."
The session felt different from his recent stress-driven workouts. Without the crushing anxiety about his upcoming trip, without the pressure of covering for absent colleagues, Jay enjoyed the physical challenge. His body had grown stronger during the months of consistent training, and he pushed through his routine with vigour.
On Wednesday evening, he went back to Café Lemon, where Fay greeted him cheerfully. "Second visit within one week? I'm honoured."
He studied the seasonal menu, finally ordering the autumn spice latte that Fay had been recommending since its introduction.
"Feeling adventurous today?" Fay asked as she prepared his drink.
"Thought I'd try something different. Work's been crazy lately, but things are finally calming down." Jay accepted the steaming mug gratefully. "This is exactly what I needed."
The latte was perfect—warming spices balanced with smooth coffee, nothing like the sterile perfection of the office's expensive machine. Jay lingered longer than usual, savouring the simple pleasure of unscheduled time.
On Thursday evening, he went to the local swimming complex. The smell of chlorine brought back memories of awkward teenage lessons and the persistent feeling of not quite belonging anywhere.
The water was cooler than expected, but after a few tentative laps, Jay found his rhythm. His technique was rusty but adequate, and the resistance of the water felt soothing after days of mental strain. Other swimmers moved past him, but no one stared or judged. Here, he was just another person trying to get some exercise.
On Friday, he had his second diving lesson. Jay arrived at the pool centre alone this time, Zoe having her own commitments. Ina greeted him with an encouraging smile.
"Ready to work on your buoyancy control today?"
"As ready as I'll ever be. I've been practising the breathing exercises you showed me."
"Excellent. That's half the battle right there."
The lesson went better than the first. Jay managed to stay relaxed underwater, his movements becoming more natural. When he surfaced after successfully completing the emergency ascent drill, Ina was beaming.
"Three more sessions and you'll be ready for open water. You're doing brilliantly."
As Jay packed up to leave, he felt pride in his progress. The October trip no longer seemed impossible—just challenging.
Each afternoon brought something different from the endless office hours he'd grown accustomed to. Normal things that normal people did—exercise, hobbies, simple pleasures. It felt good to reclaim parts of himself that had been buried under spreadsheets and takeaway containers.
The freedom to get his own life back felt wonderful and lonely in equal measure, and he couldn't shake the feeling that something important was slipping away even as he tried to hold onto himself.
Ian never asked Jay to stay. Never said the work couldn't wait. But leaving the office at reasonable hours meant catching Ian's eye as he packed up, seeing something complicated pass across his boss's features. Jay felt torn between the pull of their shared evenings and the need to maintain work-life balance.
Ian watched Jay leave each evening with mounting dismay he couldn't voice. The return to normal hours was logical, healthy even, but Ian missed their companionship terribly. The empty office felt cold without Jay.
Jay deserved a life beyond these walls, friendships and hobbies and experiences that had nothing to do with work. But knowing it was right didn't make the emptiness any easier to bear. Ian should have been happy for him. Instead, he just wanted him back.
Saturday's second tailor appointment loomed with dread. Jay arrived prepared for condescension this time, which made the experience marginally more bearable. The staff remained dismissive, discussing his measurements and alterations as if he weren't present.
"The adjustments are coming along nicely," the senior tailor announced. "The fit through the seat is particularly flattering. Mr. Turner will be pleased."
Jay endured the fitting in silence, counting minutes until he could escape back to reality. One more session in two weeks, and this ordeal would be finished.
The taxi ride home took him through the transition zones of the city—from manicured lawns back to comfortable authenticity. As his building came into view, Jay felt his shoulders relax. It might not have marble floors or designer furnishings, but it was home. His studio apartment with its large windows and view of the small park felt more valuable than any penthouse.
He climbed the stairs to his flat, breathing deeply as he unlocked his door. The space was modest but bright, filled with books and the accumulated comfort of belonging. This was his world—cafes, swimming pools and gym sessions, places where he was himself and not someone who needed to be dressed appropriately for someone else's purposes.
Whatever Leo was scheming, whatever complicated dynamics existed in the rarefied air of Turner & Associates, Jay could survive them as long as he remembered where he truly belonged. Ian might be the finest man he'd ever known, but their worlds were fundamentally different.
Chapter 23: Fragility
Scene 1
Wounded pride
When September started, the weather turned so perfect it made the city feel like a different place entirely. The oppressive heat of summer had lifted, leaving behind warm sunshine and a fresh breeze that smelled of autumn. Jay walked to work and tried to sort through the tangle of thoughts that had been consuming him since August ended.
He'd spent an entire year molding himself into an acceptable version of Jay Hall—upgrading his wardrobe, learning the language of corporate hierarchy, studying investment terminology in his spare time. All to ensure that the people surrounding him wouldn't find him too displeasing, too obviously out of place.
The tailor appointment had stripped away that veneer, reminding him exactly what he was in their eyes. Not a colleague or even a customer, but a project. The staff's condescension had stung not because it was unexpected, but because it forced him to confront how much of himself he'd been willing to sacrifice for acceptance.
He was a man of humble origins. There was nothing shameful in that. He worked hard, paid his bills, treated people with kindness. What right did anyone have to make him feel lesser for the circumstances of his birth?
He was still the same shy, somewhat insecure man he'd always been. The experiences of the past year had built his confidence in many ways, but they'd also shown him how far he still had to go to truly belong in Ian's world. Maybe that was okay. Maybe he didn't need to belong everywhere as long as he could find places where he fit naturally.
At least in his own neighborhood, people saw him as an equal, not as some charity case who needed to be managed and manipulated. The baristas at Café Lemon didn't look down on him. His neighbors nodded hello without calculating his net worth first.
He arrived at Turner & Associates feeling simultaneously grateful for the opportunity and resentful of the games that seemed to come with it. While Ian remained as considerate and brilliant as ever, Jay approached each day with a new wariness he couldn't quite shake.
At his desk, Jay worked diligently. Every report was flawless, every client interaction smooth. But when five o'clock arrived, he packed his things and left, saying only a polite "Good evening" to Ian as he passed.
"Have a pleasant evening," Ian replied. 
"You too. Don't work too late."
The late summer air beckoned him toward the small park near his apartment. Jay found a bench beneath an oak tree and simply sat, watching office workers hurry past on their way to evening plans. A year ago, he might have felt envious of their obvious belonging in the world. Now he wondered why he'd been so eager to abandon his own place.
On Tuesday, he went to the gym, where Rus welcomed him cheerfully.
"Look who remembers he has muscles. Thought you'd disappeared into some corporate black hole."
They talked between sets, a straightforward interaction Jay enjoyed the most. No hidden agendas, no calculation of words and meanings. Just two people talking about ordinary things.
On Wednesday, he followed the same script—competent work and departure at the stroke of five.
"Leaving at a reasonable hour again?" Amy observed as she gathered her own belongings.
"Trying to enjoy the nice weather while it lasts. I spent so much of the summer buried in work, I forgot what parks looked like in daylight."
Jay spent his evening at Café Lemon, drinking his usual coffee. The pumpkin spice latte had been nice, but today he wanted the comfort of what he liked best. Fay greeted him warmly, asking about his week without any ulterior motive beyond friendly curiosity.
By Thursday, Ian couldn't ignore the pattern any longer. He'd watched Jay's withdrawal with growing unease—the way his assistant's smiles had become more polite than warm, the subtle reserve he maintained during their interactions. Nothing dramatic, but unmistakably different.
"Jay." Ian approached his desk in the afternoon. "How are you finding the return to normal hours?"
"Very pleasant, thank you. It's good to have some time for personal activities again."
"Of course. The weather's been lovely for outdoor pursuits."
"It has." Jay continued organizing his desk, not meeting Ian's eyes. "I've been taking walks in the parks. I haven't had much chance to do that lately."
The explanation was reasonable, but Ian caught something guarded in Jay's tone. "Is everything alright? You seem a bit... quiet lately."
"Nothing's wrong." Jay's smile appeared and disappeared quickly. "Just enjoying the nice weather while it lasts."
Ian nodded, recognizing the deflection for what it was. Jay wasn't lying, but he wasn't telling the complete truth either. Something had changed, something that went deeper than seasonal shifts or workload adjustments.
Ian watched him leave that day and felt his stomach twist. He wanted to be the person Jay felt comfortable confiding in, wanted to help with whatever was troubling him. The defenses he'd so carefully maintained now felt like prison walls, keeping him from reaching the person he cared about more than was wise.
On Thursday evening, Jay was at the swimming pool again, moving through laps. The repetitive motion cleared his mind. Here, surrounded by the democratic equalizer of chlorinated water, everyone was just another body seeking exercise.
An elderly man in the next lane gave a friendly nod as they reached the wall simultaneously. "Nice technique. You've been swimming long?"
"Not really. Just getting back into it." Jay pulled himself up to sit on the pool edge. "I forgot how good it feels."
"Best medicine there is. Washes away all the day's nonsense."
Jay smiled. No one here cared about his background or his suitability for their social circles.
Friday brought Jay's third diving lesson, and Ina's praise for his improving buoyancy control felt more meaningful than any corporate commendation. When she scheduled his final sessions for the following weeks, Jay felt excitement about the October trip.
Ian watched these changes from his office, the way Jay seemed to be retreating into himself despite maintaining outward politeness.
He wanted to ask what had happened, what had caused this sudden chill to appear between them. But what right did he have to pry into Jay's personal emotional landscape? If Jay chose to reclaim his evenings, if he preferred parks and cafes to his company, that was not only his right, that was the best course of action.
Yet Ian felt the loss acutely. The empty office hours stretched longer. He'd grown dependent on those stolen moments of companionship, and now they were gone as suddenly as they'd appeared.
Late Friday evening, long after Jay had left for his diving lesson, Ian sat alone in his office in growing darkness. He missed Jay desperately. The distance Jay maintained now felt like a rebuke, though Ian couldn't identify what he'd done to deserve it.
Meanwhile, Jay walked home through the gathering dusk. The streets were welcoming, filled with people who belonged to his world. 
He thought about Ina's encouragement, about the elderly swimmer, about Fay. These people saw him as he was and found him acceptable. He didn't need to change himself for their approval or worry about measuring up to impossible standards.
Scene 2
Confusion
Luc's absence caused an unexpected disruption. His desk remained empty as the team filtered in on Monday, and it wasn't until Ian called the morning meeting that they learned he'd requested sudden sick leave for the entire week.
"Is he alright? He looked pretty rough last week," Amy asked.
