"The request was brief. We should respect his privacy while ensuring his responsibilities are covered," Ian replied.
Jay felt a twist of worry in his stomach. Luc had been subdued since his return from holiday, deflecting personal questions. The curt dismissals, the hollow smiles—Jay recognized the signs that he was struggling beneath the surface.
During his lunch break, Jay sent a text: "Hope you're feeling better soon. Let me know if there's anything I can do to help."
The reply came back within minutes: "Fine. Just need rest. Thanks."
The brevity felt like a door slamming shut, but Jay couldn't shake his concern.
"I can stay late to handle Luc's backlog," Jay offered during the afternoon team meeting. "Most of his projects overlap with things I've worked on before."
"That's not necessary. You've already covered more than your share during the summer months. I can manage the overflow," Ian said, but his expression was complicated.
"I really don't mind. The workload isn't that heavy, and I know his client files."
"If you're certain you don't mind the extra hours."
"I'm certain."
That evening, Jay was back at working late alongside Ian. But something felt different—a tension in the air that hadn't existed during their summer overtime sessions. Ian seemed more closed off, the comfort of their shared work replaced by polite formality.
Jay tried to dismiss it as his own changed perspective. Perhaps he'd been reading too much into their previous interactions, imagining intimacy where only courtesy existed. But when he glanced down at the watch on his wrist, the weight of it felt real and meaningful. Too expensive, too personal to be a mere gesture.
On Tuesday, Luc gave another curt reply when Jay checked in again: "Still resting. Don't need anything."
The dismissal stung a bit. Jay went to the gym that evening, pushing himself through an aggressive workout that left him exhausted but no less worried about his colleague.
"You're distracted tonight," Rus observed as Jay nearly dropped a weight. "Work problems?"
"Colleague problems. Someone's going through a rough patch and won't accept help."
"Some people have to hit bottom before they'll reach out. All you can do is be there when they're ready."
The rest of the week continued in the same strained pattern. Jay stayed late Wednesday and Thursday, working through Luc's client files. The atmosphere was tense, and Jay worried—about Luc, about the tension between him and Ian.
By Friday, Jay had rescheduled his diving lesson to stay late again, though they were largely caught up with the backlog. As the office emptied around them, he made a decision that felt both necessary and terrifying.
"Ian?" Jay appeared in the doorway of Ian's office, his heart beating faster than the situation warranted. "Could I speak with you for a moment?"
Ian looked up from his laptop. "Of course."
Jay closed the door behind him and took the chair across from Ian's desk. "Is something wrong? This week has felt... different."
"I could ask you the same question."
Jay managed a half-smile that felt more like a grimace. "Nothing's wrong."
"It's not true, is it. I don't want to pry, truly I don't, but recently you've been..." He trailed off, then forced himself to continue. "Did I do something to displease you?"
Heat rushed to Jay's face so quickly he felt dizzy. "Of course not. You never—you've never done anything of the sort."
"Then what is it? You've been different since the beginning of the month. I know it's not my place to ask, but—"
"I had a rough weekend." Jay's words came out in a rush, cutting off Ian's probing. "Some unpleasant experiences that left me feeling unsettled. The feeling's been lingering, but it's nothing serious. Nothing to do with you or work."
He tried to inject some lightness into the heavy atmosphere, but Ian's expression didn't change. The concern in his eyes remained, along with something that looked almost like hurt.
"I see. I'm sorry you've been dealing with something difficult."
"It's really nothing dramatic. Just... reminded me of some things about my place in the world, I suppose. Made me more cautious."
The words were loaded with meanings Jay wasn't ready to explain and Ian wasn't in a position to probe. Jay touched his watch unconsciously, drawing comfort from its weight.
"If there's anything I can do to help—"
"There isn't. But thank you for asking. And for caring enough to notice."
Ian studied Jay's face for a long moment, and Jay had the uncomfortable feeling that his boss could see right through his explanations to the wounded pride and confusion beneath.
"You know I want you to feel comfortable here. Safe."
"I do feel safe." The words were true, even if they weren't complete. "You've created the best work environment I've ever experienced. Whatever I'm dealing with has nothing to do with that."
Ian nodded slowly, but the heaviness around him didn't lift. If anything, he looked more troubled than before, as if Jay's reassurances had only confirmed his worst fears.
They returned to work in silence, but the tension remained. The camaraderie of their previous overtime sessions felt like a memory from another life, replaced by performed civility that felt somehow worse than outright coldness.
As they prepared to leave, Jay felt the weight of everything unsaid pressing down on him, but he couldn't explain the full truth without revealing Leo's manipulations and his own growing confusion.
"I hope Luc will be feeling better by Monday."
"Me too."
The lift arrived, and they descended in silence. But as they parted ways in the lobby, Jay caught Ian watching him with that same worried expression, as if he were afraid Jay might disappear if he looked away.
Scene 3
Unspoken things
Jay was at the gym on Saturday, pushing through his routine while his mind churned over the previous week's tensions. Rus immediately spotted the distracted way Jay approached each exercise.
"You're lifting like your head's somewhere else entirely. Work troubles again?"
"Yeah. Can I ask you something? About work situations?"
"Shoot."
"Things got awkward with my boss recently, and I can't figure out how to fix it. We used to work well together, but now everything feels strained."
"Best advice? Stay as far away from your boss as possible. Nothing good comes from getting too close to the person who signs your paychecks."
"Right. That makes sense."
Under normal circumstances, Jay would have agreed completely. It was sound advice, wisdom that kept careers intact. But with Ian, everything felt different. The relationship they'd built—how could he explain that to someone who'd never experienced it?
Rus's casual dismissal only added another weight to Jay's growing collection of worries. If everyone else saw fraternising with a boss as inherently problematic, what did that say about the bond he and Ian had shared during those long evening hours?
Even by his own standards, getting close to a supervisor was foolish. How had it happened that he felt so connected to Ian? When had respect turned into something so much deeper?
The weekend passed on preparations and anxious thoughts. The final tailor appointment was scheduled for the afternoon, but this time Jay's mind was elsewhere. The staff remained condescending, but their words barely registered. His thoughts were consumed by the heavy atmosphere between him and Ian, the frigid politeness that felt worse than outright conflict.
How had Ian even noticed something was wrong? Jay had done his best to maintain composure, to ensure his personal crisis didn't affect their working relationship. Was Ian truly that perceptive, or was Jay more transparent than he realized?
Sunday brought his second-to-last diving lesson. Ina praised his progress, noting how much more confident he'd become underwater, but even her encouragement couldn't lift Jay's mood completely.
"One more session and you'll be ready for open water," she said as Jay surfaced from a successful emergency ascent drill. "You've come so far since that first nervous lesson."
"Thanks to your patience," Jay replied, but the praise felt distant. Everything felt distant lately, as if he were experiencing life through a fog of confusion and worry.
Only one lesson remained, and then he'd be ready for his October adventure. The trip that had once terrified him now felt like a welcome escape from the tension that seemed to follow him everywhere.
Luc returned on Monday, but the relief was short-lived. Their colleague looked terrible—pale, drawn, with dark circles under his eyes that spoke of sleepless nights and ongoing struggles. He sat at his desk, deflecting concerned inquiries with curt nods and forced smiles.
Jay waited until mid-morning before intercepting Luc in the kitchen, timing his approach when they were alone.
"Good to have you back. How are you feeling?"
"Better. Thanks." Luc's response was strained.
"Listen, I know you probably don't want to talk, but if you need a shoulder to cry on, I'm available. I know a place that serves the best hot chocolate in the city."
For a moment, Luc smiled. Jay remembered how Luc had taken him to that same café after his mistake with the deadline, offering comfort without judgment when Jay had needed it most.
"Thanks, Jay. I appreciate it. Maybe soon."
Later that morning, Zoe stopped by Jay's desk.
"How's the apartment hunting going? Last time we talked, you had several viewings lined up for this month."
Jay realized he hadn't thought about moving in weeks. The list of potential flats, the excitement about finding a better place—all of it had been pushed aside by recent events.
"I'm postponing it until November. Things are a bit chaotic right now with the upcoming trip and everything. Better to wait until things calm down."
"Fair enough. The market isn't going anywhere."
The idea of uprooting himself, of changing another fundamental aspect of his life when everything already felt uncertain, seemed overwhelming. His current studio might not be perfect, but it was home, safe when little else felt stable.
The week crawled by under a cloud of strained politeness. Jay and Ian were particular about their interactions, each exchange feeling simultaneously normal and completely wrong. They were both trying so hard to act naturally that every conversation became stilted.
Jay couldn't shake the feeling that something was amiss, but he couldn't identify what or how to fix it. Ian seemed equally aware of the tension, his tone more formal than usual, his expressions guarded as if they were in Jay's first weeks at the company.
The weather remained beautiful—warm September days that should have lifted everyone's spirits—but the morale didn't improve. Luc's obvious distress cast a pall over the team, creating an atmosphere that Jay couldn't remember experiencing before at Turner & Associates.
Wednesday evening, Jay stayed late again to help catch up on work. They worked in silence, maintaining polite distance.
"How are the trip preparations coming along?" Ian asked during a brief break.
"Well, I think. The diving lessons are almost complete, and I have all the documentation sorted. The business trip should help with the whole international travel anxiety."
"I'm glad it will be useful." Ian's response was measured, appropriate, and completely lacking the warmth that had characterized their previous conversations.
Jay wanted to ask what had changed, wanted to bridge the gap that seemed to be widening between them despite their best efforts to pretend everything was normal. But how could he address a problem neither of them could properly define?
Ian, meanwhile, fought a losing battle against his own spiraling thoughts. He was terrified that his worst fears were coming true. Somehow, Jay had realized the depth of Ian's feelings and was trying to extricate himself from an uncomfortable situation.
He was ultra-proper, ultra-mindful, scared that one wrong move would send Jay fleeing entirely. If Jay felt unsafe, Ian would never forgive himself.
Friday arrived with no improvement in the strange tension. As Jay prepared to leave at the end of the day, he caught Ian watching him with an expression that looked almost like grief.
"Have a good weekend," Ian said.
"You too. Try not to work too late."
It was the same exchange they'd had dozens of times before, but it felt like a goodbye to something precious that was slipping away. Jay left the office feeling as if he'd lost something important without understanding what or how.
All because a tailor had been rude, because Leo had planted seeds of doubt, because Jay's wounded pride had made him retreat just enough to trigger Ian's deepest fears about wanting too much.
Scene 4
Sleepover
Jay was finishing his dinner when his phone buzzed with an unexpected message: "Can you come over? Could use the company. Sorry for short notice."
He stared at the text from Luc for only a moment before typing back: "Of course. Send me your address."
Jay had changed into his last pair of jeans and a t-shirt, stopped at the liquor store for the most expensive whiskey they had, and within an hour was standing outside an impressive building in the financial district. Luc had mentioned before where he lived, but seeing it in person was different—all glass and steel and elegance that screamed wealth.
Luc answered the door with red-rimmed eyes and a pitiful expression.
"You brought the good stuff. Come in. Fair warning—the place is ridiculous."
The apartment was indeed ridiculous. Huge windows with a spectacular view of the city, and the living room alone was larger than Jay's entire studio. Everything was sleek and modern, from the leather furniture to the abstract art on the walls.
"Four of us split this place," Luc explained, noticing Jay's wide-eyed expression. "The rent is absolutely obscene, but we all love playing successful yuppies. My roommates cleared out for the weekend—couldn't handle my brooding anymore."
They sat on the massive sectional sofa, Luc immediately pouring generous measures of whiskey into two glasses. Jay accepted his reluctantly, still not over how much he hated the burning sensation of alcohol.
"So," Luc said after draining half his glass in one go. "Cam dumped me."
Jay had seen the signs all week—the forced smiles, the way Luc had deflected every concerned inquiry.
"I'm sorry. That's awful."
"Is it though?" Luc laughed bitterly, already reaching for the bottle again. "I saw it coming. We'd been arguing for months about everything—my career, where we wanted to live. Eight years together, Jay. My entire adult life since university. The only real relationship I've ever had that lasted more than three months."
Jay sipped his whiskey and tried not to wince at the taste. "Eight years is a long time. Of course it hurts, even if you knew it was coming."
"She wanted marriage, kids. And I kept making excuses, putting her off, because I knew I wasn't ready. Maybe I'll never be ready for that kind of commitment. But she was tired of waiting for me to grow up."
"You weren't wrong to wait if you weren't ready. Forcing it would have made both of you miserable."
"Would it? Or am I just selfish and immature like she said?" Tears were gathering in Luc's eyes now. "Maybe I am just a shallow playboy who's afraid of real responsibility."
"That's not true. You're one of the most dedicated people I know. You work hard, you're loyal to your friends, you're kind and thoughtful. Being uncertain about marriage doesn't make you selfish."
Luc broke down then, shoulders shaking as he sobbed into his hands. Jay moved closer, offering the support he'd wished for during his own dark moments. They sat together while Luc grieved, the whiskey disappearing from the bottle.
"You probably never got dumped," Luc said eventually, his words slightly slurred. "You're so proper and serious, probably had girls queuing up for the privilege of dating someone so together."
Jay nearly choked on his whiskey. "Not at all. I was very much dumped, called boring and disappointing too."
Luc's eyes widened with horror. "That's awful! What kind of person would say that? They had no idea what they had."
The memory still stung after all these years—a girl from college, older and supposedly more experienced, who'd approached him with such confidence and charm. She'd made him feel special, chosen, until the night she'd told him that he was uninspiring in bed and that she needed someone with more passion.
"It was in college. A girl who thought she could teach me things. Turns out I was a poor student." Jay took another sip of whiskey, the alcohol making the confession easier.
"Jay." Luc's voice was full of sympathy. "That's terrible."
"I never tried again after that. Seemed safer to focus on other things."
They spent the evening trading stories of disasters and anxieties, the whiskey making everything feel both more profound and more manageable. When Jay finally looked at his watch, it was past midnight.
"I should probably head home," he said, starting to stand.
"Don't go." Luc caught his arm, desperation bleeding through the alcohol. "Please. I don't want to be alone with my thoughts tonight."
"Alright. I'll take the couch."
The sectional was more comfortable than Jay's own bed. It was, he realized with some amusement, his first ever sleepover—a milestone at thirty that seemed both pathetic and charming.
Morning arrived with harsh sunlight and the sound of Luc moving around the kitchen. Jay found him attempting to make scrambled eggs, his movements clumsy and his face pale with hangover.
"I think I'm dying," Luc announced dramatically. "But I'm making breakfast as penance for keeping you here all night."
The eggs were terrible, overcooked and underseasoned, with a rubbery texture that defied explanation.
"Now I finally understand why you got dumped," Jay said with a straight face.
For a moment, Luc looked stricken, and Jay wondered if he'd pushed too far too soon. Then Luc burst into laughter, the first trace of humor Jay had seen from him in weeks.
"That was cruel. Accurate, but cruel."
They sat at the ridiculous marble breakfast bar, picking at the inedible eggs and drinking coffee that was thankfully much better than the food.
"You know, this doesn't mean there's something wrong with you. Sometimes people just aren't compatible, no matter how much they care about each other. You'll find someone who wants the same things you do."
"Maybe. Right now I just want to not feel like my entire life just exploded." Luc managed a weak smile. "Thanks for staying, Jay. I know it's not exactly how you wanted to spend your Friday night."
"There's nowhere I'd rather have been," Jay said simply, and meant it.
He left before noon, declining Luc's offer of actual breakfast from a nearby café, and headed to the gym for a late session. Rus took one look at him and grinned.
"Someone had a fun night."
"I was out drinking with a friend who needed support."
"Good. I'm glad you actually have friends after all. Was starting to worry you were a complete hermit."
Jay felt lighter as he worked through his routine, glad he'd been able to help Luc through the worst of his crisis. At least one problem in his life had a clear solution, since someone like Luc, charming, successful, good-hearted, would definitely find happiness again.
It was one less thing to worry about in a month that had been full of complications. The awkward tension with Ian remained unresolved, but at least Luc would be alright.
Scene 5
Getting ready
Luc's condition showed visible improvement when Jay saw him at work on Monday. The red-rimmed eyes were gone, replaced by a determined expression. Jay found him at the coffee machine during the morning break, looking more like his old self.
"How are you holding up?" 
"Better. Marginally. Still feels like someone rearranged my internal organs, but better. Thanks again for Friday night. I needed that. Sorry about the eggs."
"They were memorably terrible. Look at it this way—at least now you can stay overtime as much as you want with no one nagging you about work-life balance. Silver linings."
"You're absolutely heartless."
"I'm learning. Give me another breakup or two and I'll be a proper shoulder to cry on."
Despite the joke falling flat, Luc smiled. "I need to get myself together. My roommates are already making pointed comments about my brooding affecting the apartment's vibe. If I don't snap out of it soon, they'll throw me out."
"Saving ten thousand on rent? However would you survive such a tragedy?"
"Now you sound just like Cam," Luc laughed.
The rest of the team seemed to sense that Jay's upcoming international trip was a milestone. Throughout the week, they offered advice with varying degrees of usefulness.
Kim was the most vocal about technical concerns. "Make sure your phone plan includes international data. The roaming charges will bankrupt you otherwise. And get a universal adapter—their plugs are completely different."
Max's advice was ridiculous. "Pack an extra suit in your carry-on in case they lose your luggage. Also, always tip the hotel concierge immediately. They remember faces, and you want to be memorable for the right reasons."
Amy was more pragmatic. "Don't drink the tap water anywhere. Even in developed countries. Trust me on this. And remember their business culture is more casual than ours."
Ela was all about sightseeing opportunities. "Make sure you get some time to enjoy the city. All work and no play makes for boring travel stories."
Even Zoe chimed in, though her wilderness-focused travel experience was less relevant. "I've never been to the New Continent cities, but I've heard their public transport is absolutely terrible. Just use taxis for everything—it's worth the expense not to get stranded somewhere."
Only Luc remained mostly quiet at his desk, still working through his emotional recovery, but Jay appreciated the team's collective concern.
Jay retrieved his suits from the tailoring house on Wednesday, a moment he had been dreading. He arrived at the appointed time, steeling himself for another round of condescending treatment.
The receptionist directed him to a private fitting room where his completed garments hung in perfect alignment, each covered with protective cloth bags bearing the shop's discreet logo. The senior tailor appeared with an assistant. 
"Mr. Hall. Your completed wardrobe."
They were three-piece suits—something Jay had never owned before. The charcoal gray was conservative and elegant, perfect for serious business meetings. The dark blue struck a balance between authority and approachability. But it was the burgundy that surprised him most—bolder than anything he'd ever owned, yet somehow still tasteful. The added waistcoats lent each ensemble a formality that felt unusual to him, heavier, more deliberate, as if the garments belonged to a different class of man.
Each suit fit perfectly. The staff remained as condescending as ever, but Jay barely noticed their attitude. He was too busy marvelling at how the expensive fabric felt.
"Everything meets with your approval?" the senior tailor asked, though his tone made it clear he couldn't care less about Jay's opinion.
"They're beautiful. Thank you."
Along with the suits came matching shirts, silk ties, leather shoes, protective garment bags, and a massive and very elegant suitcase. Everything charged to the company account, naturally.
Jay left with his arms full of expensive clothing and his distrust of Leo deeper than ever. The suits were gorgeous, but the motivation behind them remained suspect, and Jay couldn't shake the feeling that he was being prepared for purposes beyond simple presentation.
Jay returned home and began packing. He laid everything out on his bed—the new suits, his designer outfit from the company dinner, a more casual selection that might be appropriate for downtime. The massive suitcase could accommodate everything with room to spare, but his closet couldn't handle the storage implications. 
Thursday morning, Jay didn't go to the office to prepare for their afternoon departure. His appointment with the celebrity hairstylist Leo had arranged was scheduled for nine. The salon had a minimalist design and hushed atmosphere screaming exclusivity, but the stylist himself was surprisingly warm.
"Excellent bone structure. We'll enhance what you have naturally," he murmured, running fingers through Jay's hair.
The result was remarkable. The cut was flattering Jay's features while maintaining an air of elegance. It required minimal maintenance, but looked like he'd stepped out of a magazine.
Back home, Jay dressed in the burgundy suit, the one he had deemed most flattering. The deep wine colour was bolder than anything in his wardrobe, but the overall effect remained appropriate. The three-piece design made him look older, more established, like someone who belonged in first-class lounges.
Ian's watch sat at his wrist, now complemented by the expensive clothing. Jay caught sight of himself in his bathroom mirror and barely recognized the reflection. He could represent the company internationally without embarrassment.
On impulse, Jay took out his phone and attempted a selfie. His photography skills were generally terrible, but somehow the lighting and angle worked. The image showed a handsome young man with kind eyes and a slight smile.
He posted it to the social media account Ema had insisted he create, adding the caption: "First business trip". His family was the only audience, so sharing didn't feel inappropriate.
The phone rang almost immediately and Ema's excited voice practically vibrated through the speaker.
"Oh my God, Jay! You look amazing! Is that a new suit? It's gorgeous! Where are you going? Are there going to be other rich people? You have to take tons of photos!"
"It's just a business meeting. Nothing too exciting."
"Nothing exciting? You look like a movie star! Mom's going to die when she sees this. Are you flying first class? What's the hotel like? Is your boss taking you somewhere fancy?"
"Yes, Ian's coming with me."
"The Ian you never talk about but clearly worship? This is so exciting! You're living like a proper businessman now!"
Jay laughed despite his mixed feelings about that description. "I should go. Need to finish packing."
"Take pictures of everything! The plane, the hotel, the food, the meeting—I want to live vicariously through your glamorous life!"
By eleven, he was ready—suitcase packed, documents organized, appearance immaculate. Soon Ian would arrive in the company car to collect him, and they'd begin this journey together—their first extended period alone together. Jay was as prepared as he could possibly be. 
Chapter 24: Business trip
Scene 1
Departure
Jay descended the stairs of his building at ten minutes to twelve, his nerves making him leave early. The sleek black limousine pulled up exactly one minute later.
The driver immediately stepped out to handle Jay's luggage. "Mr. Hall? I'll take care of this for you."
Ian was seated inside, impeccably dressed in a charcoal gray suit. He looked up from his phone as Jay got in.
"Good morning, Jay. I trust you slept well?"
"Yes, thank you." Jay smoothed down his three-piece suit, feeling self conscious about the formal cut. "The timing worked out perfectly."
"The new suit is quite elegant. Very appropriate for our meetings."
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks. "Leo arranged it. Along with the others."
Ian's expression immediately sharpened, concern replacing politeness. "Leo arranged your clothing? Did he... say anything to you?"
Jay was quiet for a moment, looking out the window at the city passing by. "He did say things, but if I'm being honest, I don't trust a word that comes from Leo's mouth."
Unexpectedly, Ian laughed. "I don't blame you. It's probably the most prudent choice. Though I should mention that Leo isn't a bad person, despite appearances. He has a rather playful personality and can be insufferable, but I do trust him." 
"I don't. And I'm unlikely to change my mind about that."
"Did he say something to upset you?"
"Not exactly. He was just being ridiculous, as always. He probably set up that fancy tailor appointment only to humiliate me."
"Was the arrangement unpleasant?"
Jay chose his words carefully. "The staff was... diplomatically speaking, rude."
"Which tailor did he send you to?"
"Saviour & Sons."
"No wonder. That's the poshest establishment in the city, but also the most patronizing place you could possibly visit."
"I bet they definitely wouldn't be rude to you though."
"Actually, they would. I'm banned there." 
 "What?"
"You need to understand something about me, Jay. I'm a pariah in high society. After my scandal, places like Saviour & Sons outright refuse to serve me."
Jay was horrified. "If I'd known that, I never would have gone there. Why would Leo send me somewhere that treated you that way?"
"Leo comes from old money—born and raised with a silver spoon. He's unaware of such details. Based on how he's treated there, he probably wouldn't think anyone could have a bad experience. He definitely wasn't being malicious."
"I can't think of any other reason I'd need such expensive suits."
Ian laughed again, and Jay was struck by how much lighter he seemed when his guard was down. "You're Leo's first successful hire. He's making sure his head-hunting skills are properly appreciated by gearing you up appropriately. He'll probably spend the next decade telling me 'I told you so' about needing a proper assistant."
Jay remained unconvinced, but Ian's openness about his past felt significant. His own bad experience suddenly seemed smaller in comparison. He had always thought of himself as uniquely outside the society where he now worked, but apparently Ian didn't have such a good relationship with it either.
"When I lost everything, Leo was the only person who helped me. We've been friends since university, and while his personality can be insufferable, he isn't malicious. He just meddles too much. You can safely disregard anything he says."
"I still hold him responsible for the tailor experience. And if they dared to shun you, I'm doubly displeased with them."
"They did excellent work though," Ian said, his gaze lingering on Jay's appearance before he caught himself and looked away.
Jay felt his cheeks warm again. Ian seemed to notice his discomfort and quickly changed direction.
"Don't let the rude tailors bother you. They're known for being unpleasant and hardly represent anything meaningful. But I'll be sure to give Leo a proper scolding for subjecting you to that experience."
"I'd rather Leo simply kept his distance from me. But I understand that's impossible, since he's practically the boss of the company."
The reminder of the hierarchy, of his position at the bottom of the corporate ladder, made Jay's shoulders tense. He'd have to keep his head down and endure Leo's meddling if he wanted to survive at Turner & Associates.
Ian noticed the change immediately, his expression growing concerned, but before he could respond, Jay's phone rang. Jay looked flustered as he glanced at the caller ID.
"Go ahead, don't mind me," Ian said.
"Hello?"
"Jay!" It wasn't Ema's voice this time, but a younger boy's excited chatter. "Ema told us you're going to another country! Can you send us a postcard? And maybe bring some candy? Or a toy?"
"Ari, this is a business trip. I won't have time to shop around—"
"Please? Ema says rich people always bring presents when they travel!"
Jay could hear Ema's voice in the background, clearly encouraging her youngest brother. "I'll try, but I can't promise anything."
"You're the best brother ever! Ema wants to talk to you again—"
The phone was clearly passed back and forth while his siblings competed for his attention, and Jay smiled despite his embarrassment at the public display.
Ian watched the interaction with obvious amusement. When Jay finally ended the call, looking mortified, Ian was smiling.
"I'll make sure we have Saturday afternoon free for shopping. Consider it compensation for the tailor visit. I'll ensure you have a much better retail experience."
"You don't have to do that—"
"I insist." Ian's tone brooked no argument. "It's nice that you have such a good relationship with your family. You should treasure it."
Jay's expression grew wistful. "Actually, we were never really close. Too far apart in age—I grew up in completely different circumstances than them. I left home as soon as I could to escape the noise of three little children. They're only nice to me now because they can extort gifts."
Ian laughed, seeing right through Jay's facade. "You don't look like you mind being badgered for presents."
"You're probably right. They are good kids."
Soon they arrived at their destination. The airport was a maze of terminals and security checkpoints, but Ian moved through it without trouble. He guided Jay through check-in, explained the security procedures, and showed him how to find their gate.
"The business lounge is through there if you ever need a space to work," Ian said, pointing down a corridor. "And that shop carries decent travel essentials if you forget anything."
They still had time before their flight, so Ian proposed they go to one of the terminal cafes. As they sat at a small table with coffee, Jay realized the awkwardness between them had largely dissolved. The change of scenery seemed to have helped, allowing them to interact more naturally than they had in weeks.
"Thank you for being so patient with all this. I know it must seem ridiculous that someone my age has never flown before."
"Everyone has a first time. There's nothing ridiculous about it."
As they talked, Jay watched Ian with new eyes. The revelation about his social exile, his status as a reject from the world Jay had assumed he belonged to, changed everything. To Jay, Ian was the absolute pinnacle of grace and competence. If someone like Ian could be cast out by high society, then perhaps the problem wasn't with the individual but with the society itself.
The high society must be blind as well as stupid, Jay thought, watching Ian explain airline protocols.
Scene 2
Plane flight
Boarding went more smoothly than Jay had thought. Ian guided him through the process, handling their carry-on luggage and directing Jay to their seats. Their business class seats were more spacious than Jay had imagined, arranged in pairs with generous legroom and privacy screens. Ian gestured toward the window seat.
"I prefer the aisle," he said, though in truth he had no particular preference. He simply wanted to watch Jay's reactions to his first flight experience.
Jay felt slightly sheepish about his obvious inexperience. "Are you sure? I don't want to inconvenience you."
"Not at all. Please, make yourself comfortable."
Jay marveled at the amenities around them—the adjustable lighting, the individual entertainment systems, the blankets already waiting for them. Everything was elegant and foreign.
The takeoff was surprisingly gentle, nothing like the dramatic lurching Jay had braced himself for. The powerful engines created a thrum as they lifted off. Jay wasn't particularly afraid of heights,  and he was mesmerized by the ground falling away below them. The city became a patchwork of lights and geometric patterns, then clouds obscured everything in white cotton.
"This is incredible," Jay murmured, his face pressed to the small window.
Ian smiled, watching Jay's wonder. "Different than you expected?"
"Completely. I thought it would be more... violent, I suppose. This is almost peaceful."
The flight attendants served lunch after they reached cruising altitude, and Jay realized how hungry he'd become in his morning nervousness. The food was surprisingly good—far better than he had expected from airline cuisine, and he ate with appreciation while continuing to peer out at the landscape passing far below.
After lunch, Jay made himself comfortable, glancing occasionally at Ian who had pulled out his tablet to read business publications. Jay had downloaded movies to his laptop and packed both a book and a magazine for the eight-hour journey, but for now he was content to watch the changing scenery outside.
"Enjoying the view?" Ian asked after they'd been airborne for an hour.
"Very much. It's amazing how smooth this whole experience has been." Jay turned from the window to meet Ian's eyes. "Though I suppose I shouldn't be surprised—having you here makes everything better."
He hadn't meant to voice that last thought quite so directly, and immediately felt exposed. He felt heat rise in his cheeks as he realized what he'd admitted, but Ian's expression only grew warmer.
"I'm glad I can help ease the experience."
As Ian returned to his tablet, Jay looked at him with a gaze that held both admiration and a sense of inferiority. He couldn't allow himself to forget his place again. No matter how Ian had been treated by high society, that was still his world. Jay was just passing through, pulled briefly into Ian's orbit by a twist of fate. His place beside Ian was temporary, borrowed, precarious, and he shouldn't make himself too comfortable.
But the yearning was there regardless—to bask in Ian's glow, to allow himself just a bit of indulgence in this fantasy of belonging.
The complicated thoughts must have shown on his face, because Ian looked up with concern.
"Everything alright? You're not feeling nauseous, are you? Some people find the altitude unsettling."
"I'm fine. Maybe the altitude is making me a bit dizzy," Jay said, though he looked somehow sad.
Ian felt a pull to reach out, to gather Jay into his arms and soothe whatever was troubling him. The urge was becoming increasingly difficult to resist, but he knew he couldn't act on it. He would ruin everything, scare Jay away, and lose him forever. 
To break the spell, Ian reached for his laptop. "Perhaps we should watch a movie. Something light to pass the time."
He pulled out two pairs of headphones and selected a well-reviewed comedy, but after twenty minutes he noticed Jay wasn't really watching. His attention kept drifting back to the window, his expression melancholy. Gradually, Jay's eyes grew heavy, and he dozed off against the seat cushion.
Ian wanted to wake him—sleeping too much during the flight would make the jetlag worse—but Jay looked so peaceful that Ian couldn't bring himself to disturb him. Instead, he turned off the movie and watched Jay sleep, memorizing the lines of his face in repose.
Jay woke after two hours, groggy and disoriented. He checked his watch and realized they were halfway through the flight. Ian was reading on his tablet again, the laptop put away. Jay blinked slowly, trying to clear the fog from his mind.
"Sorry for drifting off," he mumbled.
"It's perfectly fine. Would you like some coffee? Or water?"
The flight attendant brought coffee at Ian's request, and the caffeine helped Jay feel marginally more alert, though he remained subdued.
"You shouldn't sleep too much more, or tonight will be rough with the time difference. Perhaps we should try a more energetic film?"
"I'm finding it hard to focus on anything," Jay admitted.
Ian set aside his tablet and turned his attention fully to Jay. "Tell me, what do you know about New Continent customs? I thought I might share some observations about what to expect."
For the remainder of the flight, Ian kept up a stream of conversation—stories about local traditions, business etiquette, amusing cultural differences he'd encountered in previous visits. Jay listened, asking occasional questions, but Ian could see the fatigue weighing on him.
By the time they landed, Jay felt thoroughly drained. Eight hours was the longest he'd ever spent in any vehicle, and despite the comfortable seats, his body felt stiff and sluggish. A driver met them at customs, handling their luggage and guiding them to a waiting car.
The drive to the hotel was uneventful. It was five in the afternoon local time, but Jay's body insisted it was eleven at night back home, and he felt increasingly sleepy with each passing mile.
The hotel was magnificent, a five-star establishment with elegant furnishigs. Their rooms were adjacent, spacious and beautifully appointed. Jay unpacked, fighting the urge to simply collapse onto the inviting bed despite the hunger gnawing at his stomach.
Ian knocked on his door at five-thirty local time.
"Dinner. I know you're tired, but trust me on this. We need to get you on local time as quickly as possible."
"I could just order room service—"
"Absolutely not. Proper dinner downstairs, and no sleeping until at least eight o'clock."
They ate in the hotel's restaurant, Ian maintaining light conversation while Jay struggled with his exhaustion. The food was excellent, but Jay could barely focus on it. Ian could see how hard the travel was hitting him, but he kept Jay downstairs until seven-thirty, ensuring he'd have a better chance of sleeping through the night.
Finally, Ian relented. "You should get some rest now. Tomorrow will be a long day. Set an alarm for morning. We have meetings at ten. I'll meet you for breakfast at seven."
Jay practically stumbled back to his room. He laid out his clothes for the following day, took a quick shower, and fell into bed almost immediately. It was two in the morning back home—later than he'd ever stayed awake in his adult life. The comfortable hotel bed embraced him like a cloud, and he was asleep within minutes.
Scene 3
Business meetings and late night drinks
Jay woke with a start at five in the morning, the remnants of a dream clinging to his consciousness—Ian's arms around him, warmth and safety that felt achingly real until consciousness intruded. He lay still for a moment, disoriented by both the unfamiliar surroundings and the lingering phantom sensation of being held.
Eleven back home. He'd never overslept this badly in his life, but at least he hadn't woken at two in the morning. The jet lag could have been worse.
A long hot bath helped loosen the stiffness from travel and sleep, and by six-thirty he was dressed in the dark blue suit, feeling more human. The hotel restaurant was already serving breakfast, and he found Ian already there, drinking coffee as if he had been awake for hours.
"Good morning. I trust you slept well?"
Jay took the seat across from him. "Better than expected. How long have you been up?"
"Since four. I found the hotel gym and managed a decent workout. Amazing what a proper fitness facility can do for jet lag recovery." Ian's smile was pleased, as if the early morning routine had energized him. 
Jay stared at him with awe. Ian had accomplished so much before dawn, while Jay only managed to return to consciousness. "That's truly impressive."
"Years of habit. The driver won't arrive until nine, so we have plenty of time."
Jay marveled at Ian's stamina as they ate breakfast together, followed by fresh fruit that was somehow more vibrant and flavourful than anything available back home. Everything about the hotel was excessive—the portions, the variety, the service.
"This is my first stay at a proper five-star hotel. I didn't fully appreciate it yesterday."
"The New Continent doesn't do anything by halves. You'll notice that theme everywhere we go."
Jay started to appreciate Leo's outfit intervention as they prepared for their meetings. The expensive suits allowed him to stand beside Ian without shame, even in surroundings that screamed wealth and power.
At eight-thirty, they returned to their rooms to gather their things. Jay couldn't resist taking photos for his siblings—posing next to the enormous bed and at the window overlooking the massive skyline that dwarfed his home city. Everything here was built on a scale that defied reason.
The Garnet Ridge Group offices occupied three floors of a gleaming tower, their reception area alone larger than most companies' entire premises. The CEO, Mac Cartwright, greeted Ian like a long-lost friend, and Jay watched Ian become instantly charming and engaging, moving with an ease that showed how natural this world was for him.
The morning meetings covered strategic partnerships and investment opportunities, topics Jay followed with interest while taking detailed notes. The tour of their offices followed—a company much larger than Turner & Associates, though Jay was becoming accustomed to opulent business environments and wasn't startled by the grandeur.
Lunch at a nearby restaurant felt more like a social visit than a business meal. Jay listened to Ian trade university stories with Mac, fascinated by glimpses of Ian's younger self. The camaraderie between old friends made Jay envious of that kind of belonging.
After lunch, they returned for another round of meetings focused on technical details. Jay was sustained by double coffees and glad that his good night's sleep had left him functional. But as four o'clock approached, fatigue began to creep in.
"We should head back to the hotel. Dinner's not until eight, so you should rest," Ian said as the meetings concluded. 
Jay accepted the advice, setting an alarm for seven before collapsing onto the hotel bed. Sleep came immediately, but the alarm left him groggy and disoriented. Only complimentary coffee in the restaurant managed to restore some semblance of alertness.
Dinner was held at an exclusive restaurant that felt more like a private club. Besides Mac, two other executives and a senior staffer joined them, and Jay quickly realized the cultural differences Ian had mentioned. Where their homeland favoured restraint, these people were boisterous and gregarious. Everyone here reminded him of Max, but dialed up even further. Jay smiled and contributed politely when addressed, but mostly observed Ian. 
Jay abstained from alcohol, but Ian shared whiskey with their hosts, and the gathering grew increasingly animated. Jay held onto a single glass of wine, while Ian matched their hosts drink for drink without showing any effects.
By eleven, the executives were thoroughly intoxicated, their conversation growing louder and more inappropriate. Jay was uncomfortable with the casual crudeness, so different from the environments he knew.
As they prepared to leave, one of the executives made a comment that froze Jay's blood.
"Old Continent certainly knows how to do business. Do your managers routinely travel with such beautiful escorts? Very progressive approach to client relations."
The silence that followed was deafening. Ian's expression went from pleasant to ice-cold fury so quickly that Jay actually took a step back.
"I beg your pardon?" Ian's voice was deadly quiet.
"Oh, come now, don't be coy. Your pretty little—"
"Mr. Hall is my assistant." Ian's words cut through the drunken rambling like a blade. "If you're suggesting what I think you're suggesting, this conversation and our business relationship end right here."
Mac tried to intervene, his face pale with embarrassment. "Zac has had too much to drink, Ian. He doesn't mean—"
"He knows exactly what he means. And I know exactly what kind of company keeps executives who think it's acceptable to sexually harass visiting employees."
Jay hung his head, used to being the target of crude jokes and casual objectification, but the humiliation still burned. He'd hoped the expensive clothes and formal setting would protect him from this kind of treatment, but apparently nothing could.
"Ian, please—" Mac began.
"We're leaving. And you can consider any potential partnership permanently terminated."
They left in a decidedly sour mood, Ian's fury palpable as they walked through the lobby. Only when they reached the car did Ian speak.
"I'm sorry you had to endure that. I definitely won't be doing business with a company that harasses my employees."
"Please don't let it affect your decision-making. Comments like that aren't unusual. It's really not worth—"
"It's absolutely a matter of principle. Some behaviours will not be tolerated under any circumstances."
Back in his room, Jay showered and prepared for bed, but sleep wouldn't come. The executive's words echoed in his mind, along with Ian's fierce defence and the promise to cancel their entire business relationship over the slight. After an hour of staring at the ceiling, he gave up and dressed in his designer outfit—his only clothing that seemed appropriate for the hotel's elegant bar.
The lounge was nearly empty at midnight, just a few other guests and jazz playing in the background. Jay ordered whiskey, hoping alcohol might help him relax enough for sleep.
He was surprised when Ian appeared ten minutes later.
"Couldn't sleep either?" Ian asked.
"I didn't figure you for someone who had trouble sleeping. Thought you had that executive discipline down to a science," Jay replied, attempting lightness.
"I also didn't figure you for much of a drinker," Ian said, signaling the bartender. In truth, he had heard Jay moving around and followed him down.
"It would be a pity not to make use of such a nice hotel. Though truthfully, that afternoon nap probably did me in," Jay said with a wry smile.
"I'm having trouble sleeping as well," Ian admitted, which was true enough. He had been at his laptop, plotting ways to destroy the man who had insulted Jay. "We're supposed to have another meeting tomorrow morning with Garnet Ridge Group executives, but I'm cancelling it along with the entire deal."
"That seems excessive. Being crude toward junior staff isn't exactly unusual in business."
"Not on my watch," Ian said firmly. His inner thoughts were far darker—how dare that man have such thoughts about his Jay? He would wreck the executive's entire career and the company along with it if necessary.
Jay seemed to sense the dangerous undercurrent in Ian's mood and changed the subject. "What's your history with their CEO? You seem to know each other well."
Ian accepted the subject change, perhaps sensing Jay needed distraction from the evening's unpleasantness. They talked for nearly an hour about university days, travel experiences, anything that kept the conversation light and meandering.
"We should probably get some rest," Ian finally said as the bar staff began subtle cleanup around them. "Though I suppose you don't need to wake up early tomorrow, given that our schedule just cleared up."
Jay smiled as they walked toward the elevators. "You certainly are tenacious when you've been drinking."
"I prefer principled, but tenacious works too."
In his room, Jay finally felt drowsy enough for sleep. The evening had been salvaged by Ian's company, even if the business trip itself had taken an unfortunate turn.
Meanwhile, Ian sat in his own room, laptop open. The fury still simmered, made worse by the memory of how defeated Jay appeared after that bastard's comment.
This was supposed to be an exclusively pleasant experience, and some insignificant man had cast a shadow over it. Ian would make sure there were consequences for anyone who dared treat Jay with such disrespect.
Scene 4
Apologies
Jay didn't turn off his alarm. At six-thirty, the shrill sound jolted him from sleep, groggy but functional enough to face another day. He dressed in the gray suit—the third from his collection, ensuring he wouldn't repeat an outfit—and made his way to the restaurant by seven.
Ian was already there, coffee in hand and breakfast half-finished, but his expression showed surprise at Jay's punctual arrival.
"I thought you might sleep in this morning," Ian said as Jay sat across from him with a full plate and coffee.
Jay stifled a dramatic yawn. "Still not used to the time zone, apparently. My body thinks it's one in the afternoon."
"Did you sleep well?"
"Well enough. Just need coffee and sustenance."
Ian wanted to suggest Jay return to bed, but that might come across too strongly. He also didn't want to dwell on the previous evening's unpleasantness first thing in the morning, so he steered the conversation toward safer topics until Jay brought it up himself.
"About yesterday evening—do you really intend to drop the negotiation?"
"I do. Some behaviours can't be overlooked, regardless of potential profit."
Jay felt awkward about being the cause of such a dramatic business decision, but he understood it wasn't truly his fault. Some foolish man had dared act presumptuously to Ian's face and would have to face the consequences. His own bad luck for being so shameless.
They were preparing to leave the restaurant when Mac Cartwright appeared, wearing a mortified expression.
"Ian, Jay—please, could I have a moment? I cannot begin to express how appalled I am by Zac's behaviour last night. He was terminated immediately, first thing this morning. There's absolutely no excuse for such conduct, and it reflects terribly on my leadership."
Jay felt his cheeks burn with embarrassment at the public nature of the apology. Ian remained stone-faced, clearly unimpressed by the gesture.
"I understand your position, but—"
"Please. Give me a chance to prove our company's integrity isn't defined by one man's inexcusable behaviour. We have a scheduled visit to our tech lab this morning—let me show you the innovation we're truly about."
Ian glanced at Jay, who nodded despite his discomfort. "I'm fine with that," Jay said.
"Very well, I suppose we could proceed with the morning appointment," Ian replied.
The tech lab was an hour's drive outside the city, a gleaming facility that buzzed with activity even on Saturday. They received a tour that showcased cutting-edge development projects, ending in Mac presenting Jay with an unexpected gift.
"This is a prototype of our latest design. Impossible to purchase, limited to fifty units worldwide. Ultra-lightweight but incredibly durable, with specifications that won't be commercially available for another two years."
Jay stared at the device. "I can't possibly accept this."
"Please. It's a sincere apology for the unacceptable treatment you received. Consider it a small gesture toward making amends. It's the least we can do after such an unforgivable breach of hospitality."
Jay looked helplessly at Ian, who appeared more amused than anything else by Jay's obvious discomfort with the lavish gesture.
"It's entirely up to you whether you choose to forgive them," Ian said solemnly.
Jay caught Ian's barely concealed mirth and rolled his eyes at Ian's feigned gravity before accepting the laptop with as much grace as he could muster. The deal, apparently, was saved.
Back at the hotel after lunch, Ian reminded Jay of his promise. "Time for that shopping expedition. We have siblings to satisfy."
"You don't have to do this," Jay said, feeling sheepish about the entire enterprise.
"Nonsense. A promise is a promise."
Ian insisted they visit the city's most impressive shopping complex. The mall was enormous, a testament to New Continent excess, with stores Jay had only heard of in magazines.
Ian guided him through specialty confectionery shops, toy stores with elaborate displays, electronics boutiques with the latest gadgets. At a cosmetics counter, a helpful clerk assembled a bag of trendy makeup for Ema without Jay even knowing what half the products were for.
"Company card for everything," Ian insisted when Jay reached for his wallet. "Leo needs to cover expenses for his meddling."
They also sent postcards—Jay writing brief messages to each sibling while Ian waited patiently nearby.
By the time they returned to the hotel, Jay was exhausted and laden with shopping bags, staring at the collection with dismay.
"How am I going to fit all this in my suitcase?"
"Don't worry about logistics. I'll handle the packing arrangements."
That evening, Ian took Jay to dinner at a restaurant that occupied the top floor of the city's tallest building. The view was spectacular, the food exceptional.
"This place has insane waiting times," Ian explained as they were seated at a table. "Nearly impossible to get into. But I still have some strings to pull, despite everything. Since we're here, it would be a pity not to take advantage."
Jay was once again grateful for his tailored clothes—at least he didn't stand out among the other diners, all of whom looked wealthy and sophisticated.
"How do you find the New Continent?" Ian asked over dessert.
"Impressive, but overwhelming. I doubt they need quite this many lanes and highways everywhere. And the parking lots are the size of small towns. They could use more green spaces—I much prefer the parks back home."
"I don't disagree. It's not really to my taste either. Too loud, too big for its own good."
"I'm glad the deal didn't fall through after all. It would have felt awkward to have my first business trip end in failure."
"It wouldn't have been a failure. Determining that a company isn't a good partner is valuable information, often more so than pushing for any deal regardless of the circumstances."
Jay had to concede the point, impressed once again by Ian's wisdom and principled approach to business. 
They left the restaurant while it was still early evening, knowing they had to wake at four in the morning for their flight home. Jay packed, setting out his more casual clothes for the morning travel, but despite his exhaustion, he couldn't fall asleep.
It had been an intense trip—successful but emotionally complicated. Jay lay in the hotel bed, processing the business dealings, the uncomfortable incident, and through it all, Ian's unexpected protectiveness.
Two days had shown him more of the world than he'd seen in his entire previous life, but they'd also revealed depths to his connection with Ian that made him increasingly uncertain about the true nature of their relationship.
Scene 5
Journey Home
The alarm at four-thirty felt like a cruel joke. Jay woke feeling like he'd been hit by a train, his body heavy with exhaustion despite going to bed early. The poor sleep had left him sluggish and disoriented, moving through the morning on autopilot.
He cleaned up, packed the remainder of his belongings, and checked the hotel room five times to ensure nothing was forgotten—documents in order, passport secured, chargers packed, toiletries accounted for. By five minutes to five, he dragged himself to the lobby where Ian waited, looking composed, his luggage already loaded in the waiting car.
While Ian appeared immaculate, Jay felt like a zombie. The drive to the airport passed in silence, Jay slumping against the leather seat, half-awake.
Ian guided him through the airport procedures. Jay barely registered the process, noting dimly that they seemed to have more bags than when they'd arrived—presumably containing yesterday's shopping haul.
At the terminal, an announcement crackled through the speakers about weather delays. Jay didn't catch the details, too exhausted to focus.
They found seats in an airport café, Ian returning with coffee and asking gently, "Do you feel like breakfast?"
Jay fumbled with the toast and eggs they ordered, managing only a few bites before giving up. Ian watched with growing concern, finally probing softly.
"Is something wrong? Beyond the obvious exhaustion, I mean."
Jay sighed deeply. "How is it possible that you're so put together despite this demanding schedule and the jetlag? I can barely function."
Ian laughed. "Years of practice. I've been flying regularly for over thirty years—it does get easier once you adapt to the demands."
Another announcement declared a two-hour delay. Jay groaned audibly.
"We were supposed to arrive at nine. Now it's eleven, and I won't be home before midnight. I'll have trouble getting up for work tomorrow."
"You don't need to come in. Business travel grants you a recovery day."
"Will you be coming in?"
Ian hesitated. "I will, yes."
"Then I'll come too."
"Jay, you don't need to force yourself—"
"If you can manage it, so can I."
Ian relented, sensing it might be a matter of pride, but privately worried about Jay pushing himself too hard. They moved to the business class lounge, where Jay collapsed onto a relatively comfortable bench and immediately dozed off, the coffee having done little to combat his exhaustion.
Ian watched over him, fighting the urge to smooth the hair from Jay's forehead or adjust the awkward angle of his neck. Jay drifted in and out of consciousness, looking young and vulnerable.
Another announcement woke Jay: the delay had increased to four hours. Jay groaned, but Ian smiled.
"Flight delays happen. They're not exactly rare. Consider it a learning experience—whenever you plan to fly, it's wise to anticipate possible delays. At least it happened on the return journey."
They returned to the café for more coffee, Jay looking marginally more alert. As they sat overlooking the terminal's bustle, Jay's expression grew wistful.
"Something bothering you?" Ian asked.
Jay's defenses were lowered by exhaustion and the strange feeling of displacement. "I'll never get used to this high-stakes society. Never stop being an imposter. As much as I enjoy and appreciate the opportunities, each one just showcases my own limitations," he blurted out.
"That's not true, is it? People are the same regardless of how they were born—some are kind, some are not. If anything, wealth and power tend to make people worse, not better. As you saw firsthand this week, being rich and successful doesn't automatically make someone deserving of respect. Kindness and integrity matter—everything else is superficial. The clothes, the manners, the insider knowledge—none of that measures a person's worth."
"I suppose. But it's hard when people higher in the hierarchy harass or insult me."
"Is it easier when someone poor harasses and insults you?"
Jay paused, thinking of various unpleasant encounters with peers over the years. Was his pride less wounded then? "No," he admitted. "I feel small no matter who it comes from."
"Exactly. Harassment is the harasser's fault, not yours. It doesn't matter if you're disparaged by a tailor, an executive, or a janitor—they're behaving badly, and it says nothing about your value. Harassment isn't more valid coming from wealthy people, just as poverty doesn't automatically make someone kind."
Jay pondered it. Maybe Ian was right. Perhaps he'd been paying too much attention to status, judging people by bank accounts and not actions. Once again, Ian's wisdom impressed him, and he looked at him with undisguised admiration.
"I tend to get lost in my head about perceived inadequacies," Jay admitted sheepishly.
"There's nothing inadequate about you at all." Ian's voice held such conviction that Jay felt heat rush to his cheeks, his gaze dropping to the floor. Praise from Ian always felt monumental.
The delay stretched to six hours. They ate an early lunch in the lounge, and Jay consumed four cups of coffee before he finally felt awake.
"Now I won't be able to sleep at all," Jay moaned as they boarded the plane.
Takeoff was less exciting the second time, and cloud cover limited the view from his window. 
"Maybe this time we'll actually manage to watch that movie," Ian joked, and they did attempt it, but they both found the comedy decidedly unfunny. They switched to a historical drama that Jay loved and Ian found absolutely terrible.
"The costumes are beautiful," Jay protested as Ian criticized the production values.
"Cheap, fake, and melodramatic," Ian countered, though he seemed amused by Jay's enthusiasm.
After plane dinner, Jay finally managed to nap, waking just before landing at three in the morning. Even Ian looked tired now.
"Now even I won't come to work Monday," Ian admitted. "Feel free to take the day off and rest properly."
The driver took Jay home first. Ian handed him the additional suitcase containing the siblings' gifts, and Jay struggled to manage everything up to his small apartment. The luggage and new clothes made the space feel impossibly crowded—he clearly couldn't delay his apartment search any longer.
Despite his exhaustion, Jay fell into bed feeling happy. The trip had been intense, challenging, and occasionally uncomfortable, but Ian had been beside him through every moment. Whatever complexities existed in their relationship, Jay's fundamental assessment remained unchanged.
Ian was the best person he'd ever known, and working for him remained the greatest privilege of Jay's life.
Chapter 25: Tropical heat
Scene 1
Reset
The morning light streamed across the room, and Jay realized from its brightness that he’d slept too long. His phone confirmed it was past ten in the morning. The jetlag had hit harder than expected, leaving him feeling disoriented and heavy-limbed. He lay still for a moment, letting his mind catch up with the reality that he was back in his small studio that suddenly felt impossibly cramped after the spacious hotel suite.
The chill in the air when he opened his window made it clear that autumn was approaching fast. Quarterly reports will start in October, and with them, the chaos that consumed everyone's lives for at least a week. He needed to get his affairs in order before work swallowed him whole again.
A proper breakfast seemed like the right place to start. He dressed in his favorite jeans and one of his two remaining t-shirts, then walked to the café down the street. The warmth of the place and the hearty breakfast of eggs, toast, and strong coffee helped clear the fog from his mind. By the time he returned home, he felt ready to tackle the mountain of unpacking that awaited him.
His own suitcase, which he had bought just months ago and considered quite nice at the time, looked shabby next to the two pristine pieces that had accompanied him home, courtesy of Ian and Leo. Both were top-of-the-line, and Jay ran his fingers over the smooth leather with appreciation.
He started with the gifts, complete with complimentary presentation boxes. Ian had insisted on the expensive options, brushing aside Jay's protests with a firm "Leo's paying" whenever Jay had tried to suggest something more reasonable. Jay prepared everything for shipping to his siblings, already dreading their reactions to such lavish presents.
They had also purchased souvenirs for their team members, which Jay thought was a thoughtful gesture.
Afterwards Jay turned to his clothes. The process was both revelatory and depressing. His casual wardrobe had already been decimated. There was nothing left to donate. He was down to one pair of jeans, one pair of sweatpants, and two t-shirts for casual wear, not counting his gym clothes. He didn't even own hoodies anymore. Everything else had been gradually replaced by an ever-growing collection of formal attire.
Ten suits now hung in his closet. The new three-piece sets from the upscale tailor commanded the center position, flanked by lighter summer suits, event wear, and the pieces he had accumulated over the winter. A tuxedo hung at one end, and two designer outfits that no longer seemed quite so outlandish completed the formal section. The resort wear, shirts, shoes, and ties that couldn't fit in the closet were relegated to the suitcases, which now formed an awkward tower in the center of his living room. His casual clothes perched precariously on top, making the whole arrangement look like some bizarre fashion installation.
His studio had transformed into what looked like a high-end clothing warehouse. Jay stood in the middle of it all, hands on his hips, surveying the space with dismay. This wasn't how normal people lived.
The afternoon trip to the post office provided a welcome break from the domestic chaos. The postal worker raised an eyebrow at the declared value of the packages, but Jay just smiled and paid the insurance fees. Once the gifts were safely dispatched, he sent Ema a photo from the hotel room with a simple message: "Trip was great. Expect deliveries promptly."
His phone rang within minutes.
"Jay! This place looks incredible! Tell me everything. Did you really stay somewhere this fancy? What was the food like? Did you meet anyone interesting? And what did you send us?"
Jay smiled as he walked home. He kept the details light, focusing on the sights and the food. By the time they hung up, some of his own confusion had subsided.
That evening, he cooked dinner for the entire week, portioning everything into containers, and prepared his clothes and equipment for work. 
The new laptop sat on his table next to his old one, the contrast almost cruel. His college computer, which had served him faithfully for years and required countless hours of part-time work to afford, now looked like something salvaged from a scrapyard. The sleek new machine was undoubtedly superior in every way, but Jay couldn't bring himself to use it. It represented something he resented—a bribe to placate Ian after Jay had been treated like a prostitute. A gesture to smooth over the humiliation he had endured.
But then Ian's words echoed in his memory. The harasser was the one who had behaved badly, and it had no bearing on Jay as a person. Ian had protected him, would continue to protect him. With Ian around, he didn't need to fear anyone trying to take advantage of him.
It was a good thought, one that helped quiet the more troubling feelings the gift evoked.
He arrived at work at seven-thirty Tuesday morning to find Ian already in the office. They exchanged their typical morning greetings, and Jay felt emboldened by their shared experience. After dropping his things and powering up his computer, Jay walked into Ian's office for a chat.
"How's the jetlag treating you?" Jay asked.
"Better than expected. I managed a decent night's sleep. What about you? You look much more alert than you did at the airport. I was worried you might struggle more with the adjustment."  
"Still feeling a bit off-schedule, but nothing coffee can't handle. When did you get in this morning?"  
"Six-thirty. Did you manage to get everything sorted at home?"
"Eventually. Though my apartment now looks like a clothing warehouse exploded in it. I had to stack suitcases in the middle of my living room just to fit everything."
"Is the space becoming a problem? We could look into storage solutions, or perhaps it's time to consider a larger apartment."
The comment was casual, but Ian's tone made Jay look at him more closely. There was a relaxation in his posture that hadn't been there in weeks, an ease that reminded Jay of their late-night conversations before everything had become complicated.
"I'm not sure I'm ready for that yet. It's just... a lot to adjust to." Jay paused, choosing his words carefully. "The whole trip was... eye-opening. I learned a lot."
"It was good to get away from the office. Sometimes perspective requires a change of scenery."
Both seeming to recognize that they were talking around something larger. Jay felt the weight of all the things he couldn't say: how the walls they had both built seemed suddenly unnecessary.
"You handled yourself admirably. I hope it wasn't too overwhelming."
"It wasn't. And thank you. For everything. For protecting me."
As he returned to his desk, Jay realized that his understanding of what he wanted changed. Jay wanted to be closer to Ian. Maybe not a friend—he was still a subordinate, after all—but something more than just an employee. Ian had become someone he could rely on, someone who had proven himself worthy of trust. And Jay wanted to be the same for him.
An anchor. Someone solid and dependable in a world that had shown Ian too little kindness.
The community Ian was born into had rejected him despite his obvious worth, despite him being a victim of a scheme. The injustice was unthinkable, but perhaps Ian was right about there being no separate worlds—just people who were small and petty regardless of their status.
Scene 2
Souvenirs
Jay began sorting through the backlog that had accumulated despite his efforts to stay on top of emails during the trip. Several tasks had fallen behind schedule, and with the month drawing to a close, he wanted everything sorted before October's chaos descended upon them.
His colleagues gradually came into the office, each asking about his trip. Jay waited until everyone was in place before making his rounds with the souvenirs. He had prepared a diverse collection: local confectionery, special edition hot sauces, designer bracelets, leather key-chains, and various other small treasures that captured the essence of their destination.
"Alright everyone, gather round. We brought back a few things," Jay announced, producing the first bag of gifts. 
The vintage band t-shirts were an immediate hit with Luc and Kim. They rifled through, attempting to claim the most coveted pieces.
"No way," Kim said, holding up a faded tour shirt. "Where did you even find this?"
"The hotel concierge recommended a vintage shop," Jay explained. 
"This one's mine. Absolutely no negotiation," Kim declared.
"You can have that one if I get this," Luc countered, clutching a shirt from a band Jay had never heard of but that apparently held cultural weight.
"You two are pathetic," Amy joked.
Jay was relieved to see that Luc looked better than he had before the trip. The hollow look around his eyes had faded, and his energy was returning. Still, Jay had prepared something special for him.
"Luc, this one's for you. It's supposed to be comforting, made with warm spices. Thought you might appreciate it," Jay said, producing a large block of chocolate.
Luc accepted the gift with exaggerated solemnity, then broke into a grin. "Are you trying to tell me I need comfort chocolate? Because that's both insulting and completely accurate."
"Just thought you might enjoy it."
"Well, since you put actual thought into this, I suppose I can forgive the implication that I'm emotionally fragile."
"You know what? After the quarterly reports are done and we're all human again, we should do something fun. Something completely different," Jay suddenly said.
"That sounds great. What did you have in mind?"
"I don't know yet, but something adventurous. Or at least more interesting than post-crisis drinks." 
Luc tilted his head, watching Jay with suspicion. "Wait a minute. Did someone switch you out for a different person during this trip? Because the Jay who left here would never suggest doing something different and fun without at least three layers of anxiety about it."
"Maybe I just learned a few things while I was away."
"Clearly. I approve of this upgraded version."
Once the gifts were distributed, the team naturally gathered around Jay's desk, eager for details about the trip. He kept his stories light and focused on the cultural aspects: the impressive hotels, the restaurants, the general atmosphere of the destination.
"The negotiations were about as boring as you'd expect. Standard corporate meetings, nothing worth mentioning. But the restaurant where we had dinner was incredible. Completely different vibe from anywhere here. You know how the Palladium feels all stately and old-world? This place was the opposite. Ultra-modern, cosmopolitan. Made me feel like I was in a movie."
He avoided any mention of the disastrous incident. That part of the trip still felt too complicated to share as casual office anecdotes.
The storytelling session gradually dissolved as everyone returned to their own work, and Jay threw himself back into his tasks. He worked through the afternoon, clearing his backlog and organizing priorities for the coming days.
At five o'clock sharp, he began shutting down his computer and gathering his things.
"Heading out?" Ian asked, appearing at his desk.
"Gym day. Already missed Saturday, and Rus is definitely going to make me pay for it. He's going to push me twice as hard to make up for the missed session."
Ian's face showed the faintest hint of amusement. "It'll be worth it. Better to face the punishment now than let it compound."
"Easy for you to say. You're not the one who's going to be doing extra sets." Jay headed for the elevator, calling back over his shoulder. "See you tomorrow."
Ian watched him go, his composed expression slipping the moment Jay disappeared around the corner. Four days together had been like a glimpse of paradise, and now having to let him walk away each evening felt like a particular form of punishment. The shared meals, the conversations that lasted late into the night, all of it had felt natural, right, like something he could build a life around.
The quarterly reports couldn't come soon enough. A week of consistent overtime meant he would get to see Jay from dawn to dusk, a prospect that filled him with anticipation even as he recognized how pathetic that made him. He forced himself to focus on his work, but thoughts of Jay persisted, threading through every task like a constant undercurrent.
At the gym, Jay presented Rus with his own gift: a t-shirt and baseball cap from an iconic athletic brand, both featuring designs that weren't available locally.
"Just a small thank you. For taking such good care of me all these months."
Rus looked touched, which made the gesture feel completely justified. "This is really thoughtful of you. I appreciate it."
"I'm glad you like them."
"Of course, you realize this changes nothing about tonight's workout. In fact, since you clearly think you can bribe me into going easy on you, I'm going to have to double your routine. Can't have you thinking gifts will get you special treatment."
"I was afraid you'd say that."
The workout was indeed brutal, but Jay met it with energy he hadn't expected. He joked with Rus between sets, feeling more relaxed than he had in weeks. There was something liberating about returning to his own spaces after the trip.
"You seem different tonight," Rus observed as Jay finished a particularly challenging set. "More energized. Things better at work?"
"Yeah, for the time being. Though that's about to change. We're heading into quarterly report season, which means I won't be able to come to the gym for at least a week."
Rus shook his head with mock disapproval. "The gym should be an obvious priority. I don't care how busy work gets, you need to maintain your routine."
"Tell that to my boss when I'm working fourteen-hour days."
"I will if I have to. Health comes first, career comes second. That's not negotiable."
Jay laughed, appreciating both the concern and the absurdity of imagining Rus lecturing Ian about workout schedules. "I'll do my best to sneak in a session or two if I can."
"See that you do. I don't want to have to start over from scratch when you come back."
On his way home, Jay made one final stop at Café Lemon. Fay was behind the counter, and her face lit up when she saw him approaching with a small bag.
"Jay! How was your trip?"
"Incredible. I brought you something. Just a small thank you for all the great coffee over the years."
Fay opened the bag to reveal an assortment of artisanal chocolates. "Oh my goodness, these look amazing! You didn't have to do this."
"I wanted to. You've been making my days better for a long time now."
"Well, in that case, let me make your evening better too. What can I get you?"
"Hot chocolate. It's getting chilly, and coffee this late would keep me up all night."
As Fay prepared his drink, they chatted about the changing weather and the melancholy that seemed to hang over the city as autumn approached.
"The days are getting shorter. Always makes everything feel more contemplative," Fay mused.
"I don't mind it as much as I used to," Jay admitted.
"There's something nice about this time of year. Everything slowing down, getting cosy. Makes you appreciate the simple things."
"I know what you mean. After a weekend of fancy restaurants, there's something perfect about just... this."
"Sounds like you had quite an adventure."
"I did. But it's good to be home."
Jay walked home feeling happy. It had been a good day. Most of his post-trip plans were complete, and tomorrow he could focus entirely on work. He felt ready for the challenges ahead.
