Perhaps he should have been exhausted after such an intense week, but he wasn't. He felt motivated and notably less tense. The business trip had reset his perspective on his work, his relationships, and his place in the world around him.
As he prepared dinner in his crowded apartment, surrounded by suitcases that served as a tangible reminder of how much his life had changed, Jay looked forward to tomorrow.
Scene 3
The grind
The quarterly reports came like a plague, turning the normally pleasant workspace into a pressure cooker of frayed nerves. Jay had hoped that experience would make this round easier, but if anything, it felt worse than before. The workload was relentless, the atmosphere tense, and everyone seemed to be operating on a hair trigger. The quarterly bonus notification that appeared on his phone might as well have been spam for all the attention he paid it. Everything outside the immediate demands of work faded into irrelevance.
Minor mistakes that would normally warrant a gentle correction now provoked sharp responses. Files that couldn't be located immediately became sources of panic. Jay was walking on eggshells, triple-checking everything and speaking in measured tones to avoid adding to the stress.
"Where's the Hartwell compliance folder?" Amy's called across the office.
Jay looked up from his screen where he was formatting the third revision of the Mayfield summary. "Client documentation server, compliance subfolder, dated last Thursday."
"I already checked there."
Jay saved his work and walked over to Amy's desk. He pulled up the network directory and searched through the folders. "Here. You were in the wrong quarterly folder."
"Thanks. Jesus, why do we have so many sub-folders?" Amy muttered.
Jay returned to his desk, immediately diving back into the spreadsheet that had consumed the last two hours, but shortly after Luc appeared.
"Jay. Can you format these client profiles? The template's giving me trouble."
"I'm in the middle of—"
"Please? It's for the board packet, and I'm drowning here."
Jay glanced toward Ian's office. Through the glass wall, he could see Ian hunched over his computer. He wanted to see if Ian needed anything, but Luc was already pulling up a chair.
"Fine. Send me the files."
From the corner of his eye, Jay caught Ian emerging from his office, moving toward the kitchen. Their eyes met briefly, and Jay wanted to join him, to have even a thirty-second conversation about something other than data formatting. But Luc was already launching into another technical complaint, and by the time Jay looked up again, Ian was gone.
The pattern repeated throughout the day. Jay would finish one task only to be intercepted by another colleague with another urgent request. Each one was necessary and time-sensitive. And each one kept Jay anchored to his desk or someone else's, never free to step into Ian's office for a check-in.
Even Ian seemed affected. The man who always maintained perfect composure appeared increasingly strained as the days wore on, his jaw tight and his responses clipped. Jay saw him staring at spreadsheets with resentment, which was so unlike him that Jay wondered if he was imagining things.
"Hartwell is more demanding than anticipated. It's just one client, but the reporting requirements are extensive," Ian muttered during one of their brief status meetings, rubbing his temples.
Jay nodded, not trusting himself to say anything more than basic acknowledgement. He was struggling to keep up with his own responsibilities, let alone provide meaningful input on strategic concerns.
The phone call from his siblings came at the worst possible moment, the caller ID showing Tim's name.
"Jay! This watch is incredible! Where did you even find something like this? Ari thinks you robbed a bank!"
"I'm glad you like it, but this is a terrible time. We're in the middle of quarterly reports and—"
"Just five minutes! We wanted to tell you about—"
"I can't. I'll call you back in a week when this is over."
"But—"
"I really can't right now. I'm sorry."
He hung up without registering their disappointment, his mind already moving to the next task. 
His gym membership went unused. The pool he had been so excited about became a distant memory. Thoughts of his diving lessons were pushed aside by the immediate crisis of deadlines and data analysis.
Saturday and Sunday blended into the weekdays, distinguished only by the fact that the building was emptier. Jay arrived early and left late, his world shrinking to the narrow corridor between his desk and Ian's office.
By six o'clock on Sunday, the office had taken on the atmosphere of a crisis centre. Papers covered every available surface. Someone had ordered dinner, and the team ate at their desks without conversation.
Jay had managed exactly two direct interactions with Ian all day, a brief handoff of formatted reports and a thirty-second status update on client communications. Each conversation had felt stilted and formal.
"I think I overextended us. Each quarter it's getting harder to manage the increased responsibilities," Ian admitted as they worked through yet another set of compliance documents.
Jay looked up from his computer, surprised by the admission. Ian never expressed doubt about strategic decisions, at least not in front of staff. "It's manageable. We're getting through it," Jay replied, though they both knew it was barely true.
Ian's smile was tired. "We are. Though I'm not sure at what cost."
When the reports were finally submitted on Thursday and the immediate crisis passed, the collective exhale from the entire team was almost audible. Jay slumped at his desk, staring blankly at his computer screen.
"That's it. We're done," Max announced.
Jay gathered his things, his mind already turning to the quarterly party the next evening. He glanced toward Ian's office, where Ian was shutting down his computer. They had worked in the same space for ten days straight, but Jay realized they had barely spoken beyond the requirements of immediate business.
The quarterly party, traditionally a celebration of successful completion, felt more like a wake for their sanity.
Jay dressed up for the occasion, selecting the dark gray three-piece suit that had made him feel confident during the business trip. Looking at himself in the mirror, he thought he looked like a senator or some other dignitary. The suit was undeniably elegant, but in the context of his normal life, it felt almost conspicuous in its quality.
Had it been this obvious during the trip? Perhaps he had been too overwhelmed by everything else to notice, but now the fine tailoring and expensive fabric seemed to announce themselves. He adjusted his tie nervously, half-expecting someone to comment, but when he arrived at the venue, no one seemed to notice or care.
The place was impeccable as always, the entertainment top-notch, the food and drink abundant. But the team drifted through it all, drained past the point of enjoyment.
Instead of networking and animated conversation, everyone gravitated toward chairs and quiet corners. The bar became a refuge, with people slouched on stools, eyes drifting between their half-finished glasses and the far wall.
Amy slumped in an armchair, Luc had claimed an entire section of seating, sprawling across it like he was recovering from a marathon. Even Max sat quietly, sipping whiskey and watching the party with unfocused eyes.
Jay sat at the bar, not interested in drinking but drawn to the relative quiet of the space.
"Rough quarter?" the bartender asked, more as a courtesy.
"The roughest yet," Jay replied, accepting a glass of wine he didn't want.
The week’s grind showed in Ian as well. His composure held, but traces of fatigue slipped through, attributed to the toll of the week’s demands. People nodded at him with sympathy, recognizing that even their seemingly indestructible CFO had been worn down.
"Even the man of steel looks beat," Kim said to Jay, gesturing toward where Ian stood in conversation with some board members. "If he's this tired, the rest of us must look like zombies."
Jay followed Kim's gesture and saw what he meant. Ian's posture was perfect, but his expression looked grim.
What Jay couldn't know was that Ian's mood had little to do with the workload itself and everything to do with the loss of their private evening conversations. The week that should have brought them together through shared overtime had instead kept them constantly surrounded by the full team, with Jay pulled in different directions by various colleagues who needed his help.
Ian had looked forward to those late nights, imagining moments where they could decompress together, share the burden of the work. Instead, he had watched Jay field constant requests from Amy, Luc, Kim, and others, his attention divided among so many people that their own interactions had been reduced to brief exchanges.
He had the perfect excuse to spend extensive time with Jay, but the circumstances made connection impossible. It was like being offered a feast while being too sick to eat.
As people began making their excuses to leave, Jay caught Ian's eye across the room. They shared a look that acknowledged the exhaustion and the relief that it was over. Tonight, they would go home to recover from a week that had tested everyone's limits.
Scene 4
Recovery and preparation
Jay sat propped against his pillows on Saturday morning, phone in hand, finally ready to face the conversations he had cut short during the week. He dialed his siblings' number, bracing himself for the inevitable complaints.
"Well, well, we were starting to think you'd forgotten we exist," Tim's voice came through with obvious irritation.
"I told you I was busy with—"
"Yeah, yeah, quarterly reports. We heard. You couldn't spare five minutes all week?"
"I'm sorry about that. The quarterly reports were—"
"Were more important than your family. You get a new, fancy job, and suddenly we're no longer worth your time." 
"You don't understand. Gifts this expensive don't come for free. I have to pay my dues by giving up about a week of my lifespan every quarter to reporting demands. It's non-negotiable."
"That's a bit dramatic, don't you think?" Tim shot back.
"Tim, stop it." Ema's voice cut through his complaints. "You don't understand the grown-up working world. Jay had responsibilities."
Jay felt a wave of gratitude toward his sister. "Thank you, Ema. It really was impossible to take personal calls."
"We know," Ema said warmly. "Now tell us about these amazing gifts. Tim hasn't taken the watch off since it arrived."
The tension dissolved as his siblings launched into enthusiastic descriptions of their presents. Jay listened with growing amusement as they detailed every feature of their gifts, comparing them to friends' possessions and generally reveling in the splendour.
"Where did you even find stuff like this?" Ari asked. "The headphones are incredible. I can hear things in my music I never noticed before."
"We went to make a deal with an electronics company. The gifts were one of the perks of the business trip. You should see the laptop I got out of it. It makes all your gifts look modest in comparison," Jay explained, stretching the truth only slightly.
"No way. What kind of laptop?" Tim asked. 
Jay glanced at the sleek machine sitting on his table, still barely touched. "Top of the line everything. More processing power than I'll ever need."
"That's so cool," Ari said, then his voice turned hesitant. "Um, Jay? What are you going to do with your old laptop?"
Jay looked at his faithful college computer, battered and slow but still functional. The idea of throwing it away had been bothering him for days. "Why do you ask?"
"Well, Mom and Dad won't buy me one, and Tim won't share his. I was just wondering..."
Jay thought about it for a moment. The old machine had served him well through college and his early working years. Maybe a few more years of heavy use by his youngest brother wasn't a bad retirement strategy. At least his hard work from college would be properly utilized.
"Alright. I'll make arrangements to send it to you."
Ari's screech of joy was so loud Jay had to hold the phone away from his ear.
"Really? You're serious?"
"Yes, but you have to promise to take good care of it. And help with homework, not just games."
"I promise! I promise everything!"
"I'll get it shipped next week."
They talked for another half hour, with Jay sharing edited stories from his trip while his siblings asked him questions. By the time they hung up, the stress had started to lift and Jay finally smiled.
The gym felt foreign after more than a week away. Jay approached the front desk with the resignation, expecting punishment.
"Look what the cat dragged in," Rus called from across the floor. "I was starting to think you'd found a new trainer."
"I told you this would happen."
Rus crossed his arms, his expression stern but concerned. "A week and a half, Jay. That's not a missed session, that's abandoning your routine entirely."
"I know, I know. But please be gentle today. I just survived the worst quarter of my career and I'm practically on death's door."
"That bad?"
"Worse. I worked seven days straight, slept maybe four hours a night, and lived on coffee and whatever food got delivered to the office."
"Jesus. Alright, we'll take it easy today. But only because you look like hell."
"I feel like hell."
"When's the last time you ate a real meal?"
Jay had to think about it. "Thursday? Maybe Wednesday."
"Right. Light workout today, and I'm writing you a meal plan. You can't maintain any kind of fitness routine if you're going to crash your system every three months."
The workout was mercifully basic, but even the simple movements felt awkward after days of hunching over computers. Jay's body protested every move, reminding him of how completely he had neglected everything outside of work.
"This is exactly why consistency matters," Rus observed as Jay struggled with a basic stretch.
"I know. It won't happen again."
"It will, though. That's the problem."
Jay couldn't argue with that assessment. "Just four times a year."
"That's four times too many if you ask me."
Jay spent the rest of the weekend on basic maintenance. He did laundry, grocery shopping, and the minimum household chores necessary to function. The remainder of the time he was horizontal on his couch, catching up on sleep and trying to process the emotional exhaustion that had accumulated during the week. By Sunday evening, he felt better.
He returned to an office that felt subdued, almost shell-shocked, on Monday morning. His colleagues moved through their tasks with sluggishly, still recovering from the trauma. 
Jay threw himself into the fallout from the quarterly reports, minor corrections to submitted documents, the inevitable cleanup that always followed such intense periods. But he was distracted, going through the motions while struggling to focus.
By seven o'clock, the office had emptied except for Jay, who was still working through a backlog of administrative tasks. Outside, the autumn darkness had set in, making the building feel isolated.
"Working late again?"
Jay looked up to see Ian approaching with two cups of coffee.
"Just catching up on things that got pushed aside last week."
Ian sat on the chair next to Jay's desk and set one of the coffee cups within Jay's reach. "I brought reinforcements. Everything alright?"
Jay stared at his computer screen for a moment before answering. "Nothing's wrong, exactly. The opposite, actually. Things are good. The week was brutal, but apart from that, I don't really have anything to complain about. I feel confident, hopeful about the future."
"That sounds positive."
"It is. Which is precisely the problem. Every time I've felt this way before, something happened to put me back in my place. So I guess I'm just bracing for impact, trying not to get too comfortable with feeling good about myself."
"Failure is part of life, especially in an environment like this. I can't promise things will never go wrong again, because they almost certainly will."
Jay's shoulders sagged slightly.
"But," Ian continued, "you already know you can survive setbacks. You've done it multiple times, and you get better at handling them each time. Look at where you are now compared to when you started here. That progression isn't coincidence or luck. You seized every opportunity. Don't forget that, even when things get difficult."
"You have more faith in me than I have in myself."
"I have a clearer perspective. You're too close to your own struggles to see how consistently you overcome them."
Jay felt some of the tension in his chest ease. "Thank you. I needed to hear that."
The following weeks, Jay focused on preparation. He resumed his pool sessions and finished the diving course. Ina put him through increasingly complex scenarios, building his confidence for the trip ahead.
"You're ready. The open water will be different, but you have all the skills you need."
"I hope so. It still feels surreal that I'm actually doing this."
"Best way to conquer fear is to walk straight through it."
At the gym, Rus gradually rebuilt Jay's routine, though he remained skeptical about the sustainability of the schedule.
"You're making good progress," Rus admitted, "but what happens during the next quarterly crisis?"
"I'll try to maintain some kind of routine. Maybe shorter sessions, but something."
"We'll see."
Jay also finally transferred his data to the new laptop, a process that took an entire evening and left him impressed by the machine's capabilities. When he packaged up his college laptop to send to Ari, he felt like he was saying goodbye to an era of his life.
The occasional overtime resumed, but at manageable levels, and the evening conversations with Ian became a regular feature again.
They would meet in the small kitchen area around six o'clock, both making their final coffee of the day while discussing everything from work challenges to random observations about city life.
"Doesn't the caffeine keep you awake?" Ian asked one evening as Jay prepared his beverage.
"I'm usually so tired I fall asleep immediately anyway."
"Are you pushing yourself too hard? I don't want you burning out."
"I don't think so. This feels sustainable, unlike the quarterly madness. I can handle the regular workload."
"Good. Because I'd rather have you working at eighty percent indefinitely than at one hundred percent until you collapse."
Jay felt a warmth that had nothing to do with the coffee. "I'm not pushing myself too hard."
"I'm glad to hear it."
As his departure date approached, Jay realized he felt as ready as he could possibly be. The diving skills were as solid as they could be and his documents were in order. Most importantly, he was returning to something good when he came back. It was a decent foundation to build from, and Jay allowed himself to believe it might last.
Scene 5
Vacation
On Friday afternoon, as Jay made his rounds with final farewells, a surprising warmth spread through the office. He had expected polite wishes for a good trip, but instead got enveloped in a series of embraces.
Max was the first to pull him into a hearty hug. "Don't do anything too adventurous down there. We need you back in one piece."
"I'll try to keep the sharks at bay," Jay replied, surprised by how natural the embrace felt.
Amy followed suit. "Can't believe you're actually doing this. Solo diving trip to the tropics. Very impressive."
Luc's hug lasted longer than the others. "You better come back in one piece, Hall. I've gotten used to having someone competent around here."
"I'll do my best."
"See that you do."
Zoe approached with a warm smile and a folded piece of paper. "This is everything you absolutely must do while you're there," she said, pressing the list into his hands along with a brief hug. "Rent an underwater camera for photos, and try to spot parrotfish if you can. Oh, and remember to equalize frequently during descents. Some people forget when they're excited."
Jay unfolded the list, finding detailed diving tips and marine life recommendations. "This is incredibly thoughtful. Thank you."
"You're going to love it."
His final conversation of the day took place in Ian's office, where Jay knocked on the doorframe in the late afternoon light.
"Do you have a moment?"
Ian looked up from his computer, immediately giving Jay his full attention. "Of course. All set for your adventure?"
"I think so. Triple-checked everything. What about your plans? A whole week off must feel strange."
"Nothing specific. Might visit some old friends, catch up on reading."
The vague response felt deliberate, but Jay didn't press. "That sounds nice. Relaxing."
"Exactly."
In reality, Ian had no plans at all. A week without Jay was an ordeal to be endured, not an enjoyable vacation. But sharing that information seemed inappropriate, potentially burdensome.
Both were aware that this was goodbye for more than a week. Jay was reluctant to leave, wanted to extend the conversation indefinitely. Ian seemed similarly hesitant to bring their time to a close.
"You should get home and finish packing," Ian said finally.
"Right. Early flight tomorrow."
"I want you to be careful out there. Follow the safety protocols, don't take unnecessary risks."
"I won't. Ina drilled proper procedures into me pretty thoroughly."
Jay wanted to say something meaningful, to acknowledge how much these conversations had come to mean to him, but the words felt too large.
Ian got up, coming around his desk. For a moment they stood facing each other, closer than their normal interactions required. Jay wanted to offer some physical gesture of farewell, and from Ian's expression, the impulse was mutual.
But the office setting, the uncertainty of what such a gesture might mean or imply, created a barrier that neither could cross. They stood awkwardly, both wanting something they couldn't name or claim.
"Have a wonderful trip," Ian said.
"Thank you. I'll see you when I get back."
Jay forced himself to turn and walk away, fighting the urge to look back as he headed for the elevator. Behind him, Ian remained standing in his office doorway until the elevator doors closed.
Jay woke up on Saturday morning at five-thirty, with his alarm still an hour away. He lay in bed for a moment, acknowledging that further sleep was impossible. The night was restless. Every half hour brought him bolt upright, checking his phone, then lying back down to repeat the process.
Finally he got up to begin his final preparations. Coffee first, strong and black to cut through the fog of exhaustion. Then a shower to wake himself up properly. One final review of his documents, passport, tickets, diving certification cards.
Everything was exactly as he had left it the night before, and the night before that, and every night for the past week.
The public transport to the airport felt surreal, a ordinary commute that was taking him toward an extraordinary personal milestone. He watched other passengers, wondering who else might be embarking on solo adventures, who else might be pushing past their comfort zones into unknown territory.
The airport was less intimidating than expected. His early arrival gave him ample time to get through check-in and security, and he located his gate without difficulty. The four-hour flight in economy class was cramped compared to his recent experience with Ian, but tolerable. More importantly, flying no longer frightened him the way it once had.
When they landed in Lagoon Belt, humid, flower-scented air rushed in and the immediate disorientation of being somewhere completely foreign set in. The taxi ride to his hotel provided glimpses of palm trees and an ocean bluer than any body of water he had ever seen.
The hotel staff greeted him, providing pamphlets about local activities and evening entertainment options. 
"Welcome party starts at seven. Great way to meet your fellow divers," the desk clerk informed him cheerfully. 
Jay thanked her and went to his room to unpack and try to calm his nerves. He spread the materials across his bed, suddenly overwhelmed by having to be responsible for his own itinerary in a place where he knew no one.
The party was larger and livelier than Jay expected. He wore his resort wear from the company retreat, feeling slightly overdressed. The gathering happened across a beachside terrace, with music, local cuisine, and an abundance of alcohol that quickly loosened tongues and inhibitions.
Jay positioned himself near the edge of the crowd, holding a beer and trying to blend into the background. His strategy worked until a boisterous instructor spotted him.
"First time diving in tropical waters?" he asked, sitting beside Jay without invitation.
"Second, but it's my first time diving solo."
"Excellent! I'm Ash, I'll be your instructor. You're going to love it out there. The visibility is incredible, and we've got some fantastic coral formations. Have you seen many tropical fish before? The variety here is amazing. There's a resident green moray that likes to hang out near the drop-off..."
Ash launched into an enthusiastic monologue about local marine life and diving conditions that lasted nearly two hours. Jay was content to listen, asking occasional questions that kept Ash talking while requiring minimal participation on his own part. The arrangement suited them both: Ash had an attentive audience, and Jay could participate socially without the pressure of carrying conversation.
By ten o'clock, travel fatigue was weighing heavily on Jay's eyelids. He made his excuses, weathering Ash's protests about leaving early.
"Come on, the night's just getting started! This is when the real stories come out!"
"I'm sure they do, but I'm completely exhausted. Long day of travel."
"Alright, alright. Tomorrow we dive, so rest up. You'll need your energy."
Jay retreated to his room, showered quickly, and went into bed, falling asleep instantly. 
On Sunday morning, Jay encountered a truly tropical environment. He walked the hotel grounds in wonder, marvelling at palm trees, white sand, and water in shades of turquoise and deep blue.
The ocean here was different from the seaside of his country in every way. Where home waters were cold and grey-green, these were warm and brilliantly coloured, with beaches that stretched for miles and waves that crashed with impressive force.
Jay spread his towel on the hot sand and lay back, enjoying the morning breeze. His mind felt completely quiet, freed from the constant undercurrent of work concerns and personal anxieties.
His first dive happened in the early afternoon. Jay had expected more nerves, but descending into the warm, clear water felt natural after the months of pool training. His small group of fellow novices moved clumsily at first, but Ash's patient guidance and the forgiving conditions made the experience enjoyable.
Swimming among fish, seeing coral formations, Jay felt a sense of accomplishment. This was purely personal, a goal he had set and achieved entirely for himself.
Later that afternoon, he explored the nearby town, a collection of old continent architecture that seemed transported from another era. At the post office, he selected postcards for his siblings and, on impulse, one for the office. The messages were brief: "Weather beautiful, diving amazing, wish you were here."
The evening's congratulatory dinner celebrating everyone's successful first dive was loud as alcohol flowed. Jay participated enough to be sociable but excused himself early, claiming exhaustion that was entirely real.
The rest of the week moved along in a similar pattern. Morning dives or afternoon sessions, depending on conditions and scheduling. Beach time when he wasn't underwater, either sunbathing or swimming in the ocean. Evening walks around the hotel grounds, appreciating the tropical flowers and the sound of waves.
The diving itself was easier than expected. His technique improved each day, and his confidence in the underwater environment grew. Jay's photos weren't particularly good, but they captured enough of the experience to serve as meaningful reminders.
Ash remained his primary social contact, still as talkative and outgoing as ever, but his attention was distributed among all the guests. Most other participants were couples or friend groups, and the few solo travellers like himself seemed content with their own company.
Jay attended enough group activities to be polite but skipped most evening parties in favour of solitary pursuits. Walks, meals at the hotel restaurant, sightseeing the local town. It suited his temperament perfectly.
By week's end, he had earned his open water certification and accumulated a collection of small souvenirs: local snacks, postcards, a few pieces of simple jewelry that caught his eye. More importantly, he had proven to himself that solo travel was not only survivable but enjoyable.
The return journey felt both too soon and perfectly timed. Jay was tanned, thoroughly exercised from daily diving and swimming, and content with his week. As the plane lifted off from Lagoon Belt, he thought about Ian's vacation, wondering if it had been equally restorative.
The thought led to a comparison between this trip and his recent travels with Ian. Both had been successful in their own ways, but the company retreat and New Continent trip had felt more significant, more memorable. Perhaps because they had been shared experiences, or perhaps because Ian had made every moment feel special.
This vacation had been good for him. But he was glad to be returning home.
Jay got back to his apartment on Saturday evening, and spent Sunday unpacking, sorting clothes for laundry and redistributing items among his collection of suitcases. The tropical warmth was already a memory as chilly November air crept through his windows. Halloween had passed entirely unnoticed while he was away, and evening was falling with the early darkness of approaching winter.
None of the seasonal transition bothered him. The overcast skies and cooling temperatures felt refreshing. He was back in the city that had become his own, in the life he had built for himself. 
Jay finished his unpacking, already looking forward to Monday morning. He was home. Or rather, he would be home tomorrow, right where he belonged: in front of Ian's office, ready to resume their shared work.
Security
Chapter 26: Uncharted territories
Scene 1
Return
Jay pushed through the building's glass doors at exactly seven-thirty Monday morning, his steps quickening as he approached the elevator. Returning to work felt like slipping into comfortable clothes after a week of formal attire. But it wasn't the office itself that drew him forward with such anticipation.
The elevator doors opened to reveal the thirty-fourth floor, most desks still empty, computers dark. But through the glass wall of Ian's office, Jay could see exactly what he had hoped for: Ian already at his desk, immaculately dressed, his blonde hair perfectly styled, completely absorbed in reading a document.
A wave of blissful calm washed over him, so profound it stopped him mid-step. The vacation had been wonderful, the diving exhilarating, the warm beaches restorative, but none of it compared to this moment of seeing Ian in his natural environment, focused and commanding and absolutely perfect. Nothing brought Jay the deep contentment he felt simply looking at Ian again.
Jay stood frozen for a moment, drinking in the sight with fresh eyes. Everything about Ian seemed more vivid after a week away: the lines of his face, the elegance of his movements as he turned pages. Being close to him again felt like coming home in the truest sense.
Jay felt even more awestruck than during their first meeting, when Ian had been too intimidating to properly appreciate. Now that same perfection felt comforting, like the fixed point around which everything else in his life had learned to orbit.
Jay moved to his own desk, unpacking his bag and powering up his computer. When he glanced up to see Ian setting aside his document and stretching slightly, Jay didn't hesitate.
He knocked on Ian's door frame. "Good morning."
Ian's face softened the moment he saw Jay. "Jay. Welcome back. How was the trip?"
"Incredible." Jay stepped into the office, closing the door behind him out of habit and sitting in the chair in front of Ian's desk. The early morning privacy felt precious, a gift of uninterrupted time together.  "The diving was everything I hoped it would be. Got my open water certification, saw some amazing marine life. The water was so clear you could see thirty meters in any direction."
"The diving went well then?"
"Better than I hoped. I actually felt competent by the end. It's amazing how different it feels when you're not fighting panic."
"You look well. Rested."
"I feel good. It was exactly what I needed."
"I'm glad to hear it. Thank you for the postcard."
Jay followed Ian's gesture toward the calendar on his desk, where his tropical postcard was pinned among important dates and meeting reminders. It made his heart flutter, but he tried not to show it. He hadn't expected Ian to display it so publicly.
"What about your time off? Did you manage to do anything interesting?" Jay asked.
"Not much, really. Catching up on personal matters."
"Come on, you had a full week. You must have done something besides household chores."
"I drove to see some old university friends and did some walking around the old campus. Nothing too exciting."
Jay sensed something held back in Ian's response, but the details mattered less than the reassurance that Ian had taken time for himself. "I'm glad you did something relaxing. You work too hard."
"Pot calling the kettle black," Ian replied with a crooked smile.
"Fair point." Jay got up, recognizing that other staff would begin arriving soon and their private time would end. "I should let you get back to your reading. I've got a pile of November assignments waiting."
"It's good to have you back, Jay."
"It's good to be back."
Ian watched Jay return to his desk through his office window, while his heart performed acrobatics that would have been embarrassing in a teenager, let alone a thirty-nine-year-old executive.
Seeing Jay again had unlocked something in his chest that he had spent a week trying to suppress. Jay looked radiant and happy, and it made Ian's emotional walls feel like tissue paper. The tan suited him, and there was an ease in his movements that made him look even more alluring.
Ian had arrived at the office at six-thirty, telling himself it was to catch up on work that had accumulated during his absence. The real reason was more pathetic: he wanted to maximize every possible moment with Jay, and arriving early meant an extra hour of shared space before their colleagues arrived to dilute his attention.
When he had found Jay's postcard in his mail pile, the phrase "wish you were here" had made his heart leap with ridiculous hope. He knew it was standard postcard language, meaningless and automatic. But he had pinned it to his calendar anyway, and spent far too much time staring at Jay's handwriting and imagining what it would mean if those words were honest.
Now Jay was here, and Ian could finally breathe properly again. He hadn't fully recognized how suffocating the week without him had been until it ended.
He had lied about his vacation. There had been no friends, no social catching up. He had driven to his university town alone, seeking distraction from the constant ache of Jay's absence. He had walked the streets, sat in cafes where he had once studied, visited the library where he had spent countless hours as a student.
The melancholy hadn't provided the escape he had hoped for. Instead, it had brought clarity. There was no point in fighting the current. He was in love with Jay and no amount of emotional discipline was going to change that reality.
What he could control was how he handled that love. He would find ways to spend time with Jay without making him uncomfortable. He would provide support and mentorship and whatever else Jay needed, regardless of how inappropriate his motivations might be. He would cross whatever lines necessary to ensure Jay felt valued and secure. He was done pretending that spending time with Jay wasn't the driving force behind most of his decisions.
It wasn't a sustainable long-term strategy, but it was all he had. And watching Jay sit back at his desk, organize his files, Ian decided this was what he wanted. Having Jay here, under his care, was worth whatever personal cost it demanded.
The elevator chimed, signaling the arrival of their first colleague. Their private time was ending, but Jay was back, and that was everything Ian needed to face another day of pretending. He turned back to his documents, already planning how to create more opportunities for their conversations.
The office filled around eight-thirty, and the morning's peace gave way to the bustle of a workday. Jay told stories about his trip, particularly to Zoe.
"Did you see any parrotfish? They're incredible to watch when they're feeding on coral."
"Several different species. And the photos I took don't do them justice, but I managed to capture a few decent shots."
"The underwater camera worked well then?"
"Better than expected. I was worried I'd be too clumsy with the controls, but it became easier after the first few dives."
Luc appeared beside them. "Look at you, all tanned and seasoned. Like a proper adventurer instead of our pale office drone."
For once, the comment about his appearance didn't make Jay self-conscious. "I do feel different. More capable, maybe."
"It shows. You look like someone who conquered something important."
"I did. The whole solo travel thing felt impossible when I first started planning it, but by the end I was enjoying the independence."
"Good for you. Nothing like proving to yourself you can handle whatever you set your mind to."
Luc was right. Jay had proven something important to himself during the past week, and returning to the office felt like bringing that new confidence with him.
Scene 2
Evening chat
Lunch at the nearby restaurant became an extended celebration of Jay's successful diving adventure. Luc and Zoe flanked him at their table, asking follow-up questions that kept the conversation animated throughout the meal.
"So the visibility was really that clear? You could see the bottom even at fifteen meters?" Zoe asked.
"Crystal clear. Like swimming in an aquarium, except everything was real and alive. The fish weren't afraid of divers at all. Some of them would swim right up to the camera. The colours were so much more vivid in person."
Zoe nodded knowingly. "The camera never captures it properly. That's why I always tell people the real experience is worth ten times more than any documentation."
Jay talked eagerly, the memory of his adventure still fresh enough to spark excitement. Both his colleagues seemed pleased by his enthusiasm, asking detailed questions about the specific dive sites he had visited.
The afternoon passed productively as Jay worked through his post-vacation backlog, but it was the late coffee break that made an ordinary day more memorable. What started as a brief kitchen encounter stretched into an extended conversation that seemed to take on a life of its own. Jay sat at the tall stool by the window table while Ian stood against the wall, both of them quickly getting lost in the discussion.
"The solitary walks were my favourite part. There's something exhilarating about exploring a new place entirely at your own pace, following whatever catches your interest."
"That sounds appealing. No schedules, no obligations to anyone else's preferences."
"Yeah. I'd wander into the town in the evenings, just observing how different everything felt. The architecture, the way people moved, even the quality of light was different."
Ian asked questions that drew out more details, seeming interested in Jay's observations about cultural differences and the contrast between tropical warmth and their current November chill.
"Coming back to falling leaves and cold mornings was jarring. Though not unpleasant. Just a reminder that I need to get serious about practical things again," Jay admitted.
"Such as?"
"The apartment search, mainly. I've been putting it off, but with winter coming and my living situation getting more cramped by the month, I really need to focus on finding something better."
"Have you started looking at specific units?"
"Not really. I keep telling myself I'll start, but then work gets busy or I get distracted by other things."
"What kind of space are you hoping to find?"
"Something larger, obviously. Maybe a proper bedroom instead of just a studio layout. Better kitchen facilities would be nice too. I've gotten tired of cooking everything in a single small pan."
Ian asked about Jay's timeline, his budget and commute considerations, discussed the neighbourhood characteristics and the pros and cons of older buildings versus modern construction, and Jay elaborated on details he hadn't fully articulated to himself before. Ian's questions prompted Jay to explore his own thoughts about what kind of home he wanted to create.
Finally Jay checked his watch and discovered with surprise that nearly an hour had passed. "I should probably get back to work though. I was hoping to make more progress on the backlog tonight."
"Of course. Though it was good to hear about your trip in detail."
Jay returned to his desk energized by the unexpected conversation. The tasks felt less tedious, and they continued to work until nearly ten o'clock. It wasn't strictly necessary, the backlog could have easily waited until tomorrow. But the shared late-evening felt less like work and more like a long awaited social call. As they finally prepared to leave, Jay hadn't minded the extended hours at all. If anything, the evening had felt too short.
Tuesday evening was Ian's least favourite part of Jay's weekly routine. At exactly five o'clock, Jay began shutting down his computer and gathering his belongings.
"Gym night?" Ian asked, though he already knew the answer.
"Six o'clock session with Rus. Haven't seen him since before the vacation, so I'm expecting some commentary about my tan."
Ian managed what he hoped was an appropriately encouraging smile. "I'm sure he'll be pleased you stayed active during your time away."
"We'll see. He tends to find something to critique regardless."
Jay headed home to change, leaving Ian alone in the office with his increasingly irrational resentment toward a personal trainer he had never met. The logical part of his mind recognized that Jay's fitness routine was healthy and important, that Rus was helping Jay build physical strength that benefited every aspect of his life.
The less logical part wanted to throttle anyone who commanded Jay's time and attention outside of work hours.
Ian sat in his office, staring at nothing in particular, contemplating his emotions. He needed Jay closer, in every possible sense. The apartment search Jay had mentioned presented an opportunity, and Ian was already mentally reviewing his contacts in real estate circles.
It was manipulative, he knew that. Jay deserved to find housing based on his own preferences and needs, not Ian's desire to engineer proximity. But helping someone find a better living situation was hardly villainous, even if his motives were entirely selfish.
He wouldn't coerce Jay into anything he didn't want. But perhaps he could ensure that Jay's options included some attractive possibilities in neighbourhoods that happened to be convenient to Ian's own life. Surely there were agents who valued his gratitude above strict profit considerations.
At the gym, Jay threw himself into his workout with vigour. Rus looked at him with approval.
"You look healthy. The week away obviously agreed with you."
"I stayed more active than I expected. Lots of swimming and walking."
"Good. Vacations are important, but I was worried you'd come back completely deconditioned. How do you feel? Any lingering fatigue from the travel?"
"I feel great. More energetic than before I left."
"That's what a proper break should do. Your body had time to recover from all that work stress."
The session proceeded smoothly, with Jay finding his strength and endurance largely intact. After he was done, Rus praised both his consistency and his approach to maintaining fitness during travel.
"You're turning into a real athlete. Remember when you first came in here, all nervous and apologetic about everything?"
Jay laughed, toweling off his face. "I was convinced I'd embarrass myself completely."
"Now look at you. Confident, strong, adventurous.  You're almost unrecognizable after less than six months."
On his way home, Jay stopped at Café Lemon, craving the comfort of the place after a good workout. The evening shift usually meant Fay would be behind the counter, but tonight a different barista greeted him.
"Is Fay not working tonight?" Jay asked as he ordered hot chocolate.
"She's been out sick for a few days. Flu season's hitting everyone early this year."
Jay accepted his drink with disappointment. Fay had become part of the cafe's charm, and her absence made it feel slightly less congenial. Still, the atmosphere remained welcoming, and he had time to sit and enjoy the ambience properly.
As he sipped his drink, Jay made a mental note to schedule his flu vaccination soon. The last thing he wanted was to repeat last winter's bout of illness, especially with the apartment search ahead.
There was a sharp chill in the air as Jay walked home from the café, a reminder that winter was approaching fast. He reheated leftover curry and, on impulse, decided to call his family. The guilt about the curt dismissal during the quarterly reports had been nagging at him since the last time he spoke to them.
"So tell us everything. Your postcard made it sound incredible," his mother's voice came through clearly as the family gathered around their phone. 
Jay launched into a story of his adventures, focusing on the diving experiences and cultural observations while ignoring the more solitary aspects of his vacation.
"It sounds like exactly what you needed. You seem more relaxed," his mother said warmly.
"I feel it too. Though I'm back to reality now. Long hours supporting my boss through the busy periods."
"This boss of yours. You always talk about him with such... admiration. Is he really as wonderful as you make him sound?" Ema interjected with obvious curiosity.
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks, though thankfully no one could see his reaction. "Ian is... yes, he really amazing. The best person I've ever worked for, probably the best person I know."
"He must be something special to earn that kind of loyalty from you."
"He very much is the greatest," Jay said simply, meaning every word.
The call ended with promises to stay in better touch. As he hung up, Jay thought he had missed the uncomplicated comfort of family connection. Outside, the November wind rattled his windows, but Jay felt unusually warm.
Scene 3
Planning activities
The rest of the week was filled with overtime as lingering issues from their time off surfaced alongside some procedural complications. Nothing required emergency treatment or the full team's attention, but enough accumulated to keep Jay consistently busy past normal hours.
Their evening coffee conversations continued, though briefer than Monday's extended discussion. By Thursday, they were wrapping up work closer to eight o'clock, without the marathon sessions that characterized crisis situations.
Colleagues gathered their belongings and made weekend plans on Friday afternoon, when Luc appeared at Jay's desk with his customary invitation.
"Drinks tonight? That new place downtown has decent cocktails and terrible music, which is somehow exactly what I need right now."
Jay shook his head apologetically. "Can't tonight. Ian and I are staying late to deal with a compliance request."
"You know, you could occasionally prioritize fun over paperwork."
Jay was about to deflect with an excuse when Luc's expression turned more pointed.
"Speaking of fun, remember when you got back from your business trip? You were feeling all confident and proposed we do something different and exciting to cheer me up after the Cam situation?"
Jay felt his stomach drop slightly. He had completely forgotten making that promise, though he vaguely recalled feeling uncharacteristically bold about social planning during those post-trip days.
"Right," Jay said carefully. "That."
"I'm holding you to it. I've been patient, but it's time to deliver on your mysterious plans."
"Of course. I'll... think of something."
Luc grinned and headed toward the elevator, leaving Jay to contemplate his complete lack of ideas about novel entertainment options.
The compliance issue was less time-consuming than anticipated, and by six o'clock they had resolved it entirely. Instead of heading to the kitchen for the coffee, Jay knocked on Ian's office door.
"Do you have a minute? I need some advice about something non-work related."
"What's on your mind?"
"I promised Luc I'll organize something fun and different for him when I was feeling confident after our trip. Well, I completely forgot about it until today, and he's calling it in. I have absolutely no idea what constitutes fun and different entertainment. My idea of leisure is reading at home."
"What kind of activity are you thinking? Something active, social, cultural?"
"I honestly don't know. Something that would cheer him up and get him away from the Friday night bar routine."
"Fencing," Ian said.
"I'm sorry, what?"
"Fencing. It's unusual, physically engaging, and most people find it surprisingly enjoyable once they try it."
Jay began laughing at the complete unexpectedness of the suggestion. "That's completely bonkers. Are you seriously suggesting I invite Luc to hit people with swords?"
"It's more sophisticated than that, but essentially yes. I tried it briefly in college. It can be quite enjoyable. Good exercise, requires focus and strategy."
"That's..." Jay paused, still grinning. "That's completely unorthodox. Where did that even come from?"
"I'm not exactly known for spontaneous entertainment planning. If you want truly creative ideas, you'd probably need to consult actual experts in that area."
"Who would those experts be?"
"Leo, obviously. He has extensive experience organizing social activities."
The mention of Leo's name immediately dampened Jay's mood. Ian noticed the change immediately. "Are you still resentful about the tailor appointment?"
"I'm resentful about everything," Jay replied sulkily.
"He can be mischievous, I'll grant you that. But his intentions are good, and he does tend to have excellent ideas for entertainment. He knows how to create memorable experiences."
Jay remained unconvinced. "I'd rather have nothing to do with Leo at all. Maybe I'll actually do the fencing thing instead."
The defeat in his voice was obvious, and Ian felt a little guilty for not being more helpful.
"Since it's Friday and we've finished the urgent matters, should we call it a day? You've been putting in long hours all week, and proper rest is beneficial," Jay asked, suddenly eager to change the subject.
Ian wanted nothing more than to stay longer, to find excuses that would extend their time together before the weekend separated them entirely. But he knew it was unfair to keep Jay at work solely for his own emotional needs.
"You're right. It has been a long week."
They gathered their things and headed for the elevator together, with Ian concealing his disappointment at ending what could have been at least two more hours of shared work.
Jay spent the evening researching fencing options on his new laptop. To his surprise, he discovered several beginner-friendly facilities offering group packages designed for people with no experience.
One option caught his attention: "introductory combat sports for absolute beginners", that promised to accommodate complete novices with safety equipment and patient instructors. Without overthinking the absolute absurdity of what he was doing, Jay booked it for the following Friday afternoon.
The rest of the weekend passed on exercise, walks, and household maintenance. He also spent time browsing apartment listings, though his home internet made the process frustratingly slow. Even the powerful new laptop couldn't compensate for the sluggish connection, and Jay stared at the machine with guilt.
"You're really wasted on someone like me," he said aloud to the laptop, unable to shake the sense that he wasn't utilizing such an expensive gift properly. He compiled a list of potential viewings with the intention of calling agents during the upcoming week, finally ready to take his housing search seriously.
When Luc joined him for coffee on Monday morning, Jay decided to address the entertainment situation directly.
"I booked an activity for Friday."
Luc's interest immediately sparked. "Really? What kind of activity?"
"Fencing."
Luc's face went through several expressions in rapid succession. "You want us to hit each other with swords?"
"More like poke each other with sticks, from what I understand. But yes, basically. I booked a group package, so we can invite the rest of the team or you could bring your flatmates or other friends."
"You know what? I'd rather keep this separate from work. Make it a purely social thing. I'll bring my flatmates, but you need to bring people too. How many spots do we have?"
"I... don't really have other friends to invite."
"Come on, there must be someone."
"I'll figure something out," Jay promised, though he had no idea how.
The solution presented itself unexpectedly on Tuesday afternoon at the gym. As Jay finished his session, he approached Rus with what felt like a ridiculous request.
"This might sound strange, but would you be interested in joining me and some friends for a... recreational activity this Friday?"
"What kind of activity?"
"Fencing. Like, with swords."
Rus's face lit up with unexpected enthusiasm. "Are you serious? That sounds amazing! I've always wanted to try melee combat sports."
"Really?"
"Absolutely. How many people can you bring?"
"Three spots available."
"Perfect. I'll bring a couple of friends. What's your number? I'll coordinate with my guys," Rus was already pulling out his phone. 
They exchanged contact information, and Jay provided the details, relieved by Rus's eagerness.
"This is going to be amazing. You're full of surprises, Jay."
The turn of events felt surreal—Jay was apparently developing an actual friendship with his trainer and committing to an evening of recreational sword fighting.
On Wednesday morning Jay informed Luc that their social experiment was officially happening. His response was suspiciously militant.
"Wait, so now we have two teams? Your gym friends versus my flatmates?"
"I suppose that's one way to look at it."
"This just became a lot more interesting. We have to make it a proper competition now."
Jay watched Luc's growing excitement with mounting anxiety. What had started as a desperate attempt to fulfil a forgotten promise was evolving into something much more elaborate than he had ever intended.
During their evening coffee in the kitchen, Jay confessed his growing anxiety to Ian while slumping slightly in his chair by the kitchen window.
"I've created a monster. What started as a simple activity has turned into a competitive social event, and now I feel completely foolish about the whole enterprise."
Ian regarded him with an expressions that Jay couldn't quite parse. "Why foolish?"
"Because it's absurd. I don't know anything about fencing, I barely know Luc's friends, and I'm about to spend an evening watching people hit each other with medieval weapons because I couldn't think of anything better."
"It sounds like you've successfully organized the novel entertainment Luc was hoping for."
"I've never held a sword in my life, and now I'm apparently leading a team into recreational combat."
Ian's response was solemn. "You'll have to endure it for the sake of your heartbroken colleague. Perhaps take a few hits for the cause."
Jay rolled his eyes dramatically. "You're enjoying my suffering. Heartless."
"I prefer to think of it as character building through adversity."
“Absolutely heartless,” Jay repeated, pouting as he returned to his desk with a defeated posture.
Thursday morning at ten o'clock, during a lull in the office coffee rotation, Jay finally made the calls he had been postponing. He contacted several real estate agents and scheduled apartment viewings for the following week. Time to get serious about the apartment search, he thought. But first, he needed to survive Friday's fencing adventure.
Scene 4
Melee combat
Jay rushed home from work on Friday, barely managing to change clothes and grab a quick dinner before meeting his team at the designated spot near the gym. He had arranged to meet Rus and his friends at six-thirty, allowing time to get acquainted before the main event.
The taxi ride to the fencing studio provided Jay's first proper introduction to Rus's companions. Mic, another trainer from the same gym, was someone Jay recognized from brief glimpses during his sessions, stocky and obviously strong, with a confident bearing. Van was more intimidating, tall enough that he had to fold himself into the taxi and built like he could lift a car if necessary.
"So how do you know Rus?" Jay asked.
"Mic and I met at the gym. Van and I go way back. We're military buddies," Rus explained. 
"Military? I had no idea."
Van grinned. "Yeah, Rus never mentions it because it was boring as hell. All that training and we never got to use any proper melee weapons. Biggest disappointment of his military career."
"That's why tonight is going to be perfect. Finally get to swing some steel around," Rus added with enthusiasm.
They arrived at the fencing studio to find Luc and his flatmates already waiting outside, and Luc's excitement was immediately evident.
"There they are! Ready for battle?" Luc called out as they approached.
Jay was introduced to three young men who had the bearing of people accustomed to success. Roc, Gav, and Lim, though Jay suspected he would struggle to keep their names straight, all seemed excited about the prospect of recreational combat.
Within minutes of entering the studio, Jay discovered that his role as organizer was entirely superfluous. The two groups converged on the staff, quickly establishing the parameters of their competition while Jay watched with growing bemusement.
"Team competition, losers buy drinks for the winners all evening," Luc declared, and both sides agreed immediately.
Jay's heart sank when one of Luc's flatmates mentioned having done fencing in college.
"I promise I'll cover any costs we incur tonight," Jay said quietly to Rus. "I don't want your friends dealing with unexpected expenses because of my idea."
"Nonsense," Rus replied confidently. "We're going to destroy them. Van here is a natural athlete, and I've got strategy."
The instructor provided basic safety equipment and fundamental techniques, but it quickly became clear that both teams were more interested in competition. Soon they were facing off in proper duels, with everyone fighting everyone else across sixteen total matches.
Jay's performance was spectacularly abysmal. He lost every single match, often within the first thirty seconds, completely outclassed by opponents who grasped the basics far more quickly than he did. His sword work resembled flailing more than fencing, and by his fourth consecutive defeat, he had accepted his role as the evening's primary source of entertainment.
However, the rest of his team was devastatingly effective. Mic and Rus, despite being complete beginners, adapted quickly to the sport's demands. Van emerged as an absolute phenomenon, his combination of reach, speed, and tactical thinking making him unbeatable. Even Lim, with his college experience, only managed victory against Mic and Rus, while losing to Van.
When the scores were tallied, Jay's team had won decisively despite his perfect losing record. Luc's team looked shell-shocked by the defeat, though they maintained good humour about their poor performance.
"At least Jay was worse than all of us," Luc said consolingly to his flatmates. "That's something."
Jay accepted this dubious distinction with grace, pleased that everyone else seemed to be enjoying themselves despite the lopsided results.
The promised drinks at a nearby pub were more enjoyable than the fencing itself. Luc and his friends honoured their agreement to cover the tab, and the evening turned into boisterous storytelling about the night's more memorable moments.
"Did you see Luc trip over his own feet fighting Van?" Gav recounted with delight. "Went down like a felled tree."
"What about when Rus tried to tackle Lim?" Mic added. "Pure aggression, zero technique. Beautiful to watch."
Jay held a single beer while the others drank with more enthusiasm. When pressed to keep up with the group's consumption, he demurred with honest self-assessment.
"I'm terrible with alcohol in general. I have to pace myself if I want to function tomorrow."
Rus, who had been matching drinks with Van and Mic, immediately began intercepting attempts to press more alcohol on Jay. "Leave him alone. Someone needs to stay coherent enough to get us home."
The gesture touched Jay. Despite barely knowing him outside the gym, Rus was looking out for him without being asked.
The conversation flowed well between the two teams. Rus, Mic, and Van represented a more working-class, downtown sensibility, while Luc's flatmates embodied the polished professionalism of young finance workers, yet the shared experience of getting beaten up had created an unexpected bond.
Stories grew louder and more exaggerated as the evening progressed. Jay laughed at retellings of his own spectacular failures, no longer embarrassed by his complete lack of sword-fighting aptitude. The good-natured teasing never turned mean-spirited, and both groups were content with how the evening had unfolded.
They finally parted ways after two in the morning, with everyone except Jay thoroughly intoxicated. They rode a taxi back to their neighbourhood, with stops to drop off Mic and Van at their respective homes before Rus insisted on escorting Jay to his apartment building.
"That was fun," Rus said as they stood outside Jay's door. "Thanks for thinking of me."
"I'm glad I did. You and your friends made the evening work."
"Van's going to be talking about this for months. He loves any excuse to prove he's the most physically capable person in the room."
Jay fumbled with his keys, the combination of exhaustion and alcohol making fine motor control challenging. "You seemed like you enjoyed it too."
"Absolutely. We should do more things like this, group activities that get everyone moving."
"I'll keep that in mind for future social planning disasters."
Rus laughed and waited until Jay successfully unlocked his door before heading back to the waiting taxi.
Jay stumbled into his apartment, too tired and drunk to manage more than removing his shoes before collapsing onto his bed fully clothed. The evening had been far more successful than he had dared hope.
Scene 5
House hunting
Saturday's gym session was filled with suffering. Jay arrived looking worn out and moving sluggishly.
"You look like hell," Rus said, though his own appearance wasn't particularly fresh either.
Jay yawned widely before answering. "I'm not built for staying out past midnight."
"Fair enough. Let's take it easy today."
Rus mercifully adjusted the workout to accommodate their fatigue, choosing exercises that were almost leisurely. Jay moved through the sets slowly, his body protesting each movement while his mind processed the previous evening's unexpected social success.
They parted ways with promises of future adventures, though Jay suspected his next social planning venture would involve less alcohol consumption.
Jay arrived at work at his usual time on Monday morning. The weekend's adventure had left him sore, but with a sense of accomplishment.
"You look like someone who had an interesting weekend," Ian observed when Jay appeared in his office for their morning briefing.
"That's one way to put it. The fencing experiment was... educational."
"How educational?"
"I lost every single match. Spectacularly. But everyone else seemed to have a wonderful time, so I'm calling it a success."
Ian tried to hide amusement. "Your team-building exercise involved duels?"
"It turned into a proper competition between Luc's flatmates and my gym friends. Luc's team got absolutely destroyed, which somehow made everyone bond over shared humiliation. The evening ended with drinks and war stories about our various defeats."
"And you survived this social experiment?"
"More than survived. I think I might actually have made some real friends. Rus and his military buddy Van were good company, and even Luc's flatmates turned out to be decent people once you get past the yuppie exterior."
"It sounds like you've developed friendships outside of work."
"I think so. Rus is a solid guy. He looked out for me when everyone was trying to get me to drink more than I could handle."
Ian listened to Jay's animated recounting of the evening's events, battling a surge of jealousy that felt both irrational and consuming. Jay's obvious pleasure in describing his expanding social connections should have been gratifying. It was evidence of healthy relationship-building. Instead, Ian catalogued each new person who now had claims on Jay's time and attention.
"It sounds like quite the adventure," Ian managed.
"It was. Though I'm hoping my next social planning challenge involves less potential for physical injury."
Ian managed what he hoped was a supportive smile, but he couldn't shake the selfish wish that Jay's best stories still came from their shared experiences.
The following week created a new pattern that Ian found deeply unsettling. Jay's scheduled apartment viewings meant he left work at five o'clock each day, abandoning their extended evening hours and kitchen conversations. Each departure left Ian alone in the office, staring at work he'd have to complete alone, drinking coffee that tasted flat.
Jay spent nearly every afternoon touring apartments, covering over twenty options across different neighbourhoods. Only Tuesday remained sacred, reserved for his gym session with Rus. Each evening he returned home with new photos, fresh notes, and growing clarity about what kind of space he wanted to call home.
The apartment hunting process turned out to be quite frustrating for Jay. He had prepared comprehensive materials consisting of printed listings with key information that he supplemented with detailed notes. His requirements were reasonable, but finding the right combination of factors within his budget was proving challenging.
The first property, a modern flat close to the office, was sleek and convenient but cramped and expensive. The second, in an older building near the historic district, offered more space and character but would add twenty minutes to his commute. The third fell somewhere between the two extremes but lacked natural light and had a kitchen that barely qualified as functional.
By Thursday evening, Jay had accumulated a folder full of detailed notes but no clear frontrunner among his options.
On Friday morning, Jay camped in the office kitchen during the coffee break, a thick folder spread across the table as he discussed his findings with Zoe and Luc. Other team members drifted over, drawn by the photos and animated conversation about Jay's housing prospects.
The folder was meticulously organized, beginning with a typed list of requirements and priorities, followed by information for each potential apartment complete with photos, floor plans, and detailed pros and cons lists. Jay's approach had whittled down the possibilities to twelve serious contenders, each meeting his basic requirements of good location, minimum forty-four square meters, and reasonable safety standards.
"You've really thought this through," Zoe observed, examining the materials.
"Maybe too thoroughly. I keep finding reasons why each option isn't quite right."
Luc picked up one of the listings. "This one near the cathedral looks nice. Historic area, good size."
"Beautiful neighbourhood, but the building is older, with potential maintenance issues. And longer commute."
"What about this modern place? Great location, all the amenities," Zoe held up another one.
"Tiny. I'd barely have more space than I do now."
Kim read the listings. "These industrial district ones look impressive. Much more space for the money, and the vibe of the area is awesome."
"They are. Great value, modern amenities, but the metro access is less convenient. It would add a fifteen minute walk to my daily commute each way."
"You're overthinking this. Pick the one that feels right when you walk in. All the analysis in the world won't tell you what it's like to actually live somewhere," Luc said.
"I suppose I could narrow it down to the top two and spend more time in each space," Jay conceded.
Amy looked over the financial calculations Jay had included with each option. "You're being too conservative with your budget. The financial district apartments would put you right in the heart of everything."
"And cost twice what I'm comfortable spending," Jay replied with a laugh.
Max, who had been quietly reviewing the materials, finally looked up. "Stop being so frugal and move somewhere that will make you happy. You can afford more than you think you can."
The conversation continued with each colleague advocating for different priorities. Jay listened to all perspectives, but it only made the decision process more overwhelming.
As the end of the day approached and colleagues began gathering their belongings for the weekend, Luc appeared at Jay's desk with a sly expression.
"Last week's adventure was definitely entertaining, but my ego is still bruised from losing to your muscle-bound friends."
Jay looked up from his computer with undisguised distrust. "And?"
"It requires compensation."
"I thought you had fun."
"I did. But being thoroughly beaten by three strangers in front of my flatmates has damaged my reputation."
"What do you have in mind for compensation?"
"A blind date. That's what you should have organized in the first place instead of a sweaty gathering of burly men."
"Absolutely not."
"I already have everything arranged. Gav is bringing someone he knows from college, and we need one more person because there will be four women. You literally just have to show up and fill out the numbers."
"Luc, I don't do blind dates. I don't do dates at all, really."
"Hear me out. What you organized last week was fun, but what I actually needed was something more... social. Romantic, even."
"Still not interested."
"You owe me for the humiliation of being defeated in sword combat by people I'd never met before. Plus you don't have to do anything, just show up and be polite. It's a numbers game, nothing more."
Jay looked at his apartment folders, then back at Luc's expectant face. The guilt trip was working, despite his complete lack of interest in romantic social activities.
"This is a terrible idea," Jay said finally.
"That's not a no."
"It's not a yes either."
"Come on, Jay. One evening. What's the worst that could happen?"
Jay could think of numerous answers to that question, none of them comforting. But Luc's persistence, combined with the guilt over the fencing evening's unintended consequences, was wearing down his resistance.
"I hate this," Jay said with resignation.
"Noted. But you're still coming."
Jay sighed heavily, already regretting his decision. "Fine. But I'm warning you now that I'm going to be terrible at this."
"You just have to show up. That's enough."
"When is this supposed to happen?" Jay asked, resigned to his fate.
"Tomorrow night. Saturday dinner, very civilized."
"Tomorrow? "You couldn't give me more warning?"
"I wanted to make sure you couldn't overthink it and talk yourself out of it."
"This is going to be a disaster," Jay muttered, but Luc was already moving on to logistical details, clearly considering the matter decided.
From his office, Ian had been listening to this conversation with growing fury. By the time Luc left, Ian was struggling to maintain composure. The thought of Jay on a date, potentially connecting with someone who might become important in his life, was almost suffocating.
He gave himself several minutes to calm down before approaching Jay's desk, trying to appear casual.
"A date, huh?"
Jay spun in his desk chair, his expression showing obvious dismay. "It's a dreadful idea. I hate everything about it and want to bail."
"Your comfort matters too. Luc should consider that before making these demands."
"I know, but I did promise to do something to cheer him up, and apparently I delivered the wrong kind of entertainment last week." Jay sighed heavily. "What's the worst that could happen, right?"
Ian wanted to enumerate exactly what could happen, starting with Jay meeting someone wonderful and ending with the complete destruction of Ian's fragile emotional landscape. Instead, he managed a supportive response.
"If it becomes unbearable, you could always leave early."
"True. Maybe I'll call you to come pick me up if it goes badly."
"Absolutely do that. I'll keep my phone by my side all evening."
Jay seemed comforted by this offer, unaware that Ian was already planning to spend Saturday evening anxiously waiting for exactly such a call.
Chapter 27: Shared traumas
Scene 1
Blind date
Saturday's gym session began with Jay's immediate need to vent his frustrations to the one person he thought would understand.
"Luc roped me into this blind date thing tonight. I absolutely don't want to do it, but he guilt-tripped me into agreeing."
Rus however seemed quite enthusiastic about the idea. "That's fantastic! Exactly what you should be doing, going out with friends, meeting some girls, having fun. You spend too much time working."
Jay stared at him in dismay. "You're supposed to be on my side here."
Rus clapped him on the shoulder. "I am on your side. That's why I think this is great for you. You need more social life, more dating experience."
"Even you're against me? Ian is the only person who actually supports my desire to avoid this disaster."
"Ian? Wait, that's your boss, right? The one who gave you all that trouble?"
"He never gave me trouble. That was all a misunderstanding."
"Jay, you need to stay away from that guy. This date tonight is exactly what you need to get some perspective."
Jay left the gym feeling thoroughly betrayed. Even Rus, who he had thought was a friend, was pushing him toward this. Ian was the only person who truly understood how uncomfortable this situation made him, and here was Rus warning him away from the one supportive voice in his life.
When he returned home, Rus's words were still echoing in his mind, but his primary emotion was defiance. He didn't want to stay away from Ian. If anything, he would much rather spend the evening with Ian than suffer through what promised to be an excruciating ordeal.
That evening, Jay stood in front of his mirror wearing his most elegant gray three-piece suit, the one that made him feel like someone important. The formal attire was probably excessive for the occasion, but he needed every bit of confidence he could muster. If he was going to survive this evening, he wanted to feel like the best version of himself.
The restaurant was upscale but not intimidating, and Jay arrived to find Luc and his flatmate Gav already waiting with another man he didn't recognize.
"Jay, this is Yan," Luc said, gesturing to a strikingly handsome man who seemed like he was accustomed to being the most interesting person in any room.
Yan's handshake was firm. "The mysterious coworker. Luc's told me you're quite successful."
Before Jay could respond, the women arrived in a flurry of laughter. They were all attractive and seemed friendly enough, but Jay was unable to retain their names beyond the first few seconds of meeting them. The social situation was already overwhelming his processing capacity.
At dinner, Yan dominated the conversation, never doubting his own charm. Jay was content to remain in the background, letting the evening unfold around him without his active participation.
As the wine flowed, however, Yan's comments began to take on a slightly sharper edge.
"You know, with that suit and that serious expression, you look like a funeral director. Very dignified, very... sombre."
Jay felt heat rise in his cheeks while Luc raised an eyebrow at the comment. The women seemed oblivious to the undercurrent, their attention focused on Yan's stories about his various adventures and connections. Jay noticed that one of them, a tall blonde with a bright laugh who might have been named Ana, was paying particular attention to Luc, which seemed to please him considerably.
After dinner, the group relocated to a nearby pub. Luc caught Jay's arm discreetly as they walked.
"I know this isn't your scene, but could you stick it out for just another hour? I don't want to make things weird by leaving too early."
Jay could see that Luc was enjoying Ana's company, and his request seemed reasonable enough. "Fine. One hour."
The new setting was no easier than the restaurant. Gav was growing visibly frustrated as Yan monopolized all three remaining women's attention, leaving both Jay and Gav as unwilling spectators to his performance. Jay ordered a single beer, having no intention of compromising his judgement further.
"Not drinking tonight? Worried about losing control?" Yan asked with mock concern.
"I don't handle alcohol well," Jay replied simply.
"Come on, funeral director. Live a little. You're bringing down the whole energy of the table."
Jay's jaw tightened, but he said nothing. Luc attempted to redirect the conversation, but Yan's attention seemed increasingly focused on Jay.
It was nearly eleven when the disaster struck. Yan, ostensibly rising a little too abruptly from his chair, brushed against one of the women at the table. Her glass tilted, and a splash of red wine landed across Jay’s jacket, blooming into a stain that would not come out easily.
"Oh my God, I'm so sorry! How clumsy of me. I'm absolutely mortified." Yan exclaimed theatrically.
Jay stared down at his ruined suit, the most expensive piece of clothing he owned, the one that had cost him so much embarrassment at the tailor's. The liquid was still spreading across the fabric, and he could smell the sharp scent beginning to set into the wool.
"It's fine. Accidents happen," Jay managed, though his voice was tight with suppressed emotion. Yan's expression made Jay suspect it hadn't been entirely accidental. "I should go. Get this cleaned as soon as possible."
Luc looked up from his conversation with Ana, clearly torn. "Are you sure you're okay?"
"I'm fine. It's just a small stain. Enjoy the rest of your evening."
Jay left the pub without looking back, walking several blocks before finding a small park where he could sit on a bench and properly assess the damage to his suit. The stain was extensive and already setting. Even a cleaning service might not be able to restore the garment completely.
He sat in the November chill and felt the full weight of the evening's misery wash over him. The date had been uncomfortable from the start, he had been subtly mocked and ignored, and now his best suit was possibly destroyed.
On impulse, he pulled out his phone and sent a text to Ian: "The evening did go badly."
His phone rang immediately.
