"Do you need to be picked up?" Ian sounded concerned, and spoke with no preamble or social pleasantries.
Jay was surprised by the immediate response. "I don't want to impose. It's late and—"
"Where are you?" Ian interrupted, and Jay could hear the sound of keys jingling. "I'm already heading to my car."
"You don't have to—" Jay's voice cracked slightly, betraying the emotional strain he was trying to hide.
"Just give me the location. I'll be there immediately."
Jay provided the address of the park, then hung up feeling simultaneously foolish for calling and overjoyed that Ian cared enough to respond so quickly. Before he had time to fully regret his decision or work himself into greater anxiety about imposing on Ian's evening, headlights appeared at the street corner.
Ian's car pulled up to the curb, and Jay stood from the bench, weighed down by a thoroughly disappointing evening. Whatever happened next, at least he wouldn't have to face the journey home alone.
Scene 2
Rescue
As Jay got into Ian's car, the first words out of his mouth were profuse apologies, his speech slightly slurred by the alcohol.
"I'm so sorry for calling you out like this. I know it's late and you probably had plans and I just—"
"It's nothing," Ian interrupted, pulling away from the curb. "Are you alright? That's what matters."
Jay slumped against the passenger seat. "I'm fine, but my best suit is ruined."
Ian glanced at the dark stain spreading across Jay's jacket. "It's red wine, right? I know a good emergency cleaner nearby. They might be able to save it. Wine stains aren't unusual, they definitely have experience with this kind of thing."
The drive took less than five minutes, barely enough time for Jay to process what was happening before Ian was pulling up outside a small shop with bright lights still on.
"Emergency dry cleaning. They specialize in situations exactly like this."
The woman behind the counter examined the stain. "We can probably manage this. The important thing is that you got here quickly. The longer these things sit, the harder they become to treat."
"It feels a bit like emergency surgery for a beloved hamster," Jay said with a weak smile.
Ian was relieved at Jay's attempt at humour. At least he seemed to be recovering from whatever had upset him earlier.
"Should I drive you home now?" Ian asked as they returned to the car.
Jay looked down at himself, white shirt, vest, no jacket, and shook his head. "I actually need a drink first. Though I probably look like a newspaper delivery boy."
"Not at all. You look charming. I know just the place that will accommodate us."
Ten minutes later they were sitting at the bar of a small pub in a more upscale district, with dim lights and live music. Jay still felt awkward about his incomplete outfit, but the atmosphere was welcoming enough.
Ian ordered whisky for both of them, then paused. "You don't actually like whisky, do you."
"I've heard it's an acquired taste," Jay replied, accepting the glass. "Seems like a good moment to do some acquiring."
Ian smiled despite his concern. "What happened tonight?"
Jay took a sip of the whisky and grimaced slightly. "The whole evening was awkward. Some arrogant guy spent the night telling me I looked like a funeral director, among other charming observations. Then he managed to spill wine all over my jacket. But at least Luc seemed to hit it off with one of the women, so the night wasn't a complete loss for everyone."
"The man was probably jealous. You looked good tonight. You still look good."
Jay laughed bitterly. "Jealous of what? He looked like a movie star himself."
"Secure men don't feel the need to prop themselves up by putting others down."
Jay drained the rest of his whisky in one swallow and signaled for another. Ian watched with growing concern as Jay's caution around alcohol seemed to have completely disappeared.
"What's wrong? You normally don't drink at all, and definitely not like this."
Jay accepted his second drink and met Ian's eyes directly. "I never drink because being drunk makes me vulnerable to other people taking advantage of me. But that's not really a concern when you're around, is it? So I might as well indulge. Besides, I need to kill the bad taste this evening left. Maybe it can even be salvaged after all."
His phone buzzed with a text from Luc asking if he was alright. Jay typed back quickly: "Fine. Took jacket to emergency cleaner, having a drink in a pub with a friend."
On impulse, he held up his phone for a selfie, capturing both himself and a surprised-looking Ian in the frame. He wanted to send it to Luc, but seemed to change his mind before pressing the button.
"Drinking makes you much more relaxed," Ian observed, looking puzzled by the sudden photo.
"I know. My defences go way down. That's exactly why it's so dangerous for me to drink." Jay's honesty was so casual, so trusting, that Ian felt an overwhelming wave of affection that was getting hard to suppress.
Jay ordered another drink despite Ian's attempts to slow him down.
"I rarely have a chance like this, and I really need it right now," Jay insisted, and Ian was unable to refuse when Jay was clearly still processing the evening's disappointment.
But within half an hour, Jay had consumed four more whiskies and was visibly intoxicated, his words slurring and his coordination becoming unreliable.
"I think it's time to get you home," Ian said, signaling for the check and calling a taxi.
The taxi ride required some management as Jay dozed fitfully against Ian's shoulder. When they reached Jay's building, Ian realized the full scope of his challenge: four flights of stairs with an intoxicated man who could barely stand independently.
Ian briefly contemplated taking Jay to his own place instead, but that seemed like an overreach. Instead, he half-dragged Jay up the stairs, pausing frequently to readjust his grip and catch their breath. By the time they reached Jay's door, Ian was thankful for every hour he spent sweating at the gym.
He had to search Jay's pockets for keys while supporting his weight, a process that felt deeply invasive. Jay was cooperative, but clearly had no real awareness of what was happening around him.
Once inside the small apartment, Jay immediately collapsed onto the couch as if his strings had been cut. Ian stood uncertainly in the doorway, torn between leaving Jay to sleep it off alone and staying to monitor his condition. The proper thing would be to leave, staying overnight would be presumptuous at best, potentially catastrophic at worst.
But worry won out over other considerations. Jay was more intoxicated than Ian had ever seen him, and leaving him alone felt unconscionable.
Ian helped Jay out of his shirt and suit, providing water and gentle encouragement to drink small sips between Jay's protests that he was fine. Getting Jay from the couch to his bed required more physical support, but Jay remained cooperative even as he mumbled incoherent responses to Ian's instructions.
"Just sleep it off," Ian murmured, making sure Jay was positioned safely on his side with water within reach. "Everything will look better in the morning."
With Jay put to bed, Ian stretched out on the couch that had dominated the small space. His heart was still racing from the evening's events, from the phone call that had sent him rushing across the city to this moment of lying in Jay's apartment listening to his unsteady breathing.
Ian stared at the ceiling and acknowledged that he had crossed several lines tonight. But Jay had trusted him enough to call when he needed help, had been vulnerable enough to admit that Ian made him feel safe, and Ian couldn't regret any of it. 
Ian pulled a blanket over himself, doubting he would actually sleep but content to maintain his protective vigil until Jay was safely recovered.
Scene 3
Accepting help
Jay woke to a splitting headache and the sensation that someone had replaced his brain with broken glass. He pressed his palms against his temples and tried to piece together the fragments of the previous evening. The blind date. The wine stain. Calling Ian. Then... nothing. A void where his memory should be.
He rolled over with a pitiful moan, trying to understand how he had gotten home, when he heard a voice from somewhere nearby.
"Take these. They'll help with the pain."
Jay's eyes snapped open to find Ian standing beside his bed, holding out painkillers and a glass of water. The sight sent Jay into instant panic. He practically launched himself upright, suddenly aware that he was wearing nothing but his underwear, then scrambled backward until his shoulders hit the wall, pulling his sheets around himself in a desperate attempt at modesty.
"What—how did I— Ian, I'm so sorry, I don't remember—"
Ian immediately stepped back, alarm crossing his features. "Jay, you're safe. Nothing happened. I promise you're completely safe."
The fear in Jay's eyes startled Ian, who had never imagined that he might be interpreted as threatening. He backed away further, putting as much distance as possible between himself and the bed.
The reassurance in Ian's voice only made Jay's panic worse, but for entirely different reasons than Ian suspected. The terrible reality was dawning on him: he had apparently made Ian Hagen stay overnight in his cramped, messy studio apartment. The most refined man he knew had been forced to sleep on Jay's tiny, uncomfortable couch. It was unthinkable.
"Did you sleep here? On my couch?"
"Jay, let me explain—"
"Oh God." Jay buried his face in his hands. "Ian, I'm so sorry. How could I let this happen? You shouldn't have had to—"
Ian raised his hands in a calming gesture. "You called me when you needed help. You were quite intoxicated, and it didn't feel safe to leave you alone. I made the decision to stay. But I can leave right now if that would make you more comfortable."
"No!" Jay's head shot up. "I mean—wait. Just give me a minute to process this disaster."
Ian watched with growing confusion as Jay's distress seemed to have nothing to do with impropriety and everything to do with hosting arrangements. "Take the painkillers first. Please."
Jay accepted the pills and water, downing both gratefully. Ian watched him for a moment, then asked, "Would you like coffee?"
"You don't need to wait on me," Jay protested, attempting to scramble to his feet. "I can—"
"Lay down and rest," Ian said firmly, placing a gentle hand on Jay's shoulder to stop him.
Jay collapsed back against his pillow with a miserable groan, pulling the covers over his head.
Ian sat on the bed, speaking in the same tone he might use to soothe a skittish animal. "It's okay to let friends help when you need it. That's what friends are for. There's no shame in needing assistance, and it's not a burden on the people who care about you."
While Ian went to make coffee, Jay remained hidden under his covers, wrestling with the magnitude of his mortification. When Ian returned and set a mug on the bedside table, Jay finally emerged enough to accept it.
Ian sat on the couch with his own cup, giving Jay space to process. After several minutes of silence, Jay managed to drink some coffee and eventually wobbled to his feet.
"I'm going to take a shower," he mumbled, disappearing into the bathroom.
The cold water helped clear his head somewhat, and when he emerged wearing sweatpants and a t-shirt, he felt better. He sat at his small table and looked at Ian with an intense expression.
"Are you feeling like eating breakfast?" Ian asked.
"I feel like dying," Jay replied flatly.
"Is the hangover that severe?"
Jay shook his head miserably. "I can't believe I made you sleep on that awful couch. Ian, I'm so sorry. The indignity of it. The sheer presumption."
Ian set down his coffee cup. "You didn't make me do anything. You were frankly not in any position to make decisions. It was entirely my choice. I apologize if I overstepped and made you uncomfortable."
"I'm not uncomfortable," Jay said quickly. "I'm mortified at my audacity. Making my boss escort my drunk self home and stay over in my uncomfortable studio—"
Ian laughed softly. "I did it as your friend, not your boss. And if I recall correctly, you did the same thing quite recently for Luc. So you must be familiar with the general concept."
"That's not the same thing."
"How so?"
Jay looked at him with exasperated disbelief. "You know why. You're... well, you're too well-bred for dilapidated accommodations like this."
Ian's laugh was more amused now. He stood up and moved toward Jay's small kitchen. "I'm making eggs for breakfast."
"Ian, you don't have to—"
"I actually lived in a studio even smaller than this one for years," Ian said, dismissing Jay's protests as he began searching through cupboards. "When my father threw me out after the scandal, I was told to leave immediately and never return. I didn't even have a chance to gather my belongings. I left my childhood home with literally nothing but the clothes on my back."
Jay stared at him, stunned into silence.
Ian continued as he worked, cracking eggs into a pan. "All my accounts were cancelled within hours. I didn't have a single penny to my name. I had to sell my watch just to have enough money to rent a place to stay. At first, I stayed in the cheapest hotel I could find, then I rented a run-down studio in a far worse part of the city than this. I lived there for eight years, until I had accumulated enough assets to comfortably buy my own apartment."
"Eight years?"
"I didn't even have a bed, unlike you. I slept on a couch that wasn't much more comfortable than yours. During that time, I quickly accepted that my old life was over, and honestly, I was too heartbroken to care much about material comfort. I was resigned to working as a waiter or whatever employment I could find."
Jay felt his world tilting slightly. He had known about the disownment, but he had never pondered the practical realities. He had assumed Ian must have had some kind of safety net, some resources to fall back on.
"Leo found me before I could sink that far. He offered me a position, but at first I was too proud to accept help. It was Leo who gave me the speech about accepting assistance from friends—almost word for word what I just told you. I'm glad I listened to him, because it allowed me to build a life with my own hands. Looking back, I realize he was right. Being unwilling to accept help during your darkest moments is unwise."
Ian finished cooking and served the eggs, setting a plate in front of Jay along with fresh coffee. Jay took a tentative bite and his eyes widened in surprise. The food was perfectly prepared, seasoned just right, and Jay stared at Ian with complete astonishment.
"You're an incredible cook."
Ian smiled as he sat down with his own plate. "During those years, I cooked, cleaned, did laundry—all the normal things everyone else does. It was a sobering experience that showed me exactly how privileged I had been growing up, having servants attend to every need."
Jay ate slowly, partly because of his lingering nausea but mostly because he was processing this revelation. As he watched Ian across the small table—this man he had admired for so long—Jay realized that despite everything he thought he knew about Ian's history, he had barely scratched the surface. Every new piece of information only deepened his respect.
Ian had survived complete rejection by his family, had built himself back up from nothing, and had somehow emerged from that experience with his integrity intact. And now he was sitting in Jay's modest apartment, making breakfast and treating Jay's hungover as if it was the most natural thing in the world.
"Thank you," Jay said softly. "For last night. For staying. For... everything."
"That's what friends do."
Scene 4
Deepening bonds
After they finished eating, Jay insisted on cleaning up despite Ian's protests that he should rest. The domestic task helped clear the last of the fog from his mind, and he moved more steadily as the painkillers and food worked their magic.
The atmosphere lightened as Jay assessed his clothes from the previous evening, which Ian had thoughtfully hung on the closet doors instead of leaving them crumpled on the floor.
"Your housing situation really is dire," Ian observed, gesturing toward the tower of suitcases dominating the centre of the room and the clothing overflowing from every available storage space.
Jay followed his gaze ruefully. "I know. I've been apartment hunting all week, but I keep running into problems with every option. Too expensive, too small, bad location, questionable building maintenance. There's always something."
Ian listened as Jay outlined his various concerns and criteria, then reached into his wallet and produced a business card.
"This is someone I know. He's a reliable real estate agent who specializes in finding good value in overlooked properties. He might be able to show you some interesting options."
Jay accepted the card hesitantly. "Ian, I'm already imposing on you so much. I can't keep asking for favours."
"That's hardly imposing. I thought I explained quite emphatically that our circumstances aren't really all that different. You don't need to look at me with such reverence. I just had more time to establish myself, and perhaps a slightly easier start. Though I'm not entirely certain about the easier start, because while my material conditions were initially better, you at least have a family who cares about you."
Jay went quiet for a moment, processing this perspective. "Your family really is awful. To abandon you when you needed them most. It's unconscionable."
Ian's response was almost reflexive. "I don't blame them. It was my own fault what happened. I deserved the consequences."
Jay stared at him in horror. "You deserved nothing of the sort! You were a victim. You were used by an unscrupulous man, and instead of comforting you in your pain, your family threw you out. They're awful people."
"In my circles, there are certain expectations about being mindful at all times—"
"I don't care about high society expectations." Jay's anger was building, his voice taking on an edge Ian had never heard before. "You were betrayed by someone you trusted, and instead of receiving support, you were punished for being human. That's not how decent people behave. That's just cruelty."
Ian looked sad and somewhat defeated, but he didn't budge from his position. "I got exactly what I deserved."
Jay stared at him with obvious frustration, then moved to the couch with a distinctly grumpy expression. "You want to talk about deserving consequences? Let me tell you about my first job. My first boss was wonderful. Charming, attentive, supportive. I thought I'd struck gold with this amazing mentor who was going to guide my career. Until after a company party where I got drunk, and he pushed himself on me. A married man with children."
Ian went very still. Though he had known this story from Jay's background check, hearing it directly was entirely different.
"I cried, I protested, I managed to get away before he could... complete what he intended. But afterwards, he told me I had asked for it. That it was obvious I wanted his attention, so I shouldn't play coy. I went to HR, but they dismissed my complaint entirely. So I quit immediately."
Jay was quiet for a moment, lost in the memory. 
"I wasn't in a good financial position. High rent, minimal savings, and the job search was brutal. I had to swallow my pride and call my mother for help. I didn't want to explain what happened, but I was so distraught that it all came spilling out. Do you know what she told me? She called him a pig. A bastard and a lowlife. She said of course she'd send money, that it wasn't my fault. She didn't say I should have been more careful or that I'd brought it on myself. She recognized I was a victim, just like you were. And that's how family should respond."
Jay met Ian's eyes directly. "Mind you, I wasn't particularly close to my mother at that point. But she still knew right from wrong. She still protected her child when he needed it most."
Ian sat in silence, processing Jay's words. Hearing Jay's personal account, seeing the trust Jay was placing in him by sharing this trauma, it felt monumental. Ian wanted to respond appropriately, to honour that trust with the right words, but he felt overwhelmed by the responsibility.
The silence stretched, and Jay watched Ian's expression, sad and confused, as if he were wrestling with concepts that challenged his fundamental beliefs. Jay's heart ached watching Ian struggle with ideas that should have been simple.
"Get up," Jay said suddenly.
Ian looked puzzled. "What?"
"I'm going to hug you now, if that's okay."
Ian nodded, still confused, and rose from his chair. Jay enveloped him in a warm, encompassing embrace, complete with a gentle pat on the back.
"It was not your fault," Jay said firmly into Ian's ear. "I'm sorry you were surrounded by people so awful they made you think you had to shoulder all of that guilt yourself. The man who wronged you was a pig, and you don't need to make excuses for any of them." He pulled back slightly to meet Ian's eyes. "Maybe Leo really isn't as bad as I thought. I might need to reassess my opinion of him."
They broke apart, and Ian's voice was quiet when he responded. "Thank you. And what happened to you was terrible. I'm sorry you went through that."
Jay shrugged with forced casualness. "We all have our stories that shaped us into the men we are."
The moment suddenly felt heavy. They had each shared their deepest trauma, their most vulnerable experiences, and the weight of that trust hung between them as they looked into each other's eyes.
Jay's phone buzzed, breaking the moment. He glanced at the screen with a grimace. "It's Luc, checking if I made it home alright and apologizing that the evening turned sour." He looked up with a snarky expression. "The main architect of yesterday's disaster has no shame acting so nonchalant."
He typed back: "Hangover is killing me. Whether I forgive you depends on how good a job the cleaner does."
The mention of the jacket reminded them both of their pending errand. "We should go retrieve your suit," Ian said. "I need to collect my car from the pub anyway."
"Ian, I really don't want to impose—"
Ian gave him a pointed look. "We just discussed the imposing issue, didn't we?"
Jay changed into more presentable clothes, and they took a taxi to the pub to collect Ian's car before driving to the emergency cleaner. Jay held his breath as the woman behind the counter brought out his suit jacket. It looked pristine, with no trace of the previous evening's accident.
Jay's relief was palpable. "It looks like new! Thank you so much."
As they left the shop, Jay turned to Ian with determination. "The least I can do is buy you lunch. After everything you've done..."
Ian seemed pleased. "I'd like that very much."
Scene 5
Weekend plans
They chose a nearby restaurant that looked cosy enough and ordered simple but well-prepared dishes. As they waited for their food, Jay got curious about Ian's eating habits.
"I have to ask. What's the deal with your meal service? I know you have everything delivered according to detailed specifications."
"I've always been fairly serious about staying fit, and at this point it's easier to outsource meal planning to ensure consistency with my training requirements."
"Are you very strict about it?"
"Not obsessive. I don't mind adjusting when circumstances call for it. I never skip business lunches or social occasions. But on regular days, I stick to the meal plan. It serves me well, and it's one less decision to make. I don't really miss having to cook."
"I feel terrible about taking up so much of your weekend. You had to make concessions on your meals, probably your gym schedule, all to babysit me."
Ian's response was delivered with perfect deadpan seriousness. "Yes, it's quite tragic. I was right in the middle of updating spreadsheet templates for the annual reporting cycle in January. Whatever will I do now?"
Jay laughed, assuming it was a joke, then gradually realized Ian's expression hadn't changed. "Wait, you're serious. You were actually going to spend your Sunday working on spreadsheets?"
"I generally spend weekends on exercise and non-urgent work projects. It helps ensure business operations run smoothly."
Jay stared at him in horror. "What about leisure time? Surely you must take some time to just... relax?"
"I do gym sessions. That's quite enough recreational activity."
"Ian. That's not leisure. That's still self-improvement."
Ian seemed puzzled by Jay's reaction, which only made Jay more worried. He had already been concerned about Ian's working habits during the week, but this revelation painted an even more troubling picture.
An idea began forming in Jay's mind. Half teasing, half serious, he said, "What if I came over next Saturday and helped with those spreadsheets? Then you'd have no excuse but to take Sunday off."
He didn't really expect Ian to agree to such a suggestion, but Ian was looking at him as if he was considering it.
"You cannot possibly mean that."
"I owe you for everything you've done, and I won't let you waste all your weekends on paperwork. Besides, spreadsheets aren't torture when you have good company."
Ian hesitated, clearly torn. "I don't want you to feel obligated—"
"I don't feel obligated."
Ian seemed to wrestle with the offer for several moments. "You don't need to push yourself. Only come if you don't have anything better to do."
Jay felt unexpected excitement. He hadn't really thought Ian would entertain the idea. "As long as you don't mind having me there, I don't mind coming. Actually, I'd enjoy it."
"Then... yes. That would be helpful."
Their food arrived, providing a brief intermission in the conversation. After a few bites, Jay felt oddly emboldened by the atmosphere and their growing closeness. 
"What do you plan to do with your free Sunday then?"
"I haven't given it much thought."
Jay narrowed his eyes suspiciously. "You're not really planning to relax at all, are you? You're going to find more work to do."
"I don't do hard work at home. Just less pressing matters," Ian protested weakly.
"That's still work!" Jay threw up his hands in dramatic exasperation. "Should I make plans for Sunday too, just to ensure you actually rest?"
Jay wasn't sure if he was overstepping badly, but Ian gave the suggestion serious thought.
"If you want to be certain I take the day off, you probably should make plans. I'm not entirely sure I know how to spend a whole day without some form of productive activity."
Jay rolled his eyes. "We're going to have to fix that. I have a week to think of something appropriately relaxing. If nothing else comes to mind, I'll just book another fencing session."
"That went so well the last time."
"Hey, everyone else enjoyed it. My complete humiliation was apparently highly entertaining."
When they finished their meal, Ian drove Jay home. Jay retrieved his rescued jacket from Ian's car and returned to his apartment, feeling strangely energized. It had been an unusually intense weekend, and he wasn't sure if he felt distressed or exhilarated.
The sudden closeness with Ian felt both precious and fragile. He knew he was imposing, but he was overjoyed Ian allowed it. He was surprised by his own forwardness in inserting himself into Ian's weekend schedule like this.
Rus's warning echoed in his mind—that he shouldn't get so close to his boss. Sound, prudent advice that he agreed with completely and chose to disregard entirely. He wanted to get closer to Ian, as close as he was allowed to be. He didn't care if it was foolish.
Standing in front of his bathroom mirror, Jay studied his reflection. When had he become so reckless? But as he asked himself the question, he discovered it didn't matter. It felt good. It felt right.
Meanwhile, Ian felt far less conflicted about the development. He felt blessed. He had wanted to get closer to Jay, and the universe had helped him achieve that goal. Now he had plans with Jay—an entire weekend together.
The memory of Jay's embrace, of his protectiveness, made his heart race. But Ian held no illusions about his position. Jay's story had served as a stark reminder—Jay trusted him precisely because he knew Ian would never touch him. It didn't matter. Ian had never harboured any romantic notions anyway.
He tried not to get his hopes up too much, knowing that disappointment would be crushing if Jay changed his mind or if the weekend turned awkward, but he couldn't help himself. He had something to look forward to. He had Jay coming to his apartment, Jay concerned about his well-being, Jay taking charge of his recreational activities.
The thought made Ian smile as he opened his laptop, finally turning to those spreadsheet templates that had been interrupted by much more important matters. The work felt less tedious knowing that next weekend, he wouldn't be doing it alone. Next weekend, Jay would be there, drinking coffee and ensuring he took proper breaks. 
It was going to be wonderful.
Chapter 28: Unusually warm autumn
Scene 1
Practical considerations
Both Jay and Ian arrived at the office shortly before eight, having slept in slightly to recover from their eventful weekend. They threw themselves into the demands of the day, only briefly acknowledging their shared time together, though there was a closeness to their interactions now, as if they’d known each other for years.
"You still look groggy," Ian observed during their morning briefing.
"I had trouble getting up. The weekend took more out of me than expected. How's your back? That couch can't have been comfortable."
"I've slept in worse places. Though I admit I was glad to be back in my own bed."
During his morning coffee break in the kitchen, Jay found Luc already there, wearing a contrite expression.
"You're lucky my jacket was saved. Otherwise I would have been very cross with you," Jay said with mock solemnity.
"I'm still mortified about how that went. Gav feels absolutely awful about Yan's behaviour and wants to make it up to you somehow. If we'd known he was going to be such a complete ass, we never would have brought him."
"He seemed a bit arrogant, but I didn't expect him to be so unpleasant."
"He can be at times, but it's never quite that bad. Gav is furious because he actually had high hopes about the evening, and Yan effectively ruined his chances with all three women. He's still moping about it."
"What happened after I left?"
"Gav was relieved you weren't interested in dating, made his own chances better, you know? But Yan monopolized all the women's attention and was insufferable on top of it. Unforgivable, really."
"No real harm done. Though I'm never agreeing to another blind date arrangement. I don't want to kill the vibe with my funeral director smile."
Luc laughed. "Don't listen to Yan. Though in retrospect, you did look a bit... intimidating. No wonder the women were too shy to approach you."
Jay was surprised. This was news to him—he'd been called many things in his life, but never that. "Intimidating?"
"Maybe Ian's rubbing off on you," Luc added with a grin.
"What about Ana? How did things work out with her?"
"We'll see how it goes. I'm not sure I want anything serious right now. Happy just dating around, keeping things casual."
"That's probably wise."
"But seriously, Gav is determined to organize some kind of apology event. He feels responsible for the fiasco."
"That's completely unnecessary. I just want to forget the whole thing happened."
Luc adopted a tragic expression. "Gav will never live this down if you don't let him make amends. He's actually considering moving out of the apartment from shame."
"Now you're just emotionally blackmailing me."
"Is it working?"
"Absolutely not."
"What if we made it fun? Invited your gym friends, found something everyone would enjoy?"
Jay narrowed his eyes suspiciously. "Are you trying to poach my trainer?"
"You saw right through my elaborate plot."
Jay fled the kitchen before Luc could extract any commitments, leaving his colleague laughing behind him.
After lunch, Jay called the real estate agent Ian had recommended. He was immediately accommodating, listening to Jay's requirements and budget constraints.
"I think I can show you several excellent options that might not have appeared in your previous searches. Different neighbourhoods, some properties that are just coming on the market. When would be convenient for viewings?"
They arranged to meet Friday afternoon, giving him time to compile a selection of suitable apartments.
That evening, Jay stayed late at the office, joining Ian for their renewed tradition of afternoon coffee in the kitchen. After a week of apartment hunting that had kept him away from the office each evening, it felt comforting to return to the overtime work.
"I'm afraid I'm starting to be more like you. Finding solace in working late."
Ian laughed. "That's an awful development."
"At least the place is pleasant. After a whole week of visiting properties, it feels nice to take it easy here."
"How did the call with the real estate agent go?"
"He seemed very helpful. We're meeting Friday afternoon to look at several places. And Luc filled me in on the weekend aftermath—apparently Gav is still angry about Yan's behavior."
Ian was content hearing that Jay's tormentor had faced some consequences, however minor, and that the weekend's drama had resolved without lasting damage. "I'm glad things worked out reasonably well in the end." 
"Luc said I looked intimidating at the restaurant. Do I really come across that way?"
"Not at all. But you definitely appear far more confident than you did a year ago."
"Is that good or bad?"
"Entirely good. You've grown into yourself. It's obvious to everyone around you."
They chatted about various work matters for a while before going back to their respective desks.
Jay returned home around nine that evening, spending the remainder of his time browsing online for relaxing weekend activities. Nothing particularly caught his attention—most suggestions seemed either too elaborate or too mundane for what he had in mind.
He still had several days to plan something appropriate for Ian's forced day of rest. The challenge was finding activities that would engage someone who seemed to find fulfilment primarily through productive work. He wanted to choose something Ian would enjoy, not just endure out of politeness. The pressure to make it perfect was probably excessive, but Jay was unable to approach the task casually.
As he scrolled through various entertainment options, Jay pondered on Ian's observation about his increased confidence. Looking back at his early days at the company, he could see the point. The anxious, uncertain person who had started working for Ian bore little resemblance to the man who now felt comfortable enough to insert himself into his boss's weekend plans. 
Perhaps that change really did make all the difference in how others perceived him. Whether it was sustainable or advisable remained to be seen, but Jay was no longer particularly concerned about such practical considerations.
Scene 2
Great social transgression
The next day Jay left work at five to head to the gym. Throughout the day, Luc had been casting him pointed glances, but thankfully hadn't pressed the issue of his promised apology for the unfortunate evening.
This time, Ian wasn't even melancholy about Jay's early departure. "Have a good session," he said cheerfully. He was sustained by the vision of their weekend together, and the anticipation made the early departure tolerable, if barely.
At the gym, Rus immediately pounced on him for details.
"So how did the date go? You look like you survived it at least."
Jay made a point of complaining about it for the entire session. "The man insulted me repeatedly, calling me a funeral director. The women completely ignored me. The drinks were awful, the pub was impossibly loud, and my best suit got ruined when someone spilled wine all over it."
"That sounds terrible."
"It was. Then I got stupidly drunk trying to salvage what was left of the evening and had such a terrible hangover I almost missed work the next day. I told you I didn't want to go."
He didn't mention a word about Ian, knowing Rus disapproved, but dramatized every detail to emphasize that he had been right not to want to go.
Rus got the message clearly. "I'm sorry I pushed you into it. How about we do something fun next weekend to make up for it?"
Jay laughed. "Luc already made the same suggestion, but no thanks. I've already made plans, and these plans definitely don't involve getting humiliated."
"Plans? What kind of plans?"
"The kind that don't involve blind dates," Jay replied, deflecting with a smile.
Rus pressed for more details, but Jay remained mysteriously silent about his weekend arrangements.
When he returned home, Jay realized he had not only made weekend plans with his boss, but he was being secretive about it. He felt it was important that absolutely no one could know, as if he was committing some great social transgression. He suspected that a subordinate fraternizing with his boss to this extent could be seen as problematic.
The secrecy should have been alarming, but instead it felt thrilling. The sensation was so unlike anything he had ever experienced that it gave him a momentary pause, but he quickly dismissed his concerns. There was no point in overthinking any of it. For once in his life, it was a moment to act, not analyse.
After three hours of browsing websites and reading reviews, he threw up his hands in frustration and decided to abandon his quest for the perfect itinerary. Instead, he would simply take Ian to do things Jay himself enjoyed. 
A scenic ferry ride along the river, where they could watch the city drift by from the water. A visit to a restaurant that served seasonal dishes for a lunch made from fresh pumpkins. Then the botanical garden he loved, where the autumn trees had turned deep red and gold, creating a spectacular display just before the leaves fell completely.
The plan felt right, personal. He just hoped the weather would cooperate, because otherwise their outdoor activities could quickly turn cold and gloomy.
He went to sleep pleased with his decision, even ecstatic. As he drifted off, he thought it was going to be perfect. A lovely autumn date with Ian. Wait. Not a date. Just an outing.
But the thought of it being a date made his heart race unexpectedly, and he fell asleep smiling at his own foolishness for entertaining such ideas.
The next day at work, Luc approached Jay conspiratorially.
"There's this amazing bar with a fantastic entertainment show this weekend. Perfect atmosphere, great drinks, very peaceful. What do you say?"
"I already have plans that absolutely cannot be changed," Jay replied.
Luc looked sceptical. "You never have plans. Do these plans involve the gym and industry reading?"
"I made arrangements with friends already, and I'm not going to change them. Tell Gav he doesn't need to blame himself or fret about Saturday night. It's forgotten."
"Friends? What friends?"
"Friends who make plans that don't require apologizing afterwards."
From his office, Ian had been listening to this exchange with glee. Jay was defending their weekend plans against social pressure, prioritizing their time together over other opportunities. The joy that made him feel was probably disproportionate to the circumstances, but he was unable to care.
Wednesday and Thursday, Jay worked late with Ian, sharing their daily coffee conversations in the kitchen and leaving together around eight. Both were in rather good moods, laughing at each other's jokes and seeming to find everything more amusing than usual.
On Friday, Jay had his meeting with the agent and left at five. Ian was so excited about the weekend that he left home early as well, because staying at work felt unbearable.
Jay met the agent in the city centre next to a property. He was a short, cheerful fellow in an elegant suit with an exaggerated smile. He drove Jay around in his expensive sedan, showing about ten different apartments and handing Jay a folder containing specifications with photos and floor plans of each place, clearly meticulously prepared.
The apartments were a bit over Jay's budget and, while nice, not quite nice enough to justify the price. But Jay still studied all the listings and added his own notes. He told the agent which ones he liked and why.
In the last place they visited, they had a conversation about Jay's ideal apartment.
"I'd love a good view and a balcony, but well designed floor plan and good transit access are most important. I'd like plenty of storage space and a bathroom that can fit a washing machine. I value functionality, but I'd like the space to feel welcoming."
The agent was extremely enthusiastic about this information, even suspiciously so. They arranged to meet the following week for another set of places that would take into account everything Jay had said.
Jay selected three he liked the most and added them to his list. The neighbourhoods were a bit too posh for his tastes, but the apartments themselves were acceptable.
As he rode the metro home, his thoughts quickly moved away from housing logistics. Tomorrow he would go to Ian's apartment. The prospect overwhelmed every other consideration.
Scene 3
Weekend work
Jay dealt with all the most pressing chores on Friday evening, doing laundry and cleaning his apartment. On Saturday morning, he finished the remaining tasks before heading to the gym. He was unusually energetic, which didn't escape Rus's notice.
"You're practically bouncing off the walls. Do your mysterious plans involve an actual date?"
Jay couldn't suppress a giggle. "No, definitely no date whatsoever."
This response only cemented Rus's suspicion, but he dropped the subject. Jay left the gym in high spirits and showered very thoroughly before dressing in one of his standard office suits and taking a taxi to the address Ian had given him.
Really, a day working with his boss in his free time should not be something he looked forward to this much, and yet here he was.
When he arrived, Jay evaluated the neighbourhood. It was definitely upscale, but not as upscale as he had imagined. He had thought Ian would live in one of the most prestigious districts of the city, but the area was closer to the financial district than to the exclusive Heights where old money resided. Perhaps practical considerations took precedence. The district was by the river and had plenty of green spaces though, so Jay approved.
At precisely noon, as arranged, Jay rang Ian's bell. Ian's voice came through the intercom immediately, as if he had been waiting nearby.
"Come up. Twentieth floor."
Ian met him at the apartment door, immaculately dressed as always. Jay had wondered if Ian might wear something more casual at home, but of course not. He probably didn't even own casual clothes.
Ian's penthouse was on one of the top floors of the building, with a large living room overlooking the city and a separate kitchen. 
"Coffee?" Ian asked after they exchanged their customary greetings.
Jay nodded, setting up a workspace at the dining table and unpacking his laptop. He noticed the painting on the wall—the same one he had admired at the company art exhibition they had attended together. It brought back pleasant memories.
"I remember that painting. From the art show," Jay said when Ian returned with coffee.
"I thought it would liven up the space."
Ian already had his computer set up at the opposite end of the table, and soon they went to work. Ian showed Jay what he was working on—mostly technical tasks involving spreadsheet templates and data organization. They divided the work and started tackling it, chatting occasionally about casual topics.
It was the first time they had worked so closely together. While Jay normally sat outside Ian's office in fairly close proximity, they had always been divided by a glass wall. Now they could see each other easily whenever either looked up, and could talk without having to raise their voices.
For Jay, sitting in Ian's personal space like this made his skin tingle. He stole glances around the apartment and at Ian when he moved from one section to another, breathing in the scent of something fresh—maybe apple and spice. Probably an air freshener, but subtle enough that it was barely noticeable.
After an hour, Ian announced, "I'll make lunch."
Jay wanted to protest but just nodded. "Do you need any help?"
"I'll be done in a few minutes."
Jay got up and stretched, savouring the opportunity to move freely through Ian's living space. Eating Ian's homemade food felt almost decadent. Their shared breakfast had been divine, and Jay's anticipation was considerable.
Ian returned within ten minutes with two plates of pasta with mushrooms and herbs. "I prepared everything beforehand and just had to cook it quickly. Various pastas with seasonal ingredients are my go-to meals when I cook for myself."
Jay ate with delight, and of course it was delicious. He offered to clean up, but Ian waved him off.
"My housekeeper comes daily and takes care of whatever needs attention."
"Even on Sundays?"
"Yes. May is an older lady who's been with me for years. She finishes the work in at most two hours on most days, and since she's my full-time employee without other work, she insists on ensuring the house is spotless at all times."
Jay thought it was nice that Ian had someone taking care of him, and he wasn't surprised that people who worked for Ian were so fiercely loyal.
They returned to work afterwards, and Jay focused properly to accomplish as much as possible. He was no stranger to tedious and repetitive tasks, and had plenty of experience working weekends since starting at Turner & Associates. He was making excellent progress.
"You don't need to go quite so hard at it. It's not urgent," Ian commented.
Jay insisted, "I need to ensure you have nothing on your mind tomorrow, so as much of this needs to be finished today as possible. Preferably all of it."
Ian smiled to himself. "Alright then, let's do our best."
After another hour, Jay asked if they should have more coffee, and Ian agreed. Jay followed him to the kitchen, which was bigger than expected and quite well-stocked for someone who didn't cook regularly.
"May rotates the selection and brings whatever gets uneaten home, so nothing goes to waste. The produce often reflects her tastes. I'm sometimes in the mood for something different from my standard meal plan, and I cook occasionally to maintain the skill."
"I doubt cooking is like violin playing. It doesn't atrophy that much."
"I'm not entirely sure about that. Plus it's relaxing to do something different occasionally. The uneaten meal containers get delivered to the community kitchen by May the next day, so I don't worry about waste."
Jay approved of the sustainability. "That's thoughtful."
"My experience of finding myself one day with absolutely nothing was eye-opening about the value of charity and avoiding waste."
Ian prepared coffee using a high-end but relatively standard machine. Jay evaluated the setup with an appraiser's eye. "I should think about getting a coffee machine for my new apartment."
"Absolutely. Having space for conveniences should be one of your priorities. There's no reason for you to suffer discomfort—you've had enough of that already."
They returned to work, and Ian brought out a fresh fruit platter with sliced varieties that were quite diverse, courtesy of May.
"She was quite happy I'm having visitors. It doesn't happen very often."
"She'd be less happy if she knew it's just a coworker doing literal weekend overtime."
"May claims it's important to eat lots of fruits, and a big variety. I don't disagree. Cut fruits appear in my diet frequently."
As time passed, they took more frequent breaks, talking about food and housing. Soon eight o'clock arrived, and Ian announced the end of the workday.
"We accomplished quite a bit," Jay agreed.
"Let me take you to dinner. It's the least I can do," Ian proposed.
Ian drove them to a nearby restaurant he frequented. He recommended the steak, which Jay ordered, while Ian chose grilled salmon.
"Steak is an occasional indulgence. I save it for business dinners. I need all the help I can get to survive them."
"You don't seem like someone who has problems with the social demands of your job."
"There's a good reason Leo handles most client relations. I can manage them when necessary, and do it well, but it's not exactly my favourite activity. I much prefer the comfort of being alone with numbers."
Jay pondered this for a moment. "Did my company today distract you from your numbers?"
"Not at all. I never realized what help a skilled assistant could be until I met you. After the Sam Connelly situation, I was certain assistants of any sort were only a liability."
Jay was hesitant, but curiousity won and he asked, "Was Sam a good assistant?"
Ian laughed, though without much humour. "Not remotely. Back then I thought he was, but that was due to my inexperience. He spent most of his time distracting me from work that needed to be done. At the time, that felt... exciting. But I was simply foolish."
Jay didn't want the evening to turn sombre by revisiting old wounds, but he was secretly pleased that Ian opened up to him like this. Ian seemed to recognize the sensitive territory and quickly changed the subject.
"Since we're mostly finished with the main course, shall we look at desserts?"
Jay winced. "You seemed so health-conscious twenty minutes ago while ordering, and now you want desserts?"
Ian laughed. "I like desserts, but let's go for their roasted fruits with honey. They're excellent and not too unhealthy."
They did, and Jay agreed they were delicious.
"I prefer the fruity and nutty options," Ian said.
Jay made a mental note—this seemed like important information.
Ian then drove Jay home, and as Jay left the car, he said, "See you tomorrow. I'll pick you up at eleven. We're taking public transport, so brace yourself."
"Sounds fine. I'll be ready."
They parted ways, both pleased with how the day had gone.
Scene 4
Sunday date
Jay dressed that morning in one of his best suits—and the boldest of his selection, the tailored burgundy three-piece that made him look like old world royalty. He selected a black tie with subtle but striking patterns, combed his hair to perfection, and regretted that he had never researched cologne. His dress watch was the only jewellery he wore, and he looked at it with warmth—the gift from Ian, matching his outfit perfectly. Tie pins or cufflinks would have completed the look, but he was too inexperienced with men's fashion to add such touches.
Still, he was reasonably satisfied with the effect. He looked good.
He took a taxi to Ian's building and arrived five minutes early to find Ian already waiting near the entrance. Jay stepped out to collect him, and they rode together to the ferry port.
The weather was perfect. November chill was in the air, but it was sunny enough that no coats were needed, and Jay felt relieved.
"I'm eternally grateful we have nice autumns. I'd hate it if the weather ruined my plans," Jay remarked as they rode.
"What exactly are these mysterious plans?"
"Nothing too exciting, so don't get your hopes up. Mostly boat rides and walks in public gardens."
"At least my forced time off is leisurely. I was half-worried you'd drag me to a fencing session."
Jay laughed. "You're completely safe. No strenuous physical activity planned. It's a rest from your gym regimen as well."
They reached the river port quickly thanks to light weekend traffic. Jay led the way to a large ferry with a small cafe on the upper deck. They made themselves comfortable, and Jay explained it was a two-hour ride upstream through the more industrial part of the city. They ordered coffees and soon the boat departed.
"Have you ever taken the scenic ferry ride?" Jay asked.
"No, I haven't. I was born in this city and have lived here most of my life, but it never occurred to me. It wasn't an activity people did in my circles, and I never deviated from expectations."
"I'm sorry if it's too plebeian, but I love it. I took it my first year after moving to the city and several times afterwards. The gentle rocking is relaxing and the views are quite nice. The city looks different from this perspective, and the parts by the river are the best-maintained areas. It probably makes me a country bumpkin for still being impressed after so many years, but I can't help it."
"It's quite charming," Ian said, watching the autumn scenery drift past. "The fall colors really are lovely."
They chatted about their pastimes. Jay told Ian about his first several boat rides, though most had been solitary and unremarkable. Ian shared stories from his youth.
"Besides violin and fencing, I enjoyed tennis. I also rode horses, though it wasn't my favorite. Nothing wrong with it, I just wasn't a horse person. We went skiing as a family, but I haven't done that in years. I still play tennis regularly, and I'm quite fond of swimming. My apartment complex has an outdoor pool that I use frequently in summer."
Time passed quickly with conversation and sightseeing, and they soon reached their destination. The restaurant Jay had chosen was not far, and they walked through charming streets.
It was a bistro with a large outdoor patio decorated for the season, with apples and pumpkins arranged in rustic baskets. They ordered seasonal classics: thick pumpkin soup with freshly baked garlic bread, pickled pumpkins, baked potatoes, and pumpkin pie for dessert. The meal was hearty but fresh, and a bonfire in the backyard brought nostalgic scents to Jay.
"We used to have bonfires like this regularly when I was growing up. Very popular in my part of the country. There's something magical about the smell of burning leaves and wood."
The atmosphere was pastoral, and Jay thought they stood out from the local crowd in their elegant suits, but he didn't mind at all.
After lunch, Jay led them to the metro and they traveled several stops to a botanical garden. Jay guided Ian through the paths, showing him his favorite spots. There was a refreshment stand in the center where Jay bought them caramelized apples on sticks, then led Ian to a secluded bench by a small pond.
"You'll need to eat carefully to avoid staining your suit," Jay warned, then retrieved a bag of seeds from his jacket. He handed half to Ian with instructions on duck feeding.
"You want me to feed ducks?" Ian asked, looking bemused.
"Naturally. It's a perfectly respectable Sunday afternoon activity," Jay replied with a deadpan expression,  demonstrating the proper seed-scattering technique.
Ian imitated him, and they watched ducks flock to the food, quacking enthusiastically.
"I do this regularly. Low cost, pleasant hobby. I always make a point of feeding the ducks at least once during vacation time. Since I don't really travel, I've made it my mission to find local activities that can be done on a budget. Duck feeding ranks quite high on that list. What did you do for fun during your studio years?"
"Nothing," Ian replied simply.
"You couldn't have done absolutely nothing."
"I never allowed myself time off. Only work, gym, occasionally tennis if I felt generous. I played violin too, but only after several years when I finally bought an instrument. By then my skill had degraded massively."
Jay felt heartbroken hearing this, and the urge to embrace Ian again overwhelmed him. But without his previous hangover-induced bravado, he lacked the courage.
Jay looked at the early setting sun, golden light making the red leaves even deeper in color. "Well, those days are over. For as long as I'm your assistant, I'll make it my mission to ensure you take proper breaks and enjoy life. Even if I have to force, trick, or manipulate you into it."
Ian laughed at Jay's solemn expression. "You're free to try, but I should warn you—I'm pretty stubborn."
They sat feeding ducks until darkness and chill set in, then Jay proposed dinner. They took the subway toward Jay's neighborhood—a rather long ride, but Sunday trains were fairly empty so they sat comfortably.
"Are you uncomfortable with so much subway time?" Jay asked.
"Not at all. I'm quite acquainted with the transit system. It feels a bit nostalgic. I don't miss living in that studio, but the experience wasn't entirely negative. Character-building, eye-opening, humbling in constructive ways. I grew up very sheltered with narrow perspectives. The circumstances that expanded my worldview were harrowing, but the growth itself was valuable."
Jay was struck by Ian's mature perspective. Truly the mark of a great man—to lose everything and still see the silver lining.
Jay chose a restaurant he knew well, more rustic and folk-oriented than Ian's usual establishments, but Jay found it charming. He recommended cabbage rolls.
"My grandmother used to make them just like these," he said.
Ian had never eaten anything similar. It wasn't bad—definitely an interesting experience.
When evening fell for good, Ian thanked Jay for the day. "This was definitely novel. I'll take a taxi home from here."
"I hope it wasn't too disappointing."
"Not at all."
Jay felt slightly sheepish, suspecting this was a once-in-a-lifetime opportunity unlikely to repeat. But even so, he was glad he had convinced Ian to stop working, if only for one day, even if it was just to feed ducks.
He walked home through the cold evening air, thinking he would treasure this moment—the day he convinced his distinguished boss to ride ferries and walk through parks with him.
Yet when he returned home, Jay felt profoundly sad. At Ian's isolation, at getting only this small glimpse of him as a person before they returned to their respective roles. He lay in bed, overwhelmed by melancholy, as if something precious had been taken from him.
Scene 5
Gloom
The next Monday was unusually heavy for Jay. Nothing was really wrong, and he worked beside Ian all day, seeing him from dawn until dusk. But it felt different. There was a glass wall between them now, both literal and metaphorical. They were not friends, certainly not anything more than that. They were coworkers, brought together by circumstances and held in place by the thin context of their working arrangements.
It felt suffocating somehow.
For one brief Sunday afternoon, they had sat as equals sharing mundane experiences that had felt extraordinary. Jay knew it was an illusion, but it had been so beautiful he couldn't bring himself to let it go.
During their afternoon coffee, Ian asked, "Is anything wrong? You seem quiet today."
"It's nothing. Just realizing that autumn is ending, making me feel nostalgic."
Jay didn't know how to voice what was bothering him without sounding greedy and ungrateful. He also didn't want to burden this wonderful man with his foolish feelings.
They worked long hours that day, until nine, then until ten, neither moving from their desks despite barely accomplishing anything meaningful. Both felt untethered, reluctant to part ways but unwilling to say it aloud. Finally it became unfeasible to prolong the evening further, and they left, barely looking at each other, as if afraid of what they might see in each other's eyes.
Jay completely abandoned dinner that day. They hadn't ordered anything, he had nothing prepared at home, it was late, and frankly he wasn't that hungry. He just showered and went straight to bed.
The next day was Tuesday, so Jay left at five to go to the gym, leaving Ian alone in his office. Ian took it unusually hard. His one weekend alone with Jay was over, and it had been amazing, but now it was in the past and Ian had nothing more to sustain him. Nothing to hope for, nothing to wait for. Just the reality of watching Jay leave for other arrangements involving other people, friends and companions who occupied the spaces in Jay's life that Ian could never claim.
And Ian had nothing. Just the dull ache in his chest and the knowledge that this was as much as he would ever get. The scraps of attention from his assistant who was too kind for his own good, who had taken pity on his pathetic boss and arranged entertainment because it was too sad to watch him work constantly.
Ian accomplished nothing that evening. He simply sat in his office, brooding.
At the gym, Jay was distracted and sighing frequently.
"Was the date a failure?" Rus asked.
"It wasn't a date, and no, it was wonderful," Jay replied, then sighed deeply. "Rus, have you ever met someone so perfect you knew you'd never be able to measure up to them?"
Rus laughed. "Ah, so you got rejected. Look, you're a great guy, and whoever turned you down is blind and doesn't understand what they had."
Jay shook his head and decided against trying to untangle this misunderstanding. "You don't understand."
"Don't worry. You'll meet someone even better."
"I seriously doubt it."
"How about we plan something fun for this weekend? Cheer you up, get you back out there."
"Yeah, I don't want to sit at home alone and overthink."
"I'll let you know what we're doing. Reserve Saturday afternoon."
Jay just nodded and sighed again, this time even more deeply.
He returned home feeling foolish. Rus had gotten a completely wrong idea, and his melancholy was for an entirely different reason. It was just that Ian was exceptional, and Jay was blessed to be able to stay close to him. But at the same time, he knew he couldn't be farther away from him, and it felt painful.
He should have made dinner, but once again he didn't feel like it. He just sat on his couch and sighed, then took a really long shower and went to bed early.
On Wednesday morning he felt groggy and sluggish. Skipping dinner two consecutive nights was reckless and foolish. He had promised himself to take better care of himself, to not disappoint Ian, to stand by his side no matter what. He couldn't allow his confusion to impact his well-being.
He ate double breakfast and that same day booked his seasonal vaccination that he had been planning to get. During lunch, he ordered an unusually large portion.
"Someone's hungry today," Amy observed as Jay attacked his meal.
"I was too lazy to cook and skipped dinner, so I need to make up for it," Jay explained to general team amusement.
"You're looking gloomy these days," Luc observed. "Are you still upset about that evening? Because if so, Gav is still hoping to organize an apology event, maybe this weekend."
"I have plans."
"You're lying. There's no way you're having plans two weekends in a row."
"It's with Rus this time."
Luc looked offended. "You'll make plans with your gym trainer but not with your favourite coworker?"
Jay managed a weak smile. "Maybe some other time."
Jay stayed late that day again, tackling non-urgent issues. The next day he had another apartment viewing, so he was going to leave at a normal hour and wanted to avoid any backlog.
There was a slight undercurrent of tension between him and Ian that went unacknowledged but was deeply felt. Last week had been joyous, but now very little of that joy remained, and they were both subdued. They both blamed it on the weather—as November slowly ended, nice days were few and far between, with temperatures reaching ten degrees, gloomy skies, and darkness falling early.
Both were aware it was deeper than weather for them, even though neither realized the other was taking it equally poorly. They exchanged strained smiles as they returned to their desks to finish the workday.
This time they left at eight, and Jay finally cooked dinner and ate, but it tasted like nothing at all. He hoped he wasn't catching some illness.
The weight in his chest remained, a constant ache he couldn't understand. He only knew that something precious felt just out of reach, and the distance between what was and what could never be seemed to grow larger with each passing day.
Chapter 29: Choices
Scene 1
The perfect apartment
Thursday's apartment viewing began with the same cheerfulness the agent had displayed during their previous meeting. His smile was even more pronounced as he guided Jay through the first few properties.
Jay was increasingly confused. The part of the city they were touring wasn't one he would have selected, and the listings were above his stated budget range. When he voiced these concerns, the agent smiled apologetically.
"Your budget can definitely accommodate these options if you're willing to make some concessions. This area is truly worth the sacrifice."
The next listing was less impressive, a cramped space with an impossibly long corridor and a bathroom barely large enough for one person, though it was more reasonably priced.
The agent shook his head with sadness. "Naturally, more modest options are available in this area, but compromises must be made."
They drove further into the district, approaching the river, and Jay began recognizing the surroundings. Ian lived somewhere nearby. They stopped beside a beautiful new building overlooking a charming park and the water beyond.
The agent's smile became positively radiant. "Now this is something special."
The building's interior was tastefully finished, and they rode the elevator to the fourth floor. Following a small corridor, they entered a brand-new unit that took Jay's breath away.
The apartment was fully furnished. The paint scheme featured peaceful, muted earth tones that complemented dark wooden floors throughout. Enormous windows flooded the space with natural light, and a balcony offered spectacular river views.
Despite being compact, the floor plan felt spacious and brilliantly designed. A huge closet was cleverly integrated into the small entryway, maximizing storage without overwhelming the entrance. The bedroom had just enough space for a proper bed and wide closet. The kitchenette was built into the wall, with modern appliances and plenty of counters despite the modest size, and with cupboards extending to the ceiling.
The living room featured a corner couch that looked both stylish and comfortable, a mounted television with a stand holding small plants, and a coffee table that didn't dominate the space. A dining table with four chairs was positioned to stay out of the way while still leaving room for a proper desk and decent-sized bookshelf.
The bathroom impressed Jay most of all. It contained both a walk-in shower and a bathtub, with a washing machine and dryer built into custom paneling that concealed them completely. Multiple drawers extended to the ceiling, providing abundant storage without cluttering the visual space.
The balcony featured an outdoor patio sofa with cushions and a small table perfect for morning coffee. Throughout the apartment, plush rugs and strategically placed plants created an atmosphere that was cosy and welcoming.
Jay walked through the space in awe. Of all the properties he had seen, this one was unquestionably the most beautiful, furnished with brand-new pieces arranged so competently that he wouldn't change a single detail.
The agent began his sales presentation, highlighting every amenity with theatrical gestures.
"Look at this perfectly fitted dishwasher! The cupboards maximize storage capacity beyond what you'd expect for the square meters. Notice how much counter space we've achieved."
He continued his tour with growing excitement. "The corner sofa is impossibly soft yet durable. It opens for emergency sleeping arrangements and could comfortably accommodate three guests. The bedroom closet is enormous, you could easily fit clothes for at least two people!"
Jay had to admit he wasn't exaggerating. Every claim was demonstrably accurate.
"The modern air conditioning utilizes the latest technology. Superior heating and insulation ensures low costs. The parks in this area are magnificent, the one next door is decent sized, and there are two more within walking distance. Multiple cafes and grocery stores nearby, plus two highly regarded restaurants. The metro station is a nine-minute walk, which might not be the most convenient, and it's not on one of the main lines. However, it provides direct service to the financial district, and the route passes through parkland, making it practically a wellness activity!"
The agent was clearly downplaying cons while highlighting pros, and with each sentence Jay became both more charmed and more distrustful. He was starting to suspect either a scam or an astronomical price, so when the agent began another tirade about advanced technologies, Jay gently interrupted.
"What's the price?"
The agent's smile became even more brilliant. "This is a special opportunity exclusively for valued partners at Turner & Associates. Unavailable on the general market, with a personal discount from our agency owner. One and a half million."
It was... suspiciously cheap. Still above Jay's budget of one million plus or minus twenty percent, but potentially manageable if he included his annual bonus. Based on previous presentations, however, properties of this calibre in this location should cost at least twice that amount.
Jay narrowed his eyes. "Was someone murdered here? Is this an unlucky number? Flooding risk?"
The agent looked offended. "Nothing of the sort! I would never compromise my reputation. Ian Hagen himself is guaranteeing my honesty in this transaction."
The mention of Ian was reassuring. Ian had recommended him personally, and no one would dare tarnish his name. But the offer was still not only too good to be true, it seemed like the agent had been building up to this from the beginning.
Jay walked through the apartment two more times. It was beautiful. He could see himself drinking morning coffee on that balcony. There was enough floor space between the living room and entrance to lay down a yoga mat for morning exercise, which he did occasionally but had stopped due to space constraints. He could probably host his entire family if he slept on the floor himself. Ian would have a comfortable place to sleep when he stayed over again...
Wait. He wasn't allowed to think such thoughts.
But speaking of Ian, this was his general area, wasn't it? He'd have to check the map when he got home.
Jay thanked the agent and headed to the metro station, exploring the vicinity. The river was close but situated in a small valley, making flooding unlikely. The park was lovely, with variety of trees and bushes and clearly fresh pavement. Well-dressed people moved about, as expected in such an upscale area, but not to the point of being intimidating.
He entered one of the recommended restaurants and ordered their apparently famous seared trout, which was decent, though Jay wasn't sure it was worth the hype evident from teenagers taking selfies at every second table.
He took the metro home, but it required changing trains in the financial district. This was a further part of town, away from the central metro network he was accustomed to. From his current station, he could take three lines practically halfway across the city. He was quite fond of the convenience, and it was less than five minutes from home.
Returning to his studio, which now looked awfully depressing in comparison, Jay realized the apartment he'd just seen was only eighteen square meters bigger, but seemed twice as spacious. And he'd already thought his studio had pretty well-conceptualized space allocation.
He looked up the area on his city map. Ian lived only a twelve-minute walk away. He could probably see Ian's building if he looked carefully enough.
He sighed heavily. It was too good to be true. It was also outside his financial comfort zone. Admittedly a restrictively tight zone, but he remained cautious about his financial security. And he couldn't shake the feeling that this was another elaborate plot, just like his hiring.
But he couldn't understand what the endgame was supposed to be.
Scene 2
Manipulation
The next morning at work, Jay was still torn about the apartment. During the briefing in Ian's office, as they finished with the day's agenda, Jay hesitated for a moment before leaving, then asked, "Can I take a moment with a personal matter?"
"Naturally," Ian replied.
"How trustworthy is the agent you recommended?"
Ian felt his pulse quicken. "I wouldn't recommend him if I wasn't certain about his reliability. Was there a problem?"
"Not a problem exactly, but he showed me a listing that's ridiculously attractive, half the market price, and it happens to be twelve minutes away from where you live."
Ian's heart skipped a beat. He had instructed the agent that if he could get Jay to move within walking distance, he would be properly rewarded, but to be absolutely discreet about it. Now he feared his frankly elementary scheme was completely transparent.
"The real estate market can be unpredictable. Connections are often half the battle in finding good opportunities," Ian said with forced casualness.
Jay sighed deeply, thinking. "Is it once again my closeness to you propping me up?" He looked at Ian with a slightly miserable expression. "I can't possibly accept sliding on your coattails so much."
Ian tried to control his voice to sound calm and patient. "Your current employment has many demands. Frequent long hours, incredibly stressful periods, constant need for impeccable conduct and presentation, highest standards of performance. But there are also benefits, and the pay is only one of them. Connections come as another, and you're well within your rights to make use of them. That's compensation for the unreasonable requirements."
Jay wasn't entirely convinced, but dropped the subject and returned to work.
During lunch that day, he showed the listing to the whole team, illustrating his concerns. Almost everyone was quite enthusiastic, with Zoe being the most excited.
"It's an absolute steal! You cannot let this offer go," she insisted.
Only Max was visibly less impressed. "It's far too small to live comfortably long-term. You'll outgrow it within a year."
Luc commented diplomatically. "It's quaint, I suppose."
Jay disregarded him completely since his tastes were unorthodox at best. The rest of the team agreed it was a great deal, with Kim being just as suspicious as Jay.
"It's likely Ian's name can open many doors. Being the CFO's assistant is an underrated position. Maybe I should have gone for it as well."
Jay remained undecided. Despite it being Friday and not having many tasks, he stayed late with Ian, mostly browsing different units and comparing prices. For the general area, the price was incredibly low, and places this beautiful were rare on the market.
Ian approached him on the way to coffee, trying to sound casual. "Are you considering the place?"
"I honestly don't know. I have a bad feeling about it, and it's not in my comfortable budget."
"Your comfortable budget is conservative for someone with your salary," Ian remarked.
Jay grew quiet, finally saying, "It's hard for me to get rid of my initial levels of frugality." He didn't want to admit to Ian that he didn't trust the stability of his position.
They went to get coffee, and Ian changed the subject. "Do you have weekend plans?"
"Yeah, I'm going out with Rus."
 "That sounds enjoyable."
While Ian wished him fun, secretly his mood darkened. Jay rarely had plans before, but now it seemed he regularly spent time with his gym trainer turned friend. Ian felt desperate, and before returning to his desk, took a gamble.
"If you want my advice, you should take the apartment. You can afford it, and I can recommend a bank that will give you attractive mortgage conditions."
"I can't possibly impose any more than I already have."
"You not only can, you absolutely should. You've seized every opportunity that presented itself to your advantage and are all the better for it. You should continue to do so. This is the time for you to arrange the life you want to live, so you need to be decisive."
The speech came out more grandiose than intended, but Ian wanted to make his point, to push Jay toward this decision. It felt bitter to be so manipulative, trying to engineer proximity while compromising his own integrity. But his need for Jay was stronger than his discomfort with his methods.
Jay returned to his desk with coffee and more conflicted feelings. He trusted Ian's judgment and knew he was probably right. But Jay wasn’t Ian. Ian was brave, unbreakable. When his life had collapsed once already, he had clawed his way back and rebuilt it from the ashes. Jay knew he couldn’t do that. He was terrified of making a wrong call, of misjudging the situation and making a mistake that would cost him more than he could bear. If he fell, he wouldn’t have the strength to rise again. That fear clung to him, even as he tried to let Ian’s assurance steady him.
The memories of the moment he discovered the circumstances that got him his position surfaced—the humiliation of being used as a pawn, of being the laughingstock of the whole company. It made him stop in his tracks.
Things had turned out okay so far. But had they really? The feeling of playing a role he didn't understand returned, and his wariness skyrocketed.
He could sense the undercurrent of manipulation in the air, subtle but impossible to ignore. Lately, he’d been feeling good, secure. His initial doubts had faded, replaced by a cautious trust in the path unfolding before him. But Jay knew too well that when something felt too good to be true, it always was. There had to be strings attached. He just couldn’t tell who held them, or what end they were pulling toward.
He would have to think about it. Maybe ask other people, make a detailed list, research more. He didn't need to decide right away. There was still time to think it through.
For now, he would go home and consider it. Tomorrow maybe he would consult Rus. At least Rus seemed to have his wellbeing in mind, even if his ideas were sometimes misguided.
Scene 3
Adventures with friends
On Saturday morning, Jay went to the gym as always. Between sets, he asked Rus about his apartment predicament.
"I'm searching for a place to buy because my current rental is tiny. The search was going so-so, but the agent I got introduced to through work is showing me a perfect place that fits my dream vision, for a low price—an absolute steal. It has some minor issues I could potentially overlook, like being on the far end of the city, near the posh heights and away from the center with less convenient metro access. But it feels too good to be true."
Rus pondered this for a moment. "If you move there, you'll stop coming to this gym."
That was true enough—the transit wouldn't be convenient, and Jay would definitely find something closer. He felt connected to his current neighborhood, and uprooting himself held no appeal.
"No matter," Rus continued. "We can still meet outside the gym. You don't really need a trainer anymore—you could manage on your own, you know the drill."
"You motivate me to stay consistent. I don't want to change trainers."
"You shouldn't let the neighborhood tie you down. We can always stay friends and keep in touch outside the gym. It may feel good to stay in the same place, but it can also lead to stagnation. You have a chance to upgrade your life. You got this opportunity through your work and should seize it. At least you'll get something out of all those stressful quarterly periods."
Rus was essentially echoing Ian's advice. Jay thought to himself that naturally Rus would say that—he and Ian were both self-assured men who didn't let doubts or anxieties hold them back.
"The offer feels suspect somehow. Like I'm being given something too good for me, as bait. But I don't understand who would want to bait me, or what for."
"Maybe they want to get in good with that demanding boss of yours."
