He climbed the stairs to his apartment with leaden steps, unlocked the door, and went inside. He didn't turn on any lights. Just stripped off his clothes—jacket, shirt, trousers all dropped in a pile on the floor—and crawled into bed wearing only his underwear.
The tears came immediately, hot and shameful. He pressed his face into the pillow to muffle the sounds. His entire body shook with it—the fear, the humiliation, the crushing weight of having failed so badly at something so fundamental.
He hadn't eaten. Hadn't washed. He just cried until exhaustion finally pulled him under into sleep.
Outside, the rain started again, pattering against the window like an accusation.
Scene 3
Packing
Saturday arrived and Jay stayed in bed until noon, staring at the ceiling. He only got up when biology demanded it, moving through his tiny apartment like a ghost.
He'd skipped the gym. The thought of seeing Rus and having to pretend everything was fine was unbearable. He just wanted to hide.
Eventually he got dressed in sweatpants and stared at his apartment. The contract lay on the table where he'd left it days ago in an official folder. Jay couldn't look at it. Every time his eyes drifted in that direction, his stomach twisted.
He needed something to occupy his mind, to stop the endless replay of Friday evening. So he focused on packing and sorting.
Summer clothes went into one box. Books into another. He organized old documents, separating the ones he deemed necessary from those destined for the throwaway pile. Tax records, rental agreements, pay stubs from jobs he'd left behind—all of it got categorized.
He didn't own quite that many things. By the end of the weekend, he was mostly done. Only the items he used daily remained unpacked—toiletries, a few changes of clothes, basic kitchen supplies.
His apartment looked sad. Boxes stacked against walls, empty shelves, the sense of a space already abandoned.
He felt less sure than ever about the move, about buying an apartment that was so ridiculously upscale. That wasn't his world. Maybe he should move, but back to his town. The slow, boring life in the boonies would probably suit him better than this impossibly demanding city landscape. At least there, expectations were manageable. Mistakes weren't catastrophic.
While clearing out his bookshelf, Jay retrieved an envelope he normally stored hidden behind a row of textbooks. Ten thousand in two-hundred bills, placed neatly in an unassuming envelope. His emergency buffer.
He'd assembled it years ago, after his first job failed and he'd had to take a loan from his mother. The shame of that had burned. After he found a new job, he'd taken a part-time position for several months to repay her and to build a more robust emergency net. Having cash on hand made him feel safer. If something happened—if he lost everything again—at least he'd have this.
These days it wasn't as needed. His bank account was impressive. His job felt stable.
But that evening, as he held the solid envelope in his fingers, he felt precarious. The mistake he'd made probably wasn't severe enough to get him fired. But it wasn't his first mistake. It was a pattern.
The demands of the job were high. He'd always known that. His colleagues pushed through worse, but they were more qualified. More resilient. And he wasn't.
He wasn't sure he could endure much more of this, each one of his failures feeling so crushing. Ian had once told him that bad things happen and will keep happening. Ian was strong—he could withstand disasters. But Jay was increasingly convinced that he himself wasn't.
Maybe he should look for a lower-stakes position. Processing invoices, just like he used to. There had been none of this enormous burden, none of this stress. He was starting to wonder if any of it was worth it, if this misery was sustainable.
He placed the envelope on the table next to the apartment contract, wondering where he should pack it. Putting it in a box seemed too insecure, even though some of the items already packed were probably worth more. His designer outfits and three-piece suits were already tucked away, after all.
Sunday evening, he tidied up the apartment and threw away the trash bags, inspecting his handiwork. His place looked more or less ready to be vacated. All of his belongings stuck in several neat boxes, a life reduced to manageable containers.
But was he ready? He wasn't sure anymore. He felt that whatever future held for him, he probably wouldn't stay where he was. But where he'd go—that remained unclear.
He took a proper shower, the first real one since Thursday. The hot water felt good against his tense shoulders. He prepared his things for Monday. Suit pressed, shirt ironed, everything laid out and ready.
Tomorrow he would receive his sentence.
He lay in bed staring at the ceiling. The apartment was too quiet. The boxes loomed in the darkness like silent witnesses to his failure. Sleep came eventually, restless and thin.
Monday was gray and cold. Jay got dressed, ate breakfast without tasting it, and took the metro to work. His stomach was in knots the entire journey.
At the office, tension radiated from him like heat. He sat at his desk, trying to focus on his tasks, but his attention kept drifting to his email. Waiting. There was no news from IT until eleven. Then the message finally arrived, sent to Ian with the team copied.
"Table successfully restored from system logs. All historical data recovered."
Everyone breathed. The relief was palpable, spreading through the workspace. Kim sent Jay a thumbs up from across the room.
Jay felt as if a stone had fallen from his heart. His mistake was fixed. Thankfully, mercifully fixed.
But it didn't take away from the severity of his error. The crushing awareness that he'd made a mistake he'd been trained to avoid. That next time might not resolve so successfully. That the IT team had worked over the weekend to fix his stupidity.
Ian called him into his office at noon.
"The table is restored. We're back on track. Let's move forward."
"I'm sorry," Jay said again, because he couldn't help it.
"I know. But it's resolved now. Don't let it consume you."
Easy for Ian to say. Ian was strong enough to move past disasters.
That day, the whole team worked long hours again. The strategy season continued, relentless and demanding. Jay stayed until nine, throwing himself into assignments.
He returned home feeling marginally better. But only marginally.
The boxes in his apartment reminded him that his life was in flux, that everything felt unstable, that he wasn't sure he belonged anywhere anymore. He heated up leftovers, ate them quickly, showered, and went to bed early. Sleep came faster this time, exhaustion finally overcoming anxiety.
But his dreams were full of falling tables and disappearing data, of Ian's disappointed face and his own inadequacy laid bare for everyone to see.
Scene 4
Dread
The next day was Tuesday, so Jay left at five to go to the gym. Rus mercifully didn't comment on his absence last Saturday, but his eyes said enough.
Jay's form had slipped visibly. He used to occasionally work out at home, maintaining his strength between sessions. That had ceased completely in the past couple of weeks. Combined with his missed gym session and lack of sleep, he struggled with the exercises. Weights that had felt manageable before now seemed heavy. His movements were sloppy.
Rus watched him fail a set and shook his head. "You need another job. This one is going to kill you."
Jay didn't respond. Internally, he agreed. The strain was visible to the naked eye.
He returned home and at least cooked a decent dinner. Chicken and vegetables, nothing fancy but with proper nutrients. After eating, he decided to finish packing his documents.
He went to the coffee table where he'd left the money envelope. It wasn't there.
Jay stared at the empty surface for a moment, then looked around. Maybe he'd moved it. He checked the kitchen counter, the bookshelf, his desk. Nothing.
He looked under the table. In the boxes. Behind furniture. Nowhere.
He tried to remember. He'd taken it out of the bookshelf Saturday. Held it. Placed it on the table. And then... Sunday evening, he'd cleaned up. Tidied. Thrown away the pile of useless documents that had accumulated on the table.
Unless.
Cold sweat broke out across his back. Unless he'd mistakenly thrown it away with the rest of the pile. Just grabbed everything and dumped it in the trash without checking.
This was somehow even worse than the table incident. That had been a work mistake, serious but ultimately recoverable. This was personal. This was his safety net, his emergency buffer. Money he'd worked like a dog for at that dreadful weekend job at the gas station.
He'd hated every moment of those months. The rude customers, the unsafe neighbourhood, the harassment he'd endured. Standing behind bulletproof glass, getting yelled at by drunk people at two in the morning. All to build this buffer, to never have to borrow from his mother again.
The amount wasn't small. Over two months of his old salary. While he earned so much more now, it was still nothing to scoff at.
But it wasn't just about the money. It was about the sacrifice. About what that envelope represented—his determination to never be helpless again. And he'd thrown it away like garbage. Just like he'd dropped that table. Just like he kept making careless, stupid mistakes that proved he was fundamentally deficient.
Jay spent the entire evening looking. He checked every box twice. He moved furniture. He searched through drawers. But his apartment wasn't that big. If it wasn't there, it was gone.
The trash got collected Mondays. Even if he wanted to go search his trash bags, it was too late.
He sat down on the floor with his head in his hands and cried for a long time, allowing the misery to consume him. The sobs were ugly and harsh, his whole body shaking with them.
Eventually he dragged himself to the shower, still crying. The water mixed with tears. He cried himself to sleep for the second time in a week, and slept poorly, waking repeatedly from formless nightmares.
Wednesday morning, he went to work feeling more defeated than ever. The metro ride was dreary. His reflection in the dark windows looked haggard.
It was obvious to him now that he couldn't go on like this anymore. The apartment was a stupid idea. This work had been a mistake.
He was happy to have met Ian. Just weeks ago he’d stood beside him in a kitchen full of light and cinnamon. Now every word he’d said then sounded ridiculous—like someone pretending to be worthy of that warmth. He still treasured the memories they'd shared, but it all felt so distant, like it had happened in another life. To a different person who'd been braver and more capable. 
He couldn't stay here. Ian couldn't shield him from himself. He shouldn't need to. Someone like Jay would only drag Ian down.
The oppressive gloom of the winter weather made his spirit heavier. During a quieter moment, Jay stepped outside to make a phone call. The agent answered on the second ring.
"I need to cancel the apartment purchase. A sudden money situation emerged. I can't move forward right now."
The agent was quiet for a moment. "I see. That's unfortunate. If the situation improves, please call me. I'll keep the property on hold for you for now."
"Thank you."
Jay hung up and looked at his watch. The gift from Ian, beautiful and expensive. He didn't deserve it. He didn't deserve the apartment or this work or security. His place was at a gas station in a rough neighbourhood. At least there, no one expected anything from him. There was no one to disappoint.
Ian could see that Jay looked even worse today than yesterday. The concern was becoming difficult to ignore. He didn't want to be overbearing, but he also wanted to help. The last time Jay had made a mistake, his bad mood had lifted eventually. But this time seemed different. Worse, somehow.
Thursday wasn't better. Neither was Friday morning. At six on Friday evening, Ian finally decided to intervene. He called Jay to his office. Jay entered and stood near the door, not meeting Ian's eyes.
"Sit down, please," Ian said gently.
Jay sat.
"I wanted to check in with you. I know this period is difficult. The strain gets to everyone, but I've noticed you seem particularly affected. Is there anything I can do to help?"
Jay didn't speak for a long moment. Then he shook his head. Just once, a tiny motion that conveyed nothing.
"Jay, talk to me. What's going on?"
"I'm fine. Just tired."
"You don't look fine."
"I'll manage."
Ian tried a different approach. "Everyone makes mistakes. The table incident was resolved. It's not something you need to keep thinking about."
"I know."
But Jay's didn't believe it. His shoulders were hunched, his hands clasped tightly in his lap. He didn't look like someone who'd made a mistake at work. He looked like someone who'd given up on himself.
Ian felt helpless. "If you need time off—"
"I don't need time off. May I go back to my desk?"
"Of course. But my door is always open if you want to talk."
Jay nodded and left without another word.
Ian watched him go, worry knotting his stomach. Something was badly wrong, and he didn't know how to help. He didn't know how to reach Jay when he'd withdrawn so completely.
Jay sat at his desk and forced himself not to cry. Ian had to coddle him like a child, handle him carefully so he wouldn't fall apart. Some assistant he was. More like dead weight.
The office felt suffocating. Jay pulled up a spreadsheet and stared at it without seeing anything. 
Around him, his teammates worked. Ela was on a phone call. Kim typed rapidly. All of them competent. All of them managing the pressure without falling apart. All of them stronger than Jay would ever be. He didn't belong here. He'd known it from the beginning, really. The joke hire. And somehow he'd convinced himself otherwise, convinced himself he could do this. 
The day dragged on. Jay stayed until nine, same as everyone else. When he finally left, the relief of escaping was undercut by the knowledge that he'd have to come back Monday and do it all again.
He took the metro home, walked up to his apartment, and stood in the doorway looking at his packed boxes.
A life ready to be abandoned. The only question was where he'd go next.
Scene 5
Faith
Jay forced himself to go to the gym Saturday morning. He almost didn't. Leaving his apartment and facing other people was overwhelming. 
Rus took one look at him and frowned. "You look terrible."
"Thanks."
"I'm serious. When's the last time you slept properly?"
Jay couldn't remember. "I sleep fine."
"Right. You want to talk about it?"
"Nothing to talk about."
"Jay—"
"I'm fine. Let's just work out."
Jay's performance was worse than Tuesday. He struggled with weights that should have been easy. Halfway through, Rus stopped him.
"That's enough. You're going to hurt yourself."
"I can finish."
"No. You can't. Whatever's going on at that job, it's destroying you."
"I know."
"Do you? Because from where I'm standing, you're just letting it happen."
Jay wanted to argue but couldn't find the energy. Rus was right. He was letting it happen. Letting everything slip away—his health, his confidence, his safety nets. All of it sliding through his fingers like water.
"I'll figure it out," Jay said finally.
"When? After you collapse? After you end up in hospital?"
"It's not that bad."
"Isn't it?"
Jay left without answering.
The next two weeks were filled with self-pity. On the surface, he functioned. But anyone paying attention could see the difference.
Luc cornered him in the kitchen during a rare lunch break. "Hey. You doing okay?"
"Fine."
"You don't look fine."
"Just tired. We're all tired."
"Yeah, but... You seem different. Like something's really wrong."
"It's just the workload. I'll be fine once strategy season ends."
"Will you? Because it seems like more than that."
Jay smiled, a thin approximation of his normal cheerfulness. "I appreciate the concern, but really. I'm managing."
Luc didn't look convinced but let it drop.
At the gym, Rus was less diplomatic. "You're still wallowing."
"I'm not wallowing."
"You are. And it's painful to watch. Whatever happened, you need to either fix it or move on."
"It's not that simple."
"Staying like this isn't helping anyone, especially not yourself."
Jay knew Rus was right, but knowing didn't change anything. He felt trapped in amber—everything around him moving forward while he remained suspended, unable to break free.
Ian tried to intervene several times. Brief conversations in his office, diplomatically phrased questions about workload and wellbeing. But Jay deflected every attempt.
"I'm fine."
"Are you sure? You seem—"
"Just focused on work. There's a lot to do."
"If you need anything—"
"I know where to find you. Thank you."
Ian sat alone in his office late one evening after Jay had left, the lighter Jay gave him for Christmas resting in his palm. The engraved message mocked him: "You've got this."
Did he? Because it didn't feel like he had anything under control. Jay was slipping away, and Ian couldn't stop it.
They'd been so close already. Moments when Ian had felt truly connected to him. And now it felt like they'd taken five steps back—back to when Jay first started, when he'd learned about being a joke hire and closed himself off completely.
Ian turned the lighter over in his fingers. What could he do to solve this? Jay had seemed to have complete faith in him once. But now that faith seemed insufficient to keep him here.
The nightmare still haunted Ian. Jay bleeding out on marble floors, dying in his arms. And then there was the evening after the gala. The slip of his tongue, calling Jay beautiful. Had that been too much? A blunder that created an obstacle where there have been none? Ian replayed the moment constantly, trying to read Jay's reaction, but memory was unreliable and tainted by his own fear.
The obstacles kept multiplying. Work stress, mistakes, whatever personal crisis Jay was experiencing. And Ian felt powerless against all of it.
As days passed, the workload remained too heavy to really address anything properly. Ian worked fourteen-hour days at the office, then came home and worked more. Dusk till dawn, strategy documents and financial projections consuming every waking moment.
He couldn't abandon his duties to check on Jay, no matter how much he wanted to. He couldn't take an afternoon off to sit on Jay's small couch and drink and talk about what was actually wrong. The work demanded everything.
It was his fault the workload was this heavy. His decisions as CFO that led to this intensity, this pressure that crushed everyone beneath it. Jay was stressed and clearly taking the situation poorly, and Ian couldn't blame him. He could only blame himself.
Late Sunday night, Ian stood at his window looking out over the city. The lights stretched endlessly, thousands of windows with thousands of lives playing out behind them. Somewhere out there was Jay's apartment. Was Jay sleeping? Or was he awake like Ian, trapped in his own spiral?
Ian wanted to call. To text. To do something that bridged the distance between them. But what could he say? He'd already tried talking, offered help that Jay refused. Pushing harder would only drive Jay further away.
So Ian did nothing. Stood at his window and felt helpless, the lighter growing warm in his closed fist.
Ian went to the office on Monday early. Jay arrived exactly on time. They exchanged greetings, discussed the day's priorities and got consumed by work.
But it wasn't the same. Jay moved through the motions, and Ian watched him with growing dread.
Because this felt like the beginning of an ending. Like watching something precious slip through his fingers, unable to hold on no matter how tightly he tried to grasp it.
The office felt cold. The winter light through the windows was harsh and unforgiving. Strategy season would end eventually, but Ian was no longer certain what would remain when it did.
Jay worked at his desk, head down, focused on his screen. Ian could see him through the glass wall, close enough to call out to but impossibly distant.
And Ian didn't know how to cross that distance. All he could do was keep working, keep hoping, and watch as the person he cared about most slowly disappeared before his eyes.
Chapter 32: Alternatives
Scene 1
Job search
February was ending but brought no respite. At least a couple more weeks of insane workload continued ahead, with only a brief break before another season of quarterly reporting began. The cycle was relentless.
The weather continued to be wet, cold, and oppressive. Winter was dreadful this year. The city felt like it was drowning.
Among this drudgery, Jay decided to start looking for another job.
It was always awful for him, stressful and unpleasant. He'd spent years avoiding even the thought of job hunting. But he felt miserable and needed to at least think he was doing something to change it. Staying felt impossible. Continuing like this felt unsustainable.
He polished his CV on Sunday afternoon, sitting at his small table surrounded by packed boxes. Adding his current employment to his credentials looked surreal. Turner & Associates, Assistant to the CFO. The words sat there on the page, impressive and damning in equal measure. One year of experience in high-stakes investment management.
He was worried it would only bring the wrong kind of attention. People would wonder why he was leaving such a prestigious position. They'd suspect he'd been fired or had done something wrong. Or they'd assume he was reaching above his actual capabilities.
He sent the CVs all afternoon, working through a list of over fifty companies that looked like places he could manage. Accounting firms. Administrative positions. Jobs where mistakes weren't catastrophic.
Over the following week, he received zero responses. Not a single one.
He checked his email obsessively, refreshing during every break, hoping for something. But his inbox remained empty except for automated rejection messages from companies with well organised HR departments.
He guessed it was on par for someone like him, and it only made him more insecure. Of course no one would want him. His fragile, bruised ego got another blow.
Work continued its brutal pace. The team pushed through strategy revisions, endless meetings, document reviews that stretched into the night. Jay tried to act normal. Ian tried to engage him in conversation a few times, but Jay kept his responses brief. He didn't want Ian to see how much he was falling apart.
Saturday came and he went to the gym out of habit more than desire. Rus noticed immediately that something was off.
"You're distracted today."
"Just tired."
"You're always tired these days. What's going on?"
Jay hesitated, then decided there was no point in hiding it. "I resigned from buying the apartment. I'm looking for a new job. Something less stressful."
Rus's expression was complicated. "Oh. That's... are you sure?"
"I'm sure I can't keep doing this."
"I mean, I get it. Your work sounds unreasonable. And not buying the apartment makes sense if you're changing jobs, it would be hard to manage a place that posh on a regular salary. But you don't look happy about it. Or relieved."
"I'm not."
"Then why—"
"Because I'm not good enough. My coworkers manage the demands much better. It's just me who keeps making mistakes. Who keeps failing. Who keeps being toppled by the stress."
"That's not—"
"It is true. I dropped an entire database table. A basic, stupid mistake that anyone with proper training wouldn't make. I'm barely holding on while everyone else seems fine."
"They probably aren't as fine as they seem."
"Maybe. But they're managing better than I am."
"Come get drinks tonight. You need to talk this through properly."
"I can't. I need the rest when I can get it. I'm barely sleeping as it is."
"Alright. But if you change your mind, text me."
Jay continued sending his CV over the following days, browsing company listings with resignation. Most places didn't feel right. Some were too similar to his first job—small firms where he'd be vulnerable. Others were looking for someone more qualified than he was.
Finally, as March started, someone contacted him. A consulting firm looking for an administrative coordinator. The pay was roughly half of what he currently made. The responsibilities seemed manageable. They wanted to schedule an interview.
Jay stared at the email for a long time before responding to confirm his availability.
He knew an interview probably meant nothing. He'd done plenty of interviews that landed him nowhere in the past. Getting called in didn't mean getting hired. Still, it was a step somewhere. Progress, maybe, or at least motion.
He looked through the glass wall at Ian, who was hard at work reviewing documents. His posture was perfect, his focus absolute. Jay felt something twist painfully in his chest.
He'd have to leave this place. Never see Ian again.
The thought made him miserable instead of bringing relief. His eyes burned with unshed tears that he blinked back furiously.
Ian was the best thing that ever happened to him. The kindest person he'd ever known. The only person who'd ever made him feel valued. He would never meet someone like him again.
Leaving his side felt unbearably crushing.
Jay turned back to his computer, trying to focus on the spreadsheet in front of him. He took a deep breath, then another, willing himself to hold it together.
Just a few more weeks of strategy season. The interview. Then maybe an offer, if he was lucky. Then a resignation letter. Then packing up his desk and walking away from this office, from this team, from Ian.
And that would be it. The best year of his life, reduced to a line on his CV that no one would believe anyway.
He worked until late that evening, staying long after most of the team had gone home. Ian was still in his office when Jay finally packed up at ten-thirty. Their eyes met briefly through the glass, and Ian raised a hand in farewell.
Jay waved back, then gathered his things and left.
He had an interview on Thursday. He needed to prepare, to figure out what to say when they asked why he was leaving such a prestigious position. He couldn't tell them the truth—that he wasn't good enough, that he kept making mistakes, that the stress was eating him alive. That he'd rather earn half the salary and live in his cramped studio forever than continue feeling like he was inadequate.
He'd have to come up with something. Career development. Seeking new challenges. Better alignment with his long-term goals. All lies, but acceptable ones.
Jay arrived at his apartment and looked at the boxes still stacked against the walls. He'd have to unpack them eventually. This studio would be his home for the foreseeable future. No river view, no clever storage, no balcony for morning coffee.
Just this small room with its narrow bed and tiny kitchen and the constant feeling that his life had gotten too big for the space and then shrunk back down to fit.
The apartment contract still sat on the table. He should probably throw it away. Or send an email to the agent officially declining. But he couldn't bring himself to do it yet. That would make it final.
Scene 2
Requesting a day off
The next day at work, HR sent an email reminding everyone about the upcoming retreat. Jay stared at the subject line. Apparently this was already the third email sent about this.
He remembered seeing the previous ones and filing them away without even looking at them. There had been no time to spare, and planning months ahead felt impossible when each day was a struggle to survive.
Now he actually read it.
The email was enthusiastic, full of exclamation points and glowing reviews of the venue. This year's retreat would be at a lakeside resort six hours south by bus, in a mountain region, scheduled for the end of May. The photos showed an upscale and opulent facility that still managed to look rustic, matching its natural setting. Crystal clear lakes, amazing hiking trails, brilliant vistas. The HR department promised varied and exciting entertainment and activities.
Jay smiled bitterly. It was unlikely he'd even still be there. Maybe he'd be gone altogether, or would have already handed in his resignation. There was no reason to think about it, to imagine himself at some resort months from now when he couldn't envision making it through the next week.
Yet he still read the entire email. He scrolled through all the photos and examined the list of planned activities.
The area looked beautiful. Peaceful. A place that existed in a different world from the gray, rain-soaked city pressing down on him.
He bet Zoe would be overjoyed. Luc would probably grumble about the long bus ride.
The workload that day was manageable enough that his colleagues actually discussed the retreat. Jay listened to their conversations with detachement, like observing people through glass.
"I'm disappointed about the room arrangements. No sharing this year. Everyone gets singles," Kim said to Ela.
Ela's response was too quiet to hear, but both of them smiled conspiratorially, which made Jay certain that last year's retreat was when they'd started hooking up. Or maybe they'd already been involved before. Either way, the shared room had clearly been a highlight.
"Finally, entertainment I'll actually enjoy. A fashion show! Thank god. Last year's activities were too artsy," Amy declared, reviewing the email on her screen. 
"I'm excited about it too. We should start thinking about outfits now," Ela said. 
"Absolutely. I'm thinking something elegant but comfortable. Resort chic."
They were already deep in discussion about color palettes and accessories.
Zoe was predictably excited about the hiking trails. "Look at this elevation map. Some of these routes go up to twelve hundred meters. The views will be incredible."
Kim was pumped about the oldies-themed disco. "I'm going to dance to songs from before I was born. It's going to be amazing."
Luc was surprisingly enthusiastic about horse archery. "This is brilliant. I've always wanted to try this. It's like something from a historical movie."
Jay honestly laughed at that. It was probably the most unorthodox of the activities offered. Of course Luc would love it.
Luc turned to him. "You should join me. How often do you get to shoot arrows from horseback?"
"Never, and I'm keeping it that way. I'm not riding horses."
"Come on. It'll be fun."
"No. Categorically no."
Max was excited about the boats. "There's kayaking and canoeing. I haven't been on the water in years. This will be wonderful."
It seemed everyone found something they were looking forward to. Even in the middle of strategy season's misery, the prospect of the retreat gave them something bright on the horizon.
Luc approached Jay. "You seem weird about this."
"I'm fine."
"We'll talk about it properly in two weeks, after things calm down. But you're coming, right? It's absolutely mandatory."
Jay just smiled and didn't answer.
He remembered the previous resort. The single most pleasant, exciting, and memorable trip he'd ever been on. The party with the amazing view where he'd actually relaxed and enjoyed himself for once. Slow mornings with Ian in their shared room, drinking coffee on the balcony. The diving lessons, the bonfires, the art show. All of it had been new, enchanting, magical.
He'd felt like he belonged there, like he was part of something special. Like he deserved to be in that beautiful place with these talented people.
He was sure this year's retreat would be similarly successful. His colleagues would have a blast. The resort looked incredible. They'd create new memories, strengthen their bonds, return refreshed and energized.
But he wouldn't be there.
Jay had other things to handle, to worry about. Practical matters that couldn't wait for some mythical future when everything would be better.
He went to Ian's office mid-afternoon and knocked on the glass.
Ian looked up immediately. "Come in."
"I need to request a day off. Thursday."
"Of course. Do you need anything? Company car rental, perhaps? If you're traveling somewhere—"
"No. Thank you. I'll manage."
"Are you sure?"
"Yes."
Ian clearly wanted to ask more, to understand what was happening. Jay felt the weight of that attention, the care behind it. Finally, Ian nodded.
"You have the day. If there's anything you need, anything at all, I'll do my best to provide it."
"I appreciate that."
Jay returned to his desk feeling sad. Ian was such an amazing man, such a great boss. In every aspect. Thoughtful, supportive, generous. He cared about his team, about their wellbeing. When Jay was clearly struggling, Ian was trying to help.
He was wasted on someone as pathetic as Jay.
Leaving was for the best. Ian deserved to only work with the most distinguished professionals. People who could match his excellence, who wouldn't make stupid mistakes or lose emergency funds or drop database tables.
There was a time when Jay had thought he was good enough to stay by Ian's side, to support him. Now the mere idea seemed ridiculous. 
He wasn't even good enough to safeguard his own savings. How could he possibly be of aid to Ian? How could he help him in any way?
He was only a burden. And the kindest thing he could do was remove that burden before he caused any more damage.
Jay pulled up his work queue and focused on the tasks in front of him. Practical things. Manageable things. One item at a time until the day ended and he could go home.
For now, he had Thursday. Whatever happened then would determine what came next.
Scene 3
The interview
Jay had the whole day off on Thursday and the interview was at two in the afternoon. He wasn't nervous, more resigned than anything. This was an act of desperation more than anything else. Whether he got the job or not felt irrelevant. 
He spent the morning reading about the company. It couldn't hurt to be prepared. It was a mid-sized firm specializing in financial services for small to medium businesses. A place that had existed for twenty years and would probably exist for twenty more without ever making headlines.
He wore one of his regular office suits, a navy two-piece that fit him perfectly. It was by any standards a presentable outfit, well-tailored and appropriate. He took his watch off and placed it on the table. The watch was too expensive, too distinctive. He didn't want to invite scrutiny. His wrist without it felt too light, like something important was missing.
He didn't take his leather bag, either. It seemed somehow wrong to carry equipment provided by his current employer to a job interview. 
He arrived comfortably before the appointed time and walked around the area. At least it wasn't raining, even if it was cold and gloomy. The building complex was in the business district, filled with offices of dozens of middle-class companies. Nothing special, nothing terrible. It felt comfortable in its ordinariness.
He entered the company with fifteen minutes to spare and got seated in a corridor with a couple of other candidates. The reception was plain—white walls, standard furniture, a few potted plants.
Jay immediately realized he looked overdressed. His suit fit too well, while the other men wore department store clothes. Nothing wrong with them, really. But Jay stood out. He felt slightly awkward, but hardly intimidated. That surprised him. A year ago, this situation would have sent his anxiety through the roof.
Another candidate left the office, looking flustered. He said his farewells clumsily and departed hurriedly, clutching his bag.
Jay waited for his turn. He'd read about the company extensively beforehand, learned about the market, the specifications, their recent projects. It was as good a place as any, if unremarkable.
When his turn came, he was led into a conference room where three people sat behind a table. The hiring panel. Two men and a woman, all dressed in business casual. They smiled as he entered.
"Mr. Hall, thank you for coming. Please, have a seat."
Jay sat and tried to judge the company culture from their appearance and behaviour. They looked normal. Friendly but not overly warm.
"Would you like some water?"
"Yes, thank you."
At least that seemed acceptable. Basic courtesy was being observed.
They asked him general questions about his work style, his strengths, his approach to problem-solving. He answered truthfully but without elaboration. When they moved to his employment history, he outlined his previous positions clearly, without mentioning the reasons why he'd quit any of them.
"And your current position at Turner & Associates? That's quite prestigious. What makes you interested in a change?"
"The work is ambitious and definitely a challenge I've enjoyed. But ultimately I'd like to advance my career in a different direction."
It was a blatant lie, but he delivered it confidently. What else was there to do?
"And why did you choose our company?"
Jay recited a dozen reasons about why he was interested in this particular market niche, their growth trajectory, their reputation in the field. All lies, naturally. He'd chosen this company because it was hiring for a position vaguely aligned with his skills. That was the extent of his interest.
He was surprised by how easily he lied. How relaxed he felt about all of this. Perhaps because he didn't care about the outcome.
The woman asked about his technical skills. He listed them systematically—database management, financial reporting, scheduling systems, correspondence protocols. All things he could do in his sleep now.
One of the men asked, "Here's a hypothetical. How would you sell an elephant?"
Jay responded with a dry joke. "I'd find an expedient butcher and market the meat by weight." 
The panel laughed. He felt slight distaste. At Turner & Associates, no one would ask trick questions. On his interview with Leo back then, he'd been offered proper refreshments. The panel had been polite and friendly despite him being a nobody and Leo being practically the company owner. The questions had been exclusively substantive—about his skills, his work style, his career goals.
The difference in standards was noticeable.
They asked about his salary expectations. He named a figure that was half what he currently made but still reasonable for the position. They seemed pleased.
He knew he was making a good impression without really trying. Months of experience at the highest level had seasoned him. The nervousness that had plagued him at his first interview was completely absent.
The interview concluded after fifteen minutes. They shook his hand, said they'd be in touch within a week, and thanked him for his time.
When he finally left, he didn't feel any particular way about the people or the position. They were well-mannered enough, no obvious red flags. The work would be manageable, the environment seemed stable. It was more at his level. Mediocre employees of a mediocre institution. People like him.
He took the metro home and let himself into his apartment. The boxes still lined the walls. The contract for the apartment he'd never buy still sat on the table.
Jay laid down on the bed and thought about what his new life might look like. Fifteen-minute commute to the business district. Sad sandwiches in a brown bag for lunch. Spending his time off in local parks instead of on tropical diving destinations. Just as he used to.
He didn't feel sad about any of it. He didn't need the luxury, the excitement. Those things had never really been for him anyway.
But then he thought about never seeing Ian again.
Never having their evening conversations over coffee and pastries. Never watching him work through the glass wall. Never hearing his practical observations. Never seeing him smile when something amused him.
Jay rolled onto his side in a fetal position and began crying.
He could survive without the money and the status and the beautiful things. He'd survived without them for most of his life. He could live without the diving trips and the company retreats. He could live without feeling like he belonged somewhere important.
But having to live and never see Ian again felt devastating. Like someone was slowly carving his heart from his chest with a dull knife.
He cried until his throat hurt and his eyes burned. The apartment grew dark around him as evening came, but he didn't get up to turn on the lights. He just lay there, curled around the ache in his chest, mourning something he'd never really had in the first place.
Eventually the tears stopped, leaving him exhausted. He stared at the wall in the darkness and tried to imagine his future. That mediocre office, those polite but unremarkable colleagues, that stable and soul-crushing life. He tried to convince himself he could do this. He wasn't sure he believed it.
Scene 4
Foolish feelings
Jay went to work Friday looking visibly depressed. His eyes were red and puffy, evidence of crying that no amount of cold water could completely disguise. The whole team was staying late that evening, pushing through the final stretch before next week's deadline. But at five o'clock, Jay packed up his things.
Luc looked up, surprised. "Leaving already?"
"Yes."
"You okay?"
"Fine. Just tired."
He left without further explanation, feeling everyone's eyes on him as he walked to the elevator.
The next week would be the final push before they needed to finalize the new strategy. Everyone would work all weekend except Jay and Kim, since neither of them actually did the strategy work itself. Their support tasks could wait until Monday.
Jay should have felt relieved about the weekend off. Instead, he felt crushed. Because the interview had actually gone well. There was a good chance he'd get the job. And that would mean leaving Ian.
He was starting to think that whatever pain and anxiety he felt due to his job, none of it was even close to the pain of having to leave Ian. When had he become so attached? When had Ian become so central to his life, like oxygen?
He was confused. Mostly he was miserable. He didn't know anymore what he should do.
He knew he liked Ian. Respected him, admired him even. But it seemed that it was way more than that. Way deeper.
Jay returned to his apartment that afternoon but couldn't stand to sit there surrounded by his packed boxes. He needed to be around people.
He went to Café Lemon and was glad to find Fay there. It was quite busy since it was Friday afternoon, but he sat at a table and ordered coffee. Then another coffee. Then a third.
The afternoon turned into evening. The café gradually emptied as people went home for their weekends. Fay came over to refill his cup yet again.
"You've been sitting here for hours. Something on your mind?"
"Can I ask your advice about something?"
"Of course." She sat on the seat across from him. "What's going on?"
Jay tried to formulate his thoughts. "The work is great but very stressful. I decided to look for a new position and give up the apartment because I couldn't cope with the demands. But leaving means I'll never get to see a particular coworker anymore, and that seems extremely sad."
"A particular coworker?"
"Yes."
"What's special about this coworker?"
Jay smiled despite his misery. "They're the best person I've ever met. Incredibly talented and dedicated. They've been through so much but never let it break them. Strong but not cruel. Principled. They've been so kind to me, so supportive. They defend me when others won't. They make me want to be better just by being around them."
"Oh, honey. You're head over heels in love."
Jay blushed madly. "That's ridiculous. They're... unavailable. Taken."
"Ah." Fay's expression turned sympathetic. "Unrequited love is the worst. Maybe leaving really is best, then. It'll hurt like hell for a while, maybe even for months, but staying would just prolong the pain, and you'll eventually move on."
"I don't want to move on."
"That's the problem with love. We rarely want what's good for us.""
"Right. That makes sense."
"Does it help?"
"Not really."
Fay squeezed his hand. "I'm sorry. That's a terrible position to be in."
Jay returned home feeling worse than when he'd left. It was silly. He wasn't in love with Ian. He admired Ian, but Ian was someone he couldn't harbour such feelings about.
Ian was someone who was absolutely out of his reach for more than one reason. Ian had been betrayed before by someone in exactly Jay's position, had his heart broken and his reputation damaged. Ian would never risk that again.
Plus, Jay would never have romantic feelings for a supervisor. That was foolish. Inappropriate.
Except the feelings were there regardless of what was wise or appropriate.
Jay sat on his bed and tried to reason with himself. This was just attachment born of proximity and stress. Ian had been kind to him during difficult times. That naturally created strong positive feelings. It didn't mean anything deeper.
But he couldn't convince himself. The thought of never seeing Ian again felt like losing a limb. Something essential, something he'd built his life around without realizing it.
He didn't know what to do with that realization.
Meanwhile, Ian sat in his office long after everyone else had left, pretending to work on strategy documents while his mind circled the same problem repeatedly.
He strongly suspected Jay had gone to a job interview. It made sense. According to his estimates, it was ten percent chance Jay needed a rest day, ten percent it was a medical visit or family emergency, eighty percent he'd had a job interview.
All the signs pointed to that. When a stressed employee requested a day off with short notice, it meant one thing. Plus the long hours, the unreasonable pressure that resulted in mistakes, the constant draining social demands. The way Ian had probably made him uncomfortable with that comment after the gala. Jay's recent withdrawal.
His instincts were correct and Jay was pulling back. The work environment had turned hostile, and Jay was building an exit strategy.
So the fact that he'd come back from his day off looking defeated was actually a good sign. Naturally it pained Ian to see him suffer, but at the same time, if the interview had gone poorly, it bought time to remedy at least some of the problems.
One more week. One more week of the intense strategy work, and they'd have some breathing room. Then he'd be able to take corrective action.
They hadn't done yearly reviews because there simply hadn't been time. But Ian would make sure Jay felt valued. A raise, more favourable conditions, something that would make him feel more secure. A company car, whatever Jay needed. Maybe a promotion if he wanted it, or a transfer to another department if working directly under Ian was too difficult or if he wanted to advance his career in a different direction.
Jay didn't need to change companies. Everything could be provided here. Turner & Associates was large enough to accommodate different career paths, different working arrangements.
It didn't matter what solution they found, as long as Jay stayed in his orbit.
Ian would make sure to be as respectful and unthreatening as possible, to remedy whatever discomfort Jay was feeling. No overbearing gestures. Everything within the limits of their respective positions.
He’d been too forward. That was the problem. The Christmas celebration, the trips, the comment about Jay looking beautiful, the way he'd monopolized Jay's time during those late evening conversations. He'd overstepped, and Jay had rightly pulled back.
Ian would fix it. He could maintain distance if that's what Jay needed.
Ian should have focused on finalizing the strategy. The documents in front of him required his attention. Decisions needed to be made, projections verified, presentations prepared.
But his mind kept drifting to all the methods he would need to use to make Jay stay.
More autonomy, less direct pressure. Flexible hours. Additional vacation days. Compensation packages. Career advancement opportunities. Whatever it took.
The thought of Jay leaving was intolerable. Ian had lost too much already. He couldn't lose this too.
Ian opened a document on his computer and started drafting notes. Strategies for retention. Approaches to take during Jay's review. Benefits to offer, both tangible and intangible. This was what he was good at—strategy, planning, finding solutions to impossible problems.
He would make this work. He'd saved himself from ruin once. He could save this too.
Scene 5
Definitely not love
Saturday at the gym, Jay was barely through his warm-up when he stopped mid-rep and blurted, “Rus, what does it feel like to be in love?”
The question made Rus laugh so loudly that two people on the treadmills turned to stare. When he finally caught his breath he said, "Where did that come from?"
"Just answer the question."
Rus wiped his eyes, still grinning. "You can't stop thinking about the person. You can't imagine your life without them. Everything reminds you of them. Why?"
Jay went quiet.
"Wait. Is this about that secret person you used to go on dates with? The one who was out of your reach?"
Jay nodded slowly. "They weren't dates. And they're taken. But yeah. It's that person. I can't imagine my life without them. Does this necessarily have to mean I'm in love? Can't I just be deeply impressed by them?"
Rus couldn't stop laughing. He actually had to sit down on a nearby bench, shoulders shaking. "Oh my god. Have you been moping all this time because you're in love?"
"No. This can't be true. I knew from day one that person was unavailable. I wouldn't fall in love with them."
"Love is a cruel mistress. She doesn't listen to our wishes."
"That's not helpful."
Rus caught his breath and looked at Jay seriously. "Alright. Here's what we're going to do. Tonight, men's night out. I'll invite Mic and Van. We'll go to a pub. Nice and cosy, no survival games in the cold. Just drinks and love advice. What do you say?"
Jay agreed. He felt like he needed some perspective. He didn't want to sit in his barren apartment anyway.
They met that evening at eight at a designated pub, warm and dimly lit, with wooden booths and the smell of fried food. Mic and Van were already there, waving them over. They ordered beers. Jay made absolutely sure never to admit it was Ian. Rus would disapprove immediately since Ian was his boss. Instead, he kept it vague.
"So there's this coworker. An amazing person. A great mentor who gave me so much and made even the hardest moments bearable. I'm afraid of disappointing them and I feel unworthy to stand by their side. Also they're taken."
"Go for it. To hell if they're taken or not. Love is worth some transgressions," Mic said.
"That's terrible advice," Van countered immediately. "You should focus on fitness and work the love out of your system by sweating regularly. Ignore the person, continue the work, and focus all your free time on gains."
"Also terrible advice. You should find a new job and get some distance," Rus said.
"But do you think I'm actually in love?" Jay asked.
All three men answered simultaneously: "Yes."
"No one mopes for weeks and whines in bars over beers unless they're in love," Mic added.
Jay slumped in his seat. That didn't help at all.
They drank for a couple more hours, each man sharing their love stories. Some were quite comical. Mic was clearly a player, recounting elaborate schemes to impress various women, most of which had backfired spectacularly.
Van's stories mostly involved getting dumped for not spending enough time with his girlfriends. "She wanted flowers, outings. Who has time for it? I spend six days a week at the gym."
"I spend six days a week at work," Jay said.
Rus scoffed. "That's no way to live."
"Why not?" Van countered. "I understand the grind. As long as it's fulfilling, who cares?"
"I was actually married once," Rus admitted. "Divorced still in my twenties. We were military spouses. That often ends poorly. Dated a couple times afterwards, but it always fizzled out."
"So we're all four hopeless bachelors with obviously poor luck in love," Mic said, raising his glass. "Cheers to that."
They clinked glasses.
"My college girlfriend dumped me for not being adventurous enough," Jay said.
Van nodded solemnly. "Women are unreasonable."
"Or maybe we're just terrible at relationships," Rus added.
"Equally possible," Mic agreed.
Jay laughed, feeling slightly lighter.
"What do you guys do when you fail badly at something?" Jay asked after another round. "I cope poorly."
The men were all quite drunk by this point. Van said immediately, "I never fail."
Rus rolled his eyes. "When I got divorced, I moped for months. But finally life goes on, you know? You just keep putting one foot in front of the other."
Mic shrugged. "I fail about three times a week. It used to bother me, but now I'm used to it. You just need more practice at failing."
"That's actually profound," Jay said.
"Don't let it go to my head."
They kept drinking and complaining about various ailments—sore muscles, jobs, the price of rent, the quality of beer these days. Eventually Jay called it a night around midnight. All three insisted on walking him home. When they reached his building, Jay somehow ended up inviting them in.
They saw the sorry state of his apartment—boxes everywhere, bare shelves, the atmosphere of abandonment—and were suddenly more understanding.
"This is depressing," Mic said.
"No wonder you've been moping," Van added.
"It's no way to live," Rus concluded.
Jay produced the bottle of whiskey still left from his Christmas company basket. They were very impressed.
"This is good stuff," Mic said reverently.
So they stayed to drink more. Someone started singing. Then they were all singing, terrible drunken renditions of old songs, laughing and stumbling over lyrics.
At one in the morning, there was a polite knock on the door.
Jay opened it to find a weary police officer. "We've received a noise complaint. Will you keep it down?"
"Yes. Absolutely. I'm so sorry."
"Have a good night."
The door closed, and everyone scattered shortly after. Jay stood in his apartment, now utterly silent except for the ringing in his ears. He'd never been more humiliated in his life. He stumbled to bed without cleaning up, fell asleep immediately, and woke the next morning with a pounding headache.
He groaned heavily. These days, whenever he woke up hungover, there was Ian around with water and painkillers. This time he was alone and had to find water on his own. He was glad he'd left part of the medicine cabinet unpacked in the bathroom together with basic toiletries. He swallowed the pill and drank three glasses of water.
It was much more bearable when Ian had been around to comfort him. Ian always knew exactly what to do—the perfect balance of care and support.
Jay sighed and thought about the conversations from the night before. His friends were wrong. They didn't know the circumstances and he'd told the story poorly, so they misunderstood and misjudged.
He wasn't in love with Ian.
But that didn't change the fact that he didn't want to live without him.
The longer he thought about it, the more convinced he became that he couldn't leave. Staying would be hard and there would be moments when he'd suffer. He'd just take Mic's advice and get used to failures. It would surely get easier with time.
If that was the price for staying with Ian, he'd pay it.
He wasn't worthy to stay at Ian's side, but he still wanted to stay. He'd struggle and get hurt, but he'd still do his best to endure for as long as he was able. He'd find a way to cope, to manage, to become strong enough to deserve his place.
He didn't have to be perfect right away. He just had to stop being so obviously fragile that everyone noticed. He could do that. He would do that.
Chapter 33: What's worth fighting for
Scene 1
Resolve
Monday morning Jay went to work and his resolve was immediately tested.
He'd decided to stay. But standing outside the office building, watching the early light reflect off the glass, he still had doubts.
He arrived at eight to find half the team already there. Some looked like they hadn't left since Sunday. The air smelled stale—old coffee, takeout containers, and unwashed bodies. Luc was inside a sleeping bag in the corner, growling something unintelligible.
The scene was disheartening.
"Morning," Jay said weakly.
Luc made a sound that might have been a greeting or possibly a threat. He extracted himself from the sleeping bag with the grace of a dying animal and stumbled toward the coffee machine.
This was what Jay was signing up for by staying. Unreasonable working hours and incredible stress. People literally living at their desks, sacrificing everything for deadlines that never seemed to ease.
He battled with himself the whole day, stealing glances at Ian's office. Even Ian looked poorly. He was wearing a shirt with a missing button, his tie and jacket gone, hair ruffled. He'd clearly stayed in the office all weekend.
When their eyes met, Ian's expression was complicated. He sent a smile that was supposed to be reassuring but looked more like a grimace of misery.
Jay felt awkward being the only well-rested person in the office. But he quickly started working and the day moved forward. He stayed until ten that evening, shuffling materials, refilling everyone's coffees, delivering snacks. More moral support than anything else.
Tuesday wasn't much better. Jay had seen long days and weekend work before, but never had people literally lived in the office for days on end. The situation felt unsustainable, like watching a building collapse in slow motion.
Around noon, his phone rang. Jay stepped into a free conference room and answered.
"Mr. Hall? This is Meg from Accord Financial Services. We'd like to invite you for a second interview with our senior management."
Jay was reluctant. But he looked through the conference room glass at the disaster unfolding in the main office and heard himself say, "When would work for you?"
"Thursday afternoon? Say, four-thirty?"
"I'll be there."
He went to Ian as soon as he found an opportunity. "I need to leave at four on Thursday."
Ian's brows furrowed. He nodded without asking why, then returned to the document in front of him.
The phone call. The early leave. It all pointed to another interview. Or perhaps Jay had scored a second interview from whatever first interview he'd attended last week.
Why wouldn't he? Jay was competent, presentable. A candidate any reasonable company would want.
Ian gritted his teeth, feeling the ground leave beneath his feet. He'd hoped he had more time. But what if Jay got the job? What if Ian wouldn't even have the chance to set things right, would just watch as everything crumbled?
When Jay left for the gym at five that day, Ian looked up. Their eyes met through the glass wall. Jay could swear Ian was on the verge of tears—an expression of such raw pain that it stopped Jay in his tracks.
He'd known this was a bad time. He hadn't expected things were quite so serious.
Jay went to the gym with a heavy heart.
Rus was cheerful. "What are you still moping about?"
"I'm not moping. Just thinking things through thoroughly."
"And?"
"I think I'll stay. But I'm scared. Things are not good at work right now. Luc has been in a sleeping bag on the floor for five days without leaving the office at all."
"That's worse than my worst memories from the military. Maybe I misjudged how miserable my time there was. Perhaps the real struggle happens in finance."
"It was never so bad before. But we acquired a new very prestigious and demanding client recently. The workload increased while the team stayed the same size. Hence the constant drama at crucial moments."
"That's just bad management."
Jay immediately got defensive. "It's not. It's just that the work is difficult."
"If people are sleeping on the floor for days, that's bad management."
"You don't understand the industry."
Rus raised his hands. "Alright, alright. I won't criticize your beloved workplace."
Jay returned home and thought some more. He pulled out the apartment contract again, checking the bank conditions for the mortgage. If he decided to stay, he'd take the apartment. He'd move. Staying in his studio filled with boxes was only adding to his depression.
One way or another, he'd have to make a decision soon.
For now, he'd go for the interview on Thursday. He'd go straight from work and see how it went, how it felt. He wasn't sure what he'd do if he got the offer. But he'd have to actually get the offer before he could make any plans.
Maybe the decision would be taken out of his hands. If he didn't get the job, he'd consider it the will of the universe and stay. If he did get the job—then he'd have to decide on his own what to do.
Wednesday Jay stayed late again, almost until midnight. Half the team now lived in the office permanently. Only Kim, Max, and Amy left at reasonable hours. Kim because he didn't really need to stay. Max and Amy because they wanted to at least see their children every now and then.
Luc, Zoe, and Ela had essentially moved in. And so had Ian.
Someone had brought Ian fresh clothes, but he still didn't look well. Jay discreetly refilled his beverages and made sure he actually ate, trying to be unobtrusive about it.
Ian looked at him with an expression so pained, it was as if he was being tortured.
Jay approached during a quiet moment and whispered, "You've got this."
But somehow this seemed to have the opposite effect. Ian's face crumpled for just a moment before he composed himself.
"Excuse me," Ian said, standing abruptly and heading toward the bathroom.
Jay stood there holding the coffee, hoping he hadn't made some blunder. Seeing Ian like this was hard. This job was hard and he was weak and scared.
But he still wanted to stay by Ian's side.
He left the coffee on Ian's desk and returned to his own work. Through the glass wall, he could see the bathroom door remained closed for a long time.
When Ian finally emerged, his face was composed again, but his eyes were red.
Jay returned to his desk and tried to focus on work. Around him, the office was in chaos. Zoe typed furiously, eyes red from staring at her screen too long. Luc was in his sleeping bag, eating something from a takeout container while reviewing documents.
This was the reality of staying. Not the glamorous parts—the international trips, the Christmas celebrations, the evenings with Ian. But this: the grinding, exhausting work that demanded everything.
Jay wasn't sure he was strong enough for it.
But the alternative—leaving, never seeing Ian again—felt worse than any amount of stress or fear or exhaustion. He'd find a way to be strong enough. He had to.
Jay stayed until midnight, then took the metro home. He fell into bed fully clothed, too tired to shower.
Scene 2
Business district
Jay came to work Thursday morning as if nothing changed.
The deadline for strategy submission was tomorrow. Only finishing touches were being applied now, last checks made. Everyone was working on fumes, already feeling the finish line.
Luc had finally showered. Ela had put on fresh makeup. Zoe looked almost human again.
Ian was still in his office, bent over his laptop, but the tension in his shoulders had eased slightly. The worst was almost over.
The atmosphere was strained but different from previous weeks. There was light at the end of the tunnel. Exhaustion was mixed with relief.
At four, Jay packed his bag and left.
He took a taxi to the business district. It wasn't a long drive. He arrived with ten minutes to spare for his four-thirty interview.
He was wearing his regular office suit, his watch, his leather bag. He hadn't bothered to try and present an image anymore. If they had a problem with his appearance, that would tell him something important about whether he wanted to work there.
He surveyed the building and the area critically. It wouldn't be a bad place to work. Better than his previous workplace, at least. That building had been dingy, filled with a persistent smell of old coffee. This building was modern, clean. Not impressive, but solid.
The interview started punctually.
There was no one else in the lobby this time. Jay was led directly to a conference room where three people waited.
"Mr. Hall, thank you for coming. I'm Dan Miller, senior partner. This is Liu Serrano, operations director, and Mos Parker, head of client services."
"Pleased to meet you," Jay said.
They shook hands and sat down.
"We were very impressed with your first interview. Meg spoke highly of you."
"Thank you."
"So, tell us a bit about your current position. What does a typical day look like for you?"
Jay described his work. The reports he prepared. The meetings he attended. The clients he occasionally interfaced with.
They listened attentively, asking follow-up questions here and there.
Liu laughed at one point. "Sounds like you're doing the work of three people."
"It can feel that way sometimes," Jay admitted.
"That's finance for you. Always demanding the impossible and then acting surprised when people burn out," Dan said.
"We try to maintain better customs here. Work-life balance, all that. Not always successful, but we do try," Mos added.
"That sounds nice," Jay said.
"Have you been in the industry long?" Sandra asked.
"A little over a year at my current position. Before that, I worked at smaller firms."
"And what made you consider leaving your current role?"
"I wanted to explore other opportunities. Turner & Associates focuses primarily on high-level investment strategy. I'd like to work more directly with operational finance."
It wasn't a lie. It just wasn't the whole truth.
They moved on to salary negotiations. The number they offered was reasonable. Not as high as what he made now, but livable. Comfortable, even.
"When could you start, assuming we move forward?" Dan asked.
"I'd need two weeks. That's what my contract states."
"Perfectly reasonable."
The conversation drifted into chitchat after that. They talked about the realities and challenges of finance. They joked about the impossible clients, the regulatory changes, the eternal struggle to balance accuracy with deadlines.
Jay was relaxed. He'd attended so many meetings and negotiations already that this didn't faze him. He didn't try to make a good impression, but he also didn't try to sabotage himself. He acted as natural as he could, given the circumstances.
The entire meeting lasted half an hour, and most of it was spent on small talk. Jay suspected they were mostly trying to get a feel of him as a person, and not check his qualifications.
"Well, Mr. Hall, thank you for your time. We'll be in touch soon."
"Thank you for the opportunity."
They shook hands again, and Jay left.
He departed in a complicated mood.
He couldn't say how the interview went. It had felt easy, almost casual. Maybe too casual. Maybe that was good. He didn't know.
But with each passing day, he was more and more convinced that he wanted to stay.
The sting of his failures had begun to fade. He looked back at himself falling apart with shame. But at the same time, he understood that just because he was feeling better now meant nothing. He was sure to fail again, to feel that overwhelming misery again. That was what he was so poorly equipped to endure. He'd crash again, maybe harder next time, until something broke irreparably.
He walked around the neighborhood for a bit.
He rarely visited the business district. His previous jobs had been located elsewhere. It was modern, with functional buildings that were neither inspiring nor offensive. There were no impressive skyscrapers like the ones he was used to, but the area was well taken care of. Good public transport access. A decent place.
He found a restaurant for dinner. It was filled with local employees, tired people in department store suits who talked animatedly, mostly complaining about their work.
Such a stark image compared to the financial district's lively crowds discussing deals and international clients.
Jay ordered a meal and ate, watching the people around him. They looked content. No one was on the verge of collapse. No one looked like they'd been living in the office for five days.
He paid and left. On his way to the metro, Jay sighed deeply.
He wouldn't mind replacing the skyscrapers and distinguished colleagues for a down-to-earth working crowd. It was Ian he was unwilling to give up.
He had to at least try, didn't he? To fight for the best thing that ever happened to him. He'd never forgive himself if he gave it up due to his own weakness.
The metro ride home was quiet. Jay stared out the window at the passing lights, thinking about tomorrow. The deadline. The relief that would come after. Ian's face when the worst was finally over. He wanted to be there for that moment. He wanted to be there for all the moments after.
Scene 3
Battleground
Finally, it was Friday. The day strategy got submitted.
Everyone was doing one last sweep through documents, presentations, graphs, and spreadsheets. Searching for typos, checking calculations, verifying that every number matched across all materials. The entire office was filled with barely functioning zombies in mismatched clothes.
Only Kim and Jay looked presentable. It seemed Kim had taken on clothes duty, traveling to everyone's houses to collect essentials. But it was painfully clear which team members hadn't left the building in days. Luc wore the same shirt since Wednesday. Ela had given up on coordinating anything. Zoe's sweater was inside out.
Ian was deathly pale. Jay hadn't seen him eat anything that day, only endless cups of coffee. The smell of cigarettes clung to him so strongly Jay could swear Ian was smoking in his executive bathroom, but he didn't comment and didn't try to push him to eat.
There would be time to take care of that later. For now, Ian was hanging on by a thread, and Jay didn't want to yank at it. One more task. Just one more, and then this nightmare period would be over.
As two o'clock approached, Ian reviewed the final documents with red-rimmed eyes. Jay watched through the glass wall, concerned.
At exactly two-fifteen, Ian submitted the files. The strategy documents were officially delivered.
The collective breath of relief was audible throughout the office. People sagged in their chairs. Someone laughed, the sound slightly hysterical.
"We did it. We actually did it," Amy said weakly.
"I'm never doing this again. Next year I'm calling in sick for all of February," Ela declared.
Celebrations started, as subdued as they were from an exhausted crowd. A few people hugged. 
Jay's phone rang.
He glanced at the screen. Unknown number. He got up and moved to the closest conference room—currently unoccupied, with glass walls that looked out over the main office.
Ian watched him go. Watched him answer the call. Watched him smile and nod to whoever he was talking to.
The sinking feeling in Ian's stomach almost toppled him.
That was it, then. A job offer. It had to be. The timing, the smile, the way Jay's posture straightened slightly. Someone was offering him something, and Jay was accepting it.
Ian was out of time.
He stood abruptly, his chair scraping loudly. His voice came out strained when he addressed the team. "Everyone go home. Get rest."
Then he fled to the bathroom before anyone could respond.
In the conference room, Jay finished his conversation. "Thank you so much for the offer. I'm very flattered. Unfortunately, I've received another opportunity that I've decided to accept. I apologize for any inconvenience... Yes, I understand. Thank you for your time and consideration."
He hung up and stood for a moment, staring at his phone. He'd turned down the job. He was staying. Now he just had to prove he deserved to.
When Jay left the conference room, everyone was sluggishly packing and departing. Kim was escorting Ela out, one arm around her shoulders to keep her upright. Zoe was already packed and moving toward the exit. Luc struggled with rolling up his sleeping bag, cursing under his breath.
"Where's Ian?" Jay asked.
"Probably already went home to collapse," Luc said without looking up. "Everyone else is on their way too."
He barely spoke besides that, looking very worn out. Jay didn't push for more conversation.
Jay slowly packed his things but stayed after everyone else departed. The office looked like a battleground. Papers scattered across desks, empty coffee cups forming small graveyards in corners, someone's shoes abandoned under a table. The cleaning crews had been instructed to avoid the space until given clearance, lest they move important documents. They'd only come now to deal with the aftermath.
He looked toward Ian's office, where everything was left as it was. Documents still spread across the desk, the chair pushed back at an angle, Ian's coat still hanging on the rack.
Jay hoped Ian was alright. He certainly hadn't looked alright.
Jay sighed and took one more look around. He'd received a job offer, and judging by how quickly the call came, they'd been quite happy with him. He'd made an apologetic speech about receiving a better offer in the meantime, which was a thin excuse since he'd interviewed just yesterday, but he'd tried to sound convincing.
He surveyed the place where he'd chosen to stay. It decidedly didn't look inviting at the moment—more like a disaster zone than a prestigious workplace. But he still smiled.
It wasn't really the office or the work he was staying for. Frankly, the work was terrifying. The pressure, the stakes, the constant possibility of failure. But one last look toward Ian's desk as he headed for the exit convinced him he'd made the right choice.
Things that are worth it in life are rarely easy.
Ian, meanwhile, was stuck in the bathroom. He leaned against the sink, gripping the porcelain edge hard enough that his knuckles went white. He was exhausted to the point of breaking, and the sight of Jay cheerfully taking what he'd interpreted as a job offer felt like a nail in a coffin.
He'd failed. He run out of time. He spent weeks watching Jay slip away while being too consumed by work to do anything about it, and now it was too late.
Except it couldn't be too late. Ian refused to accept that.
It took him an unreasonably long time to collect himself enough to even think about returning home. He splashed cold water on his face, avoiding his reflection in the mirror. He looked terrible and he knew it.
He had the weekend to rest, recuperate, and be in the best form possible for Monday. He couldn't delay it a day longer.
On Monday, he had to convince Jay to stay. He had to find the right words, the right approach to communicate what Jay meant to him without crossing any lines.
Ian didn't know how he'd do it, but he knew he had to.
He finally left the bathroom and made his way to the elevator. The office was empty now, everyone gone. Just him and the silence and the weight of his failures.
Scene 4
Promotion
The weekend passed very differently for both men.
Jay went to the gym Saturday, his body enjoying the activity after weeks of neglect. He relaxed, did chores, caught up on sleep. On Sunday he traveled to the new apartment district and spent the entire day walking around the neighborhood.
The weather had calmed recently. The river looked much less menacing, even if the water level was still high. The area was beautiful, even in gloomy winter weather. The Sunday crowds were stylish—couples strolling hand in hand, families with expensive strollers, elegant ladies walking designer dogs.
Jay didn't feel any connection with these people, but he didn't have any bad feelings about them either. His previous dread was gone. For now, at least, he felt ready.
Ian, meanwhile, slept all Saturday. The exhaustion had accumulated to dangerous levels, and his body demanded rest. He only took breaks for proper meals, forcing himself to eat slowly and mindfully.
On Sunday he worked out intensively. The last couple of weeks had wrecked his form and it would take time to recover, but the movement cleared his head. He exercised, ate well, and planned his Monday meeting with Jay.
He rehearsed his lines in front of a mirror like he was preparing for a major negotiation. And it felt like the most major one in his life. Every word had to be perfect. Every approach analysed from all angles.
He went to bed early. He needed to be in top shape.
When Jay arrived at work Monday at seven-thirty, Ian was already there. In Jay's mailbox sat two emails. The first congratulated the team for successfully delivering the strategy, promising a dedicated meeting later that week about the aftermath. The second invited him for his delayed performance review.
Jay hadn't expected it. He hadn't prepared himself for it. He thought to himself that it would be funny if he actually got fired on the next workday after rejecting a job offer, and wondered if maybe they'd still hire him.
But he wasn't really nervous. He knew Ian. Ian wasn't someone who would surprise an employee with termination without prior notice.
The review was scheduled for nine. Jay entered Ian's office, closed the door behind him, and sat in the chair across from the desk. Ian still looked pale, still seemed worn out, but his clothes and hair were immaculate and he'd regained his former composure. Back straight, hands folded, expression controlled.
Ian started speaking in a very diplomatic way, praising Jay's contributions and work ethic, his ability to manage multiple priorities.
Then he apologized for the recent stressful period. He wasn't obsequious about it, but it was a very heartfelt apology. He took the blame for the pressure and everything that resulted from that pressure, assuring Jay that things would change for the better.
"While busy periods are normal in this industry, what happened these past couple of months was a failure of leadership on my part. I will remedy that."
Jay was surprised by that display. "It's quite alright. Everyone understands—"
"It's clearly not alright. Everyone can see it. This isn't even a matter of compensation for overtime, though that will naturally be addressed as well. It's a matter of putting undue strain on people."
Jay didn't know what to say. It was as Rus had said—bad management. But he absolutely refused to admit Ian was a bad manager.
Ian's tone lightened slightly. "While it was my failure and I admit it openly, I'll also ensure this gets fixed. I won't let my mistakes define me."
Jay smiled at that, recognizing Ian was likely addressing his own past errors, showing that there was no point dwelling and casting blame, but he needed to look forward. "That's a very wise approach."
Ian paused for a moment, gathering himself. "Do you have anything to add from yourself?"
This was the moment. If Jay wanted to hand in his resignation, this was probably it. Ian braced himself.
But Jay just smiled and shook his head. "No. I think you've covered everything."
Ian didn't address it directly, but his mind was already running recalculations. Maybe Jay had rejected the offer after all. Perhaps it wasn't very enticing. That gave him some leeway, but he still needed to be decisive.
He pushed a folder across the desk. "Your new contract."
Jay opened it and started reading. His previous contract had been standard—three years with a two-week termination period. This was permanent, with a three-month termination period. You didn't get a permanent contract until you'd worked somewhere for three years. Not unprecedented, but unusual to get it so early.
Then he saw the salary. A forty-five percent pay rise. Twenty thousand monthly. That was... obscene.
