He kept reading. Additional vacation days. A company car if he chose to get it. An extended benefits package normally reserved for executives, apparently courtesy of the CFO position. More freedom in task assignments. A title change from assistant to coordinator.
That was excessive.
He read the contract twice, in silence, shuffling through the pages listing benefits. He looked at Ian, then back at the pages. Then he laid the contract down.
"This seems a bit over the top."
"It seemed appropriate. You do much more than assistant duties. Your position and compensation should at least reflect that."
"I can't possibly accept this."
Ian clenched his jaw hard. He spoke softly and quietly, putting attention into every word. "I can offer you another position if these conditions are not satisfactory. You could be moved to the regular team. Or switch departments, if you feel you need a change."
"I like being your assistant. I want to stay in the role."
Ian looked doubtful. "Are you certain?"
"Yes."
"Then you'll stay in your current role. Just your title will change to better reflect the duties you already cover. Assistants don't support regular team members in their standard assignments, after all."
Jay was still unconvinced. "The pay is unreasonable."
Ian smiled bitterly. "You'd still earn the least out of the whole team. While your duties are equally strenuous."
Jay sat for a moment and looked down at the contract again. Then at Ian, sitting there with his perfectly controlled expression. Hands still. Breathing even. Back ramrod straight.
Something unsettled Jay about it.
"I thought I was going to get fired," Jay said, half-joking.
"I would never fire you," Ian said very solemnly. "I apologize once again if the impression I made was one of displeasure. The period was stressful for everyone and I coped poorly."
"Yeah, you and me both," Jay said, which got a real smile from Ian for once.
Jay sighed and took his pen out. He signed both copies and gave one to Ian. Only then did Ian visibly relax, his shoulders dropping slightly, his breathing deepening.
"It seems I'm going to move after all. I really don't have any more reasons to delay it, and my boxes are all packed anyway."
Ian immediately recommended a moving service. "I can have the arrangements made today if you'd like."
"That's not going to be necessary. I don't have nearly that many belongings. I'll just get my buddies to help me. They owe me anyway."
"Oh?"
"They came over and were so loud the police were called, then left me alone to deal with it. Some friends."
Ian actually laughed, and the atmosphere finally relaxed. With that, Jay left.
He looked at his new contract blankly as he sat at his desk. It seemed he really did get a much better offer, just as he'd told the interviewer on Friday. But it felt suspicious. Ian's stress levels were obviously off the charts. He needed to at least try to remedy it.
Jay went to the kitchen and prepared a warming herbal tea with root spices. He brought it to Ian's office and set it down on the desk.
Ian looked at him strangely. "You don't need to serve me refreshments."
"My new contract included more freedom in tasks," Jay said innocently.
Ian sighed. "Thank you."
Jay returned to his desk grinning.
It seemed like at least something had been resolved favorably.
Scene 5
Mental fortitude
The following workdays were still busy. Occasional client inquiries about the strategy had to be addressed, and normal work that had been severely neglected needed catching up. But the long hours ceased for most of the team.
Max and Amy stayed late most days. The people who'd camped in the office left like clockwork at five, traumatized by the idea of overtime. Ela practically ran for the exit. Luc set an alarm on his phone and departed the instant it rang.
Jay stayed late as well, accompanying Ian. He watched attentively to make sure Ian returned to normal eating habits and stopped secretly smoking.
"You're hovering," Ian said Monday evening, looking up from his documents.
"I'm observing."
"You're monitoring my every move like I'm a flight risk."
"Are you eating properly?"
Ian sighed. "Yes."
"You look less pale than last week, I'll give you that."
Ian rolled his eyes overtly, but secretly he was overjoyed. The crisis seemed averted. But he couldn't get complacent. It was his fault the crisis had appeared in the first place. He needed to be more mindful, more proactive.
On Tuesday, Jay left for the gym. 
"So? What happened with the job stuff?" Rus asked.
"I'm keeping my current position. And proceeding with the apartment purchase."
Rus stopped mid-stretch. "You're kidding."
"I'm not."
"That's a mistake."
"You've mentioned that."
"Because it's true! That place is going to kill you."
They moved to the weights. Rus continued nagging throughout the entire session. Jay let him, knowing Rus needed to voice his concerns even if Jay had no intention of taking the advice.
"I just need to get more used to failure," Jay said between sets.
"That's literally the worst life philosophy I've ever heard. Did Mic tell you that? Because he's the worst person to take advice from."
But Jay was already certain of his decision. The fear hadn't disappeared entirely, but it had become manageable. Something he could live with.
That evening, Jay called the agent. "Is the flat still available?"
"Very much so!" The agent sounded relieved. "Has your money situation been resolved?"
Jay cringed, remembering his lost envelope. "It hasn't. But I thought more about it and decided I like the flat enough to make some sacrifices."
"Excellent! I'll come collect the contract right away and we can proceed with the purchase."
"That's not necessary. I can come to your office—"
"I insist. I'll be there within the hour."
Jay tried to protest, but the agent was unrelenting. True to his word, he arrived exactly fifty-three minutes later.
He was delighted when he saw Jay was already packed into boxes. "Perfect! This will make everything much easier. I'll proceed as promptly as possible."
Jay was once again suspicious about the man's enthusiasm, but maybe being a real estate agent required that level of energy.
On Wednesday, Ian announced a team meeting. Everyone gathered in the conference room.
"First, thank you all again for your extraordinary work during the strategy period. I want to apologize for the strain it placed on everyone. To address this, I'm offering additional vacation days, an additional bonus at the end of the month, and spa vouchers for everyone to get proper relaxing massages."
There were murmurs of appreciation. Ela smiled.
"Additionally, we'll be hiring new team members. Leo is already proceeding with preliminary CV collection and setting up first interviews with HR. I'll start interviewing candidates in April, after quarterly reports are complete. Hopefully we can hire people by May so they can join the company retreat and better integrate into the team. I'm thinking two to four people, possibly five if we find the right candidates."
The room went quiet. Everyone exchanged glances.
After the meeting, Jay approached Luc. "Why does everyone seem shocked about hiring?"
"Because Ian hates hiring. He's notoriously picky. Impossibly high standards plus very specific particularities. It's a nightmare process."
"He can't be that picky if he chose to keep me," Jay joked.
Amy, overhearing, rolled her eyes. "You're a rare and precious gem. The result of years of Leo's unsuccessful headhunting attempts."
Jay blushed and returned to his desk feeling sheepish. He'd heard stories about Ian being very demanding, which he'd concluded were grossly overblown. Ian was nothing but kind, fair, and generous.
The work itself was difficult and required endurance, he had to admit. It wasn't long ago when he'd been on the verge of breaking, and his position was hardly crucial. He could imagine it required great mental fortitude to be a regular team member.
It seemed the office would get more lively in coming months. There were six free desks in the main office and another one next to Jay. They could comfortably fit seven more people.
Apparently it had taken a long time for Ian to gather his current team, and many people had been fired along the way for failing to meet his exacting standards. Jay was curious to see exactly what qualities Ian looked for in subordinates.
This period brought another curious development. Every now and then, Jay would receive a call from some HR department offering an interview. His inbox would occasionally produce similar emails.
He politely answered all of them, informing each that he'd already secured another position. The awkwardness increased with each one as he had to scuttle to a free conference room or occasionally to the bathroom to take the calls.
"I appreciate the offer, but I've already accepted another position," he said into his phone Thursday afternoon, hiding in a conference room. "Yes, I understand. Thank you for considering me. I apologize for any inconvenience."
Ian watched from his office, first with concern, then with growing amusement as he overheard Jay's fervent apologies to the unfortunate HR people. Exactly how many applications had Jay sent?
Ian wanted to comment but reminded himself not to be too forward. He had to tread carefully. He didn't want to make Jay uncomfortable.
Jay was surprised they were reaching out with such delay. He'd initially thought no one was interested, but it seemed it took companies quite some time to process applications and reach out to candidates. And he wasn't as unpopular as he'd initially thought.
Chapter 34: Damp weather
Scene 1
Warming soup
Team members took turns taking time off to recuperate before the next busy periods. The office was missing one or two people constantly. Jay expressed no desire to join them. Strategy season hadn't been nearly as bad for him as for the others, despite his mishap with the database. He'd survived. They needed the rest more.
Ian continued working twelve-hour days, but he returned to normal, his face recovering its glow. The dark circles faded. The tension in his shoulders eased. Jay watched it with relief.
March was ending, but the weather remained poor. Rain battered the windows most mornings, and the sky hung gray and heavy through the afternoons. However, with the immediate crisis temporarily resolved, the team returned to their daily lunch outings. Weather grumbling was constant but less pronounced. Everyone was glad to leave the office.
"It's supposed to rain until April," Kim announced Monday afternoon, scrolling through his phone.
"Fantastic. I love perpetual dampness," Ela muttered.
"We could eat at our desks," Amy said without enthusiasm.
"Absolutely not. We earned our lunches out. Weather be damned," Luc said firmly.
Jay used the first opportunity that presented itself to push for his favourite. "What about Willow & Sage?"
"That little place with the soup?" Zoe asked.
"Yes. I haven't been there in months."
"Tragic existence," Luc joked.
"Then Willow & Sage it is," Amy decided.
They went that afternoon, crowding through the door and shaking rain from their coats. The warm, cosy atmosphere immediately lifted Jay's spirits. Rough wooden tables, soft lighting, herbs hanging in bunches from exposed beams. The smell of bread and simmering stock. He'd missed this place.
They sat at a long table near the back. Jay ordered warming soup with herbal bread. The exact meal he'd been craving for weeks.
He hunched in the corner with Zoe and Luc, listening to Zoe describe her weekend hiking trip with good-natured enthusiasm. Luc looked better than he had in weeks, colour returned to his face, energy restored.
"It was a great success despite the miserable weather. Being able to finally walk around instead of constantly sitting at a desk was brilliant," Zoe said.
The food arrived. Jay took his first spoonful of soup and closed his eyes briefly. Perfect. Rich and earthy with just enough salt.
"You look like you're having a religious experience," Luc observed.
"Maybe I am."
Luc watched him for another moment, then set down his fork. "So what was bothering you so much lately?"
The table went quiet. Jay opened his eyes.
"We were the ones living in the office. But you were the one who looked like you were being actively tortured."
Jay grew quiet. He tore off a piece of bread, focusing on it. "Personal matters."
"Personal matters," Kim repeated with great cheerfulness. "Are you sure it wasn't still the dropped table matter? Because that was honestly hilarious. The look on your face when you realized—"
"It wasn't that. And I don't want to talk about it. But I appreciate your concern, even if you're terribly insensitive about it."
Kim grinned. "Fair."
"We're not trying to pry," Amy said more gently. "You just seemed… different. For a while."
"I'm fine now," Jay said. "Really."
"If you're sure."
"I'm sure."
The conversation moved on. Jay focused on his soup, letting the warmth seep through him. The bread was dense and fragrant with rosemary. Each bite pushed back the unpleasant memories of the lost envelope, the months of saving gone in a moment of carelessness. He definitely didn't want to publicly admit to that blunder. Some failures were too mortifying to share.
By the time they returned to the office, the rain had intensified into a proper downpour. They arrived soaked, dripping puddles onto the marble floor.
Jay returned to his desk and opened his email. Another message from HR about the May retreat. He accepted the invite and clicked through to the signup sheet.
The activity list was extensive. Horseback riding. Archery. Pottery. Dancing. Kayaking. Cooking classes. Hiking trails. Paddle boats.
Nothing as adventurous as horseback archery, but the hike seemed comfortable enough. He selected it. Cooking classes could be interesting. He added those as well. Paddle boats sounded fun. He picked that too.
He was scrolling through the remaining options when Ian emerged from his office. He approached Jay's desk, glancing at the screen.
"Did you choose the boats?" Ian asked.
Jay looked up. "Yes. Just now."
"I'm quite fond of those."
"Can we be on the same boat then?"
"Naturally. I'll make arrangements."
"Thank you."
Though Ian thought to himself he probably wouldn't need to. Leo would make sure they got grouped in everything.
Ian returned to his office. Jay turned back to his screen, excitement building. Spending time doing fun things with Ian again. Just a couple of weeks ago he'd given up on this life entirely. Now he had something to look forward to.
He scrolled through the signup sheet one more time, confirming his selections. He could imagine Ian on the boat, hair catching the wind. Or hiking together through the woods. Cooking side by side in the lodge kitchen like they had at Christmas.
Jay agreed with Kim that separate room were a downgrade. The morning coffee with Ian while they were both still in their respective beds was the best. He caught himself at this thought and blushed furiously. What was he even thinking? Fay's diagnosis echoed in his mind: head over heels in love.
Preposterous. He just wanted to hang out with someone he admired. That was a perfectly normal friendly wish. Nothing more complicated than that.
He closed the signup form and returned to his spreadsheets, determinedly ignoring the way his pulse had quickened at the thought of sharing a boat with Ian. Just friends. Colleagues. Nothing strange about looking forward to activities with a person you respected.
The rain continued outside. Jay worked through the afternoon, occasionally glancing toward Ian's office with fondness. Completely nothing weird about that at all.
Scene 2
Submitting the paperwork
As April loomed, Jay wanted to get as many affairs in order before the next hectic period. He arranged the bank visit to sign the loan paperwork.
The bank accommodated his preferred time on Friday afternoon. Since he'd already provided all the details in the pre-approval stage, he just went to officially submit his application.
The bank lady was as helpful and friendly as the last time, giving him obviously preferential treatment. She helped him expediently with the paperwork. He had all the necessary documents ready, including property valuation conveniently delivered by the real estate agent.
"Excellent. The paperwork will be ready to sign in two weeks. Once you do, the money will be transferred within a couple of days," she said as she gathered the papers into a neat stack.
"Two weeks. So early April."
"Exactly. We'll call when everything is ready."
"Thank you."
The whole process took less than twenty minutes. Jay left the bank with feeling dizzy. It was really happening. He called the real estate agent from the street.
"Mr. Hall," the agent answered cheerfully. "How did it go?"
"Everything's submitted. They said two weeks for the paperwork, then a couple of days for the transfer."
"Perfect, perfect. I'll handle the notary work. Just let me know when the funds are confirmed, and we'll schedule the final signing."
"I will. Thank you."
"It's a beautiful property. You're making an excellent choice."
Jay ended the call and stood on the sidewalk for a moment. It was final then. In about three weeks, he'd move. Right in the middle of April, after Easter.
He hadn't canceled his lease yet. He had a three-month termination period, but wanted to be secure in the new place before he felt comfortable abandoning his current flat. And despite how gloomy it looked stacked with boxes, he was still very fond of it. Leaving still saddened him.
That evening, he called his mother.
"Jay?" She sounded surprised. "Is everything alright?"
"Everything's fine. I wanted to ask about Easter. Do you have plans?"
"Plans? No, we'll stay at home. Traditional dinner. Why?"
"I was thinking I could visit."
"Visit?" Her voice lifted with joy. "Really? Oh, that would be wonderful!"
In the background, Jay heard his youngest brother Ari's voice: "Is that Jay? Is he bringing gifts?"
Jay heard his mother's muffled response, then Ari yelled louder: "We'll send a wishlist!"
His mother laughed. "You heard."
"All you care about is gifts," Jay grumbled, but he wasn't really annoyed.
"They're young," his mother said warmly. "They miss you. We all do."
"I miss you too."
"Will you stay overnight?"
"I'd like that. I'll arrive on Saturday and leave Sunday afternoon. It's a long drive, after all."
"Of course, of course. I'm so happy you're coming."
Ari yelled something else in the background about chocolate.
"I'll bring something," Jay promised.
After they hung up, Jay felt lighter. It had been too long since he'd visited home. Work had consumed everything for months, but now that things had stabilized, he could finally take the trip.
He used the weekend to handle the shopping. On Saturday he went to a large grocery store, working through his mental list. Variety of sweets first—chocolate eggs, marshmallow candies, fruit jellies. He moved on to food. Since he was going to visit, he wanted to help cook. He filled his cart with groceries that had long shelf life. Olive oil. Hard cheese. Cured meats. Aged sausages. Variety of pastas. Canned vegetables. Things that could lay around for two weeks in his pantry without spoiling.
Then items from his siblings' texted wish list, which had arrived within an hour of his call. Then he tackled clothing. His storage problems were about to be mostly solved, so he could finally get some casual clothes. He went to one of the nice boutiques that had been recommended to him last year.
The shop was quiet when he entered. A young clerk approached immediately.
"Good afternoon. Can I help you find something?"
"I need casual jeans and shirts," Jay said.
"Of course. What's your size?"
Jay told him. The clerk nodded and began pulling items from displays and racks.
"Try these. And these shirts should work well. The colours suit you."
Jay took them to the fitting room. He was pleased to discover that shopping for clothes was no longer an excruciating ordeal. It was hardly pleasant, but definitely manageable. Last year he'd needed to prepare beforehand and write himself an itinerary. Now he at least simply entered the store and picked things that were most acceptable.
Ian's advice echoed in his mind: Find an expert, and do what they say.
He tried on the jeans first. The fit was good. The shirts were comfortable, soft cotton in muted colours. Blue, gray, dark green.
"How's everything?" the clerk called.
"Good. I'll take these."
He emerged from the fitting room with his selections. The clerk rang them up efficiently.
"Anything else you need?" he asked.
"No, this is fine. Thank you."
"Have a good day."
Jay left with his purchases, feeling accomplished. Tasks handled. Plans in motion. Everything falling into place.
He went to work on Monday in an uncommonly good mood—something rare for him lately. Ian was already there and looked up when Jay arrived.
"Good morning," Ian said.
"Good morning."
"You seem in better spirits."
"I am. I'm moving forward with the apartment and the mortgage. I'll probably move within a month. Now that the decision is final and the paperwork is submitted, I feel calmer about it. I'm no longer stressing about making the right choice."
"That's excellent news."
"And I'm visiting my family for Easter."
"That sounds nice."
"It's been too long. My youngest brother already demanded gifts."
"How old is he?"
"Thirteen. Still thinks I'm made of money."
"Younger siblings often do. You look much better than you used to in the past month."
Jay laughed. "The strategy season was hard on everyone, me included, even if I didn't have as much stake in it."
"I'll make sure it's easier next year. There are some promising candidates lined up. Leo actually put in the work on high-stakes headhunting. Their salaries are going to hurt, but it's better than having the whole team burn out and quit."
Jay returned to his desk, organizing his workspace for the day. April was going to be busy, but he felt ready for it.
Scene 3
Intentions
March ended and the hectic period of quarterly reporting began. The entire team returned to work to deal with the workload, the brief vacation period ending abruptly.
But before Jay could fully immerse himself in the work, a bank notification arrived with his salary. He opened it at his desk, expecting it to be higher, given the quarterly bonus, the strategy bonus, his new pay rise. But the number started him. Eighty thousand.
Four times his normal salary.
He stared at the screen, refreshing it twice to confirm. The number didn't change.
This time he went to accounting to confirm. He was comfortable enough with them now to simply ask. The accounting department occupied a corner on their floor, separated by frosted glass doors.
Jen looked up when he entered, her face brightening immediately. "Jay! What can I do for you?"
"I think there might be an error with my salary."
"An error? What's the issue?"
"It's… higher than expected."
She typed rapidly, scanning the screen. Then she smiled. "No error. Your new contract came into effect at the beginning of the year, so you received compensation for the difference."
"The difference," Jay repeated.
"Right. Your quarterly bonus is also based on your new salary, not the old one. That, plus the additional strategy bonus, gives you the result you saw."
"So it's correct."
"Completely correct. You look surprised."
"I am."
"You earned it." She turned back to her screen but kept talking. "So how have you been? It feels like forever since we've chatted. You're always so busy—"
"I should get back to work. The quarterly reports."
"Oh, of course. But we should grab coffee sometime—"
"Maybe. Thank you for clarifying."
He retreated quickly, returning to his desk. He looked at his balance once again on his phone. He was being paid obscenely well. It shouldn't shock him anymore, but it still did.
But the musings about money would have to wait. He returned to his assignments, opening the first file on his list.
The team braved the workload better this time. The processes concerning Hartwell, their newest and most demanding client, had been streamlined and weren't causing as many problems as previous seasons. The workload was still heavy, but nothing compared to what had happened in the past months.
Tuesday evening, Luc shut his laptop at ten o'clock and stood triumphantly.
"At least I don't need to camp out in the office," he chanted, grabbing his coat. "At least I don't need to camp out in the office."
"We're coming back Saturday," Kim pointed out.
"Still counts as going home."
Lunches got replaced with takeout again. Working until ten every evening became the norm. But the weather outside was still miserable, rain and gray skies constant, so staying in felt less like a punishment. And at least everyone could return home, even if they had to come in Saturday.
Jay already dreaded his next gym visit. Rus would definitely scold him for his job being unreasonable and for making a mistake by staying.
But Jay's mood was much better. He'd almost forgotten about the misery of his February blunders. Time and intense periods that followed took his mind off things. The increased workload didn't bother him. He was even glad for it.
Since the office was always busy recently—first with absences of people taking vacation days, requiring longer hours from the rest, now with everyone working hard on quarterly reports—he didn't have as much time to stay alone with Ian.
It wasn't that he didn't want to be in Ian's company. Quite the opposite. He appreciated and treasured it. But Fay's words about being in love kept randomly reappearing and making him flustered. It was ridiculous, naturally, but the idea didn't want to retire from his mind and kept popping up at random moments.
He didn't feel ready to spend prolonged time alone with Ian. What if he suddenly started blushing for no reason? That would just make everything unnecessarily awkward.
Wednesday afternoon, Ian emerged from his office to refill his coffee. Jay was at his desk, absorbed in a spreadsheet, one hand in his hair, the other clicking through cells.
Ian watched for a moment, then returned to his office without speaking. Better not to interrupt.
Ian had his own reservations. He was still beating himself up over his blunder of calling Jay beautiful. It was around that time that Jay started to withdraw. He was sure it was at least part of the reason.
Jay valued his peace and security above all. If he felt in any way threatened, he'd flee. Everything was going so well, and Ian had to ruin it by making Jay doubt his intentions.
His intentions were absolutely pure. Regardless of what he thought and felt about Jay, he would never make him uncomfortable. He would rather die than violate the trust they'd built. Jay needed to be absolutely certain of it.
But since Jay was still here, still showed care openly, the damage wasn't too serious. Ian could still rebuild what they had. Make Jay feel safe enough again for them to share those tender moments, just like they used to. He just needed to make sure to never make any more stupid mistakes.
He'd be slow about it, but he'd get the intimacy they shared back. He really didn't want anything more.
On Friday Amy caught an error in one of the summary reports that required two hours of backtracking. Max stayed until midnight helping her fix it. Kim cursed his computer system for freezing during a critical update.
Saturday was quieter, just half the team working through remaining items. By Monday afternoon, the last files got submitted.
The team erupted in cheers.
"Finally," Ela breathed, collapsing back in her chair.
Luc stood up. "We're getting drinks. Everyone. No excuses."
"I have plans—" Kim started.
"Cancel them."
"I don't actually have plans."
"Then you're coming."
Jay joined this time. He felt it was deserved. They'd struggled for months, and now finally they'd reached the finish line. Even Zoe was there.
They went to Murphy's, their favorite pub, crowding into a large booth near the back. The atmosphere was boisterous from the start, everyone vividly complaining about being put through hell.
"I barely slept," Ela said dramatically. "I aged ten years."
"You look the same," Max pointed out.
"That's kind, but I know I look terrible."
Jay ordered a beer, then another. The alcohol made everything slightly fuzzy and warm. He complained alongside everyone else.
"Database management is absolutely unfair," he announced to Kim, who was sitting across from him.
Kim's face lit up. "I knew it! I knew you were still depressed about the dropped table."
"It's a legitimate grievance."
"It was months ago."
"And I'm still bitter."
"Jay holding a grudge. Write this down, someone."
"I'm just pointing out that database management is poorly designed."
"Keep going. Tell me more."
"The restore process is needlessly complicated."
"Amazing."
Jay smiled to himself. His great mistake that had affected him so much was nothing more than an anecdote to tease him about over beer.
The evening continued. People ordered food. Conversations overlapped and blended together. Jay told a story about his youngest brother demanding gifts that had Zoe laughing. Kim and Luc argued about some technical specification.
By ten o'clock, people started leaving. Zoe left first, Kim and Ela followed shortly after.
Eventually only Jay and Luc remained.
"I should go," Jay said, checking his phone. "I have to get up early tomorrow."
"Overachiever," Luc muttered.
"Says the man who lives at the office."
"Not anymore. I'm a changed man."
Jay left. Outside, the rain had stopped. The streets were wet and reflective under the streetlights. Jay called a taxi and waited, hands in his pockets, looking up at the dark sky.
Scene 4
Denial
The atmosphere lightened as the quarterly reporting period ended. Overtime ended overnight, and everyone, Jay included, left at five for the remainder of the week. The beginning of April was still cold and damp, but the daily lunch outings returned enthusiastically.
Easter and the upcoming quarterly party dominated the conversations.
"Four days off. Four entire days," Ela said dreamily at Monday's lunch. 
"I'm sleeping for three of them," Luc announced.
"What about the fourth?"
"More sleeping."
On Tuesday at the gym, Jay cornered Rus by the weight rack.
"I need a favor."
"What kind of favor?"
"I need you and the guys to help me move. In a couple of weeks."
"Move? So you're actually doing it?"
"Yes. And you all owe me for that unfortunate incident where the police got called because you were too loud in my studio."
Rus had the decency to look sheepish. "That was one time."
"One time too many."
"Fine, fine. I'll get the guys to organize a van. When do you need us?"
"I'll let you know the exact date once everything's finalized. Probably mid-April."
"We'll be there. But I still think your job is unreasonable."
"I know you do."
"Working until ten every night for a week straight—"
"It was manageable this time."
"That doesn't make it reasonable."
"It's getting better."
At lunch on Wednesday, Jay made his announcement.
"I'm buying the apartment."
The table erupted.
"Finally!" Zoe said.
"Congratulations!" Amy reached across to pat his hand.
"About time. You've been agonizing over this for months," Max added.
Luc grumbled into his soup. "Insufficient space for entertaining, though."
"I'm not planning to entertain."
"That's what everyone says until they actually want to have people over."
"It has a balcony," Jay pointed out. 
"A balcony is not entertaining space." 
"It's better than what I have now." 
"Barely."
The topic moved to Easter plans. Everyone shared theirs—family dinners, short trips, lazy weekends at home.
"I'm visiting my family," Jay said.
"That's nice," Ela said. "How long has it been?"
"Too long." 
"What are you doing for Easter?" Zoe asked Luc.
"Enjoying myself. No girlfriend means no suffering through her family nagging me about my career prospects and when we're getting married."
"That's the spirit," Max said dryly.
Jay had already placed a request for the company car beforehand. He was picking it up that afternoon, since the next day was the party and the day after was already off for the holidays.
He collected it after work. The limousine was waiting in the garage, sleek and black and completely excessive. He was no longer surprised, just resigned.
He drove it home, acutely aware of every other vehicle on the road. When he arrived at his building, he parked right outside his window, where he could see it from his flat.
It's insured, he told himself. Safe area. Nothing will happen. He repeated this to himself several times but still felt nervous.
The next day was Thursday, the final day before Easter, and the quarterly party. Everyone left work promptly to have enough time to prepare.
Jay wore his original navy suit. It was the only appropriate evening attire he'd left unpacked. He arrived by taxi, scanned the venue, approved of the lavish setting, and, as tradition demanded, found the buffet.
This time the team looked in much better spirits. The worst was behind them. Everyone was looking forward to the warm season. Vacation plans were the main topic of the evening.
"What about you, Jay?" Amy asked. "Any plans?"
"Just making myself at home in the new apartment."
Everyone nodded and offered advice with various levels of usefulness.
"Get good curtains," Zoe said. "Light control is everything."
"Invest in a proper mattress," Max added. "Your back will thank you."
"Don't buy too much furniture at once," Kim said. "Live in the space first, see what you actually need."
Jay nodded along, but remembered none of it.
Later in the evening, he spotted Luc at the bar, leaning close to a woman from legal. She was pretty, graceful, with a cheerful laugh.
Jay waited until Luc stepped away to order another drink, then intercepted him.
"What's up with you and Kia?"
Luc grinned. "What do you mean?"
"A couple of months ago, you said dating at the workplace was an awful idea."
"That was when I was in a relationship and level-headed. Now that I'm free, I'm much less concerned with what's wise and more with what feels good."
"That's terrible logic."
"Look at Kim and Ela. They're perfectly fine."
Jay had to admit it was true. Kim and Ela had been dating in secret for months, and their relationship hadn't impacted work or the team in any way. They weren't even all that sneaky anymore. Jay was sure everyone was aware. They simply chose not to address it directly.
"Still," Jay said.
Luc laughed and clapped him on the shoulder. "Anyway, why are you asking? Are you interested in someone? Maybe Jen from accounting? She's been trying to hit on you for ages."
Jay sighed deeply. "She's just being friendly."
"She's definitely not just being friendly."
"Even if it was more, I'm not interested. She's nice enough but we really don't have anything in common."
"Fair. But you should date someone eventually. You're too young to be this solitary."
"I'm fine."
"If you say so."
The party continued in good spirits. Ian found Jay in the other half of the venue, loading a plate of appetizers for what had to be the fourth time that evening.
"You already have a reputation for being a glutton," Ian said, sounding amused.
Jay shrugged. "At least it's better than having a reputation for dropping tables."
"That was months ago. No one remembers."
"I remember."
"What do you think of the venue?"
Jay looked around. "It's nice enough. But I liked the summer one last year much more. And I can't even remember the fall one."
"At least everyone looks better than last time. So maybe there's hope."
They chatted a bit more about the ambiance and the food. Eventually, Ian excused himself.
"I should go entertain Turner Senior. He's been glaring at me from across the room."
"Good luck with that."
"Enjoy the rest of your evening."
Ian left. Jay watched him go for a moment, following his path through the crowd. He was undoubtedly the most handsome man in the room. There were plenty of good-looking men at the party, but no one was quite as magnetic as Ian.
No wonder Jay felt flustered at the idea of being in love with him. He was sure everyone had their moments of gay panic when looking at Ian. Who wouldn't. Ian was... well, difficult to resist.
Jay sighed deeply again. He was sighing a lot that evening.
He finished his appetizers, made polite conversation with a few more colleagues, then said his farewells and called a taxi back home. He checked on the limousine through his window—still there, untouched—and collapsed into bed.
Tomorrow, he'd rest. And the day after, he was going to see his family.
He fell asleep thinking about the drive ahead and about the groceries waiting to be packed. Not about Ian's profile in the dim party lighting, or the way his voice had softened when they spoke. Definitely not about that.
Scene 5
Festive breakfast
Jay spent Easter Friday cleaning his apartment. Soon he'd move out. He wanted everything to be neat and tidy.
He moved his boxes near the exit, stacking them against the wall. Then he packed some of the remaining items he was still using occasionally—textbooks, a few dishes, the rest of his clothes. He left only the most immediate ones and toiletries. By the end of the day, the place was ready to be vacated.
Saturday morning he loaded the groceries and gifts into the limousine. He wore his newly bought clothes that looked a bit too elegant to truly pass as casual in his mind. Even the jeans from the upscale boutiques seemed somehow more posh. He dug out one of the sports jackets from one of the boxes to finish the look. He checked himself in the mirror. Nice, but not too ostentatious. He was reasonably content with the result. 
He left early. Traffic was minimal on the holiday weekend, so he arrived at his mother's house by noon. His mother greeted him at the door, pulling him into a warm embrace.
"Jay! You're here!"
"Hi, Mum."
"Come in, come in. You look wonderful. So grown up."
"Jay!" Ema practically tackled him with a hug. Tim and Ari crowded close behind.
"Did you bring the gifts?" Ari asked immediately.
"Hello to you too."
"But did you?"
"Yes, I brought gifts."
"I knew it!"
"Let him breathe, you three. Come in, Jay. You must be tired from the drive."
"I'm fine. Traffic was light."
His stepfather appeared briefly in the hallway.  "I'll be in the garden if anyone needs me."
They exchanged polite nods before he retreated to the garden to do whatever garden activities he preferred. That was their dynamic. Cordial distance.
His siblings, however, had no such reservations. They descended immediately.
"Did you attend any great balls recently?" Ema asked.
"A few," Jay admitted.
"Did you meet any movie stars?" Ari demanded.
"No movie stars."
"That's boring."
"Sorry to disappoint."
Jay unloaded the groceries in the kitchen. His mother followed, already pulling out pots and pans.
"You didn't have to bring all this," she said, peering into the bags.
"I wanted to help with the cooking."
"You don't need to cook. You're a guest."
"I insist."
They baked hams and stuffed poultry and chopped vegetables for salads. His mother directed, Jay followed. The kitchen filled with comforting smells—herbs, roasting meat. The kids drifted in and out, stealing bits of food and asking questions about the city.
In the afternoon, they painted eggs. All of them participated eagerly, even Tim who claimed it was childish but picked up a brush anyway. Ari made his bright green. Ema created delicate patterns. Jay painted simple stripes.
"Yours are boring," Ari announced.
"They're classic."
"Boring."
As evening approached, the siblings cornered him.
"Take us for a ride in the limousine," Tim said.
"Please, Jay. Just a short drive."
"It's not my car. It's a company car."
"But you're allowed to use it, right?"
"Yes, but—"
"Then let's go!"
Jay sighed theatrically. "Fine. But only for an hour."
They piled in, Tim claiming the front seat immediately. Jay drove them by the lake, despite the poor weather. The kids pressed against the windows, pointing out the landmarks. Ari fiddled with every button he could find. Ema took approximately fifty photos.
They only returned when it turned dark, the siblings chattering the entire way back.
The next day was typical continental Easter. Festive breakfast full of eggs, various hams and sausages, and three kinds of potato salads. It was loud and lively, everyone talking over each other.
Jay dispensed the gifts after breakfast. The kids were ecstatic.
"You got exactly what I wanted!" Ema squealed, hugging the weird-shaped lipsticks.
Ari tore open the superhero set with abandon. "This is perfect!"
Tim examined the modular hammock system. "Thanks, Jay."
There was also an entire bag of sweets, with some designer pralines for his mother and stepfather.
"You didn't have to spend so much," his mother said softly.
"I wanted to."
Lunch was equally excessive. Multiple courses, everyone eating until they couldn't move. Jay stayed over until afternoon, spending the whole day lounging about and eating. It felt domestic, nostalgic even.
His mother narrated family news while Jay mostly listened. Distant relatives getting married, neighbors moving away, local scandals Jay had no context for. Finally, he told them. "If everything resolves well, I'm buying a small apartment."
The kids erupted.
"You must absolutely host us over!" Ari insisted.
"It's quite small," Jay warned.
"We don't care."
"Ari and I will sleep on the floor. We want to see the city," Tim said. 
"We'll be so good. You won't even notice us," Ema promised.
"I'll definitely notice you."
But he promised anyway. "When I’m properly moved in, I’ll invite you."
His mother's eyes filled with tears. "Jay. I'm so happy for you."
She held his hand across the table, squeezing tightly. Before he left, she hugged him for a long time.
"I know your childhood wasn't the easiest. And I'm so glad you're doing well now."
Jay returned the embrace. "It was quite alright, Mum. I remember my early years fondly, despite everything. The neighborhood was quaint, if nothing else. The countryside was beautiful. It was a good place for a kid to grow up."
"You're kind to say so."
"I mean it."
She held him a moment longer, then released him. "Drive safely."
"I will."
His mother packed him a bunch of leftovers and homemade cakes. He loaded them into the car, waved goodbye, and drove back to the city.
He returned home late and tired. The apartment looked even emptier than before, boxes stacked by the door like silent sentinels. He showered quickly and dropped into bed.
Monday he spent eating the leftovers and browsing interior decor websites. The apartment was furnished, but minimally. While it looked nice, Jay already thought about functional improvements. Maybe a different table. Maybe another bookshelf. Perhaps some proper curtains. It still felt fragile to plan things like that. Like he didn't trust something wouldn't go wrong at the last moment.
Chapter 35: Home
Scene 1
Help
Jay arrived at the office at seven thirty on Tuesday morning. The building was quiet, the hallways empty. He made his way to his desk and set down his bag. The office felt peaceful.
He prepared coffee and opened his workstation. Outside, the sky was overcast. The temperature had climbed a few degrees. Spring was supposedly approaching, though the city remained stubbornly cold. He still wore his winter coat. The weather had been terrible for months now. He couldn't remember the last time April felt this frigid.
Ian arrived just before eight.
"Good morning," Ian said as he passed.
"Morning."
Ian paused at the threshold of his office. "How were your holidays?"
"Good. Very good, actually. I spent the weekend at my mother's house. We had the full Easter feast. Multiple courses, eggs, hams. I probably gained five kilograms. I'll have to work out twice as hard at the gym today."
"It sounds like you enjoyed yourself."
"I did. It was nice to spend time with my family. My mother cried when I told her about the apartment."
"She must be proud of you."
"I think she is. What about you? Did you take any time off?"
"I researched potential candidates for the new positions. We have several promising picks. I have a good feeling about them. Hopefully by May the team will expand."
"That would be a relief."
"Yes. It would. You look better."
"I feel better. The break helped."
"Good. That's good."
Ian entered his office, and Jay returned to his desk. Work felt manageable again after the relentless months of strategy season. The backlog was shrinking. They were catching up. The other coworkers began arriving soon. Amy came in first, then Kim and Ela. By nine the office was alive again.
"How was your holiday?" Zoe asked as she passed Jay's desk.
"Great. I visited my family. You?"
"I stayed home and read books."
Luc appeared shortly after, looking unusually cheerful. "Jay! You're back. Did you bring any leftover cake?"
"My mother sent me home with enough food to last a week."
"You should share."
"It's in my fridge at home."
"Tragic."
Max wandered over with his coffee. "Welcome back, Jay. Good holiday?"
"Very good. Yours?"
"Excellent. I went hiking with my wife. The trails were muddy but beautiful."
Everyone exchanged stories. Ela had visited her sister. Amy had reorganized her house. The atmosphere was relaxed. The pressure of the previous months had finally lifted. Jay worked through the morning. Shortly before lunch, his phone rang. An unfamiliar number.
"Hello?"
"Good morning, Mr. Hall. This is Jasper Bank. I'm calling to inform you that your mortgage paperwork is complete. You can come in to sign at your convenience."
"Oh. That's excellent news. Tomorrow at four?"
"That works perfectly. We'll see you then."
"Thank you."
Jay ended the call and sat very still. It was real. It was actually happening. He got up and walked to Ian's door. Ian looked up from his monitor.
"Something wrong?"
"No. The opposite, actually. The bank just called. My mortgage paperwork is ready. I need to sign tomorrow. Would it be alright if I left early? Around three thirty?"
"Of course. Take whatever time you need."
"Thank you."
Jay returned to his desk. His hands were shaking slightly. He called the agent next.
"Mr. Hall! Perfect timing. I was just about to reach out to you."
"The bank called. I'm signing tomorrow at four."
"Wonderful. I'll meet you there. We'll handle everything at once. If all goes smoothly, you'll have your keys by the end of the week."
"This week?"
"Absolutely. These things move quickly when the paperwork is in order."
"I see."
"We pride ourselves on it. I'll see you tomorrow at the bank."
After they hung up, Jay set down his phone. This week. He'd expected another month of waiting. Instead, everything was accelerating. That afternoon at the gym, Jay found Rus immediately.
"Are you free this weekend? The mortgage paperwork is ready. I'm signing tomorrow. The agent says I'll have the keys this week."
"This week? That's fast."
"Very fast. I was wondering if you'd be available Saturday afternoon? For the move?"
"Saturday? Yeah, I can do that. I'll bring the van. You want me to ask the guys?"
"If they're free, that would be helpful."
"Consider it done. Don't skip Saturday morning's session, though. It's your last workout before you abandon us for your fancy new neighbourhood."
"I'm not abandoning anyone."
"Sure. That's what they all say."
"I'll be there Saturday morning. Promise."
"Good."
The next day, Jay left work at three-thirty. The taxi ride to the bank felt surreal, like he was watching someone else's life unfold. The agent was already waiting outside.
"Mr. Hall. Right on time."
"Hello."
They entered together. A woman led them to a private office. The paperwork was extensive. Jay read through it, though he trusted Ian's recommendations. Everything was as discussed before. Interest rate, down payment, payment schedule, terms and conditions. He noticed something when he reached the purchase contract.
"There's a parking spot listed."
"Yes, spot 34 in the underground garage," the agent confirmed.
"And a storage unit."
"Unit 73B. That's my fault for not mentioning it earlier. It came with the property."
Jay looked at him. The agent's expression was completely innocent.
"I see."
He signed anyway. It was too late to object now, and the rest of the contract was reasonable. The money transfer happened instantly. They left the bank and drove to the notary's office. More paperwork. More signatures. The agent produced utility contracts.
"We should handle these now as well," the agent said.
They found a coffee shop and claimed a secluded table.
"Internet providers?" the agent asked, pulling up a comparison sheet.
Jay scanned the options. "This one. The fastest available."
"It comes bundled with full cable television access."
"That's fine. I didn't even have a television before," he said absently.
"You'll enjoy it. The cable package includes quite a lot."
Jay signed the last form. The agent handed him a set of keys.
"Congratulations, Mr. Hall. I’ll forward these forms to the relevant offices. The utilities will be active within twenty-four hours. You're free to move in whenever you'd like."
Jay accepted the small key ring. Two keys, plus a fob for the building entrance.
"Thank you."
"It was my pleasure. If you need anything else, don't hesitate to call."
Jay took a taxi back to his studio. It was nearly eight when he arrived. He set the keys on the kitchen counter and looked around. Boxes everywhere. His entire life packed and ready to go.
He texted Rus. "Saturday move is confirmed. Got the keys today."
The response came quickly. "We will be ready. See you Saturday."
Jay made himself dinner from the leftovers his mother had packed. Just a few more days and he'd leave this place behind. The apartment felt strange now, like it already belonged to someone else.
Thursday morning, he arrived at work early again. When Ian came in, Jay went straight to his office.
"It's done," Jay said from the doorway.
Ian looked up. "The apartment?"
"Everything's signed. I have the keys. I'm moving in Saturday."
"This Saturday? Do you need help?"
"Rus and some friends from the gym are coming with a van. I couldn't possibly ask my boss to help me move boxes."
"I'm not just your boss. I'd like to think I'm also a friend. And soon I'll be your neighbour. But if having me there would be awkward—"
"No," Jay interrupted quickly. "It wouldn't be awkward at all. I'd love for you to be there. I just didn't want to impose."
"You're not imposing."
"Then I'd appreciate your help."
"What time?"
"Afternoon. Probably around two?"
"I'll be there."
Ian felt guilty about the manipulation, but he wanted to be there. This was important. He wouldn't miss it.
At lunch, Jay mentioned it to the others. "I'm moving on Saturday. To the new apartment."
"Finally! Do you need help?" Luc asked. 
"I have a few people coming already. But if you're free—"
"I'm free. I'll be there. I want to see this place anyway."
Zoe looked up from her salad. "I can help too. I don't have plans Saturday afternoon."
"Are you sure? It's just moving boxes."
"Someone needs to make sure Luc doesn't snoop through all your things."
"I'm going to snoop. I'm very curious about how normal people live."
"You're exhausting," Zoe said.
"I'm helpful."
"Alright. Saturday afternoon, then. Thank you," Jay said.
Kim grinned. "Bringing half the office to your new place. This is basically a housewarming party."
"It's not a party. It's just moving boxes."
Jay looked around the table at his coworkers—his friends—and smiled. He'd come to this company alone and uncertain. Now he had more help than he knew what to do with.
Scene 2
Interior design
Jay left work at five on Thursday and headed straight for the upscale department store downtown. He wanted to make his first night in the new apartment special.
The store was enormous. Jay started in the alcohol section. He needed to thank his friends properly for helping with the move. He selected a few bottles of whiskey, wincing slightly at the price but adding it to his cart anyway. Then three different wines. An assortment of crunchy snacks and fancy dips.
But those were for his guests. For himself, for the first night, he wanted something more.
New pyjamas. Soft, comfortable ones in deep blue. Fluffy slippers. A long bathrobe in cream. Brand new toiletries with rich, fresh smells. He opened several bottles, testing the scents—bergamot and cedar and something citrus.
Then towels. Thick, colourful ones. He wanted to coordinate his bathroom, make it bright and cheerful. Spring was coming. He picked lime green as his theme. Bath towels and hand towels in matching shades.
A new iron. A kettle. Pots and pans. A hairdryer. He'd never used one before, but now he had space for it. Why not?
Bath products. Sponges. Bath bombs in a glass jar. Salts in various colours and fragrances. Subtle scent sachets for his new closets. Fragrance sticks for the living room.
He really knocked himself out. He didn't look at the cost at all. His recent paycheck and raise had put him in comfortable territory again. The apartment cost was no longer extravagant. He had savings on top of it. To hell with frugality. He was moving into his own home. He would splurge.
He also bought soil and a fresh pot for his spider plant. He'd researched its preferences beforehand and consulted a store assistant with photos of the plant from different angles.
"This one needs good drainage. And indirect light. This ceramic pot would be perfect size," the assistant said, examining the photos. 
"I'll take it."
His companion deserved upgrades too.
When he took the taxi back to his studio, he was loaded with packages and struggled up the stairs, bags cutting into his hands. He really couldn't wait for a place with an elevator.
He immediately did laundry, loading two full rounds into the machines in the basement. The last rounds of laundry in this laundromat before he ascended to having a personal machine in his bathroom. He packed everything in a fresh box labeled "for the first night."
Then he re-potted his plant, transferring it to the new ceramic pot. It looked dignified now, sitting on the table. He'd need a decorative stand for it as well.
He spent the rest of the evening browsing the website of a big furniture store, selecting small decor items. Bathroom rugs. Cushions. Lighting fixtures. He added them all to his cart, planning to get them the next day.
He had trouble falling asleep that night. His head filled with images of rugs, curtains, cushions and boxes. When the alarm rang at six the next morning, he didn't feel rested at all.
He got up anyway and took a cold shower. He couldn't even drink coffee or have breakfast. Everything was already packed, and he had no food left in the fridge.
He left for work early and bought breakfast at his favourite bakery on the way, which he ate at his desk.
He couldn't focus on work. His attention drifted to clothes hangers and sock storage solutions. Every now and then he'd open the furniture store web page and compare plates and glasses.
Ian approached his desk at one of those moments. Jay had three different dish sets open in separate tabs.
Ian looked at the screen with a barely contained smile. He pointed to one set. "This one looks best."
Jay's face burned. "I'm working. Mostly."
"Of course. The blue trim is elegant."
"You think so?"
"I do."
Ian didn't move away immediately, giving suggestions on various items, and Jay was too excited to feel truly embarrassed. As soon as five came, he almost darted out of the office.
"See you tomorrow!" he called to Ian, Luc, and Zoe.
He took a taxi straight to the furniture store. It was massive, floor after floor of arranged displays. He spent hours touching rugs and measuring tables. He bought everything he'd planned. Bathroom rug to match the towels. Colour-coordinated containers for soap and body wash and shampoo. A really fluffy living room rug and a matching mat for the door. Silky bed sheets and bedding. Pillows and cushions. The decorative stand for his plant. Lightning fixtures that looked fancy. Cleaning and laundry supplies.
The checkout took twenty minutes. The total made him wince, but only slightly. He ate dinner in the store restaurant, surrounded by an entire cart full of items. He'd gotten overexcited.
Getting everything to the taxi was an ordeal. He called for one of the large vehicles and threw apologetic looks at the driver as he loaded the trunk with furniture boxes, some of them quite heavy.
"Moving day?" the driver asked.
"Tomorrow. Just getting ready."
"That's a lot of ready."
Jay laughed breathlessly. "Yes. Thank you for your patience."
"No problem. I've seen worse."
Jay thanked his foresight to start going to the gym. A year of regular workouts definitely helped with the heavy lifting.
As they drove toward his new apartment, the vision of getting everything upstairs seemed bleak. Even with an elevator, it would be difficult. He thought for a moment, then called Ian.
"Hello?"
"Hi. Are you already home?"
"I am. Is something wrong?"
"No. Nothing's wrong. I was wondering if you could come over in fifteen minutes? I'm on my way to the apartment and I have an excessive amount of packages."
He could almost hear Ian grin through the phone. "I'll be there."
When they arrived, Ian was already waiting in front of the building. He was still in his suit with a coat over it. The evening air was cold.
"I'm so sorry. I got overexcited," Jay said immediately.
He opened the trunk. Ian looked at the mountain of boxes and tried to suppress his grin.
"You certainly did."
"I can manage if—"
"Don't be absurd."
Ian began unloading the boxes, wielding the heaviest ones as if they weighed nothing. Jay gave a generous tip to the driver when they were done. They stood in front of the building entrance, looking at the tower of boxes in dismay.
"I got overexcited," Jay said pitifully.
"It's understandable."
"Is it?"
"Yes."
Ian picked up two of the largest boxes. "Let's get these inside."
It took several trips. Jay opened the door with his new key, stepping inside and looking around. The apartment looked dull in the evening lamplight. Bare and impersonal.
"Would you like tea? Or coffee?" Jay asked, then remembered. "Oh. The new kettle is still at my studio.'"
"It's quite alright."
Ian was already taking off his coat and shoes, clearly intending to help unpack.
"You really don't have to—"
"I'm already here. Just tell me where things go."
Jay wanted to protest, but frankly he needed the help. "The bathroom things go in the bathroom. Kitchen items in the kitchen. Everything else in the living room for now."
They got to work. Jay started the first load of laundry in the washing machine—the new sheets he planned to use. The machine had a massive display with countless options. He skimmed the user manual and selected what seemed appropriate, watching with fascination as it started.
"This is so much better than the basement laundromat," he murmured.
"I'd imagine so."
While it washed, he began unloading dishes with Ian and loading them into the dishwasher. He hand-washed two glasses and filled them with tap water.
"The building has filters installed. Apparently the tap water is actually good."
He tasted it. It didn't taste like pipes at all.
"It is," Ian confirmed.
They placed rugs and lights and candle holders. Cushions on the sofa. Remains of boxes went into a pile by the door. Jay moved the laundry to the dryer. They rinsed cupboards, washed dishes, dried them, placed them in their new homes. After over three hours, they looked at their handiwork. The place looked much more cosy.
Then Jay took down the generic wall decor that came with the apartment. Abstract prints in muted colours.
"This is depressing," he said.
"Agreed."
"Let me put these in the storage. Want to see it?"
They went down to the basement level. The storage unit was quite large, placed right next to the designated parking spot. He placed the unwanted art inside and looked around.
"This is huge. I won't have to worry about space for years."
He turned to Ian. He had zero doubts this arrangement was also Ian's doing. The parking spot, the storage, the impossibly good mortgage rate. All of it. He didn't comment. He just sighed deeply and smiled the most radiant smile Ian had ever seen. Jay looked so happy. It was worth everything.
"Thank you. For everything."
"You're welcome."
"We should head back up. The laundry should be done by now," Jay said finally.
"Yes. Of course."
They took the elevator back to the fourth floor. The apartment felt warmer now. Jay pulled the bedding from the dryer. It was still slightly damp but warm and soft.
"I'll iron the sheets properly tomorrow and make the bed," Jay said, checking his watch. "It's already past eleven."
"I should go."
"I couldn't have done this without you."
"I'm glad I could help."
Ian collected his coat and shoes. Jay walked him to the door.
"See you tomorrow?" Ian asked.
"Tomorrow. I'm looking forward to it."
After Ian left, Jay took one last look around. The apartment glowed in the lamplight. He set out the fragrance sticks, placing just three in the container so the scent wouldn't overwhelm, then locked the door behind him.
One more night in the old place. Tomorrow he'd move his boxes and leave for good. Tomorrow, he'd begin a new chapter of his life.
He returned and fell asleep almost immediately, exhausted and content, dreaming of lime-green towels and filtered water and Ian's smile in the warm light of his new home.
Scene 3
The move
Jay woke up at seven on Saturday morning. He set out his clothes for the day, then packed the rest of his things. The sheets from the bed. The last glass. Everything else was already boxed and ready.
He ate breakfast at a nearby café, then stopped at a local bakery on his way back. He bought a proper cake and a selection of pastries for the evening. Then he headed to the gym for one last session in his old neighbourhood.
He was both excited and frigid with anxiety. Just a few more hours.
Rus patted his back reassuringly. "You look like you're about to throw up."
"I might."
"Things will be alright. It's just moving."
"I know."
They arranged to meet at one at Jay's old apartment. Then Jay went back for his last shower there. He wore clean clothes—his new jeans, sneakers, and a fresh gym shirt. He packed everything else, including his still-damp towel in a plastic bag.
He did one last survey of every nook and cranny. The fridge was already cleaned and unplugged. The old appliances donated. He was ready.
Rus arrived punctually at one with Mic and Van in a worn white minivan he'd borrowed from his brother-in-law.
"Ready?" Rus asked.
"Ready."
They got to work right away, moving the boxes down the stairs one by one. They were done in twenty minutes.
"Good thing you don't have many possessions," Mic said, loading the last box.
"Makes the job easier," Van agreed.
As they secured the boxes in the vehicle, Jay did one last inspection of the apartment. He took several photos. The place looked clean, modest, barren. It was a depressing sight.
He picked up his spider plant and joined his friends in the minivan. They departed for his new home.
"There will be additional people waiting for us. My coworkers volunteered to help," Jay said as they drove.
"The more the merrier," Rus said.
Jay sent messages that they were on their way. He sat in the passenger seat while Mic and Van talked in the back. He looked out the window at the passing city. It was late April already. The weather had mercifully gotten a bit warmer.
When they arrived, people were already waiting in front of the building. Zoe and Luc each held a large bag. Ian stood next to several big packages, one of them thin, tall, and wide. He wore jeans, sneakers, and a sweater. It was the first time Jay had seen him wear something that looked like actual casual wear. His hair was slightly ruffled, as if he'd run his hand through it to complete the look. Or perhaps it got tousled when he wrestled with whatever he'd brought.
Next to him stood Leo, equally loaded with big bags.
Jay's face fell.
They climbed out of the van. Ian stepped forward with a proposal.
"We can park in the underground spot. It's close to the elevator. Much easier."
But before they could move, Jay addressed Leo directly. "I don't recall inviting you."
Leo made an innocent face. "I invited myself."
Rus sensed the tension. "Something wrong?"
Jay made a face. "This is Leo. The man who hired me. The son of the owner of the company."
"Oh. Is this the guy who hired you because you're pretty?" Rus looked Leo up and down.
Jay's face went red. "Yes."
Rus burst out laughing. Everyone else joined in, tension breaking like a snapped wire.
Leo looked scandalized. "It seems Jay has been telling stories about me."
"Accurate stories," Jay said.
"I want to make amends to my favourite employee." Leo placed a hand over his chest dramatically. "And for the record, I didn't hire you for your looks. I hired you for your mettle. The HR department already did the preliminary selection. They only sent me presentable candidates. Your looks were just a bonus."
"Questionable judgement on all counts," Jay muttered.
"I'm properly apologetic. I brought housewarming gifts. This seemed like the best moment to fix our relationship. You won't be so heartless as to chase me off, will you?"
Jay sighed. "Suit yourself."
They parked in the underground spot. Jay made proper introductions. Luc already knew Rus and his friends from the fencing debacle, but the others were new.
"I thought Ian was the boss," Rus said, looking confused.
"I am," Ian confirmed. "The hierarchy is rather relaxed."
Rus eyed Ian suspiciously but said nothing.
They began taking boxes up. With so many people and the elevator, the entire process took record time. Everyone made themselves comfortable inside while Jay directed which boxes went where.
People volunteered to help unpack. Jay eventually gave in and started assigning tasks.
Ian and Leo went to the bedroom to unpack clothes. Zoe and Luc dealt with the dishes. Rus, Mic, and Van stayed in the living room unpacking books and various trinkets.
Jay moved around everyone, instructing and coordinating. He placed his spider plant in a prominent spot on the bookshelf. He shooed Luc away from trying to rummage through his underwear drawer.
"I was just trying to help organize," Luc protested.
"Organize somewhere else."
Jay watched for a moment as Ian expertly hung his suits in the massive closet, organized by colour and formality level. Then he sorted the ties with equal expertise.
Rus was folding the empty boxes into a neat pile near the door. Very soon everything was unpacked, and things without a final destination got shoved into the entrance closet for Jay's further decision.
Jay boiled water in the new kettle for refreshments. As the work was drawing to a close, Leo made an offer.
"I'll order takeout from a local place I know and like. My treat."
Jay accepted reluctantly. He'd wanted to do that himself.
The food arrived as they were finishing up, barely past four. The entire production took less than three hours. Jay was impressed by what a big and motivated party could accomplish in such a short time.
They took places around the apartment. The dining table could fit four people comfortably. Rus, Mic, Van, and Luc sat there. Ian and Leo sat on the sofa. Zoe took the armchair. Jay sat on a cushion in front of the coffee table.
Jay insisted they unload the takeout onto proper plates.
"I bought new plates and I want to test drive them."
"Fair enough," Luc said.
Jay had bought everything in sets of eight, thinking of the size of their team at work. It perfectly matched the number of people present.
The food was Mediterranean but very diverse. Leo had ordered enough for twice as many people.
"At least you'll have leftovers to last you a couple of days while you're getting used to the place," Leo said.
Jay opened the alcohol bottles. Everyone poured themselves their preferred drinks. Zoe made a cocktail. Luc and Leo hoarded wine. Rus's team and Ian preferred straight whiskey. Jay poured himself a glass of wine.
Someone took photos. Laughter and chatter filled the apartment. It was small, and such a crowd barely fit, but somehow it didn't feel cramped. It felt convivial. Then the gifts were handed out.
Rus and his friends produced a bag with fitness equipment. A yoga mat, dumbbells, stretching bands.
"Don't neglect your form now that you won't have me regularly watching after you," Rus said.
"I won't. Promise."
Zoe brought several big boxes of different candles. "There's always a time for mood lighting." She also produced a big cake.
Luc handed over a large, modern, abstractly shaped crystal vase and a bouquet of fresh flowers that he'd somehow managed to keep hidden but that hadn't withered at all.
"It's too big for the place," Jay said, setting it on the coffee table.
"It adds character," Luc insisted.
"It does," Zoe agreed.
Leo took out the newest generation console in a limited edition version with a whole box of accessories. Jay looked at it suspiciously.
"I heard you have younger siblings who will definitely visit. At least you should keep them entertained."
From the second bag he produced an entire selection of clearly expensive alcohol and soft drinks. Then he reached for the largest bag resting in the corner. Out came a proper sound system, complete with surprisingly large speakers. "The least we could do was make it a proper party."
Rus and his friends cheered. Jay looked miserable. "I don't want a police visit on my first night here."
He got ignored. The party started in earnest.
Ian tried to comfort him. "The soundproofing in these buildings is great. It's a good opportunity to test it."
Ian then handed over his gift. The big box turned out to be a painting. It was from the same artist Jay had enjoyed at the art show last year, a piece vaguely similar to the one Ian had in his living room.
Jay was touched. "This is beautiful."
They hung it where the generic art had been before in the central living room. It livened up the place.
Ian also handed him a huge bag of various groceries. "For a good start."
And another bag of bed sheets and towels in coordinated colours. "For when you'd like to change the colour scheme."
He also produced another large cake.
"That's three cakes," Zoe observed.
"We'll manage," Luc said confidently.
Jay set everything down. He looked at Ian, then at everyone else in the apartment. At the gifts. At the laughter. At the home they'd helped him create. He was so touched by everyone's generosity that he didn't even complain about them getting rowdy. Even Leo seemed less offensive somehow.
"Thank you. All of you. Really."
"You're welcome," Zoe said.
"It's what friends do," Luc added.
Leo raised his glass. "To Jay's new home."
"To Jay's new home!" everyone echoed.
Jay raised his own glass and drank. The wine was good. The food was good. The company was good. Everything was good.
Scene 4
Housewarming party
The party continued. Luc and Rus plugged in the audio system. Jay pulled out his laptop to play some music.
Luc's eyes widened. "That's a top-of-the-line model."
Jay looked sheepish. "It's courtesy of our foreign clients we signed last year."
"Kim would die from jealousy if he saw it."
"Best not to show it to him then."
Music filled the apartment—something upbeat that made people start moving unconsciously.
In the meantime, Mic was attempting to charm Zoe. She listened politely for approximately thirty seconds before shutting him down completely.
"I appreciate the interest, but no thank you."
Mic retreated to the balcony railing to sulk over his whiskey. Leo joined him, clapping him on the shoulder. "Chin up. Plenty of other opportunities."
"She didn't even hesitate."
Inside, Jay found Zoe by the drinks table. "I'm so sorry about Mic. He's absolutely a menace. Don't feel guilty about telling him off."
Zoe smiled. "I don't. I've handled worse."
Van had migrated to the couch next to Ian, examining him with open curiosity. "You're pretty well-built for a banker."
"I work out regularly."
"Yeah? What's your routine?"
Ian described his training schedule. Van's face lit up with immediate interest. They began comparing their fitness achievements and diet plans. Van got more and more animated by the minute. Hydration, protein, creatine. Jay caught only the general idea and shook his head in theatrical resignation. Rus joined their conversation as well. Soon the couch and its orbit turned into a fitness discussion club.
Zoe turned to Jay. "Want to make a quick run to the store? We need ice for drinks. And I want coconut milk for cocktails."
"Let's go then."
They left for the nearby grocery store. When they returned twenty minutes later, they saw Van without his shirt, flexing his muscles while Luc and Leo cheered enthusiastically.
Jay facepalmed. "What's going on?"
Rus explained happily. "Van is doing a demonstration of the superiority of his particular workout philosophy."
Jay moved to the kitchen area with Zoe and their shopping. Zoe made fancy drinks for everyone interested, which was only herself, Jay, and Luc. Rus joined them but only to add ice to his whiskey glass. Ian came as well.
They began chatting more seriously. The alcohol seemed to make Rus more blunt.
"You're Jay's boss, right?"
"I am."
"You've been making Jay stressed. Working unreasonable hours. That's poor management."
Jay tried to intervene. "Rus—"
"I'm not wrong."
Leo looked highly amused.
Ian's expression was calm. "You're right. It was my failure. We're expanding the team to ensure the workload is more equally divided. We should have done this much earlier."
Rus approved. "Good that you at least see your faults."
"Rus, please—" Jay tried again.
They both completely disregarded him.
"You're different than I imagined. I can see why Jay is so fiercely loyal. A man with such gym discipline must have his priorities in order."
Ian looked briefly startled, then recovered. "Thank you."
Meanwhile, Luc started arm wrestling with Van on the small coffee table. He lost spectacularly.
"I told Jay he needed more space for entertaining. If the living room had enough room I'd definitely win," Luc grumbled.
Everyone laughed.
The scene continued to be rowdy with music and cheering. Jay went outside to do a loudness check. The soundproofing was impressive. Maybe they really wouldn't have the cops called on them.
Eventually Leo excused himself. "I should head out. Thank you for tolerating me."
"Thank you for coming. And for the gifts," Jay said diplomatically.
"You're welcome. Enjoy the console."
Zoe left shortly after. "This was fun. Your place is lovely, Jay."
"Thanks for the candles."
The rest stayed and partied for two more hours. Once ten came and they made no move to leave, Ian noticed Jay getting sleepy. He ushered the company away.
"Time to go," Ian said firmly.
"Already?" Rus protested.
"It's getting late."
"We can come to my place. I've got more whiskey." Luc said.
Jay handed them a box with leftover cake and two bottles of whiskey from Leo's stash.
"Thanks for everything," Jay said.
"We'll return for the car some other time," Rus said.
Then they were gone, and Jay was alone with Ian in a completely wrecked apartment. But Jay was in such a good mood he didn't mind. He looked around at the chaos and smiled.
Ian began collecting dishes. Jay moved to protest, but Ian was already loading the dishwasher.
"You don't have to," Jay protested.
"I want to."
"You've already done so much."
"It's just cleaning."
They worked together. Leftovers into the fridge, dishes into the dishwasher, trash into bags. Ian accompanied Jay to the trash building, a separate structure near the street, locked and surprisingly orderly.
"This is so much better than my old place," Jay said, impressed.
"It's a well-maintained building."
They returned to wipe the surfaces, wash and store plates, and load another dishwasher full of glasses. Jay was amazed at the utility.
"A washing machine. A dishwasher. It's like living in the future."
"You should get a robot vacuum."
Jay laughed. "That's ridiculous."
Ian collected his coat and moved toward the door. "I should go."
"Stay a bit longer?" Jay asked.
"It's late. You're tired."
"I know. But I don't feel like ending the evening quite yet. My first night in my new home. It feels weirdly scary. What if I wake up and it turns out to be a dream?"
"It's not a dream."
"But what if it is?"
Ian turned back. "I can stay if it will make you more comfortable."
"The couch is definitely much more comfortable than my last one. And I definitely liked it much more when you were there when I woke up hungover than the alternative of having to get my water myself."
"You're drunk."
"Only a little."
"I'll stay then."
Jay suddenly remembered something. "I wanted to iron the sheets before sleeping."
"I'll do it. You should shower."
"Bath. I want to try the bath."
"Then take a bath."
Jay disappeared into the bathroom. Soon after, steam began seeping under the door, filling the air with the scent of at least seven bath products. The whole place started smelling like an expensive perfumery.
Ian found the iron and ironing board in the entrance closet, set them up in the bedroom, and got to work on the sheets. Then he made the bed properly.
The bathroom door opened eventually. Jay emerged in soft new pyjamas, wearing his bathrobe, a lime-green towel wrapped around his hair, fluffy slippers on his feet. He looked absolutely blissful.
"That was amazing. The bath is huge. I could practically swim in it."
"I'm glad you enjoyed it."
Jay moved to the kitchen and began heating milk in a small pot, adding honey and spices. He poured two mugs and brought them to the couch. They sat together, drinking warm milk like children. The apartment was quiet now, music off. Jay's eyes were drooping. He finished his milk, brushed his teeth, and stumbled toward the bedroom.
"Thank you," he called out. "For everything."
"You're welcome. Sleep well."
Ian tidied up the rest of the place. He checked that everything was secure. The door was locked. The windows were closed. The kitchen was clean.
Then he dropped onto the couch. It really was bigger and more comfortable than Jay's old one. It could even be opened to make a proper bed, though Ian didn't bother. He lay down as he was, fully dressed, and stared at the ceiling.
It had been a great day. Jay was happy. Safe. Surrounded by people who cared about him. Living in a beautiful space that he could truly call home.
Ian closed his eyes and let himself feel the joy of having helped make this happen. Of being here for Jay's first night. Of being trusted enough to stay.
Scene 5
First morning
Jay woke up the next morning and groaned immediately. His head felt like someone had stuffed it with cotton and then set it on fire. The light coming through the window was too bright, the world too loud, everything too much. A voice cut through the fog.
"Here. Take this."
He lifted his head with difficulty. Ian stood beside the bed, holding out a glass of water and a painkiller.
Jay accepted both, swallowing the pill with a croaking "Thank you."
"You're welcome."
Jay collapsed back against the pillow. "I can't believe I made you babysit my hangover again."
Ian laughed. "I'm starting to have some experience in this matter. I'll make coffee."
He left the bedroom. Jay lay there for another minute, gathering the strength to move. Eventually he managed to sit up, then stand. He shuffled out of the bedroom on unsteady feet. He made his way to the armchair and collapsed into it, pulling his legs up under himself and burying his head in his arms. The nausea was tolerable if he stayed very still. The headache was another matter.
"It will pass," Ian said softly.
Jay groaned.
Ian placed a coffee in front of him and sat on the couch on the opposite side. "How was your first night in your own home?"
Jay lifted his head and smiled wistfully. "It still doesn't feel real."
