Ian thought he looked adorable in his pajamas. So pitiful and fragile and vulnerable. His defenses were down completely.
Eventually, Jay stood up when his headache began to subside slightly. "I'm going to take a shower."
"Take your time."
Jay disappeared into the bathroom and turned the water as cold as he could stand. He stayed under it for a long time, letting it wash away the worst of the hangover. When he emerged, he was wrapped in his bathrobe with his hair dripping.
"Better?" Ian asked.
"Much better. I'm glad I didn't drink too much. This is bearable."
They heated up leftovers from the party for breakfast. Jay ate slowly, testing his stomach's tolerance. It held up well enough.
"Do you want to sit on the balcony?" Jay asked when they finished eating.
Ian looked toward the windows. "It's quite cold out."
"I know. But I want to try it anyway. We can make tea. Something warming."
"Alright."
Jay got dressed and prepared root spices tea while Ian cleared the dishes. They huddled themselves in colourful soft blankets and sat on the patio couch outside, watching the river and the gray skies. The weather was miserable by any objective measure. Cold, overcast, threatening rain. But Jay thought it looked beautiful. His headache and nausea receded. He'd never felt happier.
He stole glances at Ian's face as he sat wrapped in the deep burgundy blanket and watched the weather. Jay was glad he got to share this moment with him. His first morning in his new home. He would treasure this for the rest of his life. They sat until their fingers grew stiff and their noses turned red from the cold. Finally Ian got up.
"We should go inside before we freeze."
"Probably wise."
They abandoned the balcony and returned inside, both smiling at their red faces.
"That was ridiculous," Ian said, rubbing his hands together.
"Completely worth it."
Inside, warmth slowly returned to their extremities. Jay made more tea. Then something occurred to him.
In the apartment, two spare sets of keys had been waiting for him, along with a folder of useful administrative information. He picked up one set and held it out to Ian. "Would you hold onto this? In case of emergency? I don't really have anyone else in the city."
Ian took the key, trying to act normal despite his heart contracting painfully in his chest.
"Of course. You can list me as an emergency contact too. I'm your boss after all."
Such complete faith. Ian would make sure to prove himself worthy of it.
"Thank you."
"You're welcome."
Eventually Ian said his farewells and left. Jay spent the rest of Sunday reshuffling things in his cupboards and checking interior decor and appliances online.
All of his belongings were already here, yet not even half of the storage was filled. His clothes were neatly arranged in the bedroom closet with plenty of space to spare. He could finally buy casual clothes. He had enough counter space to buy a coffee maker. All of it was unreal.
Rus came by in the evening to collect his car.
"We all crashed at Luc's place," Rus explained. "Just sobered up enough to drive now."
"I'm glad you had fun."
"It was a great party. Your place is amazing."
"Thank you for helping with the move."
"Anytime. Keep in touch, alright?"
"I will."
The burgundy blanket that Ian had used on the balcony smelled like him. Jay noticed in the evening when he was folding it. It was a divine, comforting smell. Jay took the blanket to bed with him, hugging it close. He fell asleep like that, wrapped in Ian's scent, feeling safe. He slept well that night.
The next day was Jay's first workday commuting from the new place. He got up at six as always, feeling unusually well rested. He drank coffee from the new kettle, leisurely ate leftovers, and prepared for work.
His commute was now ten minutes shorter overall. The metro ride was only ten minutes, but the walk to the metro was a bit longer. He left his home at seven as he always did and arrived at work at seven twenty-five. He still didn't have an optimal schedule and felt awkward with the new route, but he was also excited.
At work he talked about nothing else but his new apartment.
"The balcony has a view of the river," he told Ela.
"That's lovely."
"The dishwasher is incredible," he told Luc.
"Welcome to modern living."
"I have an entire closet just for clothes," he told Amy.
"That's practical."
At five, Jay went to Ian's office. Ian was reviewing documents, but looked up when Jay entered.
"Do you need something?"
"Not really. I just wanted to talk about coffee makers."
"Coffee makers?"
"I'm trying to decide between models. Do you have time?"
"I have time."
They spent two hours discussing coffee makers, storage solutions, and kitchen organization. Jay felt a bit embarrassed to take so much of Ian's time with such trivial matters, but the excitement overrode the guilt.
In the following days, the initial excitement waned to manageable levels. On Tuesday he left at five with a gym bag in hand. He took the metro back to his old neighbourhood.
Rus looked surprised when he walked into the gym. "You came back."
"I still have a paid membership. It would be a waste not to use it. I'll keep returning until the end of the month at least."
"Fair enough."
They chatted while working out. Rus brought up Jay's coworkers.
"I understand now why you're so fond of your job despite its unreasonableness. Your coworkers seem to be great people."
"They truly are. The whole team is amazing."
"And your boss isn't as scary as I expected."
"Ian's not scary at all."
"He's very dedicated to fitness. I respect that."
"He's dedicated to everything he does."
For the rest of the week, Ian was away in the afternoons holding interviews with candidates for senior analysts. Jay left at five since there was nothing urgent to do. Staying in an empty office to tackle less urgent tasks held no appeal.
Instead, he explored his new neighbourhood. He walked through parks. He cooked dinner in his new kitchen, experimenting with recipes he'd never had space to try before. He sat on the balcony with warm milk and honey and spices, watching the river and the sky.
This was so much better than his old place. Better than anything he'd imagined having. He kept expecting to wake up and find it was all a dream. But each morning he woke up in his new bed, in his new apartment, and it was still real.
Clarity
Chapter 36: Foolish fantasies
Scene 1
Ray Shore
May arrived with gentle warmth and clear skies. The endless rain finally stopped. Trees bloomed in full force, painting the streets in white and pink. Jay walked to the metro each morning through the park near his apartment, listening to birds sing in the branches overhead. The air smelled fresh and green. Everything felt new.
He stopped one morning to watch sunlight filter through the blossoms. A month ago he'd been huddled against the rain, miserable and lost. Now he stood in his new neighbourhood, on his way to a job he'd chosen to keep, with a home that was actually his. The difference was staggering.
On Monday morning, Ian called a team meeting before the work began. Everyone gathered in the conference room looking curious.
"I wanted to give you advance notice. We're bringing on a new senior analyst today. His name is Ray Shore. He's a former classmate of mine and exceptionally skilled at analysis."
Amy looked pleased. "That's excellent news."
"There's something you should know beforehand. Ray is brilliant, but his personality can be difficult. He's blunt. He has no social graces whatsoever. He may come across as rude."
The team exchanged glances.
"How rude are we talking?" Kim asked.
"Not malicious. Just direct to the point of being uncomfortable. He doesn't engage in pleasantries or small talk. He has no interest in office politics or relationship building."
"Sounds delightful," Luc muttered.
"I can vouch for his character. He's not cruel or unkind. He simply doesn't see the point of social conventions. Try not to take it personally."
"We'll manage," Max said. "If you trust him, that's enough for us."
"As long as he pulls his weight, I don't care if he never says hello," Kim added.
"That's the attitude I'm hoping for. Give him space to work his way. He'll demonstrate his value quickly."
Ray Shore arrived at nine thirty. He was tall and solidly built, with shoulder-length black hair that looked hastily combed. His suit fit properly but somehow still managed to appear rumpled. His expression was almost bored. He would have been handsome if he'd shown any animation at all.
Ian met him at the door. "Ray. Good to see you."
"Ian." Ray's tone was flat.
"Let me show you around."
Ian introduced Ray to the team one by one. Ray made no attempt at conversation. He didn't smile. He barely looked at anyone directly. When Ian finished the introductions, Ray immediately claimed the desk furthest from everyone else.
Kim brought over his new equipment. Ray took it without a word of thanks and started setting everything up. Within minutes he was clicking through files, completely absorbed.
At noon, the team headed out for lunch at a nearby seafood restaurant. Ray remained typing at his desk.
"Should we invite him?" Zoe asked.
"I don't think he'll come. But someone should ask," Max said.
Luc walked over. "We're heading to lunch. Want to join us?"
Ray didn't look up from his screen. "No."
"Alright. See you later."
Ray said nothing. Luc returned and they left together.
At the restaurant, Ela ordered a shrimp dish and smiled broadly. "The weather is finally perfect. I've been waiting for this."
"The new hire is certainly interesting," Amy said.
"He's quiet," Luc said. "I'll give him that."
"If Ian brought him personally, he must be exceptional," Max said. "We'll just have to get used to his quirks."
Kim stabbed at his fish. "As long as he's competent, I don't care if he never speaks to us. Honestly, it might be refreshing."
Ela laughed. "You're terrible. But I agree. If he's as good as Ian says, maybe our overtime sessions will finally end."
Everyone perked up at that thought.
"That would be incredible," Zoe said.
"I'll happily tolerate any personality if it means reasonable hours," Luc said.
They ate their meals in better spirits. The sunshine streaming through the windows helped with the mood.
In the afternoon, Ray spent three hours in Ian's office. The door remained unlocked. Jay could hear every word from his desk.
Ray's questions were detailed and relentless. He demanded specifics about every aspect of their methodology, their data sources, their analysis frameworks. He challenged assumptions. He pointed out potential weaknesses. He showed absolutely no deference to Ian's position.
"That model is outdated," Ray said at one point.
"It serves our purposes," Ian replied.
"Barely. You should update it."
"We've considered that."
"Then why haven't you?"
"Resource allocation."
"That's a poor excuse."
Ian answered everything patiently. Jay was both impressed and slightly terrified. He'd never heard anyone speak to Ian so bluntly.
Finally Ray closed his notebook. "That covers the immediate concerns. I'll begin the portfolio analysis tomorrow."
"Thank you. Let me know if you need anything else."
Ray left the office without further comment. He returned to his desk, made some notes, then shut down his computer. At exactly four o'clock he picked up his bag and walked out.
Luc stared after him. "Did he just leave at four on his first day?"
"Apparently," Kim said.
"Bold."
The rest of the team began departing around five. Jay stayed longer. He had correspondence to finish and several reports to format. The office slowly emptied until only he and Ian remained. At six, Jay walked to Ian's office. Ian was reviewing documents, but looked up immediately.
"Yes?"
"I just wanted to ask about Ray."
"What would you like to know?"
"You mentioned you were classmates. Were you close?"
"Not particularly. We were civil, but not friends."
"What was he like back then?"
"Brilliant. Uncompromising. He was there on a full scholarship, top of the class in every subject that mattered. He had no patience for the social aspects of university life."
"Was he always this blunt?"
"Always. Most students from wealthy families found him insufferable. He made no effort to network or build relationships. He treated professors and fellow students with equal indifference."
Jay tried to imagine Ian in that environment. Ian, who must have been one of those wealthy students. "Did that cause problems?"
"Constantly. He made many enemies. People who could have helped his career later. He didn't care."
"Why not?"
"I don't think career advancement interested him. He studied because he enjoyed it, not because he wanted status or wealth. After graduation his employment history was erratic. Brilliant performance followed by sudden departures."
"Because of his personality?"
"Partially. He refused to participate in office politics. When management made decisions he considered stupid, he said so. Directly. That doesn't tend to lead to promotion."
Someone who valued competence over everything else, who couldn't be bothered with games or social niceties. It explained the bare-bones interactions today.
"Do you think he'll fit here?" Jay asked.
"I do. This team is full of people who care more about results than appearances. Amy, Max, Luc, all of them. They'll appreciate his abilities once they see them. And Ray will appreciate working somewhere that doesn't punish him for being himself."
"You really trust him."
"I trust his competence absolutely. And I know he's not malicious. Difficult, yes. Unpleasant, sometimes. But not cruel."
"Thank you for explaining," Jay said.
"Of course. If you have concerns about working with him, let me know."
"I don't have concerns. Just curiosity."
"That's understandable."
Jay returned to his desk and worked for another hour before heading home. On the metro, he thought about what Ian had said. Ray had been there at university with Ian. He'd seen Ian during those years when Ian was still one of the rich kids, before everything fell apart.
What had Ian been like then? More carefree, probably. Before all the wounds that shaped him into the man he was now. Before all the pain that carved him into someone so contained.
Jay wanted to know. He wanted to understand every part of Ian's history, every piece that made him who he was.
He wanted to ask Ray about it, but it was obvious Ray wasn't someone who would answer that kind of question. He barely spoke at all, let alone about personal matters.
Jay got off at his stop and walked through the park toward home. The evening was warm and pleasant. Birds still sang in the trees. The light was golden and soft.
He thought about Ian sitting in a university classroom, surrounded by wealthy peers, while Ray sat somewhere nearby, glaring at everyone with contempt. The image made him smile.
He unlocked his apartment and stepped inside. The space still felt new and wonderful. He made dinner, ate on the balcony, watched the river turn dark as the sun set.
His life was good. Better than it had any right to be. This was his home now. His space. And somehow, Ian was woven through all of it. His neighbour, his boss, his friend. The person Jay couldn't stop thinking about, no matter how hard he tried.
Scene 2
Work-life balance
The next morning Ray arrived at exactly eight o'clock. He said nothing to anyone, claimed his desk, put on headphones, and began working. His fingers moved across the keyboard, pausing occasionally. Otherwise he was motionless.
At noon, Luc approached his desk again. Ray pulled off one side of his headphones.
"Lunch?" Luc asked.
"No."
"You sure? We're trying a new place today."
"I'm sure. Stop asking. I won't join your lunch outings. Or any other outings."
"Fair enough. What about the company retreat later this month?"  
"Under no circumstances would I join a company retreat."  
"Got it. Message received."
Ray put his headphones back on. Luc returned to the group and they headed out together. At the restaurant, Luc recounted the conversation with obvious amusement.
"He told me to stop asking. Just like that."
"At least he's clear about his boundaries," Amy said.
"I couldn't help myself. I asked if he'd at least come to the company retreat this month."
"You didn't," Zoe said.
"I did. He looked offended. Said under no circumstances would he attend a company retreat."
Max laughed. "I can respect that level of commitment to solitude."
"It'll take some getting used to. But I suppose we just won't talk to him at all," Ela said.
"Seems that way. I'm fine with it," Kim said.
They ate their meals in good spirits. Ray's peculiarities were becoming a great source of entertainment.
Back at the office, the afternoon passed quietly. At five, most of the team began packing up. Jay remained at his desk, working through correspondence.
Ian appeared beside him. "No gym today?"
"Not today. The commute is too long now. Going all the way across the city twice in one evening isn't ideal."
"What will you do instead?"
"I'll still go on Saturdays. And I'm thinking about working out at home. I have the space now, and some equipment. Maybe I'll try running too. The area seems good for it."
"There are excellent routes near the river. I run there sometimes."
"Could you recommend some?"
"Of course. The path along the south bank is about five kilometres. There's also a loop through the park that's shorter but has more hills. I'll write them down for you."
"Thank you."
The rest of the week followed the same pattern. Ray arrived at eight and left at four. He ate lunch at his desk, leaving only for coffee and the bathroom. Occasionally he went to Ian's office for clarification, but otherwise worked independently. He seemed to be conducting an audit of their entire methodology, checking historical data and reviewing old models.
On Thursday, Ray sat next to Max for an hour. Jay couldn't help overhearing.
"Walk me through your portfolios," Ray said.
"What specifically do you want to know?"
"Everything. Start with your initial assessment."
Max explained. Ray asked follow-up questions. They were nuanced and detailed. Jay understood maybe half of what they discussed. The technical depth was beyond him.
Friday morning, Ray repeated the process with Amy. He sat beside her desk and worked through her portfolio management decisions from the past year. Amy responded with the same patience Max had shown, explaining her reasoning and providing documentation when requested.
In the afternoon, Ray went to Ian's office with a folder of notes. Jay could hear them through the open door.
"I've completed the initial assessment," Ray said.
"What did you find?"
"The core methodology is sound. There are inconsistencies in documentation and some classification delays, but nothing that compromises the analysis quality. I have recommendations for improvement."
"I'd like to hear them."
"Your sector rotation model needs updating."
"I'm aware."
"When are you planning to address it?"
"Next quarter."
"That's too late. You should prioritize it."
They discussed timelines, methodologies, implementation strategies. Ian gave Ray specific direction on which models to update and how to approach them. Ray took detailed notes.
When Ray returned to his desk, he typed frantically for the rest of the afternoon. At four o'clock exactly, he stood up and left.
Luc called after him cheerfully. "Have a great weekend, Ray!"
Ray didn't acknowledge him. The door closed.
Luc turned to Kim with a grin. "I'm going to keep doing that."
"Why?" Kim asked.
"It's funny."
The office gradually emptied. Jay stayed, as he had every evening that week. The backlog was mostly cleared now, but he was reluctant to leave early. He told himself it was to stay on top of correspondence, but really he just preferred being here while Ian was here.
At six, everyone else had gone. Ian emerged from his office and walked to Jay's desk.
"What are your impressions of Ray?" Ian asked.
Jay looked up. "I'm not sure yet. He's definitely a personality."
"That's diplomatic."
"We'll only know if he's a good match once he actually starts producing work. For now, he seems to believe strongly in work-life balance."
Ian laughed. "That's one way to put it. Ray refuses to work overtime. He expects to receive his contractual pay for his contractual hours, and not a second longer."
"That's unusual in finance."
"Very unusual. If there's too much workload, he considers that a management issue, not his problem."
"I can see how that wouldn't make him popular."
"I don't disagree with his philosophy. My own workaholism is unhealthy. The frequent overtime during busy periods is unreasonable. Perhaps having someone like Ray will help us improve."
"Maybe."
Ian leaned against the edge of Jay's desk. "I wanted to ask you something. Next week we're holding the final round of interviews for junior analyst positions. I'd like you to join me."
Jay stared at him. "Join you for interviews?"
"Yes."
"I don't have any hiring experience."
"I'll send you the CVs on Monday to review. These candidates will be doing many of the tasks you currently handle, so it seems reasonable to involve you in the selection."
"What tasks?"
"Printing, proofreading, basic formatting, document organization. The office-wide administrative work. You're supposed to be my personal assistant, not assist the entire department. Having dedicated junior staff will let you focus on more important responsibilities."
Jay felt his stomach twist. "I don't mind the administrative work."
"I know you don't. But it's not the best use of your abilities. This way the senior analysts can work more efficiently without constantly pulling you away from your primary duties."
"I see."
"We'll discuss it more next week. I just wanted to give you advance notice."
"Thank you."
Ian returned to his office. Jay sat at his desk, staring at his computer screen. Junior analysts to handle administrative work. Work that he did now. Work that made him useful to everyone, not just Ian.
He'd always been the junior before. The new hire. The inexperienced one. Now apparently he was senior enough to help hire people beneath him.
He packed his bag at seven and said goodbye to Ian. The ride home felt strange. At his apartment he ate dinner while his mind was elsewhere. On Saturday he took the metro across the city to his old gym. Rus greeted him at the entrance.
"You actually came back," Rus said.
"I said I would."
Halfway through the workout, Jay brought up the junior analyst situation.
"It's weird. I'm supposed to help interview them."
"Why is that weird?"
"I've never hired anyone. I don't know what I'm doing."
Rus laughed. "You're thirty, Jay. Almost thirty-one. This is natural progression."
"I don't feel ready."
"Nobody ever feels ready. You'll figure it out."
"What if I make a mistake?"
"Then you'll learn from it. Stop overthinking."
Jay completed his set in silence. Rus watched him with amusement.
"Before you know it, you'll be a director," Rus said.
Jay sent him a look. Rus burst out laughing and patted his back.
"Relax. You're doing fine."
At home that evening, Jay thought about what Rus had said. He was thirty years old. He'd been working at Turner & Associates for almost two years. He had his own apartment now, a permanent contract, a senior title. Of course he wasn't a junior anymore.
New life, new responsibilities. That was how it worked.
He went to his bookshelf and pulled out the leadership textbooks he'd bought months ago when he wanted to better understand Ian's work. He'd never read them properly, just skimmed through sections here and there.
Now he sat down and started from the beginning. Recruitment techniques, interview strategies, candidate evaluation. He read through Saturday evening and all day Sunday, absorbing as much as he could.
He wasn't going to make hiring decisions. Ian would do that. But he at least wanted to understand the theoretical framework. He wanted to be useful, not just present. By Sunday evening he'd filled a notebook with observations and questions. He felt slightly more prepared, though still uncertain.
Everything was changing. His responsibilities, his role, his understanding of where he fit in the company. He just had to keep moving forward, even when he didn't feel ready.
Scene 3
Interviews
During their morning briefing, Ian handed him a stack of files. Jay felt deeply uncertain. 
"These are the CVs for this week's candidates. Twenty in total. I wanted to give you some context before we begin. What we're looking for, what to watch for."
"Alright."
"All of these candidates are qualified on paper. They have the degrees, the grades, the technical knowledge. That's not what we need to evaluate."
"Then what do we evaluate?"
"Whether they'll be a good match for this team. This industry attracts sharks. People who are ruthless, calculating, willing to do anything to advance. I'm allergic to that sort of person."
Jay thought about his own hiring, about Leo's scheme, about how Ian had been burned before. "I understand."
"I don't expect saints. But I want people who aren't mercenary about life. People who care about more than just advancement and money."
"How do we tell the difference?"
"That's the difficult part. Everyone will try to make a good impression. We need to look past that impression and try to gauge how they actually operate. Kindness can be indistinguishable from manipulation. Bluntness is easier to trust than calculated charm. But the nastiest people are often the best at hiding behind a pleasant facade."
"That sounds impossible."
"It's challenging. Very few people are like Ray, who show their hand immediately and never hide who they truly are."
Jay opened the first folder. The CV inside was impressive. Prestigious university, excellent grades, internships at major firms. "These credentials are all better than mine."
"Credentials don't determine success here. Character does."
"I don't feel qualified to judge that."
"You will. Trust your instincts. Pay attention to how people present themselves, what they choose to emphasize, how they respond to unexpected questions. The interviews start at four this afternoon."
Jay spent day hour reviewing the CVs. Each one was more impressive than the last. Top universities, honours programs, relevant experience. He made notes on each, but his observations felt inadequate. He couldn't tell who would actually be good at the job.
At four o'clock, Ian collected him from his desk. "Ready?"
"Not really."
"You'll do fine."
They walked to a small conference room near the lobby. Jay recognized it immediately. This was where his own interview had taken place two years ago. He'd been terrified then, awed that he was even allowed inside this building. Now he sat on the opposite side of the table. The reversal felt surreal.
A woman from HR joined them. She was middle-aged, elegantly dressed, holding a tablet and several folders. Jay knew her name was Lea, but they never spoke before.
"Shall we begin?" Lea said.
The first candidate arrived at four. A young man, wearing a perfectly tailored suit. He confidently shook hands with everyone.
Ian gestured to the seat across from them. "Please, make yourself comfortable. Would you like water, coffee, tea?"
"Water would be great, thank you."
Jay poured water from the pitcher on the table and passed it across. The candidate thanked him with a bright smile.
Lea began with the standard questions. Background, education, previous experience. The candidate answered smoothly.
Ian took over with more specific questions. "Tell me about a time when you disagreed with a decision your supervisor made. How did you handle it?"
"At my previous internship, my supervisor wanted to use a particular risk assessment model that I felt was inappropriate for the client's portfolio structure. I prepared an alternative analysis and presented it privately. We discussed the merits of both approaches, and ultimately she decided to use a hybrid method incorporating elements of my suggestion."
"How did that make you feel?"
"I appreciated that she was willing to consider alternative viewpoints."
Jay wrote in his notebook: seems confident, answers well prepared.
The interview continued for twenty minutes. When the candidate left, Lea made notes on her tablet.
"Initial impressions?" Ian asked.
"Very polished. Perhaps too polished," Lea said.
"I agree," Ian said. "Jay?"
"He seemed nervous despite the confident delivery. His hands were shaking slightly when he drank the water."
"Good observation."
They saw three more candidates that afternoon. Each interview was similar. Jay took notes on each one, recording small details. One candidate talked too much. Another made consistent eye contact but never smiled.
By six they were finished. Four candidates, two hours total including discussion time. In Ian's office afterwards, Jay reviewed his notes. He'd written observations next to each name. Seems nervous. Talks excessively. Very composed. Self-assured, maybe too much.
"What are your impressions overall?" Ian asked.
Jay looked up. "Honestly? I have no idea who's best. The talkative one seemed the most competent, but also excessively self-assured. Apart from that, they all seemed very similar."
"I had the same impression. It's the first day, though. We'll see more variety as the week continues."
"Do you already have preferences?"
"I'm especially wary of people who appear too charming. I can't have that on my team."
Ian was completely serious, and Jay understood exactly where that reservation came from. He suspected he'd kept his own job partially because of how uncharming he'd been, but he didn't say that aloud.
Four candidates arrived each afternoon starting at four o'clock for the rest of the week. Jay had developed his own evaluation system. He paid attention to how they spoke about their previous workplaces, how they answered questions about failure, what they emphasized about themselves.
On Wednesday at five-thirty, a young woman entered the conference room. She wore glasses and old-fashioned bangs, dressed in a conservative suit that didn't quite fit properly. Her handshake was gentle.
"Thank you for this opportunity," she said.
Lea began the standard questions. The woman never rushed her answers.
"Why did you choose finance?" Ian asked.
"Family pressure, mostly. My parents both work in banking. They expected me to follow. I've done well enough in the coursework, but I've always struggled to fit in with the culture."
"What aspect of the culture?"
"The competitiveness. The aggression. Everyone seems to be fighting for position constantly. I find it exhausting."
"What are you looking for instead?"
"Somewhere I can do good work without politics. Somewhere that values competence over showmanship."
Jay wrote: modest, honest, struggling to fit in. He felt instant recognition. Not due to family pressure, but the difficulty fitting into a world that felt foreign.
Ian asked several more questions. The woman answered thoughtfully, occasionally mentioning her family or her pets. Jay marked her as his first choice.
On Thursday afternoon, one candidate made a dry joke that Jay found particularly funny. He had an awkward delivery and seemed uncomfortable with formal interview settings. Jay marked him as his second choice, though he recognized humour was probably a poor criterion.
On Friday came a woman in her thirties with short hair and a no-nonsense demeanour. Her CV showed military service followed by a career change.
"Why did you leave the military?" Ian asked.
"Incompatible work philosophies. I disagreed with command decisions that I felt prioritized optics over effectiveness. I was told to follow orders without question. That didn't work for me."
"So you chose finance?"
"More or less at random. I needed a second career. I finished my degree while working security jobs, then found positions at several firms. It turned out I was good at analysis."
She stood out among the younger candidates with more prestigious backgrounds. Jay marked her as his third choice. Her unusual path reminded him of his own indirect journey to this office.
By Friday afternoon they'd completed all twenty interviews. Ian and Jay gathered in Ian's office for the final discussion. 
Ian turned to Jay. "What do you think?"
"Three choices. The woman from Wednesday at five-thirty, the awkward man from Thursday afternoon, and the former military woman from today."
"Interesting selections. Why those three?"
"The first one was honest about struggling with the industry culture. The second had a sense of humour. The third had an unusual background and seemed uninterested in playing games."
"I selected the nervous man from Monday and the quiet woman from Tuesday. But my third choice would also be the former military candidate. The first two seemed harmless. Non-threatening. People who would do their work without causing problems."
Jay thought that was certainly a criterion, though a depressing one.
Ian continued. "But I've been thinking about my approach. I've been selecting based on who seems safest, least likely to cause trouble. That's defensive hiring."
"Is that wrong?"
"It's limiting. You selected based on who showed actual personality. That's probably a better strategy."
"You're the one with experience."
"And you're the one who works with everyone daily. You understand team dynamics better than I do. We'll hire your top two choices."
Jay felt panic spike immediately. "What if I'm wrong?"
"They're junior positions. It's common for new hires to quit within the first month. If they're not a good match, they'll leave and we'll try again."
"But—"
"I trust your judgement. Your observations throughout the week have been sound. Your instincts are good. You saw things I missed. Have more faith in yourself."
Jay stared at his notebook, face burning. "I just don't want to make a mistake."
"You won't. And if something goes wrong, it's my responsibility, not yours. I'm making the final decision. I simply happen to agree with your assessment."
"Alright."
"Lea will contact the selected candidates on Monday. They'll start in two weeks if they accept."
The meeting ended. Jay returned to his desk in a daze. Ian trusted him. Trusted his judgement enough to hire based on his recommendations. That should have felt good. It did feel good. But it also felt insufficient. As if the praise only highlighted something missing. Some gap between what Ian was willing to give and what Jay wanted to receive.
He packed his bag at seven and took the metro home. The whole journey he felt his face burning, Ian's words replaying in his mind. At his apartment, sat on the balcony despite the cool evening air, watching the river and thinking about Ian's confidence in him.
Why did it make him feel both happy and profoundly sad? He wanted this acknowledgement. He'd worked hard for it. But receiving it felt like looking across a distance he didn't know how to cross.
He went to bed early, and sleep brought dreams immediately. 
Jay dreamed about sitting across from Ian, about being valued and understood. He dreamed about things he couldn't let himself want while awake. When he woke Saturday morning, he felt hollowed out. The dreams had been vivid enough to feel real, leaving reality disappointing by comparison.
He made coffee and sat on the balcony wrapped in the burgundy blanket. The river flowed past, indifferent to his confusion. Somewhere twelve minutes away, Ian was probably awake too, following his strict schedule, living his controlled life.
Jay wondered if Ian ever thought about him outside of work. If he ever felt this ache of wanting something more than what they had. Probably not. Ian had been through too much to risk anything. And Jay was just his assistant.
Scene 4
Intrusive thoughts
Jay took the metro across the city to his old gym. Rus was warming up on the rowing machine.
"You're here early," Rus said.
"Couldn't sleep."
Jay moved to the weights, but his mind was elsewhere.
"Something wrong?" Rus asked.
"Ian made me choose the new hires."
"That's good, isn't it?"
"It feels like too much responsibility. What if I picked wrong?"
"You're overthinking this. Your boss trusts you. That's a positive thing."
"I just don't want to mess up."
"You won't. And if something goes wrong, that's not on you anyway. This is a good opportunity. You're finally living up to your potential."
"Maybe."
"Maybe nothing. I was worried about you staying at that job, but it seems like it was the right call after all. You're doing well."
"I suppose."
Near the end of his workout, Jay mentioned the gym search.
"I'm thinking of finding a place closer to home."
Rus sighed theatrically. "I'm surprised you still come here at all. You practically live on the Heights now."
"The Heights are much farther north," Jay grumbled.
"Sure they are. But seriously, I'm happy for you. You look better. You're thriving. The move and promotion seem to have taken your mind off that unrequited love situation at least."
Jay turned sheepish immediately. Love. The word had started to sound like a curse.
"Right," he said quietly.
Rus clapped him on the shoulder. "Keep your head up. Things are looking good."
After the gym, Jay spent the afternoon walking through his neighbourhood looking for alternatives. Every gym he found was aggressively upscale. Sleek equipment, pristine floors, staff in designer athletic wear. Everything looked expensive and fragile. He missed the rough atmosphere of his old gym, where nobody cared if you dropped weights or sweated on the machines.
He left each one feeling more disheartened. He didn't want to worry about breaking equipment or looking out of place.
In the late afternoon, he called his landlord to terminate his lease. The company dealt with rentals near the old town. To Jay's surprise, they wanted to meet next Wednesday to finalize everything.
"You have a three month notice period," the woman explained. "But the area is sought after. We'll find a new tenant quickly. Better to handle it now."
"Wednesday works fine."
Jay hung up feeling dazed. That was much faster than he expected. In the afternoon he called his mother.
"Jay! How are you?"
"Good. I wanted to tell you about the new apartment."
"Tell me everything!"
He described the space, the balcony, the neighbourhood. His mother made delighted sounds throughout.
"We have to visit. The children are already talking about it."
Jay heard his siblings chattering excitedly in the background.
"You're all welcome. Maybe in July? After my busy period at work ends."
"That's your birthday. We absolutely must have a party."
"It's a small apartment. Not really built for parties."
"Nonsense. We'll make it work. We absolutely must celebrate properly."
Tim's voice came through the phone, slightly muffled. "Tell Jay about my birthday!"
His mother laughed. "Your brother is turning eighteen in autumn. You have to come for that party."
"That's right in the middle of our busy season. I can't promise anything."
"You have to try."
"I'll do my best. And I'll send a proper gift regardless."
"You better," Tim said.
"Send photos of your new place!" Ema shouted from somewhere nearby.
"I will."
After the call ended, Jay spent an hour photographing his apartment. He captured the balcony view, the kitchen, the bedroom, the living space. Then he took pictures of the neighbourhood, the river path, the parks. Even his mediocre photography skills couldn't hide how charming everything was.
He sent the photos to his family group chat. Responses came immediately.
"It's beautiful!"
"You live THERE now??"
"The river view is incredible"
"When can we visit???"
Jay smiled at his phone. The enthusiasm was warming, even if it made him feel slightly fraudulent. This life still didn't feel entirely real.
But even the busy day couldn't distract him from thoughts of Ian. As evening approached, Jay filled the bathtub. The hot water relaxed his muscles but did nothing for his thoughts. He imagined what it would be like to be held by Ian. Would it be warm? Comforting?
He thought about the brief embrace they'd shared months ago. Jay hungover and vulnerable, Ian broken and honest, both of them desperate for comfort. He remembered Ian's firm frame, that deep lovely scent.
Jay closed his eyes. He knew he couldn't think these things, but his mind betrayed him anyway.
After the bath he went to bed feeling stupid. Nothing good would come from such foolish fantasies. Ian was his boss, his mentor, his friend. Someone who'd shown him extraordinary kindness and trust. These thoughts were inappropriate and pointless.
Jay wandered restlessly on Sunday. He explored the neighbourhood thoroughly, checking parks he hadn't visited yet, walking past gyms that were farther away and all equally unsuitable. He found a charming pier further north and sat watching the river for an hour, trying not to think about anything at all.
He didn't want to sit alone in his apartment with his thoughts. That felt dangerous.
Monday at work felt oddly normal. Work meant structure, purpose. Ray arrived at eight and disappeared into his headphones. The rest of the team went about their business. Jay focused on his tasks.
At six, after everyone else had gone, Jay walked to Ian's office. Ian looked up from his computer.
"Do you need something?"
"I wanted to ask about gym recommendations in the neighbourhood. The ones I found are all very upscale."
It was a legitimate question. But mostly he wanted a pretext to talk to Ian, to be near him.
"Ah. I'm probably not the best person to ask. I mostly work out at home. My trainer comes on Saturdays to monitor my form."
"You have a home gym?"
"Yes. It seemed practical."
"That makes sense."
"During summer I also swim laps in the pool at my apartment complex. The outdoor one."
The image hit Jay suddenly. The memory of Ian's tanned body from last year's company retreat pool party. Broad shoulders cutting through water. Wet hair. Droplets on skin.
Jay's face burned.
"I need to go," Jay said abruptly.
"Is something wrong?"
"Stomachache. I should head home."
"Do you need—"
"No. I'm fine. Sorry. See you tomorrow."
Jay fled before Ian could say anything else. He grabbed his bag and left the office, not looking back. He heard Ian say something else but didn't catch the words.
On the metro, he buried his face in his hands. What was wrong with him? It was a disproportionate reaction. Completely foolish. Ian would think he was insane.
At home, Jay collapsed on the couch and stared at the ceiling. He couldn't let his weird thoughts make their relationship awkward. He had to control himself better, but his mind kept wandering to inappropriate places, imagining things that would never happen, wanting things he had no right to want.
He went to bed early, determined to sleep away the confusion. Tomorrow he'd be normal. He'd stop reacting like an idiot every time Ian said something perfectly ordinary.
But as he lay in the dark, wrapped in the burgundy blanket, Jay couldn't stop himself from imagining that warmth. He couldn't stop himself from wishing for something more than what he had, even as he knew that wishing was pointless.
He fell asleep eventually, exhausted from fighting his own thoughts. His dreams were full of water and sunlight and someone just out of reach, no matter how far he swam.
Scene 5
Rescue
On Tuesday, Jay tried to compose himself better. When Ian appeared at his desk at nine, concern was evident on his face.
"How are you feeling?"
"Much better. I must have eaten something wrong yesterday."
"Are you certain?"
"Completely certain. It passed quickly."
Ian accepted this explanation, though reluctance showed in his expression. "Good. I'm glad you're alright."
"Was there something you needed?"
"Yes, actually. Both candidates accepted our offer. They'll start in June, along with the second senior analyst."
Jay smiled, though it felt strained. "That's wonderful."
"Are you sure you're feeling better? You look tired."
"I'm fine. Really."
"If you need anything, let me know."
"I will. Thank you."
Ian returned to his office. Jay turned back to his computer, relieved. Two weeks until the new hires arrived. Normally he'd be anxious about the change, about training new people and adjusting dynamics. But right now he was glad for the distraction from his troubling thoughts.
Ray had already grown into his place. He became a silent monolith at the far desk. Occasionally he consulted team members, but his interactions were always brief and strictly work-related. Jay still hadn't spoken to him once. Jay's administrative duties were completely outside Ray's scope of interest.
By Wednesday, the topic around the office was the upcoming retreat. They'd depart Thursday afternoon for three days at the resort.
Ian tried to convince Ray to attend.
"It would be good for team cohesion," Ian said.
"No," Ray replied without looking up from his screen.
"The location is quite nice."
"No."
"You wouldn't have to participate in activities."
"No."
Ian accepted the refusal with good humor. "Understood. We'll see you Monday then."
Ray returned to his work without further comment.
Jay watched the exchange with amusement. Ian and Ray's interactions were unlike anything he'd seen before. Ray's brusqueness would have seemed rude with anyone else, but Ian  never took offense or pushed too hard.
That evening Jay returned home and pulled out his largest suitcase, the one Ian had given him during their international trip. He opened it on his bed and began packing.
His clothes from last year's retreat were still perfectly appropriate. He selected two of his three-piece suits for any formal occasions. Resort wear for the evenings, newly bought casual clothes, gym clothes for hiking. Everything fit easily with room to spare.
This time he felt no anxiety about packing. Last year he'd worried constantly about having the right things, about fitting in, about making mistakes. Now he knew that with Ian there, he had nothing to worry about.
Apart from his own unreasonable thoughts.
But surely those were just a passing anomaly. He knew himself. He'd never been one to entertain idle fantasies, not in his whole almost thirty-one years of life. This wouldn't be an exception.
He closed the suitcase and set it by the door, ready for Thursday.
Ian sat in his apartment Wednesday evening, reviewing retreat arrangements on his tablet. Everything was confirmed. Transportation, accommodations, activity schedules. All the logistics were handled.
But his mind kept returning to Monday. Jay's sudden departure, the unconvincing excuse about a stomachache. The way he'd looked flushed and uncomfortable before fleeing.
Ian hoped he hadn't pushed too hard with the recruitment responsibilities. He'd really believed Jay's instincts were sound, that involving him was the right choice, but maybe it had been too much pressure.
He wanted the new hires to work out specifically because of Jay. He wanted them to take over Jay's more tedious duties so Jay would have more time. More time to spend with him.
Ian had been bitter about the missed opportunities over the past year. All those evenings they should have been spending together in the lounge, but instead were drowning in unreasonable workload. All those moments stolen by busy seasons and overtime and constant demands.
He used to like his busy evenings. He'd arranged his entire life to ensure his evenings were full of work. He'd needed the distraction, needed something to fill the empty hours.
But now work was hindering him. And he'd worked hard to remedy that.
Recruiting Ray had been a calculated move. Ray had been working remotely for some obscure company on the New Continent after his last position ended spectacularly badly. Ian had sent Leo to track him down, intercept him, convince him to accept the job.
Leo had found him in a cramped apartment, working for minimal pay, and probably miserable despite his perpetual indifference.
"Never," Ray had said immediately when Leo explained the situation.
"Just hear me out," Leo had said.
"I don't do office politics."
"Ian knows that. He's offering you peace. Nobody will bother you. Regular hours, engaging work, good pay. You'll be left alone to do what you do best."
Ray had been skeptical. But Leo was persuasive, and eventually Ian had gotten on a call himself to make guarantees.
"You'll work your contracted hours and leave," Ian had said. "No expectations beyond competence. No social obligations. No games."
"Why would you want someone like me?"
"Because you're brilliant and reliable. That's more valuable than someone charming who causes problems."
It had taken three conversations before Ray agreed. But Ian had been certain it would be worth it. Ray's intellect was razor-sharp, better than Ian's own in many ways. He'd easily handle the heaviest workload from the senior analysts, freeing up everyone's time.
The second senior analyst had been hired through the standard process. She had excellent credentials, extensive experience, and impeccable references. Ian had been reluctant to authorize her hire anyway, his instincts warning against anyone too polished. But his integrity had won. She was objectively the best candidate among those he'd deemed acceptable.
At least she was happily married with three children. Ian didn't expect drama from that quarter.
The junior hires were wild cards. But juniors were easier to replace if something went wrong. They didn't need to be perfect, just acceptable enough to reduce workload and free up the evenings.
His evenings with Jay.
The retreat was just two days away now. Three whole days in close proximity to Jay. Breakfast and dinner, evening conversations, shared activities. Ian would make sure Jay was happy and comfortable, that he enjoyed every moment.
He wished they could share a room again as they had on the last retreat. But they'd have the second-best thing. Meals together, long talks, the boat trip where they'd be on the same small boat.
Ian had reviewed the activity details extensively. Six people per boat, which meant he and Jay would share with four others. Not ideal, but better than being separated. They'd have at least those few hours together on the water.
He'd make sure the retreat was special. That Jay had a good time, felt included and valued. That was what mattered.
Ian walked to his window. Twelve minutes south, Jay was probably packing, preparing for the same trip. Maybe sitting on his balcony, watching the same river that flowed past Ian's building.
So close. Yet the distance between them felt impossible to cross.
Ian had grown expert at suppressing his feelings, at maintaining perfect control. But lately that control felt fragile. Every smile from Jay threatened to crack it. Every shared evening made it harder to maintain the necessary distance.
Three days at the retreat. Ian would have to be careful. Attentive but not obvious. Helpful but not overbearing. He'd perfected this balance over the past year, but it never got easier.
Chapter 37: Thunderstorm
Scene 1
Pine woods
Ian arrived at Jay's building at exactly seven. He used his emergency key to access the entrance and parked in the underground garage. Jay was waiting by the elevator with his large suitcase.
"Good morning," Ian said.
"Morning. Thank you for this."
They loaded the suitcase into Ian's trunk and drove to the office together. The streets were quiet this early, the city still waking. Ian glanced at Jay in the passenger seat. He offered to pick Jay up the previous afternoon, and Jay had accepted without hesitation, without the anxious overthinking that used to come with such suggestions in the past. This was exactly what Ian had hoped for. Slowly and seamlessly blending their lives together
Late May remained chilly. The year continued to be rather cold. But for Ian, the spring was unusually beautiful this year.
The workday passed quickly. Everyone rushed to finish their tasks before the four o'clock departure. The office filled with luggage as people brought their bags in throughout the morning. Ela arrived with three suitcases, causing widespread amusement.
"Are you moving there permanently?" Luc asked.
"I need options," Ela said defensively.
At four everyone abandoned their desks and grabbed their bags. Kim carried his own suitcase and one of Ela's, but she still struggled with the remaining two. Everyone else was too loaded to help.
Ian had two massive suitcases. Jay managed his single suitcase and laptop bag. Zoe had one large bag and tried to help Ela, but couldn't manage both.
They were trying to reorganize when Ray picked up Ela's wandering suitcase and headed for the elevator. Everyone stared after him. Ray followed them to the waiting buses, set down the suitcase, and left without looking back.
Jay smiled to himself. Gruff but not unkind. Just as Ian had said.
The buses were different from last year. Only two instead of four, but these were brand new luxury coaches. Wood paneling, reclining armchairs, individual screens and climate controls. Definitely an upgrade. Jay felt secretly delighted. He'd get to sit next to Ian for six hours.
Staff loaded their luggage while everyone boarded. Jay claimed a seat next to Ian, who immediately offered him the window.
"You'll want to see the scenery," Ian said.
"Thank you."
The armchairs were enormous and comfortable. Individual separators gave each seat privacy. Jay immediately started pressing buttons, adjusting his seat up and down, testing the heating and air conditioning. He was still amazed by such displays of wealth.
Ian watched him with amusement. "Comfortable?"
"Very. This is incredible."
The bus departed with minimal delay. Outside, the sky was gray and threatening. Everyone had checked weather forecasts obsessively all week, but predictions kept changing. Current forecasts showed possible storms tomorrow.
Zoe leaned across the aisle. "I hope the weather clears."
"We'll have fun regardless," Amy said.
The drive would last six hours with a dinner stop at the halfway point. Jay turned to the window, watching the city give way to countryside. Bus staff dispensed refreshments. Coffee, tea, pastries. Jay accepted coffee.
"Have you spent much time in the mountains?" he asked Ian.
"Some. My family had a ski lodge when I was young."
"You mentioned skiing once before."
"We went every winter. A family tradition."
"Were you good at it?"
"Competent. My father insisted we all learn properly. He hired instructors every year."
"Did you enjoy it?"
"I did. It was one of the few times I felt truly free. Just the mountain and the speed. No expectations or protocols. We'd spend mornings on the slopes and afternoons by the fire."
"That sounds wonderful."
"It was. I haven't been back since I was eighteen."
Jay understood what he wasn't saying. Since he was disowned. Since his family cut him off and took everything away.
"Would you want to go back?" Jay asked.
"Perhaps. Someday. Have you ever been to the mountains?"
"No. I grew up in the south, but we never traveled much."
"You would have liked it. The air is different there."
"Maybe we could go sometime."
The words came out before Jay could stop them. He felt heat rise to his face, but Ian's expression didn't change.
"I'd like that," Ian said simply.
Jay asked more questions and Ian answered, describing the lodge, the slopes, the instructors who'd taught him. Despite the bus being full, the sound management was excellent. Everyone existed in their own small cocoon of space.
They talked the whole journey. Jay felt relieved that his strange thoughts from earlier in the week weren't making things awkward. This felt as good as always, sitting and talking with Ian, sharing stories and observations.
After three hours they reached the midpoint stop. A secluded restaurant built in traditional regional style, rustic but clearly expensive. Everyone was glad to stretch despite the comfortable bus.
The feast was impressive. Long wooden tables, huge portions of traditional food. Beer in enormous mugs, platters of ribs and game, roasted vegetables, fresh bread. Everyone ate and drank and relaxed into the evening.
Jay sat next to Ian with Luc on his other side. The beer was strong and the food was rich. Luc told increasingly absurd stories about previous company events. Everyone laughed.
The feast dragged on for over an hour. When they finally returned to the bus, Jay was exhausted. He reclined his seat fully. The motion of the bus was soothing and his eyes drifted shut. He woke briefly when the bus slowed, but couldn't make himself sit up. Sleep pulled him back under.
Ian watched Jay sleep beside him, fighting the urge to smooth the black hair that had fallen across his forehead. Jay looked peaceful, his face relaxed and soft. Beautiful.
They arrived at ten. Darkness had fallen completely, and light rain misted the windows. Thunder rumbled in the distance.
"Jay. We've arrived."
Jay woke up disoriented. "Already?"
"You slept for over two hours."
"Oh."
The resort was gorgeous. Modern buildings constructed from stone and dark wood, huge glass walls revealing warm interiors. Lights hung from trees, illuminating stone paths. Through the mist, they could see the lake rippling with rain.
They gathered their things and filed off the bus. Resort staff unloaded luggage. An elegant woman in a dark uniform handed out envelopes containing keys and guidebooks.
"Welcome to Pinewood Resort. Each department has a private lodge. Please follow the marked paths. If you need anything, the main building is always staffed."
She led them along a stone path to their lodge. 
"Your team has the entire Oakwood Lodge. The main floor has a common area with a fireplace, and there's a small kitchen if you need anything. Your rooms are upstairs. Breakfast is served in the main dining hall starting at seven."
Everyone claimed their rooms quickly. Ela and Kim took the largest room together, no longer bothering with pretence. 
Luc disappeared up a narrow spiral staircase at the back. "There's an attic room up here!"
Amy chose a spacious room with a balcony. Max and Zoe selected small rooms with lake views.
Jay and Ian took rooms next to each other facing the woods. Jay pushed open his door and found a cosy space with a large bed, a desk, and a window seat.
"This is lovely," he said.
Ian appeared in the doorway. "Your room is identical to mine. Looks comfortable."
"I'm exhausted."
"As am I. We should rest."
"Goodnight, Ian."
"Goodnight, Jay."
They disappeared into their rooms. Jay showered quickly and fell into bed. His last thoughts before sleep were of Ian in the room next door. Just a wall between them.
The rain intensified outside, drumming against the roof and windows. But inside the lodge was warm and dry, the fireplace crackling softly in the salon below.
Jay pulled the blanket up to his chin and smiled in the darkness. Three days here. Three days of activities and meals and conversations. Three days near Ian. He couldn't wait until morning.
Scene 2
Fragrance
Jay woke to the sound of heavy rain against his window. He checked his phone. Quarter past six. The weather forecast showed a storm incoming, expected to last most of the day.
He dressed in casual clothes and descended the stairs. The salon was quiet except for the crackling fire. Ian sat in one of the armchairs near the fireplace, tablet in hand, coffee cup on the side table.
"Good morning," Jay said.
Ian looked up. "Morning. Did you sleep well?"
"Very well. You're up early."
"Habit. Coffee's fresh if you want some."
Jay poured himself a cup from the pot on the counter and joined Ian by the fire. The warmth was welcome after the cool upstairs hallway.
"The weather is terrible," Jay said.
"Unfortunately, yes. I was just reviewing the schedule changes. All outdoor activities are cancelled. No paddle boats, no river rafting."
"That's a shame."
"Were you looking forward to them?"
"A little. But I wasn't too invested."
Ian scrolled through the screen. "The alternative activities are indoor. Fashion workshops, styling sessions, a fashion show tonight followed by dinner."
Jay made a face. "Not really my preference."
"Nor mine. But there are other options. Dancing workshops, yoga, various tastings."
"What are you planning to do?"
"I haven't decided yet."
Jay took the tablet and flipped through the digital pamphlet. Most of the fashion activities didn't appeal to him at all. He kept scrolling until something caught his attention.
"There's a scent workshop," he said, pausing on that entry.
"In the fashion corner, yes."
"I've always wanted to learn more about that."
Ian raised an eyebrow. "Really?"
"So I won't end up smelling like an overzealous teenager on their first date during some important occasion. I'm never sure what's appropriate."
Ian looked slightly surprised, but only for a moment. That was on point for Jay, worrying about violating subtle social norms he was unfamiliar with.
"Is that strange?" Jay asked sheepishly.
"Not at all. I'll join you."
Jay felt a surge of delight. "Really?"
"If you don't mind the company."
"Of course not. I'd like that."
They sat together watching the rain fall on the lake through the glass wall. The water was gray and choppy, the trees beyond swaying in the wind. It was beautiful in its own way.
Slowly other team members started descending from their rooms. Zoe appeared first, took one look at the weather, and groaned.
"This is terrible."
"Good morning to you too," Jay said.
"I wanted to do the hiking trail today. It's supposed to be spectacular."
"Maybe tomorrow," Ian said.
"Maybe." She poured herself coffee and collapsed into an armchair. "This is so disappointing."
The others trickled down over the next hour. Max arrived looking fresh. Amy appeared perfectly composed. Luc stumbled down at half past seven, clearly not a morning person. Ela and Kim were the last to emerge, just before eight.
"Still raining," Ela observed.
"All day according to the forecast," Kim added.
"Well, at least there are indoor activities."
Everyone gathered around the fire with their coffee, discussing options. 
"Well. No point in waiting for it to clear. Let's get breakfast," Luc said.
The resort had provided rain capes in a basket by the door. Everyone grabbed one and an umbrella from the stand. They made a soggy procession along the stone path to the main building, hunched against the wind.
The breakfast hall was still pretty empty. They loaded their plates with eggs and pastries and fresh fruit, then claimed a large table near the windows.
"Remember last year?" Luc said. "That beautiful seaside weather?"
"Uncommonly warm for the season," Amy agreed.
"Our weather luck ran out," Max said cheerfully.
Zoe just ate her eggs and continued looking miserable.
Eventually conversation turned to activities. Ela was determined to do everything fashion-related.
"All of it?" Kim asked.
"Every single workshop if possible. The styling, the makeup, the photo shoot, the show."
"I want nothing to do with any of that."
"You're not even a little curious?"
"Not even slightly."
They stared at each other. It was clear they weren't thrilled about separating. Originally everyone wanted water activities in the morning, then split up afterwards. But now everything had changed.
"I'll do all the fashion things. I don't mind," Amy said.
Ela brightened. "Really? Thank you."
Kim relaxed visibly. "What are the rest of you doing?"
"Modern dance workshop," Luc said immediately. 
"Disco music. Music from my youth," Max said.
"You're not that old," Luc said.
"I'm old enough to remember when disco was current. That makes me ancient."
"I like the retro vibe. I'll join you," Kim said. 
"Me too. Jumping around is better than moping about the rain," Zoe said without enthusiasm.
"What about you two?" Luc asked Jay and Ian.
"Scent workshop," Jay said.
"That sounds very sophisticated."
"It's practical," Jay insisted.
"Sure it is."
They finished breakfast and headed toward their various activities. Ela and Amy went toward the fashion corner, already discussing dress options. Kim, Luc, Zoe, and Max headed for the dance studio.
Jay and Ian followed the fashion corridor but turned into a smaller room labeled Scent Workshop. It was elegant and intimate, with white counters and rows of glass bottles. A pleasant older woman greeted them warmly.
"Welcome, welcome. First time exploring fragrance?"
"Yes," Jay admitted.
"Wonderful. Let me show you the basics."
She led them to a counter covered in bottles. "These are the building blocks. Top notes, heart notes, base notes. Each category serves a purpose."
She explained the theory while Jay listened intently. Ian already knew most of this but remained attentive, occasionally asking questions that drew out more detailed information.
"Now, let's explore what appeals to you," she said.
"I've always been curious about your scent. It's very distinctive," Jay admitted to Ian. 
"It's called Ambre du Solstice."
Jay repeated the name in his head several times to remember. "I've always been impressed by how rich and deep it is. But not overwhelming. Just warm."
"What do you prefer?" the woman asked Jay.
"Lighter, more fresh scents."
"Let's try some options."
She guided Jay through various scents. Mint and lemon and lavender and cedar. He smelled each, testing combinations, asking questions about composition.
"These are lovely," he said, holding up a mint and cedar combination.
"Very clean. Versatile for any occasion."
"Could I mix something similar?"
"Absolutely. Here, try this."
She set out several bottles and guided him through creating his own blend. Ian watched, giving occasional suggestions. They adjusted ratios, testing results.
"This is perfect," Jay said finally, smelling the result on his wrist.
"Let me try," Ian said.
Jay held out his wrist. Ian took his hand and brought it closer, inhaling deeply.
"Yes, that suits you very well."
Jay's heart was racing as he pulled his hand back.
"Now let's create something together. A shared scent, if you'd like."
"Together?" Jay asked.
"Why not? It's a lovely memento of your time here."
Jay looked at Ian. "Would you want to?"
"Yes."
They worked on it together, combining elements from both their preferences. The warm depth of Ian's choice with the fresh brightness Jay favoured. It took several attempts to balance properly.
"That's beautiful. Rich but fresh. Perfectly balanced."
Jay smelled it and grinned. It warm and clean and somehow exactly right.
"We'll take two bottles," Ian said.
She prepared them while Jay kept smelling his wrist. They had their own fragrance now. Something that belonged to both of them.
They left the workshop with their bottles. Jay was absolutely delighted, unable to stop smiling.
"That was wonderful," he said.
"I'm glad you enjoyed it."
"Did you?"
"Very much."
They went to a tea ceremony next to refresh themselves after all the smelling. The room was empty except for a young woman in traditional dress who welcomed them and guided them to cushions on the floor. She prepared tea with graceful movements. The ceremony was meditative, focusing on each small action. She served them in tiny cups, the tea fragrant and delicate.
The rain kept hitting the lake surface beyond the windows, creating rippling patterns. The room was warm and serene.
"We smell like a perfumery," Jay whispered.
Ian's mouth twitched. "Probably."
Jay kept smelling his wrist between sips, grinning each time. The scent was divine. When the ceremony concluded, the woman bowed and departed, leaving them alone in the room.
"Should we head back?" Ian asked eventually.
"I suppose we should."
They stood and made their way to the dining hall for lunch. The rest of the team was already there. Ela and Amy were dressed extravagantly in long, elaborate dresses with heavy makeup. The others wore sports clothes and were breathing heavily.
"How was dancing?" Jay asked.
"Exhausting. I'm too old for this," Max said.
"You kept up fine," Luc said.
"I'll be sore tomorrow."
"Worth it though. That was actually fun," Kim added.
They loaded their plates with herbal meat pies and cured meats. The food was good, regional and warming. Jay liked it well enough, though last year's tropical grilled skewers had been phenomenal.
He looked around the venue as he ate. The glass ceiling and walls gave an amazing view of the surrounding area, but the overcast skies and rain made it look vaguely foreboding. Thankfully the food and jovial atmosphere mitigated the unfriendly aura outside.
Soon everyone finished eating and departed to prepare for their afternoon activities. Jay and Ian walked back through the rain to their lodge, umbrellas barely keeping them dry. Inside, the fire was still burning. They stood near it, drying off.
Jay smelled his wrist again. He couldn't stop smiling.
Scene 3
Fashion
Jay and Ian grabbed their evening clothes from the lodge and returned to the main building. Better to change there than risk getting their formal wear wet walking back and forth. The fashion area included numerous changing rooms and styling stations. Staff moved between them with clothes and accessories. Music played in the background.
Jay went to the changing room to get dressed. His blue three-piece suit fit perfectly. He fastened the sapphire cufflinks Ian had given him, then the matching tiepin. The effect was striking. He looked like a prince.
Jay stepped out and found Ian waiting, dressed in a slate gray three-piece suit. They had both applied their shared scent. Jay caught the fragrance as Ian approached and smiled.
"You look excellent," Ian said.
"So do you."
They moved toward the styling area. The rest of their team was scattered throughout, in various states of preparation. Some were still arriving. Others were already fully dressed and posing for photographers.
A stylist approached Jay immediately. She looked him up and down with an approving expression.
"Perfect. Absolutely perfect. We'll just need some minor hair adjustments."
"Thank you."
She gestured to a hairdresser. "Take him to station three. Classic style."
Before Jay could respond, two other stylists descended on Ian.
"No, no, no. You absolutely cannot show up to the party looking like this."
"I beg your pardon?" Ian said.
"Trust us. We have a vision."
They whisked Ian away before he could protest. Jay watched him disappear into a private styling room, looking resigned. He suppressed a smile as he allowed himself to be led to the hairdresser.
The process took less than twenty minutes. The hairdresser worked efficiently, creating an old-fashioned style that made Jay look distinguished. When she finished, he thanked her and wandered back into the main area.
Luc appeared at one point looking like a movie star from a spy film. Perfectly tailored tuxedo, slicked-back hair, an air of dangerous charm.
"How do I look?" he asked, striking a pose.
"Like you're about to order a martini," Jay said.
Kim and Ela emerged together, both wearing opulent outfits with elaborate accessories. Their ensembles matched, coordinated in deep burgundy and gold.
"You two look incredible," Jay said.
"We know," Ela replied happily.
Amy wore a long flowing dress with sparkling diamond jewellery. She looked timeless, like she'd stepped out of a classic film. Zoe eventually appeared in extremely high heels and a short seductive dress, looking quite amused by the result.
"These shoes are death traps," she said.
"You look amazing though," Jay replied.
"I know. It's worth the pain."
Max arrived dressed like old royalty, all velvet and brocade. He looked both magnificent and slightly ridiculous.
"Don't say a word," Max warned.
Jay grinned. "You look very refined."
"I look like I'm attending a coronation."
"That too."
Jay kept glancing toward the private styling room where Ian had disappeared. Nearly two hours had passed. What were they doing to him?
Finally, the door opened.
Ian emerged, and Jay forgot how to breathe.
The outfit was completely unlike anything Ian would ever choose. A top that was far too tight, showing every line of his athletic build. An asymmetrical jacket draped over one shoulder. A long scarf in bold colours draped loosely around his neck. Skintight pants that left absolutely nothing to the imagination. Tall boots that added another few centimetres to his already imposing height.
It had no right to look as good as it did. But Ian's natural musculature and perfect proportions somehow made it work. He moved with complete confidence, as if he wore this sort of thing every day.
"Well?" Ian asked, approaching Jay.
"You look incredible."
"This is definitely not what I would have picked myself."
"But it works."
"Does it?"
"Yes."
Leo appeared from somewhere, wearing what looked like several furs stitched together with gold chains in bizarre places. He took one look at Ian and burst out laughing.
"Oh, this is perfect. Absolutely perfect."
"You really have no right to laugh at anyone, looking like you robbed several grandmas and stole their prized foxes from before the war."
"These are vintage pieces."
"They're ridiculous."
"So are those pants."
"At least I look good."
Leo grinned. "You do. I hate to admit it, but you do."
The staff began herding everyone toward where the fashion show would take place. Jay walked beside Ian, very aware of how different they looked. His own classic suit contrasted sharply with Ian's avant-garde outfit. They filed into a large hall with rows of seating facing a raised runway. Jay sat next to Ian.
The show began with dramatic music and lasted maybe two hours. The host presented collections from designers Jay had never heard of. Models walked the runway in elaborate creations, some beautiful, some bizarre. Ela nodded knowingly every now and then, discussing something animatedly with Amy in hushed voices. Jay had no idea what was happening, but at the very least most of the outfits looked presentable.
When the show finally ended, staff directed everyone to a photography area, where photographers had set up backdrops and lighting.
"Individual shots first," someone announced. "Then groups."
Jay posed awkwardly, unsure what to do with his hands. The photographer gave him direction and he did his best to follow. The results, when shown on the screen, were surprisingly good.
Ela and Amy were absolutely delighted with their photos. Kim looked pleased. Even Zoe couldn't complain, holding an old-fashioned cigarette pipe and pretending to smoke like the fancy socialites from the prewar era.
The group photos were chaotic but fun. Their entire team crowded together, then individual departments, then random combinations. Jay ended up with a folder full of images by the time they finished.
"These are fantastic," Ela said, flipping through hers.
"Very glamorous," Amy agreed.
By the time they were done, evening had fully arrived. Staff directed them toward the dinner hall.
The space was enormous. Glass walls and ceiling stretched overhead. Lights floated in midair, suspended by invisible wires. The storm had intensified. Lightning illuminated the sky in dramatic flashes, visible through the transparent roof. Thunder rolled constantly, deep and ominous.
The theme was old time cinema. Everyone remained in their glamorous outfits. Staff circulated with trays of champagne, caviar, lobster. A live band played vintage music in the corner.
It was completely different from last year's refined elegance. This was playful and dramatic, enhanced by the violent weather outside.
Jay grabbed a glass of champagne and wandered between tables. People laughed and showed each other photos from earlier. Some danced to the band's music.
Jay kept looking for Ian. Every time he spotted him across the room, his heart skipped a beat. Ian looked so different in that outfit, but he owned it completely. He could have been a model with how naturally he presented himself. Jay knew he was staring. He tried to be discreet and failed completely.
The evening progressed through multiple courses. More champagne appeared. The band played faster songs and more people danced. Lightning continued to flash overhead, adding to the atmosphere.
Finally, late in the evening, Jay found Ian in a relatively secluded corner. The party was still going strong, but this section was quieter.
"Are you enjoying yourself?" Ian asked.
"Very much." Jay moved closer, catching their shared scent on Ian. He smiled. "You look great."
"Thank you. Though I have to say, this outfit is excruciatingly uncomfortable."
"Really?"
"The pants are far too tight. And I'm fairly certain the boots have given me a blister."
Jay laughed. "We could excuse ourselves. It's already after dessert. There really isn't any reason to stick around. I can pretend to be super drunk and you can escort me again."
"That's a brilliant idea."
They approached their team's table. Ian put a hand on Jay's shoulder.
"I'm taking Jay back to the lodge. He's had enough to drink."
"Already?" Luc asked.
"And I need to get off these pants as soon as possible," Ian added.
Everyone laughed. Ela waved them off.
"Go, go. We'll see you tomorrow."
They grabbed coats and umbrellas at the entrance, but the rainfall was absolutely insane. Even with protection, they were quite wet by the time they reached their lodge.
Jay undressed in his room, hanging his suit properly so it would dry without damage. He heard Ian moving around next door, presumably removing his cursed fashion outfit with much less care.
They both showered and changed into pyjamas, then reconvened in the living room. The fireplace still burned warmly. Ian retrieved a bottle of wine from somewhere and two glasses.
"To commemorate our successful fashion debut," he said.
Jay laughed and accepted a glass. They sat together on the sofa, close enough that Jay could still catch hints of their shared scent. Outside, the storm raged violently. Wind howled. Rain hammered the windows. Thunder shook the building. Inside, everything was warm and comfortable.
"It's not fair," Ian said after his first sip of wine.
"What isn't?"
"I had to dress up as a clown while you were left alone looking dignified."
Jay grinned. "It was definitely Leo's doing."
"Obviously."
"I can't believe you stay friends with someone who clearly wishes you ill."
"He doesn't wish me ill. He just enjoys chaos."
"Same thing."
"He wasn't always like this. When we were in college together, he was quite different."
"Really?"
"He was younger, in an earlier year. Quite awkward. Our families knew each other, so I took him under my wing. I mentored him through the social aspects of university life."
Jay tried to imagine Leo as an awkward teenager and failed. "I can't picture that at all."
"He was very earnest. Tried too hard to fit in. Made terrible jokes that nobody laughed at."
"And now you're stuck with him."
"I am. Though I admit, life is rarely boring with Leo around."
Jay sipped his wine, watching the fire. "I always thought rich people were naturally worldly and polished. That they never had awkward phases."
"Everyone has awkward phases. Wealth doesn't prevent that."
"I suppose not."
They talked until almost one in the morning, about the day's activities, the ridiculous fashion show, the photos they'd taken. Ian told more stories about Leo's college years, each one more absurd than the last. Jay shared memories from his own university days, the dormitories and terrible cafeteria food.
The wine disappeared slowly. The fire burned down to embers. Outside, the storm continued its assault. Finally, reluctantly, they got up.
"We should sleep," Ian said.
"Probably."
They walked upstairs together. At their adjacent doors, they paused.
"Today was wonderful," Jay said.
"It was. Sleep well."
"You too."
Jay entered his room and got into bed. Late-night talks with Ian over drinks were definitely the best thing in the world.
Scene 4
The Green Fairy
Jay's alarm went off at six thirty. He'd only managed about five hours of sleep, but he silenced it immediately and got out of bed. He wouldn't miss morning coffee with Ian for anything.
