"I thought if I stayed late and showed dedication, everyone would see how serious I am."
"They saw it. But dedication without awareness isn't helpful. You have to read the room. Understand when to push forward and when to step back."
Gal looked at him intently. So intently it scared him a bit. Her expression was too focused, too hopeful.
"You're very kind," she said.
"I'm just trying to help."
"No one else bothered to explain. They just exchanged looks and whispered about me."
"That's not—"
"You're different. You understand."
Jay felt a bad feeling creep over him. This wasn't gratitude. This was something else. He'd wanted to be kind, to give the encouragement he'd wished someone had given him. But the way she'd looked at him made his stomach turn.
"I should get back to work," he said. He left the kitchen quickly. Later that afternoon, he stopped by Ian's office.
"Do you have a moment?"
"Always."
Jay closed the door. "I talked to Gal earlier. I was trying to be helpful, but I think I might have made things worse."
"How?"
"She looked at me like I'd given her something to hold on to. I just wanted to tell her not to take it personally."
"You were being kind. That's not a mistake."
"But I have a bad feeling about it."
"I'll keep an eye on the situation."
"Maybe I shouldn't have said anything."
"You were trying to help. That's not wrong."
Jay wasn't convinced, but he returned to his desk and tried to focus on work.
On Friday, everyone including Ian left the office by five. It was the day of the quarterly party. Everyone wanted time to prepare. Jay had a hairdresser appointment at five thirty. He'd booked it weeks ago, knowing he'd need a trim. Jay left the salon at six fifteen with his hair freshly cut and styled.
He went straight home to get changed. No need to eat dinner. Getting stuffed at the party was the highlight of the evening.
Scene 3
Impossible love
The quarterly party was held at a restaurant in the financial district. The entire company attended, filling the private event space with conversation and laughter.
Jay arrived at the venue at seven thirty. He'd chosen his best gray three-piece suit with the sapphire cufflinks Ian had given him last Christmas. He'd tried to go for a serious, stately look, but somehow he'd ended up looking absolutely gorgeous.
The venue was already crowded. People from different departments mingled near the bar. Music played in the background.
Luc spotted him first. "There he is. Looking sharp, Jay."
"Thank you."
"Rin is over there looking terrified. Go save him."
Jay found Rin standing awkwardly near a table. "How are you holding up?"
"Everyone here is so confident. I feel like I'm wearing a sign that says new hire."
"You'll be fine. Just relax and talk to people."
They chatted for a while. Jay introduced Rin to a few analysts from other departments.
The evening progressed pleasantly. Jay talked to Amy about her upcoming vacation. He listened to Max's stories about his daughter's graduation. He laughed at Kim's observations about the catering.
Ian arrived at eight. He wore perfectly tailored dark suit. He looked composed as always. His eyes scanned the room and found Jay immediately. Jay felt his heart skip.
Gal arrived shortly after. She wore a black dress and heels. She'd clearly put effort into her appearance. She made her way to the bar and ordered a drink. Then another. Then another. By nine, she was visibly drunk.
Jay was talking with Kim when Gal appeared beside him. She touched his arm.
"Jay. You look amazing tonight."
"Thank you."
"Can I get you a drink?" she asked.
"I'm fine, thank you."
"Are you sure? I'm going to the bar anyway."
"I'm sure."
"Come sit with me." She tugged at his sleeve. Her fingers pressed against his wrist.
"Gal, maybe you should have some water."
"I don't want water. I want to talk to you."
"How are you enjoying the party?" he asked politely.
"It's nice. But I'd rather talk to you."
"You should mingle. Get to know everyone."
"I'd rather get to know you better."
The way she said it made Jay's stomach drop. There was no mistaking her intention now.
"Gal—"
"Do you want to step outside? It's quieter on the terrace."
"I'm fine here."
"Just for a few minutes. We never get to talk at work."
"That's because we're working."
"Exactly. So now we can actually have a conversation."
Jay looked around for help. Luc had moved to another group. Amy was deep in conversation with Liv. Max was nowhere to be seen.
"Gal—"
"I think about you all the time. At work. At home. You're so—"
"Gal, you've had too much to drink."
"I know what I'm saying." She leaned against him. "I really like you."
Then he saw Ian across the room. Ian was watching him. Their eyes met. Ian set down his drink and walked over.
"Excuse me. I need to speak with Jay."
"Oh. Can it wait? We were just—"
"No. It's urgent."
Ian placed a hand on Jay's shoulder and guided him away. Gal stood there, looking hurt and confused.
They walked through the crowd and out onto the terrace. The night air was warm and still. City lights glittered in the distance. The sounds of the party were muted here.
Jay took a deep breath. "Thank you."
"Are you alright?"
"Yes. She's just... persistent."
"She's inappropriate."
"She's young. She doesn't understand boundaries."
"She understands perfectly well. She's choosing to ignore them."
They stood at the railing, looking out at the city. Jay was acutely aware of Ian beside him. Close enough to touch.
"I feel bad for her," Jay said.
"Don't."
"She's probably embarrassed. Struggling to fit in."
"Jay. What she's doing isn't acceptable. Following you around. Bringing you coffee uninvited. Asking personal questions. Trying to get you alone. That's not struggling to fit in. That's harassment."
"It's not that serious—"
"It is exactly that serious. Just because she's young and short doesn't make it not harassment. If she was a two-meter-tall man doing the same things, how would you feel?"
Ian was right. If it was a large, imposing man hounding him like this, Jay would be terrified.
"You're right. I didn't think of it that way."
"No one should be pursued like this. Male, female, doesn't matter. It's not appropriate. And I won't tolerate it on my team."
"What are you going to do?"
"I'll speak with her Monday. Make it clear this behaviour needs to stop."
"She'll hate me."
"She'll be embarrassed. But that's not your responsibility. You've done nothing wrong."
Jay looked at Ian. The city lights illuminated his face—sharp features, composed expression. But there was concern in his eyes. Protectiveness.
"Thank you for helping."
They stood there in silence. Jay could smell Ian's cologne—deep and sensual. It was intoxicating. He wanted to move closer, to close the distance between them.
Ian was rescuing him again, like a knight in shining armour. Like someone who cared deeply about his well-being. Jay's heart ached with the realization he could no longer deny.
He was in love with this man. Deeply, completely, hopelessly in love.
Not just admiration. Not respect or gratitude. Love. The kind that made it hard to breathe when Ian stood this close.
"We should go back inside," Ian said.
"In a moment."
They stayed on the terrace a little longer. The party continued without them. Jay memorized this moment—the night air, the city lights, Ian beside him in the darkness. Neither of them wanted to go back inside. Neither of them wanted this moment to end.
When they finally returned, Gal was gone.
"She left," Luc said when Jay asked. "She looked upset. Nearly crying."
Jay felt guilty, but Ian was right. What she'd been doing wasn't acceptable. She needed to learn that.
The party continued. Jay talked with more colleagues, drank another glass of wine, but his thoughts were elsewhere. On the terrace. On Ian's cologne. On the way Ian had placed a hand on his shoulder and guided him away.
Jay left around eleven, when people began drifting out, calling goodbyes, making plans for the weekend. He walked to the metro station with Luc and Rin.
"That was fun," Rin said. "Though I ate too many appetizers."
"I'm going to sleep for twelve hours," Luc said.
They parted ways at the station. Jay took the train home alone. He entered his apartment, turned on a single lamp, changed out of his suit, and sat on the couch.
The burgundy blanket was folded on the armchair—the one Ian had used when he'd stayed over. Jay picked it up and held it to his face.
It still held traces of Ian's scent. Faint now, but unmistakable.
Jay lay down on the couch and pulled the blanket over himself. He buried his face in the fabric and tried to breathe normally.
But he couldn't. His chest felt tight, his heart too large for his ribcage.
Ian was so perfect. So tender and lovely. The way he'd noticed Jay's discomfort. The way he protected Jay from things Jay didn't even realize he needed protection from.
How could Jay go on with his life when his heart was swallowed whole by these feelings?
How could he sit across from Ian every day, see him through the glass wall, work beside him during late evenings, and pretend he didn't feel this way? How could he survive loving someone who would never love him back?
Jay curled tighter around the blanket. Ian was everything Jay had ever wanted and everything he could never have. The impossibility of it crushed him. He lay there in the darkness, breathing in the fading scent of Ian's cologne, and let himself fall apart.
He cried quietly. Not dramatic, heaving sobs. Just silent tears sliding down his face, soaking into the burgundy fabric.
He loved Ian. He loved him so completely it hurt.
And there was nothing he could do about it. Nothing except continue as he had been. Working beside him. Accepting whatever small moments of closeness Ian offered. Pretending his heart wasn't breaking every single day.
Eventually, exhaustion pulled him under. He fell asleep on the couch, still clutching the blanket, still breathing in the ghost of Ian's scent.
In his dreams, they were on the terrace again. But this time Ian didn't step back. This time Ian moved closer, cupped Jay's face in his hands, and—
Jay woke with a start. Dawn light filtered through the windows. The blanket had slipped to the floor.
He sat up slowly, disoriented and hollow. His eyes felt swollen. The dream faded, leaving only the cold reality of morning. Jay got up and folded the blanket. He returned it to the armchair where it belonged.
Then he went to the bathroom and washed his face. In the mirror, he looked tired and sad. He made coffee and sat on the balcony. The city was waking up around him. The river sparkled in the morning light.
He thought about Ian twelve minutes away. Probably still asleep. Probably never thinking about Jay the way Jay thought about him.
It hurt. It would always hurt, but Jay would endure it. Because being near Ian, even with this impossible love, was better than not being near him at all.
Scene 4
I notice what you need
Saturday's gym visit brought Jay some relief. The exercise cleared his mind, burning away the fog of emotions.
Rus found him at the weight rack. "Birthday coming up, right?"
"Tomorrow."
"Any plans?"
"Not particularly. My family is coming next week to stay over the weekend."
"We should make a party then. Get everyone together. Celebrate properly."
Jay was sceptical. "I don't need a party."
"Everyone needs a party once in a while."
"I'm not really a party person."
"I know. But think about it. Just drinks at a bar. Nothing elaborate."
"My siblings are still children. I don't think—"
"Perfect. We'll make it fun for everyone." Rus was smiling a devious smile.
"Rus—"
"Trust me. It'll be great."
Jay didn't commit, but Rus looked pleased anyway, like he'd already decided it was happening.
After the gym, Jay went shopping for clothes. He needed to look nice for Ian's visit. But not obvious that he was trying so hard. Not desperate. He visited four different boutiques. The first three didn't have what he was looking for. Everything was either too casual or too formal. Too bright or too dull.
At the fourth shop, he found cream trousers and a dark green shirt. The trousers fit perfectly. The shirt was well-cut, flattering without being ostentatious. He bought them and went home.
Sunday was his birthday. He woke early and cleaned the apartment to perfection. He wiped down surfaces, organized the kitchen, arranged books on the shelf. Everything had to be just right.
At noon, he showered and got dressed. He checked his reflection multiple times. Too much? Not enough? It seemed acceptable.
Ian arrived at two in the afternoon. Jay heard the knock and opened the door.
Ian stood in the hallway holding a large package. He was dressed casually—dark trousers, a light blue shirt, no tie.
"Happy birthday," Ian said.
"Thank you. Come in."
Ian entered and set the package on the kitchen counter. "I hope you like it."
"You didn't have to go to so much trouble."
"It was no trouble."
They stood in the kitchen. Jay gestured to the package.
"Should I open it?"
"Please."
Jay removed the wrapping. Inside was a coffee maker—sleek, compact, elegant. White with chrome accents. The brand name meant nothing to him.
"I like and trust the brand. I've had mine for years. It's reliable and makes excellent coffee."
There were also several bags of different coffee blends. Dark roast, medium roast, something labeled "morning blend," another called "evening decaf."
"I didn't know which you'd prefer, so I brought a selection."
Jay stared at it. "This is too much."
"You mentioned wanting a coffee maker but not knowing which to choose. I thought I could help."
"Thank you."
"Shall we set it up?"
They unpacked the machine together. Ian explained the functions, showed him how to fill the water reservoir, how to adjust the strength settings. Within fifteen minutes, they had it running.
"Let's try it," Ian said.
They made coffee and took their cups to the balcony. The weather was beautiful, warm and clear. The river sparkled in the afternoon sun. Jay sipped his coffee. It was excellent.
"How do you feel? After yesterday," Ian asked.
"I'm fine."
"Gal's behaviour was unacceptable. I'll speak with her Monday."
"You don't need to—"
"Yes, I do. This can't happen again."
Jay felt a complicated mix of emotions. Guilt for Gal. Gratitude toward Ian. And underneath it all, the aching awareness that Ian was protecting him again.
"Thank you for intervening."
"It was necessary."
They drank their coffee. Jay was shaken for an entirely different reason than Ian thought. Not because of Gal's harassment. But because of this—sitting on his balcony with Ian, drinking coffee from a machine Ian had chosen for him, feeling the weight of impossible love pressing against his ribs.
"This coffee is really good," Jay said, desperate to change the subject.
"I'm glad you like it."
The afternoon stretched on. Ian stayed, and they kept talking. About work, about the upcoming week, about nothing in particular. The conversation was comfortable, like the retreat, like those long winter evenings in the office.
"I've been trying to run," Jay confessed at one point. "Along the river. But I'm terrible at it."
"How so?"
"My form is awful. I get winded immediately. I feel ridiculous."
"Running takes practice. It's not something you just know how to do."
"You run, don't you?"
"I used to run regularly. I don't do it often these days."
"You make it seem effortless."
Ian smiled slightly. "It's not. But I've been doing it for years. I could give you tips if you'd like. We could run together one of these days."
Jay's heart skipped. "You'd do that?"
"Of course. I'd welcome a change of pace from the gym."
"That would be helpful. Thank you."
They finished their coffee and Jay made more. It was late afternoon when Ian finally got up to leave.
"I should go. Let you enjoy the rest of your birthday."
"Thank you for coming. And for the gift. It's wonderful."
"I'm glad."
They walked to the door. Ian paused in the hallway.
"Happy birthday, Jay. I hope this year brings you everything you want."
"Thank you."
They looked at each other. Jay wanted to say so much. That having Ian here had made his birthday wonderful. That he wished Ian could stay. But he said nothing. 
Ian smiled and left.
Jay closed the door and leaned against it. The apartment felt empty.
He looked at the coffee maker sitting on his counter. A gift that said "I pay attention. I notice what you need."
Ian had stayed for hours, and he had no idea how much it meant. No idea that every moment with him was both a gift and an agony.
Jay's eyes burned. He went to the couch and picked up the burgundy blanket. He pulled it around his shoulders and sat there as evening fell, wishing things could be different.
Meanwhile, Ian drove home wistfully. He kept thinking about how Jay had looked today. How comfortable Jay seemed in his new home. How domestic it had felt, sitting on the balcony drinking coffee together.
Jay had found peace here. A beautiful apartment, a stable career, confidence in himself. Ian would do anything to protect that peace Jay had found. Anything to keep him safe and happy. Even if it meant never telling Jay how he felt. Even if it meant watching from a distance as Jay eventually found someone else.
Because Jay deserved happiness. Ian would protect that possibility, whatever the cost.
He parked in his building's garage and took the elevator up to his apartment. The space felt cold after the warmth of Jay's home.
Ian poured himself a drink and stood by the window. Twelve minutes away, Jay was probably making dinner. Maybe trying out the coffee maker again. Maybe thinking about the week ahead.
Ian hoped he was happy. Hoped the birthday had been good. Hoped Jay knew how much he was valued, even if Ian could never say it the way he wanted to.
Scene 5
Complications
On Monday, Ian had a talk with Gal behind closed doors first thing in the morning. It lasted fifteen minutes. When she emerged, her face was blotchy and red. She went straight to the bathroom. She clearly cried there.
When she returned to her desk, she started acting erratically. Dropping files with a thud. Getting up suddenly and leaving the office without a word. She didn't go out for lunch with the rest of the team. She sat at her desk, eating something from a plastic container, not talking to anyone.
Jay felt guilty. If he'd had better social skills, this situation wouldn't have needed to come to this. If he'd been clearer from the start, discouraged her more effectively—
Ian appeared at his desk during the afternoon.
"Do you have a moment?"
"Of course."
They went to Ian's office. Ian closed the door.
"I wanted to let you know I spoke with Gal this morning. Made it very clear that her behaviour needs to change immediately."
"She seemed upset."
"She was. But that's not your concern."
"I feel responsible—"
"You're not. This is my responsibility. You did nothing wrong. I'll deal with whatever problems arise."
Jay looked at him. Ian's expression was gentle, like he'd shoulder any burden to keep Jay safe. Jay felt something melt in his chest. 
"Thank you," Jay said.
Jay tended to stay late on Mondays. There was always additional work that piled up during the weekend, even during slower periods. But that day he left with the rest of the team after five.
Everyone was relieved the difficult reporting period was behind them and they could finally live like normal people. Luc stretched dramatically. Kim mentioned plans to catch up on sleep. Jay nodded along. But privately, he didn't stay because the situation weighed on him. His feelings, the trouble at work, all of it compounding to make him feel troubled.
He went for a long walk through his new neighbourhood to clear his head. The evening was warm, the streets busy with people finishing their workdays. He walked along the river, then through residential streets.
He'd never been one to rely on other people for protection. He'd always tried to carry his burdens himself. It was safer that way. Less disappointing when people inevitably let you down.
But with Ian, it felt natural. Ian was someone who solved problems, who shielded others, who stood at the helm and weathered whatever storms came his way. Reliable, unbreakable.
More than that, Jay wanted to be protected by him. It didn't hurt his pride or make him feel less. It made him feel cared for. And it was an intoxicating feeling.
He walked until the light began to fade, then returned home. He made dinner and sat on the balcony, thinking about his life.
On Tuesday, the atmosphere was even tenser. Gal had ceased sighing and moping. Instead, she'd turned passive-aggressive.
She responded to questions with technically correct answers that somehow managed to sound hostile. When Zoe asked her to review a file, she said "Fine" in a curt tone. When Rin accidentally bumped her desk, she glared at him.
The old workers just rolled their eyes and ignored her moods. Max remained jovial and unbothered, Kim made a comment under his breath that made Ela snort.
But Rin was clearly bothered by it. He laughed nervously and tried to get out of Gal's way, moving around the office like he was on a minefield.
Meanwhile, at lunch, Luc was being weird.
"So Jay. What kind of food do you like?"
"What?"
"For eating. What's your preference?"
"I don't have one particular favourite."
"But if you had to choose. What would you want?"
"Why are you asking?"
"Just curious. And what about venues? Do you like loud places or quieter ones?"
Jay narrowed his eyes. "What are you planning?"
"Nothing. Just making conversation. Tell me about your family. How many siblings do you have?"
"Three."
"Ages?"
"Luc, what is this?"
"Just getting to know you better."
Jay didn't believe him for a second.
Later that afternoon, Luc was not-so-secretly convening with Zoe and Ian in Ian's office behind closed doors. Through the glass wall, Jay watched them. Luc was gesticulating wildly, pointing at Jay repeatedly. Zoe was nodding. Ian looked patient but slightly resigned.
Jay was unhappy the doors provided decent soundproofing because he was certain they were scheming.
At three, Ian sent him a text.
"Meet me in Conference Room B in ten minutes."
Jay went at the appointed time. Ian was already there, seated at the table with a look that made it clear he was about to deliver bad news.
"What's happening?" Jay asked.
"Apparently Rus contacted Luc about your birthday party."
Jay's heart sank. "Oh no."
"Luc is being very spirited about it. He and Zoe are organizing it for Saturday. It's not exactly a secret, but they fully intend to organize everything and present you with a fait accompli."
"Of course they do."
"I'm against the surprise aspect. If you don't want this, I will very much effectively dissuade them from the idea."
Jay sighed dramatically and shook his head in resignation. As if he didn't already have enough on his plate. His overeager friends had decided to make things with his family awkward.
"Let them do what they want. I can drag my mom and siblings to a pub for an hour. What's the worst that can happen? At least Rus and Luc won't be nagging me for the rest of my life for being antisocial."
"Are you sure?"
"Yes. They clearly want to do this. I'm not going to fight them on it."
"Alright."
"You're free to come as well. Since they roped you into it. If you feel like it, naturally."
"I'll be there."
Jay nodded and got up to leave. Ian watched him with worry.
"Are you alright?" Ian asked.
"Fine. Just tired."
Ian could see that Jay was in a peculiar mood. He blamed the Gal situation for it. The tension in the office, the guilt Jay clearly felt despite having done nothing wrong.
Ian resolved to deal with the situation quickly. He'd just let Gal go. She was on a probation contract anyway, like any new hire. It wasn't a problem legally or procedurally. But he decided to wait until the end of the month. He didn't want to make ripples and make Jay feel even more guilty before his family arrived. At least this way, Jay would hopefully have a good time that weekend.
Jay spent the rest of the week leaving at five with the rest of the team. He went shopping for groceries, cleaned his apartment again, prepared for his family's visit. On Wednesday, he bought fresh linens for the guest bed. 
Thursday evening, he returned straight home to cook. His mother had called to say they'd departed at two in the afternoon, so they should arrive around six or seven. He intended to welcome them with a homemade meal.
Chapter 40: The storm before the calm
Scene 1
Family visit
Jay stood in the kitchen, checking the oven one last time. The roast chicken was golden brown, the vegetables caramelized at the edges. The soup simmered on the stove, filling the apartment with the scent of herbs and broth. He'd made the kinds of things his mother used to cook when he was young. Simple, hearty food that tasted like home.
His phone buzzed at quarter to seven.
"We're five minutes away," his mother texted.
Jay grabbed his keys and headed downstairs. The evening air was pleasant. He waited at the entrance, watching the traffic until his mother's car pulled up to the curb. The doors opened and chaos erupted.
"Jay!" Ema was the first out, running toward him with her arms outstretched.
"Look at you. Did you grow taller?"
"Maybe a little."
Tim emerged next, followed by Ari, both stretching after the long drive. His mother climbed out last, looking tired but happy.
"There's underground parking. Follow me."
He led them around to the garage entrance, waving at the security camera as the gate rolled open. His mother followed him down the ramp, eyes widening at the spotless garage with its bright lights and neatly numbered bays. Jay walked ahead, gesturing toward a reserved space marked with his name.
“This one’s ours,” he said.
She parked, still taking in the polished surroundings, while the boys jumped out to start unloading the bags from the trunk.
"I can carry mine," Ema insisted, dragging her backpack.
"Let me help," Jay said, taking the heavier cases.
They piled into the elevator. Ari pressed the button for the fourth floor and grinned when the doors closed smoothly.
"This is so fancy," Tim said.
"It's new construction. Everything's still shiny," Jay explained.
The elevator opened into a bright hallway with clean white walls. Jay unlocked his door and held it open.
"Welcome."
They came in, dropping bags and shoes in the entryway. Ema ran straight to the windows.
"You can see the river!"
"And the park. Come on, I'll show you around."
The apartment wasn't large, but it felt spacious. His mother walked through slowly, running her hand along the countertop in the open kitchen.
"It's beautiful. So clean and bright."
Tim and Ari immediately zeroed in on the entertainment console beneath the television.
"Is that the new model?" Tim asked.
"It is."
"Can we play later?"
"After dinner."
Ema was still at the window, pressing her hands against the glass. "I love this view. Can I take pictures?"
"Take as many as you want."
His mother opened the refrigerator, inspecting the contents with approval. "You're eating properly. That's good."
"I cook most nights now."
"I'm glad to hear it. The place suits you. Tidy, organized, but cosy."
"Thank you."
"Now, what smells so good?"
"Dinner's ready. Sit down, I'll serve."
They gathered around the small dining table, which Jay had extended to its full length. He brought out the soup first, ladling it into bowls. Then the chicken, potatoes, and roasted vegetables.
"This looks amazing," his mother said.
"I wanted to make something homey."
They ate with enthusiasm. Ema described her school play. Tim talked about his football team making the regional finals. Ari complained about his maths teacher but admitted he was doing well in history.
"And how's work?" his mother asked Jay.
"Busy. We just finished quarterly reporting."
"That sounds stressful."
"It was. But it's over now."
"Your boss treats you well?"
"Very well. Ian's excellent."
"I'm glad."
After dinner, Ema retrieved a bag from the entryway. "We brought presents."
"You didn't have to—"
"Housewarming and birthday. Open them."
She handed him three painted cache pots, each decorated by one of his siblings. Ema's was covered in tiny flowers and vines. Tim's featured abstract geometric patterns in blue and silver. Ari's had bold orange stripes on a green background.
"We made them for your plant," Ema explained. 
Jay looked at the pots, then at his siblings. "These are wonderful. Thank you."
His mother produced a wrapped package next. "From me."
Inside was a set of high grade kitchen knives. Beneath them were jars of homemade jam and a container of cookies.
"Mom, this is too much—"
"Nonsense. You need good tools if you're cooking regularly. And I know you like my preserves."
Jay set the jars on the counter, then picked up the orange-and-green pot. It was garish and loud, completely unlike anything else in his apartment. But it had been painted by his youngest brother with care.
He retrieved his spider plant and put into the pot. The colours clashed magnificently with the tones of the room, but somehow it worked.
"Looks great," Ema declared.
"It really does," Jay agreed.
They loaded the dishwasher together, his mother insisting on helping despite Jay's protests. Tim and Ari wiped down the counters. Then came the question of sleeping arrangements.
"You and Ema take the bedroom. Tim and Ari can have the couch. I'll sleep on the floor."
"That's not fair to you—"
"It's fine. I've already set everything up."
He showed them the mattress he'd arranged, tucked beside the table with pillows and blankets. It looked cosy enough.
His mother relented. "If you're sure."
"I am."
They prepared for bed. The boys claimed the bathroom first, emerging in sleep clothes and immediately commandeering the console. Ema and his mother took longer.
Jay showered last and returned to find Tim and Ari deeply engaged in a racing game.
"Don't stay up too late," he said.
"We won't."
His mother appeared in the doorway, already in her nightgown. "This is a lovely apartment, Jay. Really. I'm so proud of you."
"Thanks, Mom."
"Sleep well."
"You too."
Jay retreated to his makeshift bed. It was a bit cramped, and he could hear the boys' game, but he didn't mind. He closed his eyes and let the sounds of his family lull him to sleep.
The next morning began with breakfast. Jay made scrambled eggs and toast.
"What's the plan?" Tim asked.
"City tour. I'll show you around. Then lunch, and we'll visit my old neighbourhood."
"Can we ride the metro?" Ari asked.
"Of course."
They left at ten, stepping out into a perfect June day. The weather was warm but not oppressive, with a light breeze coming off the river. Jay led them through the park to the metro station.
"This is so much nicer than our trains," Ema said, marvelling at the clean platforms and modern carriages.
When they reached their stop, Jay led them toward the Northlight Financial Center. The glass tower gleamed in the sunlight, imposing and elegant.
"That's where I work. Thirty-fourth floor."
"It's huge," Tim said.
"Can we go inside?" Ema asked.
"Just the lobby."
They entered through the main doors. The reception area was all marble and polished metal, with a massive chandelier hanging overhead. The receptionist at the desk looked up and smiled.
"Good morning, Mr. Hall." She waved at them cheerfully. His siblings stared.
"She knows your name?" Ari whispered.
"I come through here every day."
"That's so cool."
They didn't linger. Jay ushered them back outside before anyone else could recognize him. His family was impressed enough already.
"Ice cream?" he asked.
"Yes!" Ema said immediately.
He took them to a small artisanal shop near the park, the one with flavours like lavender honey and brown butter. They each chose different ones and ate while walking through the paths. 
"This is really good," his mother said, savouring her pistachio cone.
"I come here sometimes after work."
"It must be nice, living so close to everything."
"It is."
For lunch, he brought them to Willow & Sage. The bistro was busy but they found a table by the window. Jay recommended the seasonal soup and the chicken sandwich. His mother ordered the quiche. The food was excellent, as always. His mother declared the quiche the best she'd ever had. The boys finished their sandwiches quickly and eyed the dessert menu.
"Maybe late. We have more to see."
They walked through the financial district. His siblings took photos of the skyscrapers. Then they took the metro to the old town, emerging in the neighbourhood where Jay had lived when he first moved to the city. The streets were narrower here, the buildings older but charming.
"I rented a studio in that building," Jay said, pointing. "Fourth floor, no elevator."
"How small was it?" Ari asked.
"Very small."
They visited the history museum, which Ari found fascinating, and took photos in front of the landmark statues in the main square.
"One more stop," Jay said.
He led them to Café Lemon. Fay was behind the counter, and she lit up when she saw him.
"Jay! And you brought guests."
"My family. This is my mom, and my siblings—Ema, Tim, and Ari."
"Wonderful to meet you all. What can I get you?"
They ordered tea and pastries. Fay brought everything to their table personally, along with an extra plate of biscuits.
"On the house," she said.
They sat for a while, relaxing after the long day of walking. By the time they left, it was late afternoon. Jay hailed a taxi to take them back across the city. The kids were tired but happy. Jay took them to an upscale restaurant near his building for dinner. Ema looked nervous.
Jay ordered for the table, choosing dishes he knew his family would enjoy. The meal was excellent. His mother praised everything. The kids were on their best behaviour.
After dinner, they walked along the riverside boulevard. The evening was beautiful. Ema and Tim took endless selfies. Ari asked questions about the buildings and Jay answered as best he could. They found a small pier with a fountain and benches arranged around it. 
"My feet are definitely going to fall off," Ema said dramatically, dropping onto a bench.
The boys sat on either side of her. Jay and his mother took the bench opposite. For a few minutes, they just rested. Then his mother spoke.
"I'm very proud of you, Jay. When you first moved here, I worried. You were so young. But look at you now. You have a beautiful home, a good job. You've built a real life here."
"I've been lucky. I found a great job. Good fortune followed."
"It's not just luck. You worked hard. You persevered through difficult times. You should be proud of yourself."
"Thank you, Mom. Let's head home before it gets too late."
By the time they reached the apartment, everyone was thoroughly exhausted.
"Tomorrow I'll take you shopping. You can each pick a birthday present."
"Really?" Ema's eyes lit up.
"Really. Within reason."
"What's reason?" Ari asked.
"You'll find out. Also, my colleagues are planning a small birthday thing tomorrow evening. Just an hour or so at a pub."
"Your work friends?" his mother asked.
"Yes. They insisted on organizing it. I hope that's alright."
"Of course. It sounds lovely."
They prepared for bed again. Jay lay on his mattress on the floor, self-conscious about all the praise, but underneath that, he felt the comfort of having people who cared about him close by.
Scene 2
Affection
Saturday morning began with the chaos of four people trying to use one bathroom. Jay made breakfast while waiting for his turn, scrambling eggs and toasting bread. Ema was already planning her shopping strategy.
"I want to look at everything before deciding," she announced.
"That'll take hours," Tim said.
"So?"
"So the rest of us will die of boredom."
"Then don't come with me."
"We're all going together," their mother said firmly. "And you'll be reasonable about what you choose."
Ema sighed dramatically but nodded.
They took his mother's car, Jay navigating from the passenger seat. The mall was busy but manageable. Ema made a beeline for the clothing stores.
"Can I get a dress?" she asked.
"You can get whatever you want."
Her eyes went wide. His mother immediately intervened.
"Within reason. You don't need six dresses."
"But they're all so pretty—"
"Pick two. And maybe a nice top."
They spent an hour in various shops while Ema deliberated. Tim and Ari sat on a bench outside the store, playing on their phones.
She tried on everything, asked everyone's opinion, changed her mind repeatedly. Eventually she decided on a floral sundress and a more formal navy one, plus a blouse with embroidered sleeves.
The boys were more decisive. Tim wanted new trekking boots and a jersey. Ari asked for books and a model ship kit. 
"You're spoiling them," his mother said.
They loaded their bags into the car and returned to the mall's food court for lunch. The siblings were thrilled with their gifts.
Jay's phone buzzed while he was eating.
"I need to inform you that I'm collecting your family at three. We're going shopping for proper attire before the event." Luc's text read.
Jay stared at the message. His stomach sank.
"What's wrong?" his mother asked.
"Nothing. Just work."
He called Luc back, stepping away from the table.
"What do you mean, proper attire?"
"It's a nice venue. They need nice clothes."
"This is supposed to be casual—"
"It is casual. Casually upscale. Trust me, they'll thank me later."
"Luc—"
"Three o'clock. Don't argue."
The call ended. Jay returned to the table, trying not to look annoyed.
"Everything alright?" his mother asked.
"Fine. My colleague wants to take you shopping this afternoon before the party."
"That's not necessary—"
"I know. But he's insistent."
They finished lunch and returned to the apartment to drop off the bags. Jay had barely set everything down when the intercom buzzed.
"Hello?"
"It's Luc. Let me up."
Jay buzzed him in. A minute later, there was a knock on the door. Jay opened it.
Luc stood there with Leo.
"Hello. We're here for the makeover," Leo said with a huge grin.
Jay stared. "Why are you here?"
"I'm helping."
"That's not an explanation."
"Can we come in?"
Jay stepped aside, deeply suspicious. His family gathered in the living room, looking uncertain.
"This is Luc and Leo. They work with me. Apparently they're taking you shopping."
Leo immediately approached Jay's mother with his most charming smile.
"You must be Mrs. Hall. I'm Leo Turner. Lovely to meet you."
"Oh. Hello."
"And you must be Ema, Tim, and Ari. Jay's told us so much about you."
"He has?" Ema asked.
"Constantly. He's very proud of you all."
Jay felt a headache forming. He grabbed Leo's arm and pulled him into the kitchen bedroom.
"What are you really doing here?"
Leo's smile didn't waver. "Can't I just want to celebrate your birthday?"
"No."
"Fine. I have an ulterior motive."
"I knew it."
"Ian turns forty in September. That's a big milestone. He needs a proper celebration."
"He hates parties."
"Exactly. Which is why I need to soften him up to the idea first. Tonight seems like a perfect opportunity. Small gathering, casual atmosphere. He'll see it's not so terrible."
"You're using my birthday party to manipulate Ian into tolerating his own birthday party?"
"When you say it like that, it sounds bad."
"Because it is bad."
"Jay, listen. Ian never celebrates anything. He never lets anyone do anything nice for him. He spends every birthday working late and pretending it's just another day. Don't you think he deserves better?"
Jay wanted to argue, but he couldn't, because Leo was right.
"He needs more in his life than just work," Leo continued. "You know that as well as I do. Maybe better. So help me out here. Let tonight be a test run. Show him that gatherings with friends can actually be pleasant."
Jay sighed. "Fine. Do whatever you want."
They returned to the living room. Luc had already charmed Jay's siblings and was explaining the plan.
"We'll split up. I'll take Tim and Ari. Leo will take Mrs. Hall and Ema. That way we can cover more ground."
"What about Jay?" his mother asked.
"Ian's picking him up at six. Jay, wear something nice."
Jay's mother looked at him questioningly. Jay nodded.
"Alright then," she said.
Before Jay could protest further, they were herding his family out the door. Ema waved enthusiastically. His mother looked bemused.
The door closed and the apartment fell silent. Jay sighed heavily and checked the time. Two hours.
He tidied up the apartment, then he showered, taking his time.
When he emerged, he dried off and went to his closet. The navy three-piece suit was the obvious choice. He dressed up, adding the sapphire cufflinks and matching tiepin. His hair had been cut recently and still held its shape. He combed it, checking his reflection.
He looked presentable. Hopefully not overdressed.
At quarter to six, he sat on the couch and waited, hoping this wouldn't turn into a disaster. The buzzer rang at ten minutes before six.
Jay opened the door. Ian stood there in a pale gray suit, looking dashing.
"Hello," Ian said.
"Hi. Come in."
Ian stepped inside, closing the door behind him. "You look very nice."
"Thanks. I'm sorry about all this. Luc turned it into an entire production."
"That's Luc's nature."
"I know, but still. It was supposed to be casual." Jay caught himself before he complained too much. The whole point was to get Ian comfortable with celebrations. "I mean, it's fine. I'm sure it'll be nice."
"I'm sure it will be."
Ian reached into his jacket and produced a small wrapped box.
"What's that?"
"A gift."
"You already gave me a gift."
"That was for your home. But since there's a party, I couldn't arrive empty-handed."
"Ian—"
"Please."
Jay took the box and unwrapped it. Inside was a set of cufflinks and a tiepin, white gold and onyx. The design was modern, geometric, more serious than the sapphire set.
"These are beautiful," Jay whispered.
"I thought they'd suit you."
Jay felt heat rise in his face. It wasn't the first time Ian had given him a gift, but this moment felt different, more weighted. His heart was beating too fast.
"Thank you," he managed.
He tried to remove the sapphire cufflinks he was wearing, but his hands trembled slightly. He fumbled with the mechanism.
"Here," Ian said gently. "Let me."
He took Jay's wrist, removing the cufflink. Then the other one. He replaced them with the new set.
Jay stood frozen, watching Ian's hands work. The touch was light, but the intimacy of the gesture made his breath catch.
Ian finished with the cufflinks and reached for the tiepin.
"May I?"
Jay nodded, not trusting his voice.
Ian removed the sapphire pin and replaced it with the onyx one, adjusting it so it sat perfectly straight. His fingers brushed Jay's chest through the fabric.
"There," Ian said.
Jay looked up and found Ian watching him. Their faces were close. Then Ian stepped back.
"We should go."
"Yes," Jay agreed, his voice slightly unsteady.
They left the apartment together. Ian's car was parked at the curb. He opened the passenger door for Jay.
Jay watched the city pass outside the window, acutely aware of Ian beside him. His wrist still felt warm where Ian had touched it. His hands were still trembling.
"Are you nervous?" Ian asked.
"A little. My family meeting my colleagues feels strange."
"They'll get along well."
"I hope so."
Jay glanced at Ian's profile, at his hands resting on the steering wheel.
He thought about those same hands fastening his cufflinks, adjusting the tiepin. The focused expression on Ian's face, the gentleness of his touch. Jay looked away quickly, his heart racing again.
This was dangerous territory. He knew that. But it felt like something more than mentorship.
It felt like affection.
Ian pulled into a parking space near the venue. He turned off the engine but didn't immediately move to leave.
"Jay."
"Yes?"
"I hope you have a good evening. You deserve to be celebrated."
"Thank you. For everything. Not just tonight. For all of it."
"It's my pleasure."
They sat for a moment longer, then Ian opened his door. "Shall we?"
"Yes."
They walked to the venue together, close but not touching, both maintaining the line neither was brave enough to cross.
Scene 3
Celebration
Jay stopped in his tracks when he saw the restaurant's entrance.
"The Palladium."
"Yes."
"Did Leo organized this?"
"I believe so."
Jay pushed through the doors and immediately spotted Leo holding a glass of wine near the private dining room entrance. He sent him a vicious glare. Leo smiled innocently and raised his glass in greeting.
The private room was elegant without being oppressively formal, servers moving discreetly between guests. Still, it was one of the most expensive restaurants in the city.
"Jay!"
His mother approached, and Jay barely recognized her. She wore a deep green dress that skimmed her knees, her hair styled and wearing makeup. Ema was beside her in a cream-coloured dress with delicate beading, looking thrilled with herself.
"You both look beautiful," Jay said.
"Leo's stylist was very skilled," his mother said. There was wariness in her voice, like she wasn't entirely comfortable with the situation.
Tim and Ari appeared next, both in well-cut suits that somehow managed to look relaxed despite being clearly designer. Tim tugged at his collar.
"These are really comfortable. I thought fancy suits were supposed to be stiff."
"You look very handsome," Jay assured him.
His mother stepped back, examining Jay with obvious pride. "Look at you. So refined."
"Mom—"
"You look like you belong here," Ema added.
"I don't know about that—"
"You do. I'm so proud," his mother insisted.
"Thank you."
Rus appeared, grinning widely, wearing a burgundy shirt and black trousers that actually fit properly. Beside him, Mic and Van were similarly well-dressed.
"Luc's work?" Jay asked.
"Obviously. Happy birthday."
"Thanks."
Luc himself was near the bar with his flatmate Gav. He waved at Jay. Most of Jay's colleagues were already present—Zoe, Amy, Max, Ela, and Kim clustered together near the windows. And Rin, standing awkwardly in the corner by himself.
Jay made his way over.
"Rin. I'm glad you came."
Rin looked relieved. "Luc said I should. I hope that's alright."
"Of course it is."
"I wasn't sure if—since I'm still new—"
"You're part of the team. Stay as long as you like."
Rin relaxed slightly.
Jay's mother approached, watching the exchange. After Rin wandered toward the drinks table, she whispered.
"That young man works for you?"
"He's a junior analyst. I've been helping him."
"You have subordinates." She sounded surprised.
Ian stood near the entrance, surveying the room. Jay's mother looked at him. Her expression cooled slightly.
"That's your boss."
"Yes. Ian Hagen."
"He seems very formal."
Ian noticed them and approached. "Mrs. Carter. It's a pleasure to meet you properly."
"Mr. Hagen." Her tone was polite but notably lacking in warmth.
"I hope you're enjoying the city."
"Jay's been an excellent guide."
"I'm sure he has."
There was a pause. Jay could feel the tension, the way his mother's shoulders had gone slightly rigid. Ian seemed to sense it too. He didn't push for more conversation, simply inclined his head and said, "Please let me know if you need anything this evening."
"Thank you."
He moved away, giving them space. Jay watched him go, then looked at his mother.
"He's a good person."
"I'm sure he is."
"Mom—"
"I'm being polite, aren't I?"
"You're being vigilant."
"Rich, powerful men don't do things without reasons, Jay. I just want to make sure his reasons are good ones."
"They are. I promise."
"Alright. I trust your judgement."
The servers began directing people toward their seats. The room had been arranged with several tables, allowing for smaller conversations. Before they sat, Jay caught Ema talking animatedly with Leo, who was telling some story that made her laugh. She pulled out her phone, clearly asking for his social media.
Jay moved quickly.
"Ema—"
"Leo's so interesting! He was just telling me about art galleries."
"That's nice, but—"
"She's a delightful conversationalist," Leo said innocently.
Jay gave him a warning look. Leo's expression remained perfectly pleasant.
"Maybe talk to some other people too."
"I will!"
She bounded off toward the drinks table. Jay turned to Leo.
"Don't be a bad influence."
"I would never."
"I mean it."
"She's perfectly safe with me. I promise."
Jay wasn't entirely convinced, but he couldn't make a scene, so he let it go.
Across the room, Tim was looking lost. Jay went over.
"You alright?"
"Yeah. Just—everyone's very sophisticated."
"You see that woman over there? With the blonde hair? That's Zoe. She likes hiking and camping. You should talk to her."
Tim's face brightened. "Really?"
"Really. Go on."
Tim approached Zoe hesitantly. Within minutes they were deep in conversation, both pulling out their phones to show each other photos. Zoe was gesturing enthusiastically about some trail. Tim was grinning.
Ari, meanwhile, had planted himself firmly behind their mother and showed no signs of moving.
Jay knelt down beside him. "You can sit with me at dinner if you want."
"It's very fancy here."
"I know. But everyone's friendly. And the food will be good."
"Okay."
Jay guided him to the seat beside his own. Ari sat and looked around with wide eyes.
Dinner was served in courses. The food was exceptional, though Jay barely tasted it. He was too busy watching his family and colleagues mingle, making sure everyone was comfortable.
His mother sat with Max and Amy, both of whom were working hard to put her at ease. Max's natural joviality helped. Amy's sincere questions about the family seemed to thaw some of her reserve.
Ema had collected half the room's social media information and was taking endless selfies with anyone who would stand still long enough. Tim and Zoe had become fast friends, now discussing trail safety and wildlife photography. Luc was entertaining Rin, who laughed at something Luc said.
Gav had joined Rus's group and they were all getting progressively louder.
And Ian—
Ian was watching Jay with a tender expression. Their eyes met across the room. Ian smiled. Jay smiled back.
Leo appeared at Ian's shoulder, murmuring something. Ian's expression turned sour immediately. Leo grinned and moved away.
After dinner came the cake, a beautiful three-tiered creation that was absolutely unnecessary but looked spectacular. Everyone sang. Jay endured it with good humour, blowing out the candles while Ema recorded everything on her phone.
Then came the gifts. His colleagues presented a stack of packages that Jay promised to open later. Rus and his friends handed over a big bag that was suspiciously heavy. Gav produced an elegant bottle.
"Thank you," Jay said. "Really. This is too much."
"Nonsense," Max said. 
Jay circulated through the room, receiving wishes and making small talk. He checked on Rin, who was looking less anxious. He thanked Leo for organizing everything, despite his lingering annoyance about the venue choice.
He found Ian standing near the windows.
"How are you holding up?" 
"Very well."
"You're not bored?"
"Not at all."
"My mother's being cold to you. I'm sorry."
"It's alright. She's protective. She has every right to be cautious around someone like me."
"She just—she's not a fan of rich people generally."
"I understand."
"It's not personal."
"I know. She loves you. She wants to make sure you're safe. I respect that."
"Thank you for being here."
They stood together for a moment, watching the party continue around them.
"You're happy," Ian said.
"I am."
"Good. You should be surrounded by people who care about you more often."
"So should you."
Ian's smile turned slightly sad. "Perhaps."
By ten o'clock, people were starting to fade. Ari had fallen asleep with his head on the table. Tim and Ema were still energetic, but their mother looked tired.
"We should head home," Jay said.
"I arranged a car. They'll take you wherever you need to go," Leo said.
"You didn't have to do that," Jay said.
"Of course I did. Can't have you taking the metro after your birthday party."
"Thank you."
"You're welcome. And Jay? I'm glad you had a good time."
"I did. Despite the venue."
"The venue was perfect."
"It was obscenely expensive."
"It was appropriate. Accept nice things graciously."
Jay shook his head but smiled. "Thanks, Leo."
"Happy birthday."
They gathered coats and bags. Jay's colleagues said their goodbyes, each offering warm wishes. Rus hugged him enthusiastically.
"Best party," he declared.
"I'm glad you enjoyed it."
Sunday afternoon arrived too quickly.
Jay's family had packed their bags and loaded the car. They'd had a late lunch together, lingering over the meal as long as possible. Now they stood in the apartment's entryway, ready to leave.
"Drive safely," Jay said.
"We will." His mother hugged him tightly. "Take care of yourself."
"I will."
Ema squeezed him next. "Can I visit again?"
"Anytime."
Tim and Ari both hugged him in turn. Then they were gone. Jay went back inside and started unpacking the birthday gifts. Advanced academic textbooks from his colleagues, topics ranging from financial theory to market analysis. Various pre-workout powders and supplements from Rus, Mic, and Van. A bottle of expensive whiskey from Gav with a note apologizing again for the disastrous blind date.
And an envelope from Leo. Inside was a handwritten note on expensive stationery.
"Happy birthday. Your new jewellery is remarkable and you should have more pieces to properly present it. Appointment scheduled for next Saturday at noon. Don't be late. - L"
It was for the same tailor as last time. Jay stared at the note. He wasn't sure if he was more mad or amused.
Scene 4
Downfall
The week after Jay's birthday party should have been pleasant. Instead, it became increasingly uncomfortable. Gal had stopped hovering around Jay's desk. At first, it seemed like Ian's intervention had worked.
But by Tuesday, Jay noticed something else. Gal was watching him. Waiting for opportunities when he was alone. In the break room. By the printer.
He started avoiding those situations. He started taking coffee back to his desk before she could approach and printing documents only when others were around.
Ian noticed. Jay could see him watching through the glass wall, his expression growing increasingly concerned.
Thursday afternoon, Jay was in the archive room, retrieving files. The space was small, lined with shelves and filing cabinets.
He was reaching for a folder on the top shelf when he heard the door open.
"Jay."
He turned. Gal stepped inside and closed the door.
"Hi," he said cautiously.
"I've been wanting to talk to you."
"I'm a bit busy right now—"
"It won't take long." 
"Gal, this isn't—"
"I just want to know why you're avoiding me."
"I'm not—"
"You are. Ever since Ian spoke to me, you've barely looked at me. Is it because of what I said at the party? Because I was drunk and I didn't mean—"
"It's fine. Really. But I need to get back to work—"
She moved closer. Too close.
"Gal, please step back."
"I just want us to be friends again—"
"We are. But right now I need space."
"Why? What did I do wrong?"
She reached for his arm. Jay pulled away, but the shelves were behind him and there was nowhere to go.
"Don't touch me."
"I'm just trying to talk—"
"Gal, leave the room. Now."
Her expression crumpled. "You're just like everyone else. You think you're better than me—"
"I don't think that—"
"You do. You all do."
She turned and stormed out. The door slammed behind her. Jay stood frozen, his heart pounding. That had been bad.
He took a long moment to collect himself, then gathered the files and headed back.
When he reached his desk, the atmosphere was wrong. People were clustered in small groups, whispering. Rin looked stricken. Zoe was on her phone, her expression grim.
Ian emerged from his office.
“Jay. I need you to come with me. Conference room.”
Jay's blood turned to ice. "What happened?"
“I promise everything will be all right. But I need you to come with me now.”
Jay followed him. Inside were two people—an older, stern woman from HR and a man with a security badge he didn't recognise.
"This is Ms. Miller from HR and Mr. Oakford from security. Ms. Gray has filed a complaint."
Jay's legs nearly gave out. "What kind of complaint?"
"She's claiming you assaulted her in the archive room."
The world tilted. "What? No. I didn't—she came in while I was working and got too close and I told her to leave—"
"Please sit down," Ian said.
Jay sat. His hands were shaking.
Ms. Miller opened a tablet. "Mr. Hall, I need you to describe exactly what happened. Every detail."
Jay recounted the entire interaction. His voice was level but his mind was screaming. This couldn't be happening.
"The archive room has surveillance cameras. We're reviewing the footage now."
"Cameras?" Jay repeated.
"Every room in this building has them."
Ten minutes later, Mr. Oakford returned with his tablet. He placed it on the table and pressed play.
The footage showed everything. Jay reaching for files. Gal entering. The conversation, audio included. Her moving closer despite his clear discomfort. Him asking her to leave. Her storming out. At no point did Jay touch her.
Ms. Miller's expression was glacial. "Where is Ms. Gray now?"
"Still in the building. Waiting in Conference Room D."
"Bring her here."
Five minutes later, Gal entered. She saw the tablet and went pale.
"Ms. Gray. You filed a false report claiming Mr. Hall assaulted you. The surveillance footage shows this is completely untrue."
"I—he—"
"You have ten minutes to collect your belongings. Security will escort you out. Your termination is effective immediately. Our legal team will be in contact regarding this incident."
Gal's face went white, then red. "You can't—"
"We can and we are. Mr. Oakford, please escort her."
Security took her out. She didn't look at Jay.
Ms. Miller turned to him. "Mr. Hall, I apologize for putting you through this. The complaint has been dismissed and removed from any records. If you need time off or would like to speak with a counselor, those resources are available."
"Thank you," Jay said automatically.
"Mr. Hagen, if you have nothing to add, I'll file my report."
"I have nothing to add. Thank you for your swift response."
Ms. Miller left. The conference room fell silent. Jay stared at the table. His hands were still shaking.
"Jay."
He looked up. Ian's expression was calm, but his eyes were furious.
"Are you alright?"
"Yes."
"You don't look alright."
"I'm fine. It's resolved."
"That doesn't mean you're fine."
Jay's throat was tight. "I will be."
"I'm taking you home."
"That's not necessary—"
"Yes, it is. Get your things."
Jay didn't have the strength to argue. He collected his bag from his desk, ignoring the concerned looks from his colleagues. 
Ian drove in silence. When they reached Jay's building, he parked and turned off the engine.
"Do you want me to come up?"
"No. Thank you. I just need to rest."
"Call me if you need anything."
"I will."
Jay went inside. He dropped his bag by the door and sat on the couch, staring at nothing.
It was resolved. Gal was gone. The legal team would handle the rest. But his hands wouldn't stop shaking.
He'd recommended her for the position. He'd been the one to tell Ian she was promising. And she'd tried to destroy him.
His phone rang at seven. It was Leo.
"I'm outside. Let me in."
"I'm fine—"
"Let me in."
"You’re the last person I want to see right now."
"No one in this company has as much experience with failed hires as I do, so I figured I’m the best man to share the current pain with."
Jay sighed and buzzed him up. Leo arrived with a bag of takeout  and a determined expression.
"You're eating dinner," Leo announced.
"I'm not hungry."
"You're eating anyway."
He unpacked containers of food and set them on the table. 
"Ian told me what happened," Leo said.
"It's resolved."
"That doesn't make it less awful."
Jay said nothing.
Leo sat back. "This isn't the first time something like this has happened at the company. We've had similar incidents over the years. One was particularly bad. Do you want to hear about it?"
"Why would I want to hear about it?"
"Because sometimes it helps to know you're not alone."
"Fine."
Leo told him about a junior analyst who'd falsified reports, blamed a senior colleague, and tried to blackmail their way into a promotion. About a secretary who'd stolen proprietary information and claimed harassment when caught. About an intern who'd vandalized equipment and accused a manager of creating a hostile work environment.
"People do terrible things when they're desperate or entitled or both. It's not your fault for not seeing it coming."
"I recommended her."
"You recommended someone who interviewed well and seemed competent. How were you supposed to know she'd turn out like this?"
"I should have—"
"Jay. Stop."
Jay looked at him.
"Something similar happened to Ian. Years ago, when he was newer to the company. A coworker accused him of inappropriate conduct."
Jay went still. "What?"
"We didn't have as many cameras back then. It turned into a nasty legal battle. Took months to resolve."
"What happened?"
"Eventually we found enough evidence to disprove the claims. But it was brutal. Depositions, interviews, the whole thing dragged through the mud."
"How did Ian handle it?"
"He was stoic. Unruffled, at least on the surface. But this was shortly after his scandal. I think he just expected people to be scum. Like it was confirmation of what he already believed about the world."
"That's horrible."
"It is. But it's also why we installed cameras everywhere after that. Why we have strict protocols. So this kind of thing gets shut down immediately instead of dragging on."
"So I got lucky."
"You got protected by systems we built specifically to protect people."
"Is Ian alright? This must have brought back bad memories."
"He's more concerned about you than himself."
Jay picked at his food. His appetite was still gone, but he forced himself to eat.
Leo stayed for another hour, telling ridiculous stories about past company disasters until Jay almost smiled despite everything.
"Thank you. For coming over."
"Anytime. And Jay? Take tomorrow off. Don't argue."
When Leo finally left, Jay felt marginally better.
He did take Friday off. He spent the day in his apartment, alternating between restless pacing and sitting motionless on the couch.
His mind kept returning to the same thoughts in circles.
He'd recommended Gal, and she'd tried to ruin him. What did that say about his judgment? 
By Saturday, the spiraling thoughts had calmed down.
This was just another ordeal to endure if he wanted to stay where he was. And he did want to stay.
He wanted to stay by Ian's side. More than he wanted safety or certainty or freedom from complications. No matter how difficult it became. No matter what he had to endure.
That resolve gave him strength.
Scene 5
Even if it hurts
Jay went to the gym on Saturday morning. Rus was there, as always, cheerful and loud.
"How was the rest of your birthday weekend?"
"Fine."
"Your family get home safe?"
"Yeah."
"You alright?"
"Just tired. Long week."
It wasn't exactly a lie. The situation with Gal felt too raw to discuss, too complicated to explain, too close to things he'd spent years trying to forget. Rus seemed to sense this and didn't push. They worked out, then parted ways with plans to meet again next week.
Jay spent the rest of the weekend at home. Cleaning. Reading. Sitting on the balcony and watching the river. Preparing himself mentally for Monday. Monday morning was warm and sunny. Jay dressed in his best office suit. He needed the armour today.
He arrived at his desk and started working. Everything felt normal on the surface. Inside, he was a mess. His facade was paper thin. At ten, Zoe stopped by his desk.
"For what it's worth, everyone knows what actually happened. You're not under suspicion."
"That's good."
"And we've all dealt with problem hires before. It's not your fault."
Jay nodded. Zoe squeezed his shoulder and left.
At lunch, Max sat down across from him.
"When I was your age, I recommended someone for a position. They lasted three weeks before getting caught stealing equipment. I felt terrible about it for months."
"What did you do?"
"Eventually accepted that I'd made the best decision I could with the information I had. That's all anyone can do."
Their support helped. But it didn't erase the underlying guilt or the uncomfortable feeling that Jay had caused this entire situation.
Ian sat in his office and watched Jay through the glass wall.
Jay was working. Typing, reviewing documents, responding to messages. But his shoulders were tense, like he was holding himself together through sheer willpower.
Ian felt fury at himself. He should have fired Gal immediately after the quarterly party incident. He'd delayed, wanting to minimize disruption, wanting to avoid making Jay feel guilty before his family's visit. And that delay had resulted in this.
Jay had been cornered. Subjected to an HR investigation. All because Ian had made a calculated decision to wait.
The cameras had resolved it quickly, but the damage was done. Jay had experienced that moment of terror, that feeling of being falsely accused with no way to defend himself. Ian knew that feeling intimately. It left scars. He'd caused this. His choice. His failure to act decisively.
Ian stood and walked to Jay's desk. Jay looked up.
"Do you have a moment?"
"Of course."
They went to Ian's office. Ian closed the door and gestured to the chair. Jay sat.
"I wanted to check in. Make sure you're managing alright."
"I'm fine."
"Jay."
"Really. It's resolved. Everyone's been supportive. I'm handling it."
Jay's smile was brittle. Everything about him screamed that he was not fine, but Ian didn't want to push. He didn't want to make it worse by forcing a conversation Jay clearly wasn't ready for.
"If you need anything. Time off, adjusted responsibilities, anything at all. Tell me."
"I will. Thank you."
"I mean it."
"I know."
They returned to work, but Ian couldn't stop worrying.
Tuesday was worse. Jay's facade was crumbling. He stared at his screen without typing. Wednesday was the same. Ian couldn't stand it anymore.
He waited until the end of the workday. He watched Jay leave the office and gave him thirty minutes to get home, then he followed.
He knew it was crossing a line. Showing up uninvited at Jay's apartment was not something a boss did for an employee. But he couldn't just stand by and watch Jay spiral.
Jay opened the door looking surprised. 
"Ian. Is something wrong?"
"Can I come in?"
"Of course."
Ian stepped inside. The apartment was tidy, as always.
"I was worried," Ian said.
"I'm fine."
"You're not."
"I will be."
"Jay. Please. Talk to me."
They sat on the couch. Jay pulled his knees up, wrapping his arms around them.
Ian was terrified that Jay was thinking about leaving again. That this incident had been the final straw. That Ian was about to lose him.
"I keep thinking about how I recommended her. How I thought she'd be good for the team."
"You couldn't have known."
"But I should have seen something."
"There was nothing to see. She interviewed well. She performed adequately for weeks. Then she made terrible choices. That's not your failure."
Jay didn't respond. He looked small and miserable. Ian wanted to take away Jay's pain, to make everything better.
"When Leo first started hiring assistants for me, most of them were disasters. One tried to seduce me within the first week. Another stole files and sold them to a competitor. Another spread rumors that I was having affairs with half the executive team."
Jay looked up. "Really?"
"Really. Leo thought it was hilarious. I thought it was a nightmare. But it taught me something. People are complicated. Sometimes they interview well and perform terribly. Sometimes they seem harmless and turn out to be dangerous. You can't predict it. You can only respond when it becomes clear."
"I thought Gal was just awkward."
"So did I. That's why I didn't act sooner. I underestimated the danger because she seemed so uncertain and unthreatening. That was my mistake, not yours. I'm sorry this happened to you. I'm sorry I didn't prevent it."
"It's not your fault."
"It is. I should have removed her immediately after the party incident. I chose to wait and that choice led to this."
"You were trying to be fair."
"I was trying to avoid disruption. And in doing so, I created worse disruption."
Ian wanted to understand what Jay was thinking. He wanted to ask if he was planning to leave. 
Instead he said, "It will get better. The gossip will fade. The memory will become just another unpleasant incident that happened and was resolved."
Jay's eyes were bright with unshed tears. "Thank you."
Ian stood to leave. He'd crossed a line by coming here uninvited, but he couldn't regret it. Not when Jay looked like he was drowning.
At the door, Ian turned back. Jay was looking so profoundly sad that Ian couldn't stop himself. He closed the distance and pulled Jay into a hug.
Jay went still for a heartbeat. Then he clung back desperately, his arms wrapping tight around Ian's waist, his face pressed against Ian's shoulder.
Ian held him like something precious and fragile. Jay's breathing was uneven. They stood like that for a long time, neither speaking, just holding on. When they finally separated, Jay's cheeks were flushed.
"Do you remember, back in the winter, we planned that outing, but it got cancelled because of the client meeting," Jay asked.
"I remember."
"Can we still do that? Someday?"
"Yes. Whenever you want."
Jay nodded. He looked like he wanted to say something else but couldn't find the words.
Ian wanted to stay. He wanted to pull Jay back into his arms and never let go. But he also knew that would be risking too much.
"I'll see you tomorrow," Ian said.
"Tomorrow."
Ian left. The door closed behind him. He stood in the hallway, leaning against the wall beside Jay's door. Inside, Jay leaned against the same wall. Both of them separated by mere centimetres. Both longing for the other. Both battling that longing.
Ian was terrified. Jay's request had sounded desperate, like a farewell, like someone trying to capture one last good memory before leaving. Ian couldn't bear the thought of losing him. He would fight for this. Make things better. Make sure Jay wanted to stay.
Jay was simply tired. Tired of wanting something he believed he could never have. But also determined to stay. To collect these sweet moments, these brief touches. Even if it hurt. Even if it was hopeless.
Jay slid down the wall and sat on the floor. The warmth of that embrace still lingered on his skin. He wanted more. He wanted everything.
But he would take what he could have. And he would be grateful for it.
